The Argument for Matter from Possibility and Its Refutation
Taftazani presents the philosophers' argument for matter from possibility, then refutes it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the philosophers argue for the existence of matter from the concept of possibility and how the author refutes it by distinguishing between essential and dispositional possibility.

Core Idea
Not all possibility is an existent attribute; the argument equivocates on 'possibility'.

The philosophers argue that before a contingent thing exists, it is possible, and this possibility must inhere in a substrate, which is matter. The author counters this by showing that 'possibility' can mean two different things: essential possibility, which is not a real attribute, and dispositional possibility, which is not present in every contingent. This lesson breaks down the argument and its refutation step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument for matter
Read the first part of the passage where the philosophers reason from possibility to the necessity of a substrate.
Notice that they consider possibility an 'existent attribute' (wujūdī).
2
Identify the refutation's structure
The author refutes by disambiguating 'possibility' into two types. Identify these two types and how each undermines the argument.
Essential vs. dispositional possibility is the key.
3
Understand the implications
Consider why the distinction matters for kalam cosmology. The philosophers need an existent attribute to prove matter, but the author shows the premise is false or the conclusion doesn't follow.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما المادة فلأنه قبل الوجود ممكن، وإمكانه وجودي يفتقر إلى الحل، وليس هو الحادث لامتناع تقدم الشيء على نفسه، بخلاف إمكان القديم. ورد بأنه إن أريد الإمكان الذاتي فلا نسلم أنه وجودي، وإن أريد الاستعدادي المخالف له في اقتضاء الرجحان والتفاوت والتحقق فلا نسلم أن كل حادث ممكن.
As for matter, it is because before existence it is possible, and its possibility is an existent attribute that requires a substrate, and it is not the contingent itself, because it is impossible for a thing to precede itself, unlike the possibility of the eternal. And it is rebutted that if by possibility is meant essential possibility, we do not concede that it is existent; and if is meant dispositional possibility, which differs from it in requiring preponderance, variation, and actualization, then we do not concede that every contingent is possible.
Source pages p. 14

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' first argument for the existence of matter and the author's refutation. The argument runs: Before its existence, a contingent (ḥādith) is possible (mumkin). This possibility (imkān) is an existent attribute (wujūdī) that requires a substrate (ḥall). This substrate cannot be the contingent itself, because that would make a thing precede itself. Therefore, the substrate must be matter (mādda). The possibility of the eternal (qadīm) is different because the eternal has no such state before existence. The author then rebuts this argument by disambiguating two types of possibility. If 'possibility' refers to essential (dhātī) possibility—the mere absence of impossibility—then the author denies that it is an existent attribute (it is a conceptual, not real, property). If it refers to dispositional (istiʿdādī) possibility—a real preparedness that admits degrees and variation—then the author denies that every contingent has such a disposition (since some contingents come into existence without a prior disposition). Hence, the argument fails on both interpretations.

إمكان
ذاتي
استعدادي
حل
قديم
حدوث
وجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that 'possibility' always refers to a real property that requires a substrate.
Correction: The author clarifies that 'essential possibility' is merely conceptual and not an existent attribute, while 'dispositional possibility' is real but not present in every contingent.

Checkpoint
What is the key distinction that refutes the philosophers' argument for matter from possibility?
A) The difference between essential and accidental possibility.
B) The difference between essential and dispositional possibility.
C) The difference between potential and actual possibility.
D) The difference between intrinsic and extrinsic possibility.

Key Takeaway
The argument for matter from possibility fails because possibility is ambiguous: essential possibility is not an existent attribute, and dispositional possibility is not universal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about your own existence before you were born, do you imagine it as a 'possible' thing waiting to happen? Is that assumption justified, or does it confuse a mere conceptual possibility with a real attribute? What does this lesson teach you about the danger of assuming that what you think about something is actually real?
?
Have you ever taken a concept—like 'possibility' or 'potential'—and treated it as a real thing that must have a location? How might that error lead you to believe in something that doesn't exist? Examine your own thinking: where else might you be making this same mistake?
?
This lesson shows that a single word ('imkān') can carry two radically different meanings. When you read the Qur'an or listen to religious arguments, how do you guard against equivocation? What inner discipline do you need to develop to not be deceived by ambiguous terms?

Second Proof for Matter from Possibility
Taftazani presents a second proof for matter and answers an objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Taftazani's second proof for the necessity of matter and how he responds to the objection from eternal possibles.

Core Idea
Possibility is an accident needing a subject—that subject is matter.

This lesson explores a deeper argument for the existence of matter based on the nature of possibility. Taftazani expands on the idea that possibility (imkān) is a real attribute that must inhere in something, leading to the concept of a material substrate. He also anticipates a crucial objection concerning eternal possibles and provides a nuanced response. This passage is pivotal for understanding how classical theologians reasoned about the building blocks of creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to get an overview. Notice the flow from proof to objection and answer.
2
Identify the main argument
Trace the logical steps: contingent before existence is possible, possibility is an existent accident, accidents require a subject, that subject is matter.
Pay attention to why possibility cannot be a substance.
3
Analyze the refutation
Find where Taftazani refutes the idea that possibility is the same as divine power. Understand the direction of dependence: power depends on possibility, not vice versa.
4
Examine the objection and response
Read the objection about eternal possibles (e.g., primordial matter) that are possible but lack matter. See how Taftazani responds by distinguishing their intrinsic possibility.
The key point: eternal possibles have no 'before existence' state, so their possibility inheres in themselves.
5
Summarize the lesson
Write a one-paragraph summary of the proof and its defense in your own words, ensuring you capture the role of matter.
This will help solidify the concept.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتجوا على ثبوت المادة بأن الحادث قبل وجوده ممكن لامتناع الانقلاب وكل ممكن فله إمكان، وهو وجودي لما سبق من الأدلة، وليس بجوهر لكونه إضافياً بحقيقته، فيكون عرضاً، فيستدعي محلاً موجوداً ليس هو نفس ذلك الحادث، لامتناع تقدم الشيء على نفسه، ولا أمراً منفصلاً عنه، لأنه لا معنى لقيام إمكان الشيء بالأمر المنفصل عنه بل متعلقاً به، وهو المعنى بالمادة. وما توهم من أن إمكان الشيء هو اقتدار الفاعل عليه فيكون قائماً بالفاعل فاسداً، لأنه معلل بالإمكان وعدمه بعدمه، فيقال هذا مقدور لكونه ممكناً، وذاك غير مقدور لكونه ممتنعاً، ولأنه لا يكون إلا بالقياس إلى القادر بخلاف الإمكان. فإن قيل: الدليل منقوض بالممكن القديم كالمواد والمجردات، فإنها ممكنة، ولا مادة لها. قلنا: إمكاناتها قائمة بها إذ ليس للقديم حالة ما قبل الوجود، حتى يكون هناك إمكان يستدعي محلا غيره.
They argued for the existence of matter by [saying] that the contingent before its existence is possible because of the impossibility of change, and every possible has a possibility, and it is existent based on previous proofs, and it is not a substance because it is relational in its essence, so it is an accident, thus it requires an existing subject that is not the contingent itself (due to impossibility of a thing preceding itself) nor something separate from it, because there is no meaning to the subsistence of a thing's possibility in something separate from it, but rather it inheres in it, and that is what is meant by matter. And the misconception that a thing's possibility is the power of the agent over it, so it subsists in the agent, is false because it [power] is explained by possibility and its non-existence by its non-existence; so it is said this is within power because it is possible, and that is not within power because it is impossible, and because it [power] is only relative to the capable, unlike possibility. Then it is said: the proof is refuted by the eternal possible, such as matter and intellects, for they are possible and have no matter. We say: their possibilities subsist in themselves, because the eternal has no state before existence such that there is a possibility requiring another subject.
Source pages p. 15

Commentary
This passage presents a second, more developed proof for the necessity of matter and addresses a key objection. The proof: Every contingent before existence is possible, and possibility (imkān) is an existent attribute (as argued earlier). Since possibility is relational (iḍāfī) by nature, it is not a substance but an accident, and thus requires a subject (maḥall) that is (a) not the contingent itself (to avoid self-precedence) and (b) not separate from it (since an accident cannot exist in a separate entity). This subject is what the philosophers call matter. The passage then refutes a false notion that identifies possibility with divine power (iqtidār); it argues that power is explained by possibility (i.e., something is within power because it is possible), not the reverse. Therefore, possibility cannot subsist in the agent. The passage then presents an objection: The proof is undermined by eternal possibles (al-mumkin al-qadīm) such as the primordial matter (al-mawādd) and separate intellects (al-mujarradāt), which are possible yet have no matter. The answer is that their possibility is intrinsic: they have no 'before existence' state that would require an external substrate; rather, their possibility is a property that subsists in themselves (qāʾima bihā). Thus, the proof only applies to contingents that come into existence after non-existence.

Key Terms

إمكان
عرض
جوهر
محل
مادة
اقتدار
قادر
ممكن قديم
مجردات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Possibility is the same as divine power (iqtidār), so it subsists in the agent (God).
Correction: Power depends on possibility: we say something is within power because it is possible, not the reverse. Also, power is relative to the capable, while possibility is not.

Checkpoint
How does the author refute the objection that eternal possibles (e.g., primordial matter) are possible yet have no matter?
A) He denies that eternal possibles are truly possible.
B) He argues that their possibility subsists in God's power.
C) He states that their possibility is intrinsic, as they have no 'before existence' state.
D) He concedes the objection and redefines matter.

Key Takeaway
Taftazani's second proof establishes matter as the necessary subject for possibility, and the intrinsic nature of possibility in eternal entities avoids the objection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your existence right now is a contingent possibility. If that possibility is an accident requiring a subject, what is that subject? Is it your body, your soul, or something else? And how does that make you feel about your own fragility?
?
The passage says that possibility is not the same as power—your ability to act depends on your possibility. Are there areas in your life where you assume you have power but lack the possibility? What would change if you acknowledged that your limits are set by possibility, not by effort?
?
Taftazani insists that possibility is a real attribute, not a mere mental construct. If possibility is real, then your potential is real. Are you treating your own possibilities as real obligations to act, or as abstract daydreams? What is one possibility you have that you are ignoring today?

Three Distinctions Between Essential and Preparatory Possibility
The author enumerates three distinctions between essential and preparatory possibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three key distinctions between essential possibility (al-imkan al-dhati) and preparatory possibility (al-imkan al-isti'dadi), and why this distinction matters for the study of contingency.

Core Idea
Essential possibility is a rational attribute; preparatory possibility is a real quality that admits degrees.

In this passage, the author responds to an objection about the nature of possibility. He clarifies that there are two distinct types: essential possibility, which is a necessary attribute of the quiddity of a possible being, and preparatory possibility, which is a real quality in matter that prepares it for change. He then draws three precise distinctions between them, each highlighting a different aspect of their difference. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for grasping the logic of contingency and change.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two types
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Note how the author first sets up the two types of possibility: essential and preparatory. This sets the stage for the distinctions that follow.
The author starts with an objection and then clarifies the two meanings of 'possibility'.
2
Examine the first distinction
The first distinction is that essential possibility does not imply preponderance of existence or non-existence—both are equal with respect to it. In contrast, preparatory possibility implies a preponderance because it is a state that brings matter closer to the agent's influence.
Notice the key phrase: 'الذاتي لا يقتضي رجحان الوجود أو العدم'.
3
Explore the second distinction
Preparatory possibility varies in degrees of proximity and distance—for example, the preparedness of an embryo for humanness is closer than that of a clot. Essential possibility, however, does not admit of any variation; the possibility of a human existing is the same regardless of the stage of development.
The author gives a concrete example using stages of embryonic development.
4
Understand the third distinction
Essential possibility is merely a rational consideration with no external reality, whereas preparatory possibility is a real quality in a thing that prepares it for the agent's bestowal of existence. This quality varies in intensity and vanishes when the potential becomes actual—though the author questions whether that vanishing is necessary.
The passage ends with a rhetorical question about the necessity of disappearance.
5
Reflect on the overall significance
Consider why this taxonomy of possibility is important for kalam. It allows theologians to discuss change and potentiality without conflating logical categories with real physical properties.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب : أنه إن أريد بالإمكان الإمكان الذاتي اللازم لماهية الممكن، فلا نسلم أنه وجودي، بمعنى كونه أمراً محققاً يستدعي محلاً موجوداً في الخارج، وقد مر بيان ضعف أدلتهم على ذلك، وإن أريد الإمكان الاستعدادي، فلا نسلم أن كل حادث فهو قبل وجوده ممكن بالإمكان الاستعدادي لجواز أن يحدث من غير أن يكون هناك مادة وأمور معدة لها ، إلى وجود ذلك الحادث، ولا يكون هذا من الانقلاب في شيء، لأن المقابل للوجوب والامتناع هو الإمكان الذاتي، لا الاستعدادي، وفي قوله المخالف له إشارة إلى التغاير بين الإمكانين، وذلك من وجوه . أحدها : أن الذاتي لا يقتضي رجحان الوجود أو العدم، بل كلاهما بالنظر إليه على السواء، والاستعدادي يقتضيه لأنه حالة مقربة للمادة إلى تأثير المؤثر فيها وإيجاد الحادث . وثانيها : أن الاستعدادي يتفاوت بالقرب والبعد فإن استعداد المضغة للإنسانية، أقرب من استعداد العلقة ، وهو من المادة النباتية ، وهو من المعدنية، وهو من العنصرية وهكذا حتى أن الهيولى الأولى أبعد الكل ولا كذلك الإمكان الذاتي. فإنه لا يتصور تفاوت واختلاف في إمكان وجود الإنسان لماهيته، وما توهم من تفاوته عند اعتبار التعلق بأمر خارج، كإمكان وجود الإنسان لماهيته بالنظر إلى العلقة والمضغة مثلاً، فعائد إلى الاستعدادي . وثالثهما أن الذاتي اعتبار عقلى ولا تحقق له في الأعيان بخلاف : الاستعدادي، فإنه كيفية حاصلة للشيء، مهيئة إياه لإفاضة الفاعل وجود الحادث فيه كالصورة والعرض أو معه كالنفس مختلفة بالقرب والبعد والشدة والضعف، بحسب حدوث شرط شرط وارتفاع مانع مانع مسماة بالقوة عند الحادث زائلة عند وجوده ككون الجسم في أوسط الحيز عند الوصول إلى نهايته وهل ذلك الزوال بواجب فيه تردد؟ عدم
And the answer: If what is meant by possibility is essential possibility, which is necessary for the quiddity of the possible, then we do not concede that it is existential, meaning that it is a realized thing requiring an existing substrate in the external world, and the exposition of the weakness of their proofs on that has already passed. And if what is meant is preparatory possibility, then we do not concede that every temporal thing is possible before its existence by preparatory possibility, because it is possible that it occurs without there being a matter and prepared things for that temporal thing's existence, and this is not a change [of species] at all, because the counterpart of necessity and impossibility is essential possibility, not preparatory possibility. And in his statement 'the opposite of it' there is an indication of the difference between the two possibilities, and that is from [several] aspects. One of them: that the essential does not imply preponderance of existence or non-existence, but both are equal with respect to it, while the preparatory implies it because it is a state that brings matter near to the influence of the agent in it and the bringing about of the temporal. Second: that the preparatory varies in proximity and distance, for the preparedness of the embryo for humanness is closer than the preparedness of the clot, and that of the plant matter, and that of the mineral, and that of the elemental, and so on, until the prime matter is the farthest of all, while this is not so for essential possibility, for no variation or difference can be conceived in the possibility of existence of a human for its quiddity, and what is imagined as its variation when considering relation to an external thing, like the possibility of human existence for its quiddity with respect to the clot or embryo, for example, reverts to preparatory possibility. Third: that the essential is a rational consideration and has no realization in concrete entities, unlike preparatory possibility, for it is a quality subsisting in a thing, preparing it for the agent's bestowal of the existence of the temporal in it like form and accident, or with it like the soul, varying in proximity and distance and intensity and weakness, according to the occurrence of condition after condition and the removal of obstacle after obstacle, called potentiality when the temporal is absent, vanishing at its existence, like the body being in the middle of space when reaching its endpoint. And is that disappearance necessary? There is hesitation. No.
Source pages p. 16

Commentary
This passage is a response to an objection about possibility. The author distinguishes two types of possibility: essential possibility (al-imkan al-dhati) and preparatory possibility (al-imkan al-isti'dadi). Essential possibility is a necessary property of the quiddity of a possible thing and does not require an external substrate. Preparatory possibility, on the other hand, is a real quality in matter that prepares it for receiving a form. The author then provides three distinctions between them: 1) Essential possibility does not imply preponderance of existence or non-existence, while preparatory possibility does, as it is a state that brings matter closer to the agent's influence. 2) Preparatory possibility varies in degrees (proximity and distance), such as the preparedness of an embryo for humanness being closer than that of a clot, while essential possibility does not admit of degrees—the possibility of a human existing is the same regardless of the stage. 3) Essential possibility is a rational consideration with no external reality, while preparatory possibility is a real quality in a thing that prepares it for receiving existence. The passage ends with a rhetorical question about the vanishing of preparatory possibility at the moment of actual existence, answering that it is not necessary to disappear (i.e., it is not necessarily followed by loss).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that essential possibility is a real attribute that requires a subject, but it is merely a rational consideration.
Correction: The third distinction shows that essential possibility is a rational consideration ('اعتبار عقلى') with no realization in concrete entities, unlike preparatory possibility.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT a distinction between essential possibility and preparatory possibility according to the passage?
A) Essential possibility does not imply preponderance, while preparatory does.
B) Essential possibility varies in degrees, while preparatory does not.
C) Preparatory possibility is a real quality, while essential is a rational consideration.
D) Preparatory possibility varies with proximity and distance.

Key Takeaway
Essential possibility and preparatory possibility differ in three ways: the former is a rational concept without degrees, the latter is a real quality that varies and implies preponderance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of something you deeply desire—like a change of character or a spiritual breakthrough. Is your hope based on 'essential possibility' (it's rationally possible) or are you actively cultivating 'preparatory possibility' in your heart? Which one actually moves you closer to that goal, and what would it mean to neglect the preparatory steps?
?
The source says preparatory possibility varies in degrees: some states are closer to actualization than others. Consider your current spiritual state (e.g., prayer, patience, humility). Where would you place yourself on the scale from 'prime matter' to 'embryo'? What one condition or obstacle must be removed or added to bring you closer to the 'human' you are meant to be?
?
Essential possibility is a mere rational consideration—it doesn't exist in the outside world. How does that hit you? Your existence is not a 'real possibility' sitting out there; it is entirely contingent, moment by moment, on the Agent who bestows existence. Does that thought make you feel fragile? Or free? And if the latter, why?

Essential Possibility Requires a Subject
Philosophers' first way: possibility requires a subject.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' first way of explaining how essential possibility can be existential, namely that it requires a subject in the external world.

Core Idea
Essential possibility is not standalone; it always needs a real substrate.

The philosophers propose that the possibility of a temporal being before its actual existence means that this possibility is attached to an existing subject. This passage unpacks the distinction between two types of existence and shows that even essential possibility, when the thing is dependent, necessitates a substrate. Let's explore how they argue this.

Guided Path
1
Context and purpose
Understand that this passage presents the first of two philosophers' ways to resolve an objection about possibility being existential. The key idea is that possibility is relative to a subject.
2
Distinction between essential and accidental existence
Notice the division of existence into two types: essential (like whiteness in itself) and accidental (like a body being white). This classification is crucial for the argument.
3
Possibility relative to accidental existence
For accidental existence, possibility clearly requires an existing subject. Example: whiteness cannot be possible for a body unless the body exists.
4
Extending to essential existence for dependent things
Even for essential existence, if the thing depends on a substrate when it exists (like accidents, forms, or soul), its possibility also requires that substrate.
5
Conclusion and takeaway
The first way shows that 'essential possibility' before existence is not independent but relational, demanding an actual subject for dependent entities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أعلم أن للفلاسفة في التقصي عن هذا الإشكال وجهين : أحدهما: أن المراد الإمكان الذاتي ومعنى كون إمكان الحادث قبل وجوده وجودياً، تعلقه بموضوع موجود في الخارج، وتقريرة أن الإمكان لا محالة يكون بالقياس إلى وجود والوجود إما بالذات، كوجود البياض في نفسه، وإما بالعرض كوجود الجسم أبيض، أما الإمكان بالقياس إلى وجود بالعرض، وهو إمكان أن يوجد شيء شيئاً آخر، أو يوجد له شيء آخر، كالبياض للجسم، والصورة للمادة والنفس للبدن ولا خفاء في احتياجه إلى وجود شيء حتى يوجد له شيء آخر، وأما الإمكان بالقياس إلى وجود بالذات، وهو إمكان وجود الشيء في نفسه فذلك الشيء إن كان مما يتعلق وجوده بالغير، أي يكون بحيث إذا وجد كان موجوداً في غيره كالعرض والصورة، أو مع غيره كالنفس، فهو كالأول في الاحتياج إلى موضوع يقوم به إمكان ذلك
Know that the philosophers have two ways in investigating this objection: One of them: that what is meant is essential possibility, and the meaning of the possibility of the temporal being existential before its existence is its relation to an existing subject in the external world. Its exposition: that possibility inevitably is in relation to existence, and existence is either essential (bi al-dhat), like the existence of whiteness in itself, or accidental (bi al-'aradh), like the existence of a body as white. As for possibility in relation to accidental existence, which is the possibility that a thing exists as something else, or that something else exists for it, like whiteness for the body, form for matter, and the soul for the body, there is no concealment of its need for the existence of something so that something else may exist for it. As for possibility in relation to essential existence, which is the possibility of a thing's existence in itself, then if that thing is such that its existence depends on another, i.e., when it exists it exists in something else, like an accident or form, or with something else, like the soul, then it is like the first in needing a substrate in which that possibility subsists.
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage presents the first of the philosophers' two ways to address the earlier objection about possibility being existential. The author explains that the 'existential' nature of possibility means it is relative to a subject. He divides existence into two types: essential existence (existence in itself, like whiteness) and accidental existence (existence as a quality of another, like a body being white). Possibility relative to accidental existence (e.g., the possibility of whiteness existing in a body) clearly requires an existing subject. For possibility relative to essential existence (the possibility of a thing existing in itself), if that thing depends on a substrate when it exists (like accidents, forms, or the soul which exist in or with something else), then its possibility also requires a subject. This shows that 'essential possibility' before actuality may still demand a substrate for its possibility if the thing itself is dependent.

الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Essential possibility never requires a subject; it is a property of the possible thing alone.
Correction: For dependent entities like accidents and forms, their essential possibility still depends on an existing substrate, as the philosophers argue.

Checkpoint

A) That possibility is a real quality in the possible thing itself.
B) That possibility is relative to a subject that exists in the external world.
C) That possibility is eternal and unchanging.
D) That possibility is the same as the actual existence of a thing.

Key Takeaway
Essential possibility, especially for dependent beings, is not independent but requires an existing substrate in the external world.

The Argument that Possibility Requires a Substrate
Taftazani defines possibility and argues against contingent self-subsistents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how some scholars argue that possibility (imkan) must be an accident that needs a substrate, and why this implies that self-subsistent entities cannot be originated.

Core Idea
Possibility before existence is like an accident in a subject; but for self-subsistent beings, that fails.

Taftazani begins by defining what it means for a thing to have possibility before it exists: that thing exists potentially in a subject. He then distinguishes between entities that depend on a subject and those that are self-subsistent. Using this distinction, he presents an argument that self-subsistent entities cannot be temporally originated because their possibility would need to be self-subsistent, which is impossible since possibility is an accident.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of possibility
Notice how Taftazani describes possibility before existence: the thing is in a subject potentially, like an accident.
2
Distinguish between dependent and independent entities
Some entities depend on a subject, others are self-subsistent.
Self-subsistent means qaim bi nafsihi.
3
Follow the argument for self-subsistent entities
If a self-subsistent entity were originated, its possibility before origination would have to be self-subsistent, but possibility is an accident and cannot be so.
This leads to a contradiction.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The argument concludes that self-subsistent entities cannot be contingent.
This is a key point in the debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشيء قبل وجوده بمعنى كون ذلك الشيء في ذلك الموضوع أو معه بالقوة، فهو صفة للموضوع من حيث هو فيه كعرض في موضوع وصفة للشيء من حيث هو بالقياس إليه كإضافة المضاف إليه، وإن لم يكن ذلك الشيء مما يتعلق وجوده بالغير، بل يكون قائماً بنفسه من غير تعلق بموضوع أو مادة، فمثله لا يجوز أن يكون حادثاً، وإلا لكان إمكانه قبل حدوثه قائماً بنفسه، إذ لا علاقة له بشيء من الموضوعات ليقوم به، وهو محال لأنه عرض لا جوهر، وضعف هذا الوجه ظاهر لأن الإشكال عائد ولأنه لو ثبت أن إمكان الحادث عرضي يستدعي محلاً، أو استدل على ذلك استدلالاً فاسداً بأنه لو لم يكن إمكان الحادث أمراً موجوداً لم يكن للحادث إمكان وجود، فلم يكن ممكن الوجود على ما في الشفاء، لم يحتج إلى ذكر من التفاصيل.
The thing before its existence in the sense of that thing being in that subject or with it potentially, so it is an attribute of the subject insofar as it is in it, like an accident in a subject, and an attribute of the thing insofar as it is relative to it, like the relation of the related to its relatum. And if that thing is not such that its existence is dependent on another, but rather is self-subsistent without attachment to a subject or matter, then the like of it cannot be something that came to be (hadith). Otherwise, its possibility (imkan) before its coming-to-be would be self-subsistent, since it has no relation to any of the subjects to subsist by it. But that is impossible because possibility is an accident, not a substance. The weakness of this aspect is apparent because the problem returns, and because if it were established that the possibility of the contingent is accidental requiring a substrate, or if that were proven by a flawed proof—that if the possibility of the contingent were not something existent, then the contingent would have no possibility of existence, and thus would not be possible, as in al-Shifa—then there would be no need to mention any of these details.
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This passage presents a definition and argument regarding the nature of possibility (imkan) before a thing's existence. The author first defines what it means for a thing to have possibility before existence: that the thing exists potentially in a subject. This possibility is an attribute of the subject (like an accident) and also an attribute of the thing relative to the subject (like a relation). The argument then considers a self-subsistent entity (qaim bi nafsihi) that does not depend on a subject or matter. The claim is that such an entity cannot be temporally originated (hadith), because if it were, its possibility before its origination would have to be self-subsistent, since it has no relation to any subject. But possibility is an accident, not a substance, so it cannot be self-subsistent. Therefore, a self-subsistent entity cannot be contingent. The author then points out the weakness of this argument: the problem recurs (because one could ask about the possibility of that possibility, etc.), and the proof from Avicenna's al-Shifa is flawed because it assumes that if possibility were not an existent thing, the contingent would have no possibility at all. The author concludes that such a proof does not require such detailed refutation.

عرض
جوهر
محل
حادث
قائم بنفسه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Possibility is a real attribute that must inhere in a subject for all entities.
Correction: The argument assumes possibility is an accident, but for self-subsistent beings, this leads to a contradiction, showing the assumption may be false.

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that a self-subsistent entity (qaim bi nafsihi) cannot be temporally originated?
A) Because its possibility would need a substrate, but it has none.
B) Because it is eternal by nature.
C) Because all self-subsistent entities are necessary.
D) Because the argument is based on a flawed proof from al-Shifa.

Key Takeaway
The argument claims that possibility before existence is an attribute that requires a substrate, so self-subsistent entities cannot have possibility and thus cannot be contingent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

Does it make you more humble, knowing that even your potential existence required a substrate that was not you? How can you live in constant recognition of your absolute dependence on Allah's will?

Refutation of the Argument
Taftazani refutes the argument by pointing out infinite regression and a flawed proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Taftazani's refutation of the argument that possibility requires a substrate, including the problem of infinite regression and the flawed proof from Avicenna.

Core Idea
The argument that possibility is an accident leads to an infinite regress or rests on a flawed premise.

After presenting the argument, Taftazani quickly points out its weaknesses. He notes that the problem returns—if possibility needs a substrate, then the possibility of that possibility would also need one, leading to an infinite chain. He also criticizes the proof from Avicenna's al-Shifa, which assumed that if possibility were not an existing thing, the contingent would have no possibility. These objections show the argument is not sound.

Guided Path
1
Understand the first objection
The problem of regression: if possibility is an accident, then the possibility of that accident would also need a substrate, and so on ad infinitum.
This shows the argument leads to an infinite chain.
2
Understand the second objection
The proof from al-Shifa is flawed because it assumes that possibility must be an existing attribute, which is not necessary.
Avicenna's argument is not accepted by Taftazani.
3
Appreciate the conclusion
Taftazani dismisses the entire line of reasoning as weak, saying such details are unnecessary.
This refutation defends the contingency of self-subsistent beings.

Two Proofs That Originated Things Are Preceded by Time
Presents two proofs for temporal precedence of originated things.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two rational arguments for why every originated thing must be preceded by time, and why this reasoning does not depend on the reality of time as an existential entity.

Core Idea
Temporal precedence of non-existence over existence is a necessary rational judgment.

This passage presents two foundational proofs for the relationship between originated things and time. The first proof uses the idea of successive events, while the second is based on the definition of 'originated' as preceded by non-existence. The author then clarifies a potential objection about the nature of precedence, grounding the argument in a necessary rational judgment rather than in questionable metaphysical assumptions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: two proofs (wajhan) followed by a clarification about the nature of precedence.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two proofs
The first proof is about successive events (hawadith muta'aqiba) requiring time. The second proof is about the precedence of non-existence over existence. Find these in the text.
Each proof begins with a linking word: 'ahaduhuma' and 'thaniyahuma'.
3
Understand the objection and response
The author anticipates an objection that precedence might be a relational attribute (itibari) judged by illusion (wahm). Understand why he says this does not affect the argument.
He argues that the judgment is a necessary rational judgment, not dependent on the reality of precedence.
4
Grasp the key conclusion
The final sentence states that time is the substrate (ma'rud) of precedence, not precedence itself. This solidifies the argument.
This distinguishes the subject from the attribute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
احتجوا على كون الحادث مسبوقاً بالزمان بوجهين : أحدهما : أنه لا بد له من سبق حوادث متعاقبة بمعنى حصول هذا بعد حصول ذاك، بحيث لا يجتمع المتقدم والمتأخر، وما ذاك إلا بالزمان . وثانيهما: أنه لا معنى للحادث إلا ما يكون وجوده مسبوقاً بالعدم، وظاهر أن سبق عدم الشيء على وجوده لا يعقل إلا بالزمان، وهذا التقرير لا يبتني على أن التقدم أمر وجودي، وأنه هو الزمان حتى يرد الاعتراض بأنا لا نسلم أنه وجودي، بل اعتباري يعرض للعدم أيضاً، والحاكم بثبوته الوهم وحكمه مردود كما في تحيز الباري حيث يحكم به الوهم بناء على أن ما يشاهد من الموجودات متحيزة، وإنما يبتنى على صحة الحكم بأن هذا متقدم على ذلك كقدم الحادث على وجوده ولا خفاء في أنه حكم عقلي ضروري والزمان معروض التقدم لا نفسه.
They argued that the originated thing is preceded by time in two ways: First: it must be preceded by successive events, meaning that one occurs after another, such that the prior and posterior do not coincide, and that is only by time. Second: there is no meaning of 'originated' except that its existence is preceded by non-existence, and it is apparent that the precedence of a thing's non-existence over its existence is not intelligible except by time. This exposition does not depend on precedence being an existential attribute, or that it is time itself, such that the objection could be raised: 'We do not concede that it is existential; rather it is relational that also occurs to non-existence, and the judge of its existence is illusion (wahm) and its judgment is rejected, as in the case of the spatial occupation (tahayyuz) of the Eternal, where illusion judges it based on what is observed of existents being spatially occupied.' Rather, it is based on the soundness of the judgment that this is prior to that, like the precedence of the originated thing over its existence, and there is no hiddenness in it being a necessary rational judgment, and time is the substrate (ma'rud) of precedence, not precedence itself.
Source pages p. 20

Commentary
This passage is a presentation of two proofs (wajhan) for the claim that every originated thing (hadith) is preceded by time. The first proof argues that an originated thing must be preceded by a succession of events (hawadith muta'aqiba), i.e., one event occurring after another without simultaneity, and this sequentiality is only conceivable through time. The second proof is based on the definition of 'originated' (hadith) as that whose existence is preceded by non-existence. The precedence of non-existence over existence is only intelligible in time. The author then addresses a potential objection: that this reasoning might depend on precedence being an existential attribute (amr wujudi) or that time itself is precedence, but he clarifies that the argument does not rely on that. Even if precedence is considered a mental relation ('itibari) that applies to non-existence as well, and even if the judgment of such precedence is attributed to illusion (wahm) which is unreliable (as in the case of the false judgment that God occupies space), the argument stands because the judgment that 'this is prior to that' (like the precedence of the originated thing over its existence) is a necessary rational judgment (hukm 'aqli daruri). Time is the substrate (ma'rud) of precedence, not the same as precedence.

Key Terms
حادث
زمان
تقدم
عدم
حكم عقلي ضروري
معروض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument that originated things require time depends on time being an existential entity (amr wujudi) and precedence being real.
Correction: The argument is based on the fundamental rational judgment that precedence is intelligible only in time, regardless of whether precedence is existential or relational.

Checkpoint
According to the second proof, why must the originated thing be preceded by time?
A) Because the existence of the originated thing is preceded by non-existence, and such precedence is only intelligible in time.
B) Because time is an existential attribute that causes the originated thing.
C) Because the judgment of precedence is based on illusion (wahm), which is reliable.
D) Because the originated thing and time are the same thing.

Key Takeaway
The originated thing's precedence by time is established through necessary rational judgment, not speculative metaphysics.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Basic Classification by Coexistence and Need
First level of alternative precedence classification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the mutakallimun's classification of precedence based on whether the prior and posterior coexist and whether the posterior depends on the prior.

Core Idea
Precedence is not just temporal; it diversifies by coexistence and dependence.

This passage presents a detailed classification of precedence. Before exploring deeper distinctions, we need to grasp the initial branches: when things coexist or not, and whether the posterior needs the prior. This first lesson covers that foundational scheme.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation once. Notice the conditional structure: 'if they do not coexist...', 'if they coexist...', etc.
The passage uses 'إن' for conditions.
2
Identify the five types
List the five types: zaman (time), rutba (rank), sharaf (dignity), illiyya (causality), tab‘ (nature). Observe how each is defined.
3
Understand the classification logic
Trace the decision tree: first check coexistence; if coexisting, check consideration; if no consideration, check need; etc.
This is a binary tree.
4
Reflect on the rationale
Ask why the mutakallimun felt the need for so many types. Consider that each type applies to a distinct situation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما يقال في بيان الحصر المتقدم والمتأخر إن لم يجتمعا في الوجود فهو بالزمان، وإن اجتمعا فإن كان بينهما ترتيب بحسب الاعتبار فهو بالرتبة، وإلا فإن لم يحتج المتأخر إلى المتقدم فبالشرف ، وإن احتاج فإن كان المتقدم أثراً في المتأخر فبالعلية، وإلا فبالطبع أو أن المتقدم إن توقف وجود المتأخر عليه، فبالعلية أو بالطبع كما ذكرنا، وإن لم يتوقف فالمتقدم إن كان بالنظر إلى كمال للمتقدم فبالشرف وإلا فإن كان بالنظر إلى مبدأ محدود فبالرتبة، وإلا فبالزمان أو أن التقدم إما حقيقي يكون بحسب الأمر نفسه، فلا يتبدل باعتبار المعتبر، وإمـا اعتباري يقابله والأول إن كان بالنظر إلى الذات فبالطبع، وإن كان بالنظر إلى الوجود، فإن كان وجود المتأخر [مثبر وطاً] بانقضاء وجود المتقدم فبالزمان ، وإلا فبالعلية : والثاني : يفتقر لا محالة إلى مبدأ به الاعتبار، فإن كان كمالاً في المتقدم فبالشرف وإلا فبالرتبة فلا خفاء في أنه ليس إلا وجه ضبط مع أن التقدم بالطبع قد يكون بالنظر إلى الوجود كما في الشرط، وأن التقدم بالزمان قد يكون للعدم دون الوجود، وبعد تمام الوجود فالزماني بالمعنى الذي ذكر فيها شامل للتقدم الذي بين أجزاء الزمان والذي بين الأب والابن بواسطة.
And what is said in the explanation of the exclusivity of the prior and posterior: if they do not coexist in existence, it is by time; and if they coexist, then if there is between them an order according to consideration, it is by rank; otherwise, if the posterior does not need the prior, it is by dignity; and if it needs it, then if the prior is an effect in the posterior, it is by causality; otherwise by nature. Or: if the existence of the posterior depends on the prior, it is by causality or by nature as we mentioned; and if it does not depend, then if the prior is considered with regard to a perfection for the prior, it is by dignity; otherwise, if it is considered with regard to a limited beginning (mabda' mahdud), it is by rank; otherwise by time. Or: precedence is either real, being according to the thing itself, so it does not change by the consideration of the considerer, or relative, opposite to it. The first: if it is with regard to the essence, it is by nature; if with regard to existence, then if the existence of the posterior is [？] by the passing away of the existence of the prior, it is by time; otherwise by causality. The second: it necessarily depends on a principle of consideration; if there is a perfection in the prior, it is by dignity; otherwise by rank. There is no hiddenness that this is only a way of regulation, despite the fact that precedence by nature may be with regard to existence as in the condition, and that precedence by time may belong to non-existence without existence, and after the completion of existence, the temporal in the sense mentioned includes the precedence between parts of time and that between the father and son through mediation.
Source pages p. 21

Commentary
This passage presents an alternative, more detailed classification of precedence and posteriority proposed by the mutakallimun. It begins by explaining a classification based on whether the prior and posterior coexist (ijtima' fi al-wujud). If they do not coexist (i.e., the prior ceases before the posterior exists), it is temporal precedence (bil-zaman). If they coexist, then: if there is an order by consideration (i'tibar), it is by rank (bil-rutba); otherwise, if the posterior does not need the prior, it is by dignity (bil-sharaf); if it needs the prior, then if the prior is an effect (atharan) in the posterior, it is by causality (bil-'illiyya); otherwise by nature (bil-tab'). The passage then offers another formulation: if the posterior's existence depends on the prior, it is by causality or nature; if not dependent, then if the prior is considered in terms of perfection (kamal), it is by dignity; if considered from a limited beginning (mabda' mahdud), it is by rank; otherwise by time. A further classification distinguishes real precedence (taqaddum haqiqi), which is inherent to the thing and does not change with the considerer, from relative precedence (taqaddum i'tibari). Real precedence: if considered in terms of essence (dhat), it is by nature; if in terms of existence, then if the posterior's existence is caused by the passing away of the prior's existence (i.e., the prior ceases), it is by time, otherwise by causality. Relative precedence necessarily depends on a principle of consideration: if that principle is a perfection in the prior, it is by dignity; otherwise by rank. The passage concludes with observations that precedence by nature may pertain to existence (as in a condition), temporal precedence may apply to non-existence rather than existence, and after the completion of existence, temporal precedence in the sense used includes both the precedence of parts of time over each other and the precedence of father over son through mediation (bi-wasita).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All precedence is temporal or causal.
Correction: The mutakallimun recognized five distinct types; temporal and causal are only two of them, and rank, dignity, and nature are equally valid categories.

Checkpoint
According to the first classification, if the prior and posterior coexist in existence and the posterior does not need the prior, what kind of precedence is it?
A) Temporal
B) Rank
C) Dignity
D) Causality

Key Takeaway
The mutakallimun classify precedence into five types based on coexistence, need, and effect: time, rank, dignity, causality, and nature.

?
The passage distinguishes between precedence by dignity and by causality. In your relationship with Allah, do you view His actions as causal (giving existence) or as dignified (bestowing honor)? How does that perspective affect your reliance on Him?
?
If you were to classify the precedence of your worldly responsibilities over religious duties, which type of precedence would you assign and is that classification consistent with Islamic teachings?

Real vs. Relative Precedence
Real vs. relative precedence and their subtypes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between real precedence (haqiqi) and relative precedence (i‘tibari) and how each is subdivided based on essence, existence, perfection, and rank.

Core Idea
Real precedence is intrinsic; relative precedence depends on the observer's perspective.

After establishing the basic categories, the passage introduces a higher-level distinction: real precedence (taqaddum haqiqi) is inherent to the thing and does not change, while relative precedence (taqaddum i‘tibari) depends on a consideration. This lesson unpacks that distinction and its subtypes.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish real from relative
Read the passage defining real precedence as 'according to the thing itself' and relative as its opposite. Understand that real does not change with the considerer.
2
Subtypes of real precedence
Real precedence is divided based on essence (dhat) leading to nature, and based on existence leading to time or causality.
Note the condition for time: posterior's existence caused by passing away of prior's existence.
3
Subtypes of relative precedence
Relative precedence depends on a principle of consideration: if that principle is a perfection in the prior, it is dignity; otherwise it is rank.
4
Connect to earlier classification
Notice that some types from the first lesson appear here as subtypes (nature, time, causality, dignity, rank). This is a different organizing framework.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما يقال في بيان الحصر المتقدم والمتأخر إن لم يجتمعا في الوجود فهو بالزمان، وإن اجتمعا فإن كان بينهما ترتيب بحسب الاعتبار فهو بالرتبة، وإلا فإن لم يحتج المتأخر إلى المتقدم فبالشرف ، وإن احتاج فإن كان المتقدم أثراً في المتأخر فبالعلية، وإلا فبالطبع أو أن المتقدم إن توقف وجود المتأخر عليه، فبالعلية أو بالطبع كما ذكرنا، وإن لم يتوقف فالمتقدم إن كان بالنظر إلى كمال للمتقدم فبالشرف وإلا فإن كان بالنظر إلى مبدأ محدود فبالرتبة، وإلا فبالزمان أو أن التقدم إما حقيقي يكون بحسب الأمر نفسه، فلا يتبدل باعتبار المعتبر، وإمـا اعتباري يقابله والأول إن كان بالنظر إلى الذات فبالطبع، وإن كان بالنظر إلى الوجود، فإن كان وجود المتأخر [مثبر وطاً] بانقضاء وجود المتقدم فبالزمان ، وإلا فبالعلية : والثاني : يفتقر لا محالة إلى مبدأ به الاعتبار، فإن كان كمالاً في المتقدم فبالشرف وإلا فبالرتبة فلا خفاء في أنه ليس إلا وجه ضبط مع أن التقدم بالطبع قد يكون بالنظر إلى الوجود كما في الشرط، وأن التقدم بالزمان قد يكون للعدم دون الوجود، وبعد تمام الوجود فالزماني بالمعنى الذي ذكر فيها شامل للتقدم الذي بين أجزاء الزمان والذي بين الأب والابن بواسطة.
And what is said in the explanation of the exclusivity of the prior and posterior: if they do not coexist in existence, it is by time; and if they coexist, then if there is between them an order according to consideration, it is by rank; otherwise, if the posterior does not need the prior, it is by dignity; and if it needs it, then if the prior is an effect in the posterior, it is by causality; otherwise by nature. Or: if the existence of the posterior depends on the prior, it is by causality or by nature as we mentioned; and if it does not depend, then if the prior is considered with regard to a perfection for the prior, it is by dignity; otherwise, if it is considered with regard to a limited beginning (mabda' mahdud), it is by rank; otherwise by time. Or: precedence is either real, being according to the thing itself, so it does not change by the consideration of the considerer, or relative, opposite to it. The first: if it is with regard to the essence, it is by nature; if with regard to existence, then if the existence of the posterior is [？] by the passing away of the existence of the prior, it is by time; otherwise by causality. The second: it necessarily depends on a principle of consideration; if there is a perfection in the prior, it is by dignity; otherwise by rank. There is no hiddenness that this is only a way of regulation, despite the fact that precedence by nature may be with regard to existence as in the condition, and that precedence by time may belong to non-existence without existence, and after the completion of existence, the temporal in the sense mentioned includes the precedence between parts of time and that between the father and son through mediation.
Source pages p. 21

Commentary
This passage presents an alternative, more detailed classification of precedence and posteriority proposed by the mutakallimun. It begins by explaining a classification based on whether the prior and posterior coexist (ijtima' fi al-wujud). If they do not coexist (i.e., the prior ceases before the posterior exists), it is temporal precedence (bil-zaman). If they coexist, then: if there is an order by consideration (i'tibar), it is by rank (bil-rutba); otherwise, if the posterior does not need the prior, it is by dignity (bil-sharaf); if it needs the prior, then if the prior is an effect (atharan) in the posterior, it is by causality (bil-'illiyya); otherwise by nature (bil-tab'). The passage then offers another formulation: if the posterior's existence depends on the prior, it is by causality or nature; if not dependent, then if the prior is considered in terms of perfection (kamal), it is by dignity; if considered from a limited beginning (mabda' mahdud), it is by rank; otherwise by time. A further classification distinguishes real precedence (taqaddum haqiqi), which is inherent to the thing and does not change with the considerer, from relative precedence (taqaddum i'tibari). Real precedence: if considered in terms of essence (dhat), it is by nature; if in terms of existence, then if the posterior's existence is caused by the passing away of the prior's existence (i.e., the prior ceases), it is by time, otherwise by causality. Relative precedence necessarily depends on a principle of consideration: if that principle is a perfection in the prior, it is by dignity; otherwise by rank. The passage concludes with observations that precedence by nature may pertain to existence (as in a condition), temporal precedence may apply to non-existence rather than existence, and after the completion of existence, temporal precedence in the sense used includes both the precedence of parts of time over each other and the precedence of father over son through mediation (bi-wasita).

Correction: Real precedence includes nature, time, and causality; temporal is only one subtype of real when considered by existence.

Checkpoint
Under real precedence, when is precedence classified as 'by time'?
A) When considered in terms of essence.
B) When the posterior's existence is caused by the passing away of the prior's existence.
C) When there is a perfection in the prior.
D) When there is a limited beginning.

Key Takeaway
Precedence is either real (intrinsic, unchanging) or relative (based on consideration), with further subdivisions based on essence, existence, perfection, and rank.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just learned that real precedence is intrinsic and does not change based on the observer. Think of a priority in your life—something you consistently put first. Is it truly intrinsic to that thing, or is it only relatively important because of your perspective? What would it mean for your soul if you have been building your life around relative priorities that could shift tomorrow?
?
The passage divides relative precedence into dignity (by perfection) and rank. Reflect on a relationship where you consider someone 'above' you. Is that based on their actual perfection (dignity) or merely on a social rank you have assigned? How would your heart change if you only ever gave precedence to others out of genuine dignity—never out of empty rank?
?
Real precedence by causality: the cause precedes the effect in existence. Consider your own actions: are you living as a cause of good in the world, or are you merely an effect—reacting to circumstances? Which type of precedence defines your daily choices, and what is one concrete step you can take today to embody causal precedence?

Refinements on Nature, Time, and Mediated Precedence
Final refinements on nature, time, and mediated precedence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the refinements that precedence by nature can apply to existence, precedence by time can apply to non-existence, and temporal precedence includes mediated relationships.

Core Idea
Nature can be about existence, time can be about non-existence, and temporal precedence includes father-son mediated precedence.

The passage ends with important clarifications that prevent misunderstanding. It notes that precedence by nature can be with regard to existence (as in a condition), precedence by time can belong to non-existence rather than existence, and after existence is complete, temporal precedence includes mediated precedence like that between father and son. This lesson examines these nuances.

Guided Path
1
Read the concluding remarks
Read the final part of the Arabic and translation. Pay attention to the exceptions and expansions mentioned.
These remarks refine the earlier categories.
2
Understand nature and existence
Precedence by nature can be in existence, such as the condition (shart) which is prior by nature to the conditioned.
Example: condition of contract precedes its validity.
3
Time and non-existence
Temporal precedence can apply to non-existence: e.g., the non-existence of a thing can precede its existence in time.
This is a subtle point: we can say non-existence precedes existence.
4
Mediated temporal precedence
After existence is complete, temporal precedence includes the precedence of parts of time (day before night) and mediated precedence (father before son via time passing).
The mediation does not affect the temporal nature.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Proof of the Existentiality of Unity and Multiplicity
Sanusi presents a logical proof for the existentiality of unity and multiplicity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical proof that establishes both unity and multiplicity as existential realities, not mere negations.

Core Idea
Unity and multiplicity are real, not just absences—a logical proof confirms this.

This passage presents a sophisticated logical argument from Sanusi's Sharh al-Maqasid that seeks to prove that unity and multiplicity are not merely conceptual negations but have actual existence in reality. By assuming the opposite and deriving contradictions, Sanusi demonstrates the necessity of their existentiality.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Let the structure of the argument come through.
2
Understand the dilemma
The proof assumes unity is non-existential, then branches into two possibilities regarding multiplicity. Notice how each branch leads to a contradiction.
The argument is a classic reductio ad absurdum.
3
Follow the contradictions
Trace how each horn of the dilemma leads to an impossible consequence: either unity becomes a negation of negation (hence existential) or the aggregate of non-existentials becomes existential. Both contradict the initial assumption.
Pay attention to the concept of 'negation of negation' ( عدم العدم ).
4
Conclusion and opposing view
The only consistent conclusion is that both unity and multiplicity are existential. Note the opposing view (that they are mere absences) is dismissed as weak.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد يستدل على وجوديتهما (۱) ، بأن الوحدة لو كانت عدمية (۲) لكانت عدم الكثرة (۳) ، وهي إما عدمية فتكون الوحدة وجودية لكونها عدم العدم(4) هذا خلف (٥) . إما وجودية فيلزم كون الجميع من العـدمـات وجودية وهو محال . وكون الوحدة وجودية لكونها جزاها هذا خلف . إذ اليست الكثرة إلا التألف من الوحدات (٦) ، يلزم كونها أيضاً وجودية، وعلى حدميتهما بأنه لا يعقل من الوحدة إلا عدم الانقسام (۷)، ومن الكثرة إلا التألف من الوحدات وكلاهما (ضعيف).
And one may argue for their existentiality (1): that if unity were non-existential (2) it would be the absence of multiplicity (3), and it [multiplicity] is either non-existential, then unity would be existential because it is the negation of a negation (4)—this is a contradiction (5). Or it is existential, then it would follow that the aggregate of non-existentials is existential, which is impossible. And [if] unity is existential because it is a part of it, this is a contradiction. Since multiplicity is nothing but the composition of units (6), it follows that it is also existential. And [the argument] for their non-existentiality: that from unity one only understands the absence of division (7), and from multiplicity only the composition of units—both (are weak).
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This passage presents a proof for the existential nature of unity and multiplicity. The proof proceeds by assuming the opposite—that unity is non-existential—and deriving a contradiction. The argument is structured as a dilemma: if unity were non-existential, it would be the absence of multiplicity. Multiplicity is then examined: if multiplicity is also non-existential, then unity becomes the negation of a negation, which is existential—a contradiction. If multiplicity is existential, then the aggregate of non-existentials (since unity is assumed non-existential) would be existential, which is impossible. Alternatively, if unity is considered existential because it is a part of the existential multiplicity, that also contradicts the assumption. The proof then concludes that both unity and multiplicity must be existential. The passage ends by noting that the opposing view (that they are non-existential) relies on understanding unity as the mere absence of division and multiplicity as the composition of units, but both arguments are considered weak.

Key Terms

الكثرة
وجودي
عدمي
عدم العدم
خلف
جزء
تألف
عدم الانقسام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That unity and multiplicity are merely negative concepts (absences) without real existence.
Correction: Sanusi's proof shows that assuming they are non-existential leads to logical contradictions, so they must be existential (real).

Checkpoint
What logical contradiction arises if both unity and multiplicity are assumed non-existential?
A) Unity becomes the negation of multiplicity, which is impossible.
B) Unity becomes the negation of a negation, which is existential.
C) Multiplicity becomes a part of unity.
D) The aggregate of non-existentials becomes impossible.

Key Takeaway
Unity and multiplicity are proven to be existential through a logical dilemma that shows their non-existentiality leads to contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on the reality of unity and multiplicity in your own life—your relationships, your worship, your sense of self—do you treat them as mere mental constructs or as real, existent things? What would change if you genuinely affirmed their existentiality as Sanusi argues?
?
The proof shows that denying the reality of unity leads to logical absurdity. Are there areas in your faith where you have been content with comfortable negations (e.g., 'I just don't have time', 'I'm not good enough') rather than affirming the positive existence of virtues like steadfastness or sincerity?
?
If unity is real and existential, then your own unity of purpose and intention (tawḥīd) is not just an idea but a reality you must embody. How will you concretely manifest that reality in your next action, rather than letting it remain an abstract concept?

The Infinite Regress Argument on Unity
First argument against existential unity leading to regress and its rebuttal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the theologians' argument that if unity were existent it would lead to an infinite regress, and the two responses to it.

Core Idea
Existent unity would require an endless chain of unities—stopped only by mental consideration.

This passage presents one of the key arguments used by theologians to argue that unity is not an existent attribute. They claim that if unity were a real existent, it would itself be one, requiring another unity, and so on indefinitely. The response cuts off the regress by invoking the nature of mental considerations.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once. Notice the structure: the theologians propose a conditional (if unity were existent) and then derive an infinite regress.
2
Identify the regress mechanism
Understand why existential unity would require its own unity: because every existent is one (musāwiq). This leads to a chain of unities.
3
Examine the two responses
The response offers two ways to stop the regress: stopping the consideration (iʿtibār) or identifying the unity of unity as itself—proving it is merely mental.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن تمسكات المتكلمين أن الوحدة لو كانت موجودة لكانت (۲) واحدة ، لكون الموجود مساوقاً للوحدة، ولكانت الوحدة مشاركه في الوحدة ومتمايزة بالخصوصيات لتكون للوحدة وحدة ويتسلسل . والجواب بأنه ينقطع بانقطاع الاعتبار، أو بأن وحدة الوحدة عينها اعتراف بأنها من الاعتبارات العقلية التي لا تحقق لها في الأعيان لما مر في الوجوب والإمكان (۳). ومنها أنه لا يعقل من الوحدة إلا عدم الانقسام، ومن الكثرة إلا التألف من الوحدات ورد بأن هذا عين الدعوى.
And among the arguments of the theologians: that if unity were existent, it would be (2) one, because the existent is coextensive with unity; and unity would share in unity and be distinguished by particularities, so that unity would have a unity, leading to an infinite regress. The response: that it is cut off by cutting off the consideration, or that the unity of unity is identical to itself, an acknowledgment that it is among mental considerations that have no actuality in external things, as was mentioned in [the discussion of] necessity and contingency (3). And among them: that from unity one only understands the absence of division, and from multiplicity only the composition of units; it was replied that this is the very claim [in dispute].
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage presents the theologians' arguments against the existentiality of unity and multiplicity. The first argument: if unity were existent, it would itself be one, because every existent is coextensive with unity. This would mean that unity shares in unity (i.e., has the attribute of being one) and is distinguished by particularities, which would require that unity itself has a unity, leading to an infinite regress (tasalsul). The response to this argument is twofold: either the regress stops when the consideration (i‘tibār) stops—meaning that the attribution of unity to unity is a mental consideration that does not go on infinitely—or one can say that the unity of unity is identical to itself, which is an admission that these are mere mental considerations (i‘tibārāt ‘aqliyya) with no external reality, as was discussed earlier in the topic of necessity and contingency. The second argument: from unity one only understands the absence of division, and from multiplicity only the composition of units—implying that both are non-existential. The response is that this is just a restatement of the claim (i.e., it does not provide a proof), so it is not a valid argument.

Key Terms
مساوق
تسلسل
اعتبار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the argument, why would existential unity lead to an infinite regress?
A) Because unity is indivisible.
B) Because every existent is one, so unity would need another unity.
C) Because unity is a mental construct.
D) Because the theologians reject existence.

Key Takeaway
The infinite regress argument against existential unity is rebutted by noting that the chain is mental, not real.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

How does this passage challenge you to rethink that mental habit? What will you do the next time you catch yourself imagining attributes as independent existences?
?
The argument shows that an infinite regress in mental concepts is harmless. Where in your own thinking do you create endless mental chains—worrying about possibilities that have no real end? How can the lesson's insight that the chain stops when 'consideration stops' help you find peace in tawakkul?
?
If unity is a mental consideration, then your understanding of tawheed is partly a construct of your mind. Does that make it less real, or more intimate? What does this imply about the relationship between your intellect and your faith in Allah's oneness?

The Definitional Argument Against Unity
Second argument from definition of unity and its rebuttal as question-begging.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the theologians' argument that unity is merely the absence of division and its rebuttal as a mere restatement of the claim.

Core Idea
Defining unity as non-division is just repeating the claim, not proving it.

The second argument relies on a definitional claim: that from unity one only understands the absence of division, and from multiplicity only composition from units. This suggests both are non-existential. Sanusi notes that this argument is circular—it is simply asserting what needs to be proven.

Guided Path
1
Read the brief passage
The passage contains the theologians' definitional claim and the laconic response. Read it once in Arabic and translation.
2
Identify the definitions
The theologians define unity as non-division and multiplicity as composition of units. Consider why these definitions would imply non-existentiality.
3
Understand the rebuttal
The response is 'this is the very claim' (ʿayn al-daʿwā). Why is that a criticism? Because the definition assumes the conclusion that unity is non-existential without proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن تمسكات المتكلمين أن الوحدة لو كانت موجودة لكانت (۲) واحدة ، لكون الموجود مساوقاً للوحدة، ولكانت الوحدة مشاركه في الوحدة ومتمايزة بالخصوصيات لتكون للوحدة وحدة ويتسلسل . والجواب بأنه ينقطع بانقطاع الاعتبار، أو بأن وحدة الوحدة عينها اعتراف بأنها من الاعتبارات العقلية التي لا تحقق لها في الأعيان لما مر في الوجوب والإمكان (۳). ومنها أنه لا يعقل من الوحدة إلا عدم الانقسام، ومن الكثرة إلا التألف من الوحدات ورد بأن هذا عين الدعوى.
And among the arguments of the theologians: that if unity were existent, it would be (2) one, because the existent is coextensive with unity; and unity would share in unity and be distinguished by particularities, so that unity would have a unity, leading to an infinite regress. The response: that it is cut off by cutting off the consideration, or that the unity of unity is identical to itself, an acknowledgment that it is among mental considerations that have no actuality in external things, as was mentioned in [the discussion of] necessity and contingency (3). And among them: that from unity one only understands the absence of division, and from multiplicity only the composition of units; it was replied that this is the very claim [in dispute].

Commentary
This passage presents the theologians' arguments against the existentiality of unity and multiplicity. The first argument: if unity were existent, it would itself be one, because every existent is coextensive with unity. This would mean that unity shares in unity (i.e., has the attribute of being one) and is distinguished by particularities, which would require that unity itself has a unity, leading to an infinite regress (tasalsul). The response to this argument is twofold: either the regress stops when the consideration (i‘tibār) stops—meaning that the attribution of unity to unity is a mental consideration that does not go on infinitely—or one can say that the unity of unity is identical to itself, which is an admission that these are mere mental considerations (i‘tibārāt ‘aqliyya) with no external reality, as was discussed earlier in the topic of necessity and contingency. The second argument: from unity one only understands the absence of division, and from multiplicity only the composition of units—implying that both are non-existential. The response is that this is just a restatement of the claim (i.e., it does not provide a proof), so it is not a valid argument.
