The Proof that Unity is Existent and Refutation of Objections
Sanusi proves unity is existent and refutes objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the classical kalam argument that unity (al-wahda) is a real, existent attribute and how objections regarding mental considerations and infinite regress are resolved.

Core Idea
Unity is not merely the absence of multiplicity; it is a real existent attribute.

Sanusi presents a rigorous proof that unity must be existent, using a dilemma regarding multiplicity. He then addresses objections from opponents who argue that unity could be a negation or lead to infinite regress. This lesson unpacks the logical steps and shows how the doctrine of mental considerations (i'tibarat aqliyya) resolves the apparent regress.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof that unity is existent
Read the first part of the passage where Sanusi argues that if unity were non-existent, it would lead to a contradiction or impossibility. Pay attention to the two possibilities for multiplicity.
Notice the use of 'خلف' (contradiction) to mark impossibility.
2
Understand the dilemma about multiplicity
The argument presents a dilemma: either multiplicity is non-existent (making unity a negation of non-existence, which is existent) or it is existent (but it would be a sum of non-existents, impossible). Both horns lead to the conclusion that unity must be existent.
3
Follow the objection and the answer using mental considerations
After establishing unity's existence, an objection is raised that the negation of a non-existent can itself be non-existent. Then an argument from the mutakallimun: if unity is existent, it would be one, leading to infinite regress. Note how Sanusi answers by invoking mental considerations (i'tibarat aqliyya).
This answer shows that regress is cut off by recognizing that the unity of unity is not a real attribute but a mental consideration.
4
Recognize the final argument and its rebuttal
The last objection defines unity as only non-division and multiplicity as composition of units. Sanusi dismisses this as merely restating the claim without proof.
This highlights the need for a real definition, not just a nominal one.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أن الوحدة لو كانت عدمية لكانت عدم الكثرة لأنها المقابل لها والكثرة إما من أن يكون أمراً عدمياً، ويلزم منه كون الوحده وجودية لكونها عبارة عن عدم العدم، هذا خلف. وأما أن تكون أمراً وجودياً وهي عبارة عن مجموع الوحدات، فيلزم كون مجموع العدمات أمراً وجودياً وهو محال أو نقول: والوحدة جزء منه فيكون وجودية هذا خلف.
ولما بطل كون الوحدة عدمية ثبت كونها وجودية، ولزم منه كون الكثرة وجودية لكونها عبارة عن مجموع الوحدات ورد بأن سلب العدمي، قد يكون عدمياً كالامتناع، واللا امتناع ومن تمسكات المتكلمين أن الوحدة لو كانت موجودة لكانت واحدة، لكون الموجود مساوقاً للوحدة، ولكانت الوحدة مشاركه في الوحدة ومتمايزة بالخصوصيات لتكون للوحدة وحدة ويتسلسل. والجواب بأنه ينقطع بانقطاع الاعتبار، أو بأن وحدة الوحدة عينها اعتراف بأنها من الاعتبارات العقلية التي لا تحقق لها في الأعيان لما مر في الوجوب والإمكان. ومنها أنه لا يعقل من الوحدة إلا عدم الانقسام، ومن الكثرة إلا التألف من الوحدات ورد بأن هذا عين الدعوى.
That if unity were non-existent, it would be the non-existence of multiplicity because it is its opposite, and multiplicity is either a non-existent thing, entailing that unity is existent because it is the negation of non-existence; this is a contradiction. And if it is an existent thing and is the sum of units, then the sum of non-existents would be an existent, which is impossible. Or we say: unity is part of it, so it would be existent; this is a contradiction. When it is invalid that unity is non-existent, it is established that it is existent, and it follows that multiplicity is existent because it is the sum of units. It is rebutted by saying that the negation of a non-existent may be non-existent, like impossibility and non-impossibility. Among the arguments of the mutakallimun is that if unity were existent, it would be one, because the existent is coextensive with unity, and unity would share in unity and be distinguished by particularities, so unity would have a unity, leading to infinite regress. The answer is that it terminates with the termination of consideration, or that the unity of unity is itself [i.e., identical to it], which is an acknowledgment that it is among mental considerations that have no realization in extra-mental beings, as has passed in [the discussion of] necessity and contingency. And among them is that nothing is understood from unity except non-division, and from multiplicity except composition of units. It is rebutted by saying that this is merely the claim.
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage presents a classical kalam argument about the ontological status of unity (al-wahda). It begins with a proof that unity cannot be non-existent (adamiyya) because if it were, multiplicity (al-kathra) would also be non-existent, leading to absurdities. The argument uses a dilemma: either multiplicity is non-existent (resulting in an infinite regress of negations) or it is existent (making the sum of non-existents existent). Both are impossible. Therefore unity must be existent. The passage then addresses an objection: the negation of a non-existent (salb al-adami) can itself be non-existent (e.g., impossibility), so unity could still be a negation. This is countered by the mutakallimun's argument that if unity were existent, it would have to be one (since existence and unity are coextensive), leading to a regress of unities. The answer is that the regress is cut off by recognizing that the unity of unity is not a real attribute but a mental consideration (i'tibar aqli). Finally, another argument is presented: we understand unity only as non-division, and multiplicity only as composition of units. This is dismissed as merely restating the claim (ayn al-da'wa). The passage thus defends the position that unity is a real, existent attribute.

الوحدة (al-wahda)
الكثرة (al-kathra)
العدمية (al-adamiyya)
الوجودية (al-wujudiyya)
التسلسل (al-tasalsul)
الاعتبارات العقلية (al-i'tibarat al-aqliyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Unity is merely the absence of division and does not have real existence.
Correction: The passage proves that unity must be existent, and the understanding of unity as non-division is simply restating the claim without proof.

Checkpoint

A) Multiplicity would also be non-existent.
B) Unity would be a mental consideration.
C) The regress would be infinite.
D) It would be a contradiction.

Key Takeaway
Unity is a real, existent attribute because its non-existence leads to absurdities about multiplicity, and the regress of unities is avoided by recognizing that unity's unity is a mental consideration.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

How would your heart's conviction change if you truly believed that Allah's unity is an existent reality, not just a negation? Commit this week to saying 'Allahu Ahad' and pausing to feel its positive meaning.

The mutakallimun debated whether the unity of unity leads to regress. In your own spiritual life, do you over-analyze matters of faith to the point of doubt? This lesson teaches that some things are mental considerations with no extra-mental reality. Are there beliefs you have excessively scrutinized instead of simply accepting? This proof shows that denying unity as real leads to absurd conclusions. Unity Without Multiplicity: Primary Categories
Categorizing subjects of unity without multiplicity accepting division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be understand how the subject of unity can be considered without multiplicity and the categories that emerge from whether it accepts division or not.

Core Idea
Unity is not always numerical oneness; it can be absolute, punctual, separate, quantitative, or aggregated.

This lesson explores the first branch of the classification: when something is a subject of unity but not multiplicity. By analyzing whether it has a concept beyond non-division and whether it accepts division, we arrive at a detailed taxonomy of simple and composite unities.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage segment
Read the Arabic passage from 'المبحث الثاني' to 'كالشجر والبيت والدرهم' and its translation. Let the author's logical flow guide you.
Notice the conditional structure: 'if... then...'.
2
Identify the main division
The first division is between things that have no concept other than non-division (absolute unity) and those that do.
Absolute unity is the simplest form.
3
Trace the acceptance of division
For things with another concept, examine if they accept division. If not, they are either a point (with position) or a separate entity (without).
A point is a geometric concept; a separate entity is immaterial.
4
Understand essential vs accidental division
If it accepts division, it can be essentially divisible (quantity) or accidentally divisible into similar or heterogeneous parts.
Accidental division leads to unity by connection or aggregation.
5
Review examples
Note the concrete examples: tree, house, and dirham illustrate unity by aggregation in nature, craft, and position.
These help solidify abstract concepts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الثاني في معروض الوحدة والكثرة

معروض الوحدة قد لا يكون معروض الكثرة، وحينئذ إن لم يكن له مفهوم سوى عدم الانقسام فوحدة على الإطلاق، وإن كان فإن لم يقبل الانقسام، فإما ذو وضع فنقطة، أو لا فمفارق، وإن قبله فإما بالذات فكم أو بالعرض إلى أجزاء متشابهة، أو متحالفة فواحدة باتصال أجزائه، فواحد بالاجتماع طبيعة أو صناعة أو وضعاً كالشجر والبيت، والدرهم. وقد يكون معروض الكثرة ولا بد من اختلاف الجهة فإن قومت جهة الوحدة جهة الكثرة فوحدة جنسية، أو نوعية أو فصلية وإلا فإن عرضت لها، فوحدة بالعرض، وإلا فبالنسبة. والوحدة في الجنس تسمى مجانسة، وفي النوع مماثلة، وفي الكم مساواة وفي الكيف مشابهة وفي النسبة مناسبة وفي الخاصة مشاكلة وفي الأطراف مطابقة، وفي وضع الأجزاء موازاة.
The second investigation: on the subject of unity and multiplicity. The subject of unity may not be the subject of multiplicity. In that case, if it has no concept other than non-division, then it is absolute unity. If it does [have another concept], then if it does not accept division, then either it has position, so it is a point, or not, so it is a separate [entity]. If it accepts division, then either essentially, so it is quantity, or accidentally [in which case] into similar parts, or [heterogeneous] parts, then it is one by the connection of its parts, and one by aggregation in nature, craft, or position, like a tree, a house, and a dirham. And [the subject of] multiplicity may [also] be [a subject of unity] but necessarily from a different aspect. For if the aspect of unity constitutes the aspect of multiplicity, then it is a generic unity, or specific, or differential. If not, then if the unity occurs to it [accidentally], then it is accidental unity; otherwise, relative unity. Unity in genus is called homogeneity (mujānasa); in species, similarity (mumāthala); in quantity, equality (musāwāt); in quality, resemblance (mushābaha); in relation, appropriateness (munāsaba); in property, similarity (mushākala); in extremities, correspondence (muṭābaqa); and in the arrangement of parts, parallelism (muwāzāt).
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage is a classification text. It opens with an investigation into the subjects of unity (ma'rud al-wahda) and multiplicity. It first considers cases where something is a subject of unity but not of multiplicity (i.e., an individual that is not multiple). If such a thing has no concept other than non-division, it is absolute unity (e.g., a simple individual). If it does have another concept, then we examine whether it accepts division. If it does not accept division, it is either a point (if it has position) or a separate entity (if not). If it accepts division, it can be divisible essentially (like a continuous quantity) or accidentally (like a body). Accidental division can yield similar parts (like water) or heterogeneous parts (like a tree's parts). If the parts are connected, it is one by continuity; if aggregated, it is one by aggregation (e.g., a tree, a house, a dirham). Next, it discusses cases where something is a subject of both unity and multiplicity, but from different aspects. If the aspect of unity constitutes the aspect of multiplicity, we speak of generic, specific, or differential unity. If not, and unity occurs to it accidentally, it is accidental unity; otherwise it is relative unity. Finally, it enumerates the technical terms for unity in various categories: homogeneity (mujānasa) in genus, similarity (mumāthala) in species, equality (musāwāt) in quantity, resemblance (mushābaha) in quality, appropriateness (munāsaba) in relation, similarity (mushākala) in property, correspondence (muṭābaqa) in extremities (like left and right), and parallelism (muwāzāt) in arrangement of parts. This classification helps kalam and philosophical discussions on unity and multiplicity.

Key Terms
معروض الوحدة (ma'rud al-wahda)
الانقسام (al-inqisam)
وحدة على الإطلاق (wahda 'ala al-itlaq)
نقطة (nuqta)
مفارق (mufariq)
كم (kamm)
وحدة بالاجتماع (wahda bil-ijtima')

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Unity always means numerical oneness.
Correction: Unity can be absolute, punctual, separate, quantitative, or aggregated, as shown in this classification.

Checkpoint
If a subject of unity has no concept other than non-division, what is it called?
A) Point
B) Absolute unity
C) Separate entity
D) Quantity

Key Takeaway
The subject of unity without multiplicity is classified first by whether it has a concept beyond non-division, then by acceptance and type of division, leading to categories like absolute unity, point, separate entity, quantity, and aggregated unities.

?

?
The passage teaches that unity can be accidental (like a house) or essential (like quantity). When you claim something is 'unified', do you usually assume it is essentially one? How might that assumption lead you to overlook the actual structure of that thing — and what will you do differently?

Unity in Multiplicity: Types and Technical Names
Classification of unity when multiplicity is also present and enumeration of unity types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the same subject can be both one and many from different aspects, and learn the specific terms for unity in various categories.

Core Idea
Things can be one in many ways, each with a precise technical name.

The previous lesson covered unity without multiplicity. This lesson examines cases where the same thing is a subject of both unity and multiplicity from different aspects. It then provides a rich vocabulary for different types of unity, ranging from generic homogeneity to parallel arrangement of parts.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage segment
Read from 'وقد يكون معروض الكثرة' to the end, along with its translation. Notice the conditional structure.
Pay attention to the phrase 'ولا بد من اختلاف الجهة'.
2
Understand the key condition
For a thing to be both one and many, the aspects must differ. The passage explains what happens when the aspect of unity constitutes the aspect of multiplicity.
This yields generic, specific, or differential unity.
3
Distinguish accidental and relative unity
If the aspect of unity does not constitute the aspect of multiplicity, then if unity occurs accidentally, it is accidental unity; otherwise, relative unity.
These are less fundamental types.
4
Learn the eight technical terms
The passage lists eight terms for unity in different categories: genus, species, quantity, quality, relation, property, extremities, and arrangement.
Memorize these: مجانسة, مماثلة, مساواة, مشابهة, مناسبة, مشاكلة, مطابقة, موازاة.
5
Apply to examples
Think of examples for each term, e.g., two humans share مماثلة (specific unity), two lines of equal length share مساواة (equality).
These terms are standard in kalam and philosophy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The second investigation: on the subject of unity and multiplicity. The subject of unity may not be the subject of multiplicity. In that case, if it has no concept other than non-division, then it is absolute unity. If it does [have another concept], then if it does not accept division, then either it has position, so it is a point, or not, so it is a separate [entity]. If it accepts division, then either essentially, so it is quantity, or accidentally [in which case] into similar parts, or [heterogeneous] parts, then it is one by the connection of its parts, and one by aggregation in nature, craft, or position, like a tree, a house, and a dirham. And [the subject of] multiplicity may [also] be [a subject of unity] but necessarily from a different aspect. For if the aspect of unity constitutes the aspect of multiplicity, then it is a generic unity, or specific, or differential. If not, then if the unity occurs to it [accidentally], then it is accidental unity; otherwise, relative unity. Unity in genus is called homogeneity (mujānasa); in species, similarity (mumāthala); in quantity, equality (musāwāt); in quality, resemblance (mushābaha); in relation, appropriateness (munāsaba); in property, similarity (mushākala); in extremities, correspondence (muṭābaqa); and in the arrangement of parts, parallelism (muwāzāt).
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage is a classification text. It opens with an investigation into the subjects of unity (ma'rud al-wahda) and multiplicity. It first considers cases where something is a subject of unity but not of multiplicity (i.e., an individual that is not multiple). If such a thing has no concept other than non-division, it is absolute unity (e.g., a simple individual). If it does have another concept, then we examine whether it accepts division. If it does not accept division, it is either a point (if it has position) or a separate entity (if not). If it accepts division, it can be divisible essentially (like a continuous quantity) or accidentally (like a body). Accidental division can yield similar parts (like water) or heterogeneous parts (like a tree's parts). If the parts are connected, it is one by continuity; if aggregated, it is one by aggregation (e.g., a tree, a house, a dirham). Next, it discusses cases where something is a subject of both unity and multiplicity, but from different aspects. If the aspect of unity constitutes the aspect of multiplicity, we speak of generic, specific, or differential unity. If not, and unity occurs to it accidentally, it is accidental unity; otherwise it is relative unity. Finally, it enumerates the technical terms for unity in various categories: homogeneity (mujānasa) in genus, similarity (mumāthala) in species, equality (musāwāt) in quantity, resemblance (mushābaha) in quality, appropriateness (munāsaba) in relation, similarity (mushākala) in property, correspondence (muṭābaqa) in extremities (like left and right), and parallelism (muwāzāt) in arrangement of parts. This classification helps kalam and philosophical discussions on unity and multiplicity.

Key Terms
وحدة جنسية (wahda jinsiyya)
وحدة نوعية (wahda naw'iyya)
وحدة فصلية (wahda fasliyya)
وحدة بالعرض (wahda bil-'arad)
وحدة بالنسبة (wahda bin-nisba)
مماثلة (mumathala)
مجانسة (mujānasa)
مساواة (musāwāt)
مشابهة (mushābaha)
مناسبة (munāsaba)
مشاكلة (mushākala)
مطابقة (muṭābaqa)
موازاة (muwāzāt)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: There is only one type of unity: numerical oneness.
Correction: There are many types of unity, such as generic, specific, accidental, and relative, each with its own technical term.

Checkpoint
What is the term for unity in species?
A) مجانسة (mujānasa)
B) مماثلة (mumathala)
C) مساواة (musāwāt)
D) مشابهة (mushābaha)

Key Takeaway
Unity can exist alongside multiplicity from different aspects, leading to essential (generic, specific, differential), accidental, or relative unity, each with specific names for eight categories.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at someone you consider a 'sinner', do you reduce them to that sin (an accidental unity) or do you remember the essential unity (mumāthala) of your shared humanity and faith? Which aspect dominates your interactions? Will you consciously shift your perspective today?
?
This lesson shows that unity can exist from different aspects simultaneously. In your own life, do you often focus on the aspects where you feel divided from others (differences in opinion, status) while neglecting the aspects where you are united (same genus, same purpose)? What is one concrete step you can take to prioritize the unity that matters most to Allah?
?
The passage distinguishes between essential and accidental unity. Are there areas in your worship where you treat accidental aspects (e.g., form) as if they were essential, or essential aspects (e.g., sincerity) as accidental? How can you realign your priorities to match the reality of your own unity with Allah?

Objection to Two Reasons and Author's Modification
Al-Taftazani presents and responds to an objection against the two reasons.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection against the two earlier reasons and how the author modified the second reason to avoid the refutation.

Core Idea
The author sharpens his argument by shifting from survival to existence.

Sanusi (or Taftazani) anticipated objections to his arguments. In this passage, he presents an objection that the first reason is not clearer than the claim, and the second is refuted by denying two-ness upon survival. He then modifies the second reason to focus on existence instead of survival, preserving the argument's force.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Notice the two parts: the objection and the modification.
The passage is short but dense.
2
Identify the two objections
The objector gives two counterarguments. First, the reason is not clearer than the claim. Second, even if they survive, they are not necessarily two because they could have united. Understand each objection.
The second objection denies that survival implies two-ness.
3
Understand the author's modification
The author changes the second reason: instead of considering survival after union, he considers the state of existence after creation. If they are existent, they are two; if not, they are annihilated. This avoids the denial of two-ness upon survival.
This shift makes the argument more straightforward.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ورد الأول بأنه ليس أوضح من الدعوى - والثاني بمنع الاثنينية على تقدير البقاء، وإنما يلزم لو لم يتحدا، فغير إلى أنهما إن كانا موجودين كانا اثنين وإلا فكما مر
The first [reason] was objected to by [saying] it is not clearer than the claim, and the second by denying the two-ness on the presumption of survival, for that only follows if they had not united. So he changed [the argument] to that if they are existent they would be two, and otherwise as before.
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage presents an objection to the two reasons given earlier. The objector says that the first reason (that difference is essential and unremovable) is not clearer than the claim itself, so it does not prove anything. The second reason (that they would either remain as two or become annihilated) is objected to by denying that two-ness follows from survival after union; two-ness only follows if they have not actually become one. In response, the author modifies the argument: instead of talking about survival after union, he shifts to the state of existence. If after being created the two are existent, they are necessarily two; if not, it is annihilation, not union. This modification avoids the objection's denial of two-ness upon survival.

Key Terms

اثنينية
تقدير البقاء
موجودين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection is that the reasons are false or the claim is false.
Correction: The objection is that the reasons are not clearer than the claim (first) or that the second reason's inference is blocked by the possibility of union.

Checkpoint
What was the author's modification to the second reason?
A) He changed it to focus on existence rather than survival
B) He gave up the argument
C) He insisted on the original
D) He added a third reason

Key Takeaway
The author's modification strengthens the argument by making it immune to the denial of two-ness upon survival.

Why Two Things Cannot Share One Existence
Al-Taftazani refutes an objection about two things existing with one existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that two things could exist with one existence leads to an impossibility that is self-evident.

Core Idea
Two things cannot share one existence without collapsing into one.

After establishing that attributes are not identical to the essence, al-Taftazani anticipates a clever objection. The opponent suggests that two things could exist with a single existence, thereby being distinct yet somehow united. Al-Taftazani quickly dismantles this possibility by analyzing the logical alternatives.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the Arabic passage once and note the opponent's claim: it is possible for two things to exist with one existence. This would mean they are two but have a single existence.
The objection is not about the things themselves but their existences.
2
Follow the refutation's alternatives
The author points out that the single existence must be either one of the two existences or a third. If it is one of them, then the other thing lacks its own existence. If it is a third, then three existences are needed, not one.
3
Grasp the final claim of necessary judgment
When the opponent says the two existences become one, the author declares that this is impossible by a necessary judgment (ḥukm ḍarūrī) – a self-evident truth. This ends the debate.
Necessary judgment means it is rationally unavoidable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ورد بجواز أن يكونا موجودين بوجود واحد، فدفع بأنه إما أحد الوجودين، أو ثالث فأجيب بأنه نفس الوجودين صارا واحداً. فادعى أن الحكم ضروري
It was objected by [the claim] that it is possible for them to be existent with one existence. He repelled [this] by [saying] that [that one existence] is either one of the two existences or a third. So it was answered that it is the two existences themselves become one. Then he claimed that the judgment is necessary.
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage presents a second objection: the opponent suggests that two things could exist with a single existence, thus they would be one while still being two? The author refutes this by pointing out that the single existence would have to be either one of the two original existences or a completely third existence. If the opponent then says that it is the two existences themselves that become one, the author responds by asserting that the impossibility of two becoming one is a necessary judgment (ḥukm ḍarūrī), i.e., self-evident. This ends the discussion of the objections to the initial claim.

Key Terms
وجود واحد
أحد الوجودين
ثالث
حكم ضروري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Two distinct things could share a single existence without becoming identical or requiring additional existences.
Correction: The single existence must be either one of the two existences or a third; neither option yields two things with one existence. If the two become one, that is self-evidently impossible.

Checkpoint
What is the author's response to the claim that two could exist with one existence?
A) He accepts it
B) He says that existence is one of the two
C) He says it is either one of the two or a third, and the claim of the two becoming one is not accepted
D) He ignores it

Key Takeaway
The claim that two things could exist with one existence fails because the single existence must be either one of the two existences or a third, and neither option allows genuine duality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just seen that two distinct things cannot share one existence without contradiction. In your own life, are you trying to combine two incompatible commitments—like loving Allah with all your heart while clinging to a sin that demands your loyalty? Which one must you let go, and what will it cost you?
?
The opponent tried to keep both duality and unity, but logic forced a choice. Where have you been intellectually dishonest with yourself, holding two contradictory beliefs about Allah or your own faith? What step will you take today to resolve that contradiction, even if it means admitting you were wrong?
?
This argument shows that truth is not a compromise: two cannot become one. Your heart knows that only Allah deserves ultimate devotion. What worldly attachment have you been treating as an equal partner? Describe one concrete action you will take this week to demote it and restore Allah's exclusive place.

The First Proof: Essential Difference Cannot Be Removed
Al-Taftazani proves impossibility because essential difference cannot be removed, and addresses an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first proof that two things cannot become one, based on the concept of essential particularity, and the objection raised against it.

Core Idea
Two things cannot become one because their essential difference defines and preserves each thing.

In this lesson, we examine the first of two proofs that al-Taftazani offers to show that two things cannot become one. The proof builds on the idea that each thing has an essential particularity (khuṣūṣiyya) that makes it what it is. If that particularity is removed, the thing itself ceases to exist. Thus, eliminating the difference between two things would destroy them, not unite them. We also consider an objection that this proof may be circular or unconvincing.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the source passage slowly, noticing the structure: the statement of the proof, the explanation using particularity, and the objection. Then read the literal translation.
2
Understand the core argument
Identify the key claim: difference is essential and cannot be removed because each thing has a particularity. Ask yourself: What does 'particularity' mean? Why would removing it destroy the thing?
This argument is purely rational, not empirical.
3
Analyze the objection
The objection says the proof is either the very claim in dispute (circular) or just a remark no clearer than the claim. Consider whether this objection is valid. How might al-Taftazani respond?
He will provide a second proof to strengthen the case.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول : أن الاثنين سواء كانا ماهيتين أو فردين منهما أو من ماهية واحدة فالاختلاف بينهما ذاتي لا يعقل زواله، إذ لكل شيء خصوصية ما. هو بها هو، فمتى زالت الخصوصية لم يبق ذلك الشيء. واعترض بأنه إن كان استدلالاً فنفس المتنازع ، وإن كان تنبيهاً، فليس هو (٢) أوضح من الدعوى إذ ربما يقع الاشتباه في كون الاختلاف ذاتياً ممتنع الزوال دون اتحاد الاثنين .
The first [aspect]: That the two, whether they are two essences or two individuals of them or of one essence, the difference between them is essential, not rationally possible to be removed. For each thing has a particularity by which it is what it is. When the particularity is removed, that thing does not remain. And it was objected: If it is a proof, then it is the very point of dispute, and if it is a remark, it is not clearer than the claim, because there might be confusion about whether the difference is essential and impossible to remove without [assuming] the union of the two.
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This passage is the first of two proofs (wajhayn) that the author promised in the previous lesson. The proof states: Consider two things, whether they are two species (māhiyyatān) or two individuals (fardān) of them or of a single species. Their difference from each other is an essential property (dhātī) that cannot be removed. Why? Because each thing has a particularity (khuṣūṣiyya) that makes it what it is. If that particularity is removed, the thing no longer exists. Therefore, two things cannot become one because that would require removing their essential difference, which would destroy them. Then an objection is raised: If this is a proof (istidlāl), then it is the very claim in dispute (the impossibility) disguised; if it is a mere remark (tanbīh), it is not clearer than the original claim. The objection suggests that the proof is either circular or unconvincing.

Key Terms
ماهيتين
فردين
خصوصية
ذاتي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the particularity mentioned in the first proof?
A) A property that can be removed
B) The essential characteristic by which a thing is itself
C) The union of two things
D) A random attribute

Key Takeaway
The first proof argues that two things cannot become one because each thing has an essential particularity that cannot be removed without destroying it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What particularity defines you as a unique servant of Allah? If you were to lose that, would you still be the same person? How does this lesson urge you to guard your identity as a believer?
?
The objection in the lesson warns against circular reasoning. In your own journey of faith, have you ever accepted a belief simply because it was repeated, without truly verifying its foundation? Today, examine one of your deep-seated convictions—is it built on a sound proof or just a comfortable assumption?
?
You affirm that Allah alone is unique and nothing can be compared to Him. But do you, in your thoughts, subtly liken Him to something you know? Confess the moments your heart slipped, and resolve to train your mind to uphold tawhid with the clarity this lesson demands.

Second Proof: The Impossibility of Union of Two Things
Al-Taftazani presents the second proof and its reformulation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second rational proof that two things cannot become one, and how al-Taftazani modifies the argument to answer a key objection.

Core Idea
If two things exist, they are two, not one—union is logically impossible.

This lesson presents the second proof against the possibility of two things uniting into one. Al-Taftazani argues through three scenarios: if both remain, they are two; if one remains, the other is annihilated; if neither remains, a third thing appears. An objection challenges the first scenario, leading the author to refine his formulation by focusing on existence after creation rather than survival after union.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Let the author's own wording sink in before analyzing.
Note the footnote markers—they indicate editorial additions, not part of the original text.
2
Identify the three scenarios after union
The passage presents three possibilities for what could happen to the two things after a supposed union. Find each one and notice what conclusion each leads to.
The third scenario is 'nothing remains' – what happens then?
3
Understand the objection and the modification
The objector denies that if both remain they are two. Read the author's response – he changes the basis of the argument from 'after union' to 'after creation'. Why does this avoid the objection?
The modification hinges on the logical principle that two distinct existences cannot be one.
4
Reflect on the overall proof
Consider how the proof covers all logical possibilities. Does the modification make the argument stronger? How does it connect to the broader topic of union in kalam?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : أن الاثنين بعد الاتحاد وإن كانا باقيين فهما اثنان لا واحد، وإلا فإن بقي أحدهما فقط (۳) ، كان هذا فناء لأحدهما وبقاء للآخر، وإن لم يبق شيء منهما كان هذا فناء لهما ، وحدوث أمر ثالث، وأياً ما كان فلا اتحاد. واعترض بأنا لا نسلم أنهما لو بقيا كانا اثنين لا واحداً، وإنما يلزم ذلك. لو لم يتحدا فعدل إلى تقرير آخر ، وهو أنهما (٤) بعد الإيجاد(ه) ، إن كانا موجودين كانا اثنين لا محالة . وإلا فإما أن يكون أحدهما موجوداً فقط، أو لا يكون شيء منهما موجوداً، فكان هذا فناء لأحدهما ، وبقاء للآخر، أو فناء لهما ، وحدوث أمر (٦) ثالث .
The second [aspect]: That the two after union, if they remain, are two not one. Otherwise, if only one of them remains, this is annihilation of one and survival of the other; and if nothing of them remains, this is annihilation of both and the occurrence of a third. Whatever the case, there is no union. And it was objected: We do not grant that if they remain they are two not one; that only follows if they have not united. So he changed to another formulation: that they after creation, if they exist, are two inevitably. Otherwise, either only one of them exists, or nothing of them exists, so this would be annihilation of one and survival of the other, or annihilation of both and the occurrence of a third.
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This passage contains the second proof. The author argues: Consider two things after a supposed union. If they both still exist, they are two, not one. If only one remains, the other has been annihilated, so there is no union of the two. If neither remains, a third thing has come into being, which is not the original two. Thus, in all scenarios, union is impossible. An objection is raised: the objector denies that survival implies two-ness; that only follows if they have not actually become one. In response, the author modifies the argument: instead of speaking about survival after union, he reformulates it in terms of existence after creation. If the two exist after being created, they are two; if not, it is annihilation. This modification avoids the objection's denial of two-ness upon survival, because it is based on the logical impossibility of two distinct existents being one existent.

Key Terms
باقيين
فناء
تقرير آخر
إيجاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The modification simply restates the same argument without change.
Correction: The modification shifts the basis from 'after union' to 'after creation', which avoids the objection that survival after a hypothetical union might still allow unity.

Checkpoint
In the modified formulation, what is the consequence if both things exist after creation?
A) They are one by virtue of creation
B) They are two inevitably
C) They could be one if united
D) They annihilate each other

Key Takeaway
This proof shows that two distinct things cannot become one, because any attempt at union results either in two things, annihilation, or a third, never true union.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The proof shows that if two created things remain after union, they are still two distinct things—not one. Do you ever, in your heart, mentally combine Allah’s attributes with those of creation, making Him somehow like His creatures? How can you use this proof to purify your understanding of tawheed?
?
The reformulation shifted from 'after union' to 'after creation' to avoid a flawed assumption. What hidden assumption about Allah might you be carrying that needs to be identified and corrected through rigorous reasoning? Will you take the time to examine it today?
?
This argument strips away any possibility of union between two things. If even created things cannot become one, how much more impossible is it for the Creator to unite with creation? What does this mean for how you worship—are you truly directing all worship to the One alone, or are you unconsciously 'uniting' Him with other objects of love or fear?

Condition of Difference and Impossibility of Two Similars
Discusses disputed condition of difference and impossibility of two similar accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the condition of difference is disputed in kalam and the initial argument for the impossibility of two similar accidents coexisting, along with its refutation.

Core Idea
Two similar accidents cannot coexist because they are distinguished only by location—but even this is disputed.

Sanusi introduces a crucial dialectical point in the theory of accidents: can two instances of the same type of accident exist in one substrate? The answer is not straightforward, as the condition of difference itself is contested. In this passage, you will encounter the starting proof and its immediate rebuttal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage has a claim, a proof, and a refutation.
2
Identify the disputed claim
The passage begins by stating that 'the condition of difference is disputed.' Ask yourself: what exactly is being disputed?
This is the starting point of the dialectic.
3
Understand the proof
The argument for impossibility: two similar accidents cannot be distinguished except by their substrate. Grasp why this leads to the conclusion.
The proof relies on the idea that without substrate, individuals of the same type are indistinguishable.
4
Examine the refutation
The refutation is a simple denial, followed by a counterexample: blackness intensities are different types, not multiple similars. See how this undermines the proof.
The counterexample shows that what appears to be multiple similar accidents can be explained as sequential different types.
5
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this dispute matters for the broader kalam project of understanding accidents and their relationship to substances.
This debate affects how we categorize attributes and their occurrence in the world.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اشتراط التغاير مختلف فيه. قال : أما اشتراط التغاير فمختلف فيه، ويمتنع اجتماع المثلين لأنه لا يعقل تمايز أفراد نوع من الأعراض إلا بالمحل. ورد بالمنع. وليس شدة سواد الجسم باجتماع سوادين وأكثر، بل السوادات المتفاوتة بالشدة والضعف أنواع متخالفة تتعاقب على الجسم.
The condition of difference is disputed. He said: As for the condition of difference, it is disputed. And it is impossible for two similars to coexist because the distinctness of individuals of a type of accidents cannot be conceived except by the substrate. It was refuted by denial. And the intensity of blackness of a body is not by the coexistence of two or more blacknesses; rather, blacknesses varying in intensity and weakness are different types that successively occur upon the body.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage presents an initial argument and response within the kalām topic of whether two similar accidents can coexist in one substrate. It begins by stating that the condition of difference between two things (for them to be considered similar or different) is itself a disputed matter. Then it asserts the impossibility of two similar accidents coexisting, offering the proof that individuals of a type of accident cannot be distinguished except by their substrate. The argument is immediately rebutted with a counterexample: the increasing blackness of a body is not due to multiple blacknesses simultaneously present, but rather blacknesses of varying intensity are actually different types (not identical) that sequentially occur on the body. Thus, the initial proof is not universally valid.

Key Terms
اشتراط التغاير
اجتماع المثلين
الأعراض
المحل
السواد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the condition of difference is essential and universally required for similarity judgments in kalam.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that the condition of difference is disputed, showing that not all theologians agree on its necessity.

Checkpoint
What is the initial proof given for the impossibility of two similar accidents coexisting?
A) They cannot be distinguished except by their substrate.
B) Their coexistence would necessitate opposites.
C) The Mu'tazila proved their occurrence.
D) They are both eternal.

Key Takeaway
The initial argument that two similar accidents cannot coexist because they are only distinguished by substrate is not universally accepted, as shown by the counterexample of blackness intensities being different types.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hold a belief about faith or theology, do you ever stop to examine the logical conditions underpinning it? This lesson shows that even the 'condition of difference' is disputed. Are you willing to question the foundations of your own beliefs, especially those you take for granted? Commit to examining one assumption this week.
?
The passage presents a proof and then a refutation. In your own life, when you are convinced of something, are you open to counterexamples that challenge your reasoning? Reflect on a time you dismissed a refutation without truly considering it. What would it mean for you to embrace the dialectical spirit of kalam?
?
The blackness example shows that what appears similar may actually be different types. How often do you judge others' actions or faith based on surface similarity, without understanding deeper differences? Ask yourself: where am I conflating things that are actually distinct? Challenge yourself to ask Allah for deeper insight.

The Core Proof: Two Similar Accidents Cannot Coexist
Sanusi states the impossibility of two similar accidents coexisting and gives the proof from essence and substrate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the foundational argument that similar accidents cannot coexist in one substrate because distinction requires the substrate.

Core Idea
Similar accidents become one if they share the same substrate.

The Mutakallimun set out to prove that two identical accidents cannot be in the same place. This seems counterintuitive, but the reasoning is precise: if two things are exactly alike in essence, they can only be told apart by where they are. If they are in the same location, they merge into one. Let's read Sanusi's formulation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the claim and the proof structure.
2
Identify the key components
Look for the claim, the proof, the objection, and the refutation. Mark 'lana' as beginning of proof.
3
Understand the proof logic
Why does shared essence plus shared substrate eliminate twoness? Trace the reasoning.
The substrate is what individuates accidents.
4
Consider the objections
Think about the two objections Sanusi addresses. How does he refute them?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : ويمتنع اجتماع المثلين يعني أن المثلين إذا كانا من قبيل الأعراض يمتنع اجتماعهما في محل واحد، خلافاً للمعتزلة لنا أن العرضين إذا اشتركا في الماهية والصفات النفسية لم يعقل بينهما تمايز إلا بحسب المحل، لأن قيامها به، ووجودهما تبع لوجوده، فإذا اتحدت الماهية وما يتبعه الهوية، ارتفعت الاثنينية ورد بالمنع الجواز أن يختص كل بعوارض مستندة إلى أسباب مفارقة، وبهذا يمنع ما ذكر في المحصل من أن نسبة العوارض إلى كل منهما على السوية، فلا تعرض لأحدهما خاصة، بل لكل منهما، وحينئذ لا يبقى الامتياز البتة، ويلزم الاتحاد وأما الاعتراض بأن عدم الامتياز لا يدل على الاتحاد بل غايته عدم العلم لأثنينيته، فليس بشيء، لأن ما ذكر على تقدير تمامه يفيد عدم الامتياز في نفس الأمر لا عند العقل فقط، وقد يستدل بأنه لو جاز اجتماع المثلين لجاز لمن له علم نظري بشيء أن ينظر لتحصيل العلم به، إذ لا مانع سوى امتناع اجتماع المثلين وبأنه لو جاز لما حصل القطع باتحاد شيء من الأعراض الجواز أن يكون أمثالاً كثيرة مجتمعة واللازم باطل للقطع بذلك في كثير من الأعراض. وبأنه لو جاز اجتماعهما لجاز افتراقهما بزوال أحد المثلين ضرورة أنه ليس بواجب وزواله ليس إلا بطريان ضده الذي هو ضد للمثل الآخر الباقي، فيلزم اجتماع الضدين. ورد الأولان بمنع الملازمة الجواز مانع آخر، فانتفاء شرط النظر وهو عدم العلم بالمطلوب، والجواز القطع بانتفاء الممكن ضرورة أو استدلالاً. المقدمات والثالث بمنع تمست المعتزلة بالوقوع، فإن الجسم يعرض له سواد ثم آخر وآخر إلى أن يبلغ غاية السواد. وأجيب بأنا لا نسلم ذلك، بل السوادات المتفاوتة بالشدة والضعف أنواع من اللون متخالفة بالحقيقة، متشاركة في عارض مقول عليها التشكك هو مطلق السواد يعرض للجسم الذي يشتد سواده على التدريج في كل آن نوع آخر.
He said: It is impossible for two similars to coexist, meaning that if two similars are of the kind of accidents, it is impossible for them to coexist in one substrate, contrary to the Mu'tazila. For us, if two accidents share in essence and essential attributes, no distinction between them can be conceived except by the substrate, because their inherence in it and their existence are dependent on its existence. So if the essence and what follows it, namely identity, are unified, twoness ceases. And it was refuted by the denial of the possibility that each is specified by accidents arising from separate causes, and by this is prevented what was mentioned in al-Muhassal that the relation of the accidents to each of them is equal; so no accident is particular to one of them, but each has accidents, and then no distinction remains at all, and union is necessitated. As for the objection that lack of distinction does not indicate union but rather is lack of knowledge of its twoness, it is nothing, because what was mentioned, if complete, indicates lack of distinction in reality, not only in the intellect. And it may be argued that if the coexistence of two similars were permissible, then it would be permissible for one who has theoretical knowledge of something to reflect in order to attain knowledge of it, since there is no obstacle except the impossibility of the coexistence of two similars. And that if it were permissible, then certainty would not be attained regarding the unity of any accident, because it could be many similars coexisting, and the consequent is false because of certainty in many accidents. And that if their coexistence were permissible, then their separation would be permissible by the removal of one of the two similars, since it is not necessary, and its removal is only by the occurrence of its opposite, which is opposite to the other remaining similar, so the coexistence of opposites would be necessary. The first two arguments were refuted by denying the entailment, because there may be another obstacle, such as the absence of the condition of reflection, which is non-knowledge of the desired, and the possibility of certainty about the non-existence of the possible by necessity or inference. The third was refuted by denial. The Mu'tazila held by occurrence: for a body is subject to blackness, then another, then another until it reaches extreme blackness. And it was answered: We do not accept that; rather, the blacknesses varying in intensity and weakness are types of color differing in reality, sharing in an accident predicated to them by analogy (tashkīk), which is absolute blackness, and the body whose blackness intensifies gradually is subject in each moment to another type.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage presents a sustained kalām proof for the impossibility of two similar accidents coexisting in one substrate, and responds to Mu'tazila objections. The proof begins from the premise that if two accidents share the same essence and essential attributes, they cannot be distinguished except by their substrate. If the essence and accompanying identity (al-huwiyya) are unified, then twoness (al-ithnayniyya) is eliminated—they become one. The objection that lack of distinction does not necessarily entail union is dismissed because the argument shows real lack of distinction, not merely in the intellect. Three further demonstrations are offered: (1) If it were possible, then theoretical knowledge could be attained without reflection, since the only obstacle to attaining knowledge is the impossibility of two similars; (2) If it were possible, we could never be certain that an accident is one rather than many similars; (3) If it were possible for the two similars to separate, one ceasing to exist, then its opposite would arise, which is also opposite to the other remaining similar, thus requiring the coexistence of opposites. The first two objections are rebutted by denying the entailment, pointing out that other obstacles (like lack of knowledge of the desired) may exist. The third is simply denied. Finally, the Mu'tazila argue from empirical occurrence: a body can acquire successively multiple blacknesses until it reaches extreme blackness, suggesting multiple similars can coexist. The response is that these blacknesses are not truly similar; they are different types of color differing in reality, but are called black through a form of analogical predication (tashkīk). Thus, no two identical accidents coexist.

Key Terms
اجتماع المثلين
الماهية
الصفات النفسية
المحل
الاثنينية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that multiple similar accidents can coexist if they have different accidental properties.
Correction: The argument shows that any accidental properties would apply equally to both, so they are not a basis for distinction.

Checkpoint
According to the argument, what is the only possible basis for distinguishing two similar accidents?
A) Their essence
B) Their substrate
C) Their accidental attributes
D) Their cause

Key Takeaway
The foundational argument against similar accidents is that identity of essence and substrate forces them to be one.

Three Supporting Proofs for the Impossibility of Similar Accidents
Sanusi presents three additional proofs against the coexistence of similar accidents and their refutations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain three additional arguments that reinforce the impossibility of two similar accidents coexisting and understand how they are refuted.

Core Idea
If two similars could coexist, we would lose certainty in knowledge and logical consistency.

After establishing the core argument, the author provides three further proofs that show absurd consequences if two similar accidents could coexist. Each proof is then countered by opponents, leading to a dialectical exchange. Let's examine these arguments in detail.

Guided Path
1
Read the three proofs
Read the Arabic and translation carefully.
2
Identify each proof's structure
Each proof is a conditional: if A then B, but B is false, so A is false. Find the antecedent and consequent.
3
Understand the refutations
The first two refutations deny the entailment by citing other possible obstacles. The third is simply denied.
4
Reflect on the dialectic
Consider how these arguments strengthen the main thesis while acknowledging counterarguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Checkpoint
What is the common method used to refute the first two proofs?
A) Denying the premise
B) Denying the entailment by citing other possible obstacles
C) Accepting the conclusion
D) Using empirical evidence

Key Takeaway
The three proofs illustrate the absurd consequences of allowing similar accidents, but each is refuted by denying the logical entailment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a difficult question in your faith, do you immediately seek reflection (nazar) or do you settle for a convenient assumption? The first proof warns that assuming no obstacle can lead to false certainty. What is one belief you hold without having properly reflected on its logical foundation, and what step will you take today to examine it?
?
The third proof shows that allowing similar accidents would force opposites to coexist—a contradiction. In your own life, do you sometimes hold contradictory views about Allah or His attributes, thinking both can be true without resolution? How will you actively resolve that tension using reasoning as modeled in this lesson?
?
This passage demonstrates that refuting an argument does not require disproving its premises; sometimes denial is enough when a premise is not necessary. Are there assumptions in your worship or practice that you accept merely because you've never questioned them? Commit to identifying one such assumption and testing it against revelation and reason this week.

The Mu'tazila's Empirical Objection and the Answer: Gradation of Blackness
Sanusi presents the Mu'tazila's occurrence argument and the response differentiating types of blackness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila's argument from progressive darkening and the response that different shades of black are distinct types, not truly similar.

Core Idea
The empirical case of increasing blackness does not prove similar accidents coexist; each shade is a different type of black.

The Mu'tazila countered the theoretical proofs by pointing to a common phenomenon: a body can become successively darker, suggesting multiple blacknesses coexist. The answer is subtle: these blacknesses are not truly identical; they differ in intensity and are distinct types of color. Let's examine this important exchange.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazila argument and response
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the empirical claim and the answer.
2
Understand tashkik
The response uses the concept of tashkik (gradation) to explain how blackness is predicated analogically.
Tashkik means the term applies to different degrees of the same quality.
3
See the distinction
Each shade of black is a different type, not a repetition of the same accident.
4
Reflect on the challenge
Why is this a strong objection? How does the response preserve the original principle?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The gradual darkening of an object proves that two identical blacknesses can coexist.
Correction: The passage explains that each shade is a different type; they are not identical.

Checkpoint
According to the response, how are the different blacknesses in a darkening body related?
A) They are identical
B) They are different types sharing the name by analogy
C) They are opposites
D) They are parts of one blackness

Key Takeaway
The empirical objection fails because the observed blacknesses are different types, not truly similar accidents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson shows that what appears to be a single quality changing can actually be a succession of distinct entities. Where in your own life do you treat something as 'the same thing just getting stronger' — like your anger, your patience, or your faith — when in reality each moment might be a new creation from Allah? What would change if you saw each 'intensification' as a new gift requiring a new response?
?
The Mu'tazila trusted their empirical observation, but the response corrected them using deeper rational principles. When have you been so convinced by what you see that you dismissed a higher truth? What would it cost you to submit your senses to the authority of divine revelation and sound reason, even when they contradict your experience?
?
The term 'tashkik' reminds us that two things can share a name without being identical in reality. Where in your relationships or self-assessment do you confuse sharing a label (like 'love', 'sincerity', or 'knowledge') with possessing the same reality? How can you begin to discern the different 'types' of these qualities in yourself today?

Refuting Objections to the Impossibility of Two Similars
Al-Taftazani refutes objections and answers the Mu'tazila's argument from occurrence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Taftazani defends the Ash'ari doctrine that two exactly similar entities cannot coexist by refuting objections and answering the Mu'tazila's argument from occurrence.

Core Idea
Two similars cannot coexist; apparent examples like intensities of blackness are actually different types.

This passage presents al-Taftazani's dialectical defense of a core theological premise: the impossibility of two exactly similar entities coexisting. He addresses objections, presents proofs, and counters the Mu'tazila's empirical argument from successive blacknesses. The discussion illustrates the rigorous method of kalam argumentation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Allow the author's own argument structure to emerge naturally.
Focus on the flow: objection → proofs → refutations → Mu'tazila argument → response.
2
Identify the two main parts
Notice the passage has two major sections: first, a refutation of an initial objection; second, a response to the Mu'tazila's argument from occurrence. Within the first section, there are three proofs for the impossibility.
The three proofs are bracketed by 'قد يستدل بأنه' and 'وبأنه'.
3
Understand the key concept of tashkik
The Mu'tazila argue that a body can have successive blacknesses, showing similars can coexist. Al-Taftazani answers that these blacknesses differ in intensity and are thus different types of color, sharing only the accidental predicate of blackness in a graded sense (tashkik).
Tashkik (التشكك) refers to equivocal predication where one term applies to multiple realities in different degrees.
4
Reflect on the apologetic method
Consider how al-Taftazani uses logical refutation: denying necessary connections (man' al-mulazama) and denying premises (man'). This is typical of Ash'ari dialectics.
The first two proofs are refuted by showing another possible obstacle (ignorance of the desired outcome). The third is simply denied.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الاعتراض بأن عدم الامتياز لا يدل على الاتحاد بل غايته عدم العلم لأثنينيته، فليس بشيء، لأن ما ذكر على تقدير تمامه يفيد عدم الامتياز في نفس الأمر لا عند العقل فقط، وقد يستدل بأنه لو جاز اجتماع المثلين لجاز لمن له علم نظري بشيء أن ينظر لتحصيل العلم به، إذ لا مانع سوى امتناع اجتماع المثلين وبأنه لو جاز لما حصل القطع باتحاد شيء من الأعراض لجواز أن يكون أمثالاً كثيرة مجتمعة واللازم باطل للقطع بذلك في كثير من الأعراض. وبأنه لو جاز اجتماعهما لجاز افتراقهما بزوال أحد المثلين ضرورة أنه ليس بواجب وزواله ليس إلا بطريان ضده الذي هو ضد للمثل الآخر الباقي، فيلزم اجتماع الضدين. ورد الأولان بمنع الملازمة لجواز مانع آخر، فانتفاء شرط النظر وهو عدم العلم بالمطلوب، وجواز القطع بانتفاء الممكن ضرورة أو استدلالاً. المقدمات والثالث بمنع. تمسكت المعتزلة بالوقوع، فإن الجسم يعرض له سواد ثم آخر وآخر إلى أن يبلغ غاية السواد. وأجيب بأنا لا نسلم ذلك، بل السوادات المتفاوتة بالشدة والضعف أنواع من اللون متخالفة بالحقيقة، متشاركة في عارض مقول عليها التشكك هو مطلق السواد يعرض للجسم الذي يشتد سواده على التدريج في كل آن نوع آخر.
As for the objection that lack of distinction does not indicate union, rather its utmost is lack of knowledge of duality, it is nothing, because what was mentioned, assuming its completeness, yields lack of distinction in reality, not only for the intellect. And it may be argued that if the coexistence of two similars were permissible, then it would be permissible for one who has theoretical knowledge of something to investigate to acquire knowledge of it, since there is no obstacle except the impossibility of the coexistence of two similars. And [it may be argued] that if it were permissible, then certainty about the oneness of an accident would not be attained, because it would be possible for there to be many similar [accidents] coexisting, and the consequent is false by certainty in many accidents. And [it may be argued] that if their coexistence were permissible, then their separation would be permissible by the removal of one of the two similars, necessarily because it is not necessary, and its removal is only by the occurrence of its opposite, which is opposite to the other remaining similar, so the coexistence of opposites would be necessary. The first two [arguments] are refuted by denying the necessary connection due to the possibility of another obstacle: the absence of a condition of investigation, which is the lack of knowledge of the desired [outcome], and the possibility of certainty of the impossibility of the possible necessarily or by deduction. The premises and the third [argument are refuted] by denial. The Mu'tazila argued by actual occurrence: for the body is affected by blackness, then another [blackness], then another, until it reaches the extreme of blackness. And it was answered: we do not accept that; rather, the blacknesses that differ in intensity and weakness are types of color differing in reality, sharing an accidental predicate called 'tashkik' (equivocal) which is absolute blackness that occurs to the body whose blackness intensifies gradually, in each moment another type.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical defense of the Ash'ari doctrine that two exactly similar entities cannot coexist. Al-Taftazani begins by rejecting an objection that lack of distinction does not prove union but only ignorance—he argues that the argument, if sound, shows real lack of distinction. He then presents three proofs for the impossibility: (1) if two similars could coexist, then one with theoretical knowledge could investigate to acquire knowledge, but the only obstacle would be impossibility of coexistence; (2) if they could coexist, we could not be certain any accident is one; (3) if they coexist, separation by removal of one requires its opposite, leading to coexistence of opposites. However, he notes that the first two proofs are refuted by denying the necessary connection—there could be another obstacle (lack of knowledge of the desired outcome). The third proof is simply denied. Then the Mu'tazila argue from actual occurrence: a body can have successive blacknesses, appearing to be multiple similars. Al-Taftazani answers that these blacknesses are not truly similar; they differ in intensity and are different types of color, sharing only the accidental predicate of blackness in a graded sense (tashkik). Thus the argument from occurrence fails.

Key Terms
اجتماع المثلين
امتناع
التضاد
التشكك

Misconception: The Mu'tazila believe that successive blacknesses on a body prove that two similar entities (blacknesses) can coexist.
Correction: Al-Taftazani argues these blacknesses are not truly similar because they differ in intensity and are different types of color, sharing only the accidental predicate 'blackness' in a graded sense.

Checkpoint
How does al-Taftazani respond to the Mu'tazila's argument that a body can have successive blacknesses?
A) He accepts it and modifies the doctrine.
B) He denies that they are true similars because they differ in intensity.
C) He claims the blacknesses are not accidents.
D) He argues that the substrate changes.

Key Takeaway
Al-Taftazani defends the impossibility of two similars by denying that successive blacknesses are truly similar—they differ in intensity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you are absolutely certain about a belief, do you ever pause to consider that your certainty might be due to ignorance of a deeper possibility, as the objection suggests about lack of distinction? How can you guard against this in your faith?
?
The Mu'tazila used observable blacknesses to argue for the coexistence of similars. In your life, when do you mistake superficial similarities for real metaphysical unity, and how can you learn to discern the deeper differences?
?
Al-Taftazani was willing to admit that some of his proofs were not sound. Is there a cherished argument of yours that you cling to even after seeing its flaws? Will you examine it honestly and let it go if necessary?

Taghayur and Classification of Difference
Defines taghayur and classifies two things as mithlan or mukhalifan.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of taghayur and how two things can be classified as similars (mithlan) or differents (mukhalifan), including the concept of opposites (mutaqabilan).

Core Idea
Multiplicity implies difference; difference is either in essence or accident.

The philosophers begin by establishing that multiplicity itself implies difference. But not all difference is the same; some things are perfectly similar in essence, while others are different. This passage lays out the key categories of difference that are foundational for understanding opposition and logical relations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic once without analysis, then read the translation. Pay attention to the key terms like تغاير, مثلان, متخالفان.
2
Understand the definition of taghayur
The passage states that multiplicity necessitates difference. Notice how difference is qualified by reality, accident, or consideration.
Taghayur is a necessary consequence of multiplicity.
3
Distinguish similars from differents
Two things are either alike in complete essence (mithlan) or not (mukhalifan). This is the primary division.
Similars share the entire mahiyya; differents do not.
4
Explore opposites
Among differents, some are opposites (mutaqabilan) defined by impossibility of co-existence in one locus at one time from one aspect.
Opposition requires more than just difference.
5
Analyze the conditions
The conditions exclude similars, non-opposite differents, and clarify the limitations of time and aspect.
Conditions prevent false inclusion or exclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفلاسفة، فالكثرة تستلزم التغاير بمعنى أن كل اثنين فهما غيران، فإن كانت الأثنينية بالحقيقة، فبالحقيقة أو بالعارض فبالعارض، أو بالاعتبار فبالاعتبار، ثم الغيران إما أن يشتركا في تمام الماهية كزيد وعمرو في الإنسانية أو لا . فالأول المثلان، والثاني المتخالفان سواء اشتركا في ذاتي أو عرضي أو لم يشتركا أصلا، ثم المتخالفان قد يكونان متقابلين كالسواد والبياض، وقد لا يتقابلان كالسواد والحلاوة والمتقابلان هما المتخالفان اللذان يمتنع اجتماعهما في محل واحد في زمان واحد من جهة واحدة، فخرج بقيد التخالف المثلان، وإن امتنع اجتماعهما، وبقيد امتناع الاجتماع في محل مثل السواد والحلاوة، مما يمكن اجتماعهما، وربما يفهم من امتناع الاجتماع في محل وردهما على المحل فيخرج مثل الإنسان والفرس، ومثل الإنسان والسواد، وفيه بحث سيجيء، وأما قيد وحدة الزمان فمستدرك على ما مر، وكذا قيد وحدة الجهة إذا قصد به الاحتراز عن مثل الصغر مع الكبر والأبوة مع البنوة على الإطلاق. والحق أنه احتراز عن خروج مثل ذلك فإنهما متقابلان ولا يمتنع اجتماعها إلا عند اعتبار وحدة الجهة وأما التقييد بوحدة المحل، فلأن المتقابلين قد يجتمعان في الوجود وفي الجسم على الإطلاق كبياض الرومي، وسواد الحبشي.
The philosophers: multiplicity necessitates difference (taghayur), meaning that every two things are two distinct entities. If the duality is in reality, then [the difference is] in reality; if accidental, then accidental; if by consideration, then by consideration. Then the two distinct things either share in the complete essence (mahiyya), like Zayd and Amr in humanity, or not. The first are similars (mithlan); the second are differents (mukhalifan), whether they share in an essential or accidental [property] or do not share at all. Then the differents may be opposites (mutaqabilan), like blackness and whiteness, or may not be opposites, like blackness and sweetness. Opposites are the two differents whose co-existence is impossible in one locus (mahall) at one time from one aspect. Thus, by the condition of difference the similars are excluded, even if their co-existence is impossible; and by the condition of impossibility of co-existence in a locus, [things] like blackness and sweetness, which can co-exist, are excluded. And perhaps it is understood from the impossibility of co-existence in a locus that it [i.e., the condition] includes their relation to the locus, so that [things] like man and horse, and man and blackness, are excluded. But there is a discussion on that which will come. As for the condition of unity of time, it is additional to what has been mentioned. Similarly the condition of unity of aspect if intended to exclude cases like smallness with largeness and paternity with sonship absolutely. The truth is that it excludes such cases, for they are opposites and their co-existence is not impossible except when considering unity of aspect. As for the restriction to unity of locus, it is because opposites may co-exist in existence and in the body absolutely, like the whiteness of a Roman and the blackness of an Ethiopian.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage defines taghayur (difference) and classifies the types of difference in the context of opposition (taqabul). It begins by stating that multiplicity requires difference, meaning that if two things are distinct in reality, their difference is real, and so on. Then it divides two things into either sharing the complete essence (similars, mithlan) or not (differents, mukhalifan). Among differents, some are opposites (mutaqabilan) like black and white, and some are not, like black and sweet. The definition of opposites is given: two differents that cannot coexist in one locus at one time from one aspect. The author then explains the significance of each condition: 'difference' excludes similars, 'impossibility of coexistence in a locus' excludes things that can coexist, and notes that the phrase might be understood as including the relation to the locus, which would exclude many cases, but that requires further discussion. The conditions of unity of time and unity of aspect are discussed; the latter is to exclude cases like smallness and largeness which are opposites but can coexist in different aspects. The unity of locus is necessary because opposites can coexist in the whole body in different parts, e.g., a Roman's whiteness and an Ethiopian's blackness can exist in one body.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Opposites are simply any two different things.
Correction: Opposites require specific conditions: they must be differents that cannot coexist in one locus at one time from one aspect.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Two things are different by virtue of multiplicity; they are either perfectly similar in essence or different, and among differents, opposites are those that cannot coexist in one place, time, and aspect.

What step can you take to resolve that contradiction?
