The Four Types of Opposition in Logic
Classifies opposition into four types with conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four types of opposition (correlatives, contraries, privation/possession, affirmation/negation) and how they are distinguished based on whether the opposites are existential and how they relate to the subject.

Core Idea
Opposition splits into four types based on existentiality and subject readiness.

In Islamic logic, opposition (taqabul) is a key concept for reasoning about pairs of terms. This lesson presents a classification schema that organizes all pairs of opposites into four distinct types. By understanding this schema, you will be able to analyze any pair of concepts and identify the specific type of opposition they fall under, laying a foundation for deeper theological and philosophical arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice how the classification unfolds through conditional statements.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two main branches
The classification first divides opposition into two broad categories: both opposites existential, or one existential and one non-existential. Note the conditions for each sub-type.
3
Learn the four specific types
Under the existential branch, we have correlatives (tadhaaf) and contraries (tadaad). Under the mixed branch, we have privation/possession (malaka wa 'adam) and affirmation/negation (ijab wa salb). Understand the defining condition for each.
4
Explore the nuance about two non-existentials
The passage discusses whether two non-existential terms can be opposites. It presents a view that they cannot, with arguments about unity of subject. Consider this subtlety.
This is a debated point; the author gives a counterview.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : فإذا كانا وجوديين، فإن كان تعق لكل محل واحد من جهة واحدة بالقياس إلى الآخر فمتضايفان، وإلا فمتضادان وإن كان أحدهما عدمياً، فإن تقيد بكون الموضوع مستعداً للوجودي بحسب شخصه أو نوعه، أو جنسه القريب أو البعيد فملكة وعدم ، وإلا فإيجاب وسلب، وإذا لم يمتنع أن يكون نقيض العدمي عدمياً كالامتناع واللامتناع، والعمى واللا عمى بمعنى رفعه أعم من البصر، وعدم الاستعداد له، فالأقرب أن يقال إن كان أحد المتقابلين رفعاً للآخر فملكة وعدم، أو إيجاب وسلب، وإلا فتضايف، أو تضاد على ما ذكر وقد يقال : لا تقابل بين العدميين، أما المطلق والمضاف فظاهر، وأما المضافان فلاجتماعهما في غير ما أضيفا إليه كاللاسواد واللابياض في الأحمر، ولكون التقابل مشروطاً بوحدة الموضوع، وبهذا خرج مثل الإنسانية مع الفرسية، والملزوم مع عدم اللازم. وفيه نظر.
He said: So if both [opposites] are existential, then if each is understood in relation to the other, they are correlatives (mutada'ifan); otherwise they are contraries (mutadaddan). If one of them is non-existential, then if it is conditioned by the subject's being ready for the existential according to its own individual, or its species, or its proximate or remote genus, then it is privation and possession (malaka wa 'adam); otherwise it is affirmation and negation (ijab wa salb). And if it is not impossible for the opposite of a non-existential to be non-existential—like impossibility and non-impossibility, and blindness and non-blindness in the sense of its removal being more general than sight and lack of preparation for it—then it is closer to say that if one of the opposites is a removal of the other, then it is privation and possession or affirmation and negation; otherwise it is correlation or contrariety as mentioned. And it may be said: there is no opposition between two non-existentials. As for the absolute and the relative, it is obvious; as for two relative [non-existentials], because they can coexist in what they are not attributed to, like non-blackness and non-whiteness in red, and because opposition is conditioned by unity of subject. By this, cases like humanity and horseness, and the implied and the absence of the necessary, are excluded. There is consideration in this.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage presents a classification of the four types of opposition (taqabul) based on the ontological status of the two opposites. The classification is attributed to an authority (maybe the teacher or a text). First, if both opposites are existential (wujudiyan), then if each is conceivable only in relation to the other (tadhaaf), they are correlatives; otherwise they are contraries (tadaad). If one is existential and the other non-existential, then the non-existential is considered relative to the subject's capacity: if the subject is ready to receive the existential (either individually, by species, or genus), it is privation and possession (malaka wa 'adam); otherwise it is affirmation and negation (ijab wa salb). Then a nuance is discussed: if the opposite of a non-existential is also non-existential (like impossibility vs. non-impossibility), then the classification is based on whether one is a removal of the other. A view is presented that there is no opposition between two non-existentials, with arguments: absolute non-existentials (like non-man) and relative ones (like non-blackness and non-whiteness) can coexist in the same thing (e.g., non-blackness and non-whiteness in a red object). The opposing view rejects this because opposition requires unity of subject. Examples like humanity vs. horseness (not opposites) and the implied vs. absence of necessary are excluded.

Key Terms
وجوديان
عدميان
متضايفان
متضادان
ملكة
عدم
إيجاب
سلب
موضوع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Privation and possession only apply to individual subjects (e.g., a specific person's blindness).
Correction: The passage states that the subject's readiness can be by species or genus, so privation/possession can apply to kinds (e.g., sight for the species of humans), not just individuals.

Checkpoint
When are two existential opposites considered correlatives (mutada'ifan)?
A) When they cannot coexist in one subject.
B) When each is understood only in relation to the other.
C) When they share the same essence.
D) When one removes the other.

Key Takeaway
Opposition is classified into four types based on whether the opposites are existential and how they relate to the subject: correlatives, contraries, privation/possession, and affirmation/negation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The passage teaches that some opposites are understood only in relation to each other, like father and son. In your relationship with Allah, can you truly appreciate His mercy without also wrestling with His justice? Which of these attributes have you neglected, and what step will you take this week to bring both into your understanding?
?

What are the 'absences' in your life—like lack of knowledge or lack of humility—that you have dismissed as insignificant? Truth Conditions of Affirmation and Negation
Author objects and answers regarding truth conditions of affirmation/negation when subject is non-existent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the truth conditions of affirmation and negation differ from other logical opposites, especially when the subject is non-existent, and how to resolve the apparent objection that privative propositions require existence.

Core Idea
Affirmation and negation cannot both be false, even when the subject does not exist.

This passage delves into a subtle logical distinction: while most pairs of opposites can both be false if the subject doesn't exist, affirmation and negation form a special case. An objection arises when considering privative propositions, but the author clarifies two senses of the predicate to resolve it. Let's examine the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read and Understand the Core Rule
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Focus on the opening statement: affirmation and negation, when used in a proposition, always have one true and one false, regardless of subject existence.
This is the key distinction from other opposites.
2
Identify the Objection
Notice the objection: if we simply predicate opposites on a subject, then affirmation and negation can both be false when the subject is non-existent (e.g., phoenix is black / not-black).
The objection relies on privative propositions requiring existence.
3
Analyze the Author's Answer
The author distinguishes two senses: in affirmation and negation we consider the contradictories themselves (second sense), while in other opposites we consider predication on a subject (first sense).
This resolves the conflict.
4
Examine the Clarification on Privative Propositions
The author explains that a privative proposition (e.g., 'The phoenix is not-black') requires existence only if the predicate is a positive concept expressed as negation; if the predicate is the very concept of negation, it is equivalent to a simple negative and does not require existence.
Thus 'The phoenix is not black' (simple negation) is true without implying existence.
5
Apply to the Example
Apply the distinction to the example: 'The phoenix is not-black' can be interpreted in two ways; in the correct sense, it is true and does not imply existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قد يكذب لعدم الموضوع أو الخلوه. أي ومن حكم الإيجاب والسلب أنهما إذا نقلا إلى الحكم والقضية، كان أحدهما صادقاً، والأخر كاذباً البتة سواء وجد الموضوع أو لم يوجد ضرورة امتناع اجتماع النقيضين وارتفاعهما بخلاف سائر الأقسام، فأنه يجوز أن يكذب فيه المتقابلان لعدم الموضوع أو لخلوه عنهما، كما إذا حمل الأعمى والبصير، أو الأسود والأبيض، أو الأب والابن على العنقاء أو على العقل. فإن قيل : إن أريد بالنقل إلى القضية حمل المتقابلين على موضوع، فالإيجاب والسلب أيضاً قد يكذبان لعدم الموضوع . كما في قولنا : العنقاء أسود، ولا أسود لاقتضاء المعدولة وجود الموضوع ، وإن أريد اعتبار التقابل بين النقيضين فهذا لا يتصور في التضايف، ولا في ملكة والعدم . قلنا : المراد الثاني في الإيجاب والسلب، والأول في البواقي، وقد يقال القضية إنما تكون معدولة مفتقرة إلى وجود الموضوع إذا أريد بالمحمول مفهوم ثبوتي يصدق عليه النقيض، عدل في التعبير عنه إلى طريق السلب وأما إذا أريد به نفس مفهوم النقيض فهو موجبة سالبة المحمول مستغنية عن وجود الموضوع لكونها في قوة السالبة فقولنا العنقاء لا أسود إذا أريد باللا أسود نقيض الأسود، أعني رفعه فهي صادقة بمنزلة قولنا ليس العنقاء أسود.
It may be false due to non-existence of the subject or its emptiness [of both]. That is, it is a ruling of affirmation and negation that when they are transferred to judgment and proposition, one of them is true and the other false absolutely, whether the subject exists or not, necessarily [due to] impossibility of conjunction of the two contradictories and their both being raised, unlike the other divisions; for it is possible that the two opposites are false in them due to non-existence of the subject or its emptiness of them, as when one predicates 'blind' and 'seeing', or 'black' and 'white', or 'father' and 'son' of the phoenix or of the intellect. For it is said: If what is meant by 'transfer to proposition' is predication of the two opposites on a subject, then affirmation and negation may also both be false due to non-existence of the subject, as in our saying: 'The phoenix is black' and 'It is not black' [the privative form] requires existence of the subject; and if what is meant is considering opposition between the two contradictories, this is not conceivable in correlation nor in possession and privation. We say: The second is meant in affirmation and negation, and the first in the rest. And it may be said: The proposition is only privative and dependent on existence of the subject if what is intended by the predicate is a positive concept that admits of a contradictory, and the expression is turned to the way of negation; but if what is intended by it is the very concept of the contradictory, then it is an affirmative with a negative predicate, independent of existence of the subject because it is in the force of a negative. So our saying 'The phoenix is not black' if what is meant by 'not black' is the contradictory of black, i.e., its negation, then it is true, equivalent to our saying 'The phoenix is not black'.
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer sequence about a subtle logical point regarding the truth conditions of affirmation and negation when the subject does not exist. The author begins by stating that affirmation and negation, when used in a proposition, are always such that one is true and the other false, regardless of whether the subject exists—this is because contradictories cannot be both true or both false. This distinguishes them from other kinds of opposites (like contraries, correlation, etc.) which can both be false if the subject does not exist or does not possess either opposite. An objection is then raised: But if we consider predication on a subject, then affirmation and negation can also both be false when the subject is non-existent, as in 'The phoenix is black' and 'The phoenix is not-black' (the privative form), because the privative form requires existence of the subject. Alternatively, if we consider the opposition between the two contradictories themselves, this is not applicable to other types of opposition like correlation or possession-and-privation. The author answers that in the case of affirmation and negation, what is meant is the second sense (considering the contradictories themselves), while in other opposites it is the first (predication). He then clarifies that a privative proposition (like 'The phoenix is not-black') is indeed dependent on existence of the subject only when the predicate is a positive concept expressed as a negation. But if the predicate is the very concept of negation (i.e., the contradictory), then it is equivalent to a simple negative and does not require existence. Thus 'The phoenix is not black' is true and does not imply the phoenix's existence because it is in force of 'The phoenix is not black' (simple negation).

Key Terms
الإيجاب والسلب
النقيضان
المتقابلان
القضية المعدولة
السالبة المحمول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All privative propositions (e.g., 'The phoenix is not-black') imply the existence of the subject.
Correction: The passage clarifies that a privative proposition only requires existence when the predicate is a positive concept expressed as negation. If the predicate is the very concept of the contradictory (i.e., the negation itself), then the proposition is equivalent to a simple negative and does not require existence.

Checkpoint
What distinguishes affirmation and negation from other types of opposites in terms of truth values when the subject does not exist?
A) Affirmation and negation can both be false when the subject does not exist.
B) Affirmation and negation are such that one must be true and the other false regardless of subject existence.
C) Affirmation and negation can both be true when the subject does not exist.
D) Affirmation and negation follow the same rule as other opposites.

Key Takeaway
Affirmation and negation are unique among logical opposites: they cannot both be false even when the subject does not exist, and the apparent counterexample of privative propositions is resolved by distinguishing two senses of the predicate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You affirm daily that 'There is no god but Allah.' But do you also live as if other things—wealth, status, comfort—are ultimate? That is a contradiction. How will you today align your actions with your declaration so that your life is a single consistent affirmation?
?
The logic of this lesson shows that contradictories cannot both be false. Your soul knows that the Hereafter is either true or false. If you are uncertain, you are living in a false middle ground. What practical step can you take today to settle your heart on the truth of the Day of Judgment?
?
When the author says affirmation and negation cannot both be false, even if the subject doesn't exist, it reveals the absolute clarity of truth. How does this challenge you to stop rationalizing your sins as 'not that bad' or 'Allah will forgive anyway'—as if truth is relative? Will you repent today with full conviction?

Rulings of Contrariety on the Subject
Author lists rulings on subject's relation to contraries.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the different ways a subject relates to contraries and when it can be free from both.

Core Idea
A subject may be bound to one contrary or free due to a middle state.

The author begins by examining the relationship between a subject and its contraries. He enumerates scenarios where the subject must possess one of the two contraries, either specifically or non-specifically, and then notes that under certain conditions, the subject can lack both contraries entirely. This lesson unpacks these rulings from the matn.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on rulings
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the three cases mentioned: necessary possession (specific/non-specific) and possible freedom.
2
Identify the three scenarios
The author lists: (1) subject not free from a specific contrary, (2) not free from one of two but not specified, (3) may be free due to a middle state or negation.
Fire must have heat; body must have motion or rest; tepid water is neither hot nor cold.
3
Understand the middle state
The middle state can be expressed by a positive term (tepid) or by negating both contraries (not just), or without a term (transparent body).
This shows alternation is not necessary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أحكام التضاد. قال: ومن حكم التضاد أن الموضوع قد لا يخلو عن أحد الضدين بعينه كالنار عن الحرارة أو لا بعينه كالجسم عن الحركة والسكون، وقد يخلو ما لاتصافه بوسط يعبر عنه باسم محصل كالفاتر أو بسلب الطرفين كاللا عادل واللاجائز أو بدون ذلك كاشفاف يخلو عن السواد والبياض. ظاهر وفيه إشارة إلى أن تعاقب الضدين على الموضوع الواحد ليس بلازم. التضاد الحقيقي يوجد بين نوعين. قال: وإن الحقيقي منه إنما يكون بين نوعين آخرين من جنس واحد كالسواد والبياض لا بين جنسين كالفضيلة والرذيلة، وكالخير والشر، أو نوعين من جنسين كالعفة والفجور، أو أنواع من جنس كالسواد والبياض والجمرة، وعولوا في ذلك على الاستقراء وفيه نظر، وأما المشهوري فقد صرحوا بأنه قد يكون بين جنسين كالخير والشر، أو نوعين من جنس كالعفة والفجور، أو أنواع من جنس كالسواد والبياض والحمرة أو من جنسين كالشجاعة والتهور والجبن.
Rulings of contrariety. He said: Among the rulings of contrariety is that the subject may not be free from one of the two contraries specifically, like fire from heat, or not specifically, like body from motion and rest; and it may be free due to its qualification by a middle which is expressed by a positive name, like tepid, or by negation of the two extremes, like 'not just' and 'not permissible', or without that, like a transparent body free from black and white. This is apparent, and in it there is an indication that the alternation of the two contraries upon one subject is not necessary. Real contrariety exists between two species. He said: And real contrariety only exists between two ultimate species under one genus, like black and white, not between two genera like virtue and vice, and like good and evil, nor between two species from two genera like chastity and debauchery, nor between species of a genus like black, white, and red. They relied in that on induction, and there is consideration in it. As for the well-known [scholars], they have stated that it may be between two genera like good and evil, or between two species of a genus like chastity and debauchery, or between species of a genus like black, white, and red, or from two genera like courage, recklessness, and cowardice.
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage consists of two parts: first, a ruling about the subject of contraries, and second, a definition of real contrariety. The author begins by quoting the matn: 'Among the rulings of contrariety is that the subject may not be free from one of the two contraries'—this can be either specifically (like fire must have heat) or non-specifically (like a body must have either motion or rest). Then he adds a case where the subject may be free from both contraries if it has a middle state (like tepid between hot and cold) or if both are negated (like 'not just'), or if it is transparent (neither black nor white). He comments that this shows alternation of contraries on one subject is not necessary. The second part defines real contrariety (al-tadad al-haqiqi) as existing only between two ultimate species under one genus (e.g., black and white under color). The author notes that some relied on induction for this, but it is questionable. He then presents the view of the 'well-known' (al-mashhuri) scholars who allow contrariety between genera (good and evil), between species of a genus (chastity and debauchery), between multiple species of a genus (black, white, red), or even between two genera (courage, recklessness, cowardice). This shows a disagreement on the precise definition of real contrariety.

Key Terms
التضاد
الضدان
الموضوع
الوسط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that contraries must always alternate on a subject, but the author indicates this is not necessary.
Correction: The author explicitly says alternation is not necessary; a subject can lack both contraries.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is an example of a subject that is not free from one of two contraries non-specifically?
A) Fire with respect to heat
B) Body with respect to motion and rest
C) Tepid water with respect to hot and cold
D) Transparent body with respect to black and white

Key Takeaway
A subject may be bound to a specific contrary, bound to one of two non-specifically, or free from both due to a middle state.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are there areas in your life where you see yourself as 'tepid'—neither fully committed to truth nor to falsehood? What might the Lord require of you—to choose a side, or to find a different kind of freedom?
?
The author notes that a subject can be free from both contraries because of a middle state or negation. In your spiritual journey, are you avoiding extremes by staying comfortable in the middle? What one step can you take today to move from neutrality toward a positive quality that pleases Allah?

Definition of Real Contrariety and Dispute
Author defines real contrariety and contrasts two scholarly views.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of real contrariety and the disagreement among scholars regarding its scope.

Core Idea
Real contrariety is between two ultimate species under one genus, but some scholars extend it.

The second part of the passage defines real contrariety (al-tadad al-haqiqi). The author presents the standard view that it exists only between two ultimate species of the same genus, but then notes that the well-known scholars (al-mashhuri) allowed contrariety between genera and multiple species. This lesson examines both positions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on real contrariety
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Identify the definition and the examples given for what is not real contrariety.
2
Understand the standard view
Real contrariety exists only between two ultimate species under one genus, like black and white under color.
It excludes contrariety between genera (virtue and vice) or between species from different genera (chastity and debauchery).
3
Learn the well-known view
Al-mashhuri scholars hold a wider definition: contrariety can exist between genera (good and evil), between species of a genus (chastity and debauchery), among multiple species of a genus (black, white, red), or between species from two genera (courage, recklessness, cowardice).
This shows a disagreement in the definition.

Source Text

The author begins by quoting the matn: 'Among the rulings of contrariety is that the subject may not be free from one of the two contraries'—this can be either specifically (like fire must have heat) or non-specifically (like a body must have either motion or rest). The Seven Divisions of the Four Causes
Enumerates seven divisions of the four causes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the seven ways in which each of the four causes can be classified.

Core Idea
The four causes can be viewed from seven different perspectives.

This passage from Sharh al-Maqasid presents a detailed taxonomy of the four causes. The author enumerates seven pairs of divisions that apply to each cause, helping the student see causality from multiple angles. Understanding these divisions is essential for precise causal analysis in Islamic philosophy and kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the repetitive structure using 'باعتبار' (according to a consideration).
The passage lists seven pairs; count them.
2
Identify the seven pairs
Each pair is introduced by 'إلى' (into). List them: simple/compound, universal/particular, essential/accidental, proximate/remote, general/specific, shared/exclusive, potential/actual.
The last pair repeats the earlier mention of potential and actual.
3
Understand the meaning of each pair
These are dichotomous classifications applicable to any cause. For example, a cause can be simple (uncomposed) or compound (composed of parts). Reflect on how each pair gives a different perspective on causality.
4
Reflect on the importance
Ask yourself why such a taxonomy matters. These divisions help in precisely describing causes in arguments and avoiding confusion between different aspects of causality.
This taxonomical precision is common in Islamic scholarly disciplines.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وختصة بالذات وبالعرض وإلى ما بالقوة وما بالفعل . أن كلا من العلل الأربع ينقسم باعتبار إلى بسيطة ومركبة، وباعتبار يعني إلى كلية وجزئية وباعتبار إلى ذاتية وعرضية وباعتبار إلى قريبة وبعيدة، وباعتبار إلى عامة وخاصة وباعتبار إلى مشتركة ومختصة، وباعتبار إلى ما بالقوة، وإلى ما بالفعل.
And it is specific to essence and accident and to what is in potentiality and what is in actuality. That each of the four causes is divided according to a consideration into simple and compound, and according to a consideration into universal and particular, and according to a consideration into essential and accidental, and according to a consideration into proximate and remote, and according to a consideration into general and specific, and according to a consideration into shared and exclusive, and according to a consideration into what is in potentiality and into what is in actuality.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage classifies the four causes according to various aspects. It first mentions that the causes are specific (or distinguished) by essence and accident and by potentiality and actuality. Then it enumerates seven divisions: (1) simple and compound, (2) universal and particular, (3) essential and accidental, (4) proximate and remote, (5) general and specific, (6) shared and exclusive, (7) potential and actual. The word 'باعتبار' (according to a consideration) is used for each division. The passage serves as a taxonomy of the four causes (material, formal, efficient, final) from different perspectives.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: These divisions are mutually exclusive; a cause can only be classified under one pair.
Correction: The divisions are not mutually exclusive; a cause can be simple in one respect and compound in another, or universal in one context and particular in another, depending on the consideration.

Checkpoint

A) Five
B) Six
C) Seven
D) Eight

Key Takeaway
The four causes can be analyzed through seven dichotomous pairs, each offering a different perspective on causality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

What is the most immediate cause, and what deeper, more distant causes might you have overlooked?
?

Are there areas in your life where you have been fixated on a single perspective on causality—perhaps blaming only external factors or only yourself? How might adopting the other six perspectives (e.g., essential vs accidental, shared vs exclusive) give you a more accurate and humbling view of your situation?
?
Consider a good deed you performed recently. Was its cause simple or compound? Was it essential to your character or accidental? How does labeling it as 'essential' versus 'accidental' affect your spiritual accountability? Does it make you more grateful or more complacent?

The Principle of Emanation: Six Considerations from the One
Explains how the first effect's six considerations enable multiple emanations and chains.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the first effect, though one in essence, can be the source of multiplicity through its six mental considerations, and how this leads to chains of effects.

Core Idea
The one first effect is virtually many through its six aspects.

The passage begins by establishing a foundational principle of emanation: from the First Principle (God), which has no multiplicity, only one thing proceeds directly. However, this single entity is not simple in every respect; it possesses six considerations (existence, identity, contingency, etc.) that allow multiple effects to arise from it. Sanusi explains how this generates chains of secondary causes, setting the stage for later objections.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and English translation to get a sense of the argument. Notice the dense style.
The passage uses technical terms like 'المبدأ الأول' and 'اعتبارات'.
2
Identify the six considerations
Find the list of six mental aspects attributed to the first effect: existence, identity, contingency, necessity through another, intellection of itself, intellection of its principle.
3
Understand the mechanism of multiplicity
See how these considerations allow the one thing to be understood in multiple ways, thereby enabling the emanation of multiple effects.
The multiplicity is in the mind, not in the essence.
4
Trace the chain of causation
Note how a second effect proceeds from the first, a third via the first principle through the second, and so on, leading to unlimited chains.
This shows how intermediaries can generate a cascade.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذكروه : أنه إذا صدر عن المبدأ الأول الذي ليس فيه تكثر جهات واعتبارات شتى كان ذلك الشيء واحداً بالحقيقة والذات، لكن يعقل له بحسب الاعتبارات المختلفة أمور ستة هي الوجود والهوية، والإمكان، والوجوب بالغير، وتعقل ذاته، وتعقل مبدئه. فيجوز أن يصدر عنه بحسب تلك الاعتبارات أمور متكثرة. ويظهر ابتداء سلاسل متعددة. وكذا يجوز أن يصدر عن ذلك الشيء الواحد الذي هو المعلول الأول معلول ثان وعن المبدأ الأول يتوسطه معلول ثالث ويتوسط المعلول الأول والثاني والثالث معلول رابع وهكذا عن كل معلول يتوسط ما فوقه أو ما تحته. وعن الواجب يتوسط ما تحته جملة أو فرادى فيكون هناك سلاسل غير محصورة ولبعض المحققين رسالة في تفصيل ذلك، وأورد نبذاً منه في شرحه للإشارات واعتراض الإمام بأن الوجود والوجوب والإمكان اعتبارات عقلية، لا تصلح علة للأعيان الخارجية، ولما كان ظاهراً أنها ليست عللاً مستقلة بل شروطاً وحيثيات تختلف بها أحوال العلة الموجودة . اعترض : بأنه لو كفى مثل هذه الكثرة في أن يكون الواحد مصدراً للمعلولات الكثيرة. فذات الواجب تعالى تصلح أن تجعل مبدأ للممكنات باعتبار ماله من كثرة السلوب والإضافات من غير أن يجعل بعض معلولاته واسطة في ذلك . ويحكم بأن الصادر الأول عنه ليس إلا واحداً . وأجيب بأن السلوب والإضافات لا تعقل إلا بعد ثبوت الغير، فلو كان لها دخل في ثبوت الغير لكان دوراً . واعترض بأن تعقلها إنما يتوقف على تعقل الغير لا على ثبوته ، والمتوقف عليها ثبوت الغير لا تعقله فلا دور . والجواب : أن المراد أنه لا يصح الحكم بالسلوب والإضافات في نفس الأمر إلا بعد ثبوت الغير ضرورة اقتضاء السلب مسلوباً، وبالاضافة منسوباً، فلا يصح الحكم باستناد ثبوته إليها للزوم الدور.
They mentioned: If something proceeds from the first principle which has no multiplicity of aspects and various considerations, that thing is one in reality and essence, but there are understood for it according to different considerations six things: existence, identity, contingency, necessity through another, intellection of its essence, and intellection of its principle. So it is possible that from it according to those considerations multiple things proceed. And the beginnings of multiple chains appear. Likewise, it is possible that from that one thing which is the first effect a second effect proceeds, and from the first principle through its mediation a third effect proceeds, and through the mediation of the first, second, and third effects a fourth effect proceeds, and so on from every effect through what is above it or below it. And from the Necessary through what is below it, collectively or individually, so there are unlimited chains. And some investigators have a treatise detailing that, and he adduced a portion of it in his commentary on al-Isharat. And the objection of the Imam (al-Razi) that existence, necessity, and contingency are mental considerations, not suited to be causes for external entities, and since it is apparent that they are not independent causes but rather conditions and aspects by which the states of the existing cause vary. He objected: if such multiplicity suffices for the one to be a source of many effects, then the essence of the Necessary Exalted would be suitable to be made a principle for the contingents by virtue of its many negations and relations without making some of its effects intermediaries in that, and it is judged that the first emanation from it is only one. And it was answered: negations and relations are not conceived except after the existence of the other, so if they had a role in the existence of the other, there would be circularity. And he objected: their conception only depends on the conception of the other, not on its existence, and what depends on them is the existence of the other, not its conception, so no circularity. And the answer: The meaning is that judgment of negations and relations in reality is not valid except after the existence of the other, necessarily because negation requires a negated thing, and relation requires a related thing, so it is not valid to judge that its existence is dependent on them due to necessitating circularity.
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage is an argument about how multiplicity can arise from the First Principle, followed by objections and answers. It begins by stating that if something proceeds from the First Principle (which has no multiplicity of aspects), that thing is one in reality. However, with respect to different considerations, six mental aspects are understood about it: existence, identity, contingency, necessity through another, intellection of its essence, and intellection of its principle. Through these considerations, multiple things can proceed from it, and multiple chains of causation begin. Then it mentions that from the first effect a second effect can proceed, and through the first principle via that second effect a third effect, and so on, leading to unlimited chains. Some investigators have written on this. Then an objection by Imam al-Razi is presented: existence, necessity, and contingency are merely mental considerations, not real causes for external entities; they are only conditions or aspects. Then a further objection: if such mental multiplicity suffices for the one to produce many things, then God's essence, with its many negative attributes and relations, could directly produce the contingent beings without intermediaries—yet the principle holds that only one thing proceeds from Him directly. The answer: negative and relational attributes are only conceivable after the existence of an other; if they caused that other, there would be circularity. Another objection: conception does not require existence, only concept of the other, so no circularity. Final answer: judgment of negations and relations in actual reality requires the existence of the other (since negation needs a negated thing, relation a related thing), so if the other's existence depended on them, circularity would still arise.

Key Terms
المبدأ الأول
اعتبارات عقلية
الوجود
الهوية
الإمكان
الوجوب بالغير
المعلول الأول
سلاسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How many mental considerations are attributed to the first effect according to the passage?
A) Four
B) Six
C) Eight
D) Ten

Key Takeaway
The first effect, though one, is virtually multiple through its six considerations, which allow a cascade of effects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your existence is merely possible in itself; it becomes necessary only through the Necessary. Does this make you feel fragile or liberated? How can you live today remembering that you are not self-sufficient?
?
The first effect knows itself and its principle. Do you truly know your own dependence on the One? What practical habit can you adopt to turn this knowledge into a constant awareness?
?
This passage describes a vast chain of causation. Where do you see yourself in that chain? Are you content to be a passive link, or do you actively seek to connect your actions to the First Principle? What one change can you make to align your will more closely with the source of your existence?

The Refutation of Mental Considerations as Independent Causes
Presents al-Razi's objection that mental considerations cannot be real causes and the response that they are only conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's objection that existence, necessity, and contingency are mere mental considerations and the response that they are only conditions, not independent causes.

Core Idea
Mental considerations are conditions, not causes.

After explaining how multiplicity can arise, the author turns to a critical objection by Imam al-Razi: how can mental concepts like existence, necessity, and contingency be real causes of external things? The passage immediately clarifies that these are not independent causes but rather conditions and aspects that modify the existing cause. This lesson focuses on that objection and its initial response.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Razi's objection
Read the sentence in Arabic and translation. Notice the claim that existence, necessity, and contingency are mental considerations that cannot be causes of external entities.
2
Identify the three mental considerations
The objection singles out three: existence (الوجود), necessity (الوجوب), and contingency (الإمكان).
3
Understand the response
The passage states that these are not independent causes (عللاً مستقلة) but rather conditions (شروط) and aspects (حيثيات) that affect the states of the existing cause.
This response is embedded in the objection itself, showing that the objector acknowledges they are conditions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Mental considerations like contingency are real, independent causes in the external world.
Correction: They are only conditions and aspects that qualify the real cause, not independent causes themselves.

Checkpoint
According to al-Razi's objection, what are existence, necessity, and contingency?
A) Real entities
B) Mental considerations
C) Independent causes
D) Divine attributes

Key Takeaway
Mental considerations like existence, necessity, and contingency are not independent causes but mere conditions of the actual cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on the causes of your successes or failures, do you often attribute them to abstract concepts like 'luck' or 'fate' as if those were independent causes? How does this lesson's distinction between conditions and causes challenge you to recognize that true causality belongs only to Allah, and that your mental constructs are merely conditions? What would change in your daily gratitude or repentance if you lived by this understanding?
?
Think of a habitual sin you justify with phrases like 'I had no choice' or 'it was necessary.' Are you mistaking a mental consideration (like perceived necessity) for a real cause? How can you, right now, identify that necessity as merely a condition of your own reasoning, and instead hold yourself accountable for the choice you actually made? What step will you take today to break that pattern?
?
Consider your deepest fear—perhaps of failure or rejection. Is it not often rooted in treating a mental consideration (like 'contingency') as a cause that determines your worth? How does knowing that these are only conditions—not independent causes—free you to trust in Allah's decree and act with courage? What specific action will you take this week that you have been avoiding because of such a mental consideration?

The Objection of Negations and Relations and the Circularity Argument
Debates whether negations and relations can be efficient causes without circularity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that God's multiple negative attributes could directly produce multiple effects, and the subsequent debate about whether that leads to circularity.

Core Idea
Negations and relations depend on an other, so they cannot be the source of that other.

A further objection is raised: if mental considerations can produce multiplicity, then why can't God's many negations and relations directly cause the contingent beings without intermediaries? The response invokes the principle that negations and relations are only conceivable after the existence of an 'other', which would lead to circularity if they were the cause. A counter-objection and final answer refine this point.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection about negations and relations
Read the objection: if mental multiplicity suffices, then God's essence with its many negations and relations could directly produce many effects without intermediaries.
This objection challenges the need for an intermediary.
2
Read the first answer about circularity
The answer: negations and relations are not conceived except after the existence of the other, so if they caused the other, there would be circularity.
3
Read the counter-objection
The objector retorts: their conception depends only on the conception of the other, not its existence, so no circularity.
This focuses on epistemic vs. ontic order.
4
Read the final answer
The final answer: judgment of negations and relations in reality (not just in mind) requires the actual existence of the other, so circularity still holds.
This distinguishes between mental conception and real-world judgment.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The conception of an attribute is enough to ground real causation.
Correction: Real-world causation requires actual existence of the relata, not just mental conception.

Checkpoint
What is the final reason given for why negations and relations cannot cause the existence of the other?
A) They are mental
B) It would lead to circularity
C) The other must exist before they can be judged in reality
D) They are too many

Key Takeaway
Negations and relations cannot be the cause of the other because their real-world application requires that other's prior existence, creating circularity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have learned that negations and relations require the existence of an other to be real. Look at your own life: what defines you? Is it what you are not (negations) or your relationships (relations)? Are you grounding your identity in something that depends on an other, rather than in the Necessary Being who exists by Himself? Write down one false identity you have relied on, and commit to replacing it with a truth about Allah that is not dependent.
?
The final answer in this lesson insists on real judgment (fi nafs al-amr) over mere conception. Is there a belief you hold that is comfortable in your mind but not truly lived? What is one area where you have only a conceptual understanding of tawhid but have not let it transform your actions? This week, take one concrete step to make that belief real in your life.
?
The objector tried to make negations and relations independent causes, but they were shown to depend on the other. Are you treating any secondary causes in your life as if they were ultimate? Consider a habit, a person, or a possession that you rely on as if it were self-sufficient. How can you realign your heart to see it as dependent on Allah, and then act on that realization today?

The Fifth Argument Against Infinite Regress: Even and Odd
Al-Taftazani presents the fifth argument against infinite regress using parity and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fifth kalam argument against infinite regress based on parity (even/odd) of the number of terms, the objection that evenness/oddness only apply to finite numbers, and the author's response.

Core Idea
An infinite series must be either even or odd—therefore it is finite.

This lesson presents the fifth argument against tasalsul (infinite regress) drawn from the properties of numbers. Al-Taftazani first states the argument, then raises and responds to a key objection. Understanding this argument deepens your grasp of how Muslim theologians used logical and mathematical reasoning to defend the finitude of causal chains.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its literal translation. Let the argument and objection unfold in the author's own words.
The passage is dense; read carefully.
2
Understand the parity argument
The argument states that any supposed infinite series of causes and effects would have a number of terms that is either even or odd. In either case, that number is less than the next number, so it is finite because it is bounded between its beginning and that next number.
Focus on the logical move from parity to finitude.
3
Examine the objection
The objection denies that something not divisible into two equal parts is necessarily odd unless it is finite. Evenness and oddness are properties of finite numbers only, so applying them to an infinite series is circular.
Consider why the objector thinks the argument assumes what it tries to prove.
4
See the author's response
The author dismisses the objection as 'apparent' (zahir) and notes that even if we bypass parity, saying every number is less than another, the denial is also apparent. The argument stands because an actual infinite series would have a determinate cardinality.
The author seems to accept the parity argument as valid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الخامس : أن السلسلة المفروضة من العلل والمعلولات الغير المتناهية إما أن تكون منقسمة بمتساويين فيكون زوجاً أو لا فيكون فرداً، وكل زوج فهو أقل بواحد من فرد بعده كالأربعة من الخمسة وكل فرد فهو أقل بواحد من زوج بعده كالخمسة من الستة، وكل عدد يكون أقل من عدد آخر يكون متناهيا بالضرورة. كيف لا ؟ وهو محصور بين حاصرين هما ابتداؤه وذلك الواحد الذي بعده. ورد بأنا لا نسلم أن كل ما لا ينقسم بمتساويين فهو فرد، وإنما يلزم لو كان متناهياً، فإن الزوجية والفردية من خواص العدد المتناهي، وقد يطوي حديث الزوجية والفردية، فيقال : كل عدد فهو قابل للزيادة فيكون أقل من عدد فيكون متناهياً والمنع ظاهر.
The fifth argument: That the supposed series of causes and effects without end is either divisible into two equal parts, thus being even, or not, thus being odd. And every even number is one less than the odd number that follows it, like four from five, and every odd number is one less than the even number that follows it, like five from six. And every number that is less than another number is necessarily finite. How could it not be? For it is bounded between two boundaries, which are its beginning and that one which is after it. It was objected: We do not concede that everything that is not divisible into two equal parts is odd; this would only follow if it were finite. For evenness and oddness are properties of finite numbers. And the discussion of evenness and oddness may be abbreviated, so it is said: Every number is susceptible to increase, thus it is less than a number, thus it is finite. And the denial is apparent.
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage presents a proof and an objection. The proof is the fifth argument (al-wajh al-khamis) against infinite regress. It argues that an infinite series of causes and effects, if it existed, would be a sequence of terms that can be counted. Any such count is either even or odd. In both cases, the number is less than another number (e.g., an even number is one less than the next odd, and an odd is one less than the next even). A number that is less than another number is necessarily finite, because it has a definite beginning and end. Therefore the series must be finite. The objection ('warda') points out that evenness and oddness are properties only of finite numbers. To apply them to an infinite series is to assume what we want to prove. The objection then suggests bypassing parity and simply stating that every number is less than another (i.e., any finite number can be increased), so if the series were a number, it would be less than a larger number and thus finite. The objector says this is also not conceded. The author responds with 'wal-mani'u zahir' — the denial is apparent, meaning the objection is weak and the argument stands. The author seems to accept the parity argument as valid against the assumption of an actually infinite series, because the series, if actual, would have a determinate cardinality which must be either even or odd.

Key Terms
سلسلة
علل
معلولات
متناهٍ
زوج
فرد
عدد
حاصرين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that evenness and oddness apply to any series regardless of finitude, so the argument is sound without qualification.
Correction: Evenness and oddness are properties of finite numbers. The objection correctly notes that applying them to an infinite series assumes the series is finite, which is what the argument aims to prove.

Checkpoint
According to the fifth argument, why must an infinite series be finite?
A) Because it is composed of causes and effects.
B) Because it would be either even or odd, and both imply finitude.
C) Because it is impossible for a series to be infinite.
D) Because every number is less than another number.

Key Takeaway
The parity argument shows that an actual infinite series cannot exist because it would have a determinate number that must be either even or odd, implying finitude; the objection that parity only applies to finite series is considered weak.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The argument shows that an infinite regress is logically impossible—there must be a first cause. If you were to trace the chain of events that led to you being here today, where do you honestly think it ends? Are you living as if that first cause is real and personal, or as if you are just an accident in an endless series? What would change if you truly believed that your existence is grounded in a First Cause who knows you?
?
The objector argued that evenness and oddness only apply to finite numbers, limiting the argument. Are there truths about God or the unseen that you are tempted to limit or reject because they seem 'too abstract' or 'too philosophical' for your life? How might this lesson challenge you to embrace rational arguments that ground your faith, rather than dismiss them as impractical?
?
The argument uses a simple mathematical property (parity) to demonstrate a profound metaphysical reality. Is there an area of your faith where you struggle with certainty because you think it cannot be rationally proven? Will you commit to studying the rational proofs of kalam to strengthen your conviction, starting from the impossibility of infinite regress?

Theologians' Classification of Existence
Taftazani presents the theologians' classification of existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the theologians classify existence into pre-eternal versus originated, and then into substance (spatially extended) and accident (inhering).

Core Idea
For theologians, existence is either pre-eternal (Allah) or originated; originated things are either spatial substances or inhering accidents.

The theologians begin with a fundamental distinction: whatever exists is either preceded by non-existence or not. This leads to a simple but powerful classification of all created things into two categories: substances (jawhar) which occupy space, and accidents ('arad) which inhere in them. This passage lays out that foundational scheme.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the first part of the passage carefully. Notice the definitions of pre-eternal (qadim) and originated (hadith).
The passage uses technical terms like 'mutahayyiz bi al-dhat' for spatially extended.
2
Identify the two main divisions
Trace how the theologians divide existence first into pre-eternal/originated, then the originated into substance and accident.
Substance is defined by spatial extension, not by independence from a subject.
3
Note the subtypes of accidents
Accidents are subdivided into those specific to living beings (life and perceptions) and non-specific (akwan and sensible qualities).
This shows the theologians' detailed taxonomy of the accidental.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الأول في الموجود الموجود عند مشايخنا إن لم يكن مسبوقاً بالعدم فقديم، وهو الواجب تعالي وصفاته، وإلا فحادث، وهو إما متحيز بالذات وهو الجوهر، أو حال فيه وهو العرض، إذ لم يثبت وجود الجواهر المجردة، وإن لم يتم دليل امتناعها، والعرض إما مختص بالحي وهي الحياة، وما يتبعها من الإدراكات وغيرها، أو غير مختص وهي الأكوان والمحسوسات . وعند الفلاسفة الموجود في الخارج إن كان وجوده لذاته فهو الواجب تعالي وصفاته وإلا فالممكن ، وهو إن استغنى عن الموضوع أي محل يقومه فجوهر وإلا فعرض والصورة الجوهري إنما تفتقر الى المحل دون الموضوع ، ومعنى وجود العرض في المحل أن وجوده في نفسه، هو وجوده في محله، لا كالجسم في المكان .
The first chapter: On Existence. Existence according to our scholars: if it is not preceded by non-existence, it is pre-eternal (qadim), and that is the Necessary Being (al-wajib) Most High and His attributes; otherwise it is originated (hadith). And it is either spatially extended in essence (mutahayyiz bi al-dhat) and that is substance (jawhar), or inherent in it, and that is accident ('arad), since the existence of abstract substances has not been established, even if the proof of their impossibility is not complete. Accident is either specific to the living—which is life and what follows it of perceptions and others—or not specific, which are the states of being (akwan) and perceptible things. According to the philosophers: existence in the external world, if its existence is for itself, it is the Necessary Being Most High and His attributes; otherwise it is contingent (mumkin). And if it is independent of the subject (mawdu')—that is, a substrate that sustains it—then it is substance; otherwise it is accident. And the substantial form (al-surah al-jawhariyya) indeed depends on a substrate (mahall) but not on a subject (mawdu'). And the meaning of the existence of accident in a substrate is that its existence in itself is its existence in its substrate, not like a body in a place.
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage is a classification of existing things according to both the theologians (ashabuna) and the philosophers. It begins by defining existence according to the theologians: if a thing is not preceded by non-existence, it is pre-eternal (qadim), and this applies only to Allah Most High and His attributes. If it is preceded by non-existence, it is originated (hadith). Originated things are then divided into two categories: (1) that which is extended in space (mutahayyiz bi al-dhat) — this is called substance (jawhar); and (2) that which inheres in substance — this is called accident ('arad). The author notes that the existence of abstract substances (al-jawahir al-mujarrada) has not been established in theology, though the proof of their impossibility is not conclusive. Accidents are further subdivided into those specific to living beings — i.e., life and its concomitants such as perceptions — and those not specific, such as states of being (akwan: motion, rest, etc.) and sensible qualities. Then the passage turns to the philosophers' classification. For them, existence in the external world is either necessary in itself — that is the Necessary Being and His attributes — or contingent (mumkin). The contingent is either independent of a subject (mawdu') — i.e., a substrate that sustains it — in which case it is substance, or it depends on a subject, in which case it is accident. A key nuance: the substantial form (al-surah al-jawhariyya) depends on a 'substrate' (mahall) but not on a 'subject' (mawdu'). The passage concludes by clarifying the meaning of an accident's existence in a substrate: its existence in itself is identical to its existence in its substrate, unlike a body's existence in a place (where the body and place are distinct). This contrast highlights the different metaphysical commitments of the two schools regarding the nature of substance and accident.

Key Terms
موجود
قديم
حادث
جوهر
عرض
متحيز
أكوان
مشايخنا

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Students sometimes think that for theologians, 'substance' is defined by independence from a subject (as in philosophy).
Correction: The theologians define substance as 'spatially extended in essence' (mutahayyiz bi al-dhat), not by independence from a subject.

Checkpoint
According to the theologians (ashabuna), what is the defining characteristic of a substance (jawhar)?
A) It is independent of a subject (mawdu')
B) It is spatially extended in essence (mutahayyiz bi al-dhat)
C) It is not preceded by non-existence
D) It is a substantial form in a substrate

Key Takeaway
The theologians classify originated existence into spatially extended substances and inhering accidents, with accidents further divided into life-related and non-life-related categories.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at your own existence, do you truly recognize that everything about you—your body, your actions, your thoughts—is originated, brought into being after non-existence? Or do you live as if you are self-sufficient and eternal? How should this understanding change your humility before Allah?
?
Taftazani classifies accidents into those specific to life (perceptions) and others. Your perceptions—your sight, hearing, knowledge—are accidents that inhere in you. They are fleeting and not self-subsisting. In what ways have you been attaching your identity to these temporary perceptions, as if they were permanent? How can you shift your focus to the pre-eternal Creator?
?
The passage states that abstract substances have not been established, but their impossibility is not proven. Are there aspects of your belief that you hold based on lack of proof rather than clear evidence? Are you content with a faith built on a 'not proven' stance, or do you seek certainty?

Philosophers' Classification of Existence
Taftazani presents the philosophers' classification of existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the philosophers classify existence into necessary/contingent and then into substance/accident based on independence from a subject, including the special case of substantial form.

Core Idea
For philosophers, existence is either necessary in itself (Allah) or contingent; contingent beings are substances if independent of a subject, accidents otherwise.

The philosophers approach existence from a different angle: they ask whether a thing's existence is self-sufficient or dependent. This leads to a classification that distinguishes substances by their independence from a subject ('mawdu''), and introduces the subtle concept of substantial form which depends on a substrate ('mahall') but not a subject.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophers' section
Read the second part of the passage carefully. Note the definitions of necessary (wajib) and contingent (mumkin).
The philosophers start with the essence-existence distinction.
2
Compare definitions of substance
Notice that philosophers define substance as independent of a subject (mawdu'), unlike the theologians' spatial extension.
This difference reflects different metaphysical frameworks.
3
Understand the mahall vs. mawdu' distinction
Substantial form depends on a substrate (mahall) but not on a subject (mawdu'). Grasp the difference: a subject sustains the accident's existence, while a substrate is where the form inheres.
The passage ends by clarifying that an accident's existence in a substrate is identical to its own existence, unlike a body in a place.

The First Division: Eternal and Originated
The author defines the criterion for a being's eternalness or origination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the criterion for dividing existence into eternal (qadim) and originated (hadith), and that the eternal is Allah and His attributes.

Core Idea
The existent is either eternal or originated based on precedence by non-existence.

The mutakallimun begin their analysis of existence by drawing a fundamental line: everything that exists is either eternal or originated. This lesson unpacks that primary division and identifies the eternal as Allah and His real attributes, setting the stage for discussions about the world's temporality.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage once, focusing on the first sentence. Notice the conditional structure: 'if not preceded by non-existence, then eternal; if preceded, then originated.'
The criterion is purely temporal, not causal.
2
Identify the criterion for qadim and hadith
Observe that the defining characteristic is being preceded by non-existence. If something never had a beginning, it is qadim; if it began, it is hadith.
Qadim here means 'without beginning', not just old.
3
See what is identified as qadim
The passage states that the qadim is the Necessary Most High (Allah) and His real attributes. There is a reference to a later proof that the world is originated.
This implies divine attributes share in eternity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما عند المتكلمين فالموجود إن لم يكن مسبوقاً بالعدم فقديم، وإن كان مسبوقاً به فحادث. والقديم هو الواجب تعالى، وصفاته الحقيقية لما سيجيء من حدوث العالم، والحادث إما متحيز بالذات وهو الجوهر بأقسامه التي ستأتي، وإما حال في التحيز بالذات وهو العرض، وإما ما لا يكون متحيزاً ولا حالاً في المتحيز فلم يعدوه من أقسام الموجود لأنه لم يثبت وجوده لما سيأتي من ضعف أدلته، وربما يستدل على امتناعه بأنه لو وجد لشاركه الباري تعالى في التجرد، ويحتاج في الامتياز إلى فصل فيتركب، وضعفه ظاهر، لأن الاشتراك في العوارض سيما السلبية لا توجب التركب.
As for with the theologians, the existent: if it is not preceded by non-existence, it is eternal, and if it is preceded by it, then it is originated. The eternal is the Necessary Most High and His real attributes, as will come from the temporality of the world. The originated is either essentially occupying space – and that is the substance with its divisions that will come – or inhering in an occupant of space – and that is the accident – or that which is neither occupying space nor inhering in an occupant of space; they do not count it among the divisions of the existent because its existence has not been established due to the weakness of its proofs that will come. And perhaps one may argue for its impossibility by saying that if it existed it would share with the Creator Most High in being non-corporeal and would need a differentia for distinction, thus becoming composite. But its weakness is apparent because sharing in accidental attributes, especially negative ones, does not entail composition.
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage defines the division of the existent according to the mutakallimun (theologians). It begins by stating the primary division: the existent is either qadim (eternal) if not preceded by non-existence, or hadith (originated) if preceded by it. The eternal is identified as Allah and His attributes. The hadith is then subdivided into three categories: (1) that which is essentially occupying space (mutaḥayyiz bi al-dhāt) – a substance (jawhar); (2) that which inheres in an occupant of space – an accident (ʿaraḍ); (3) that which is neither. The theologians reject the third category because its existence is not proven, as will be explained later. The author then presents an argument for the impossibility of such non-corporeal entities: if they existed, they would share with God in being non-corporeal (tajarrūd), requiring a differentia (faṣl) to distinguish them, leading to composition (tarkīb). The refutation is that sharing in negative attributes (like non-corporeality) does not necessitate composition.

Checkpoint

A) It has always existed.
B) It is not preceded by non-existence.
C) It is a necessary being.
D) It is not a substance.

Key Takeaway
The fundamental division of existence is between the eternal (not preceded by non-existence) and the originated (preceded by non-existence).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You are preceded by non-existence. Does that reality press you to seek what is truly eternal, or do you live as if this world will never end?
?
The Eternal One is Allah and His attributes. How much of your life is spent pursuing what is eternal versus what will vanish?
?
If the criterion for being eternal is not being preceded by non-existence, and you know you are originated, what does that demand of you today?

Subdivisions of the Originated and Rejection of Non-Corporeal Entities
The author classifies originated entities into substance, accident, and a rejected third category.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three categories of originated entities according to the mutakallimun and why they reject the third category of non-corporeal substances.

Core Idea
The theologians classify all originated things as either substances or accidents, rejecting non-corporeal entities.

Having established the primary division, the author now details the types of originated beings. The theologians recognize only two actual categories: substances (occupying space) and accidents (inhering in substances). A third hypothetical category—neither spatial nor inhering—is dismissed due to lack of proof, and a challenge to its possibility is rebutted.

Guided Path
1
Read the three categories
Read the passage carefully and identify the three mentioned categories: substance, accident, and the unnamed third. Note what defines each.
The third is defined negatively: not occupying space and not inhering.
2
Notice why the third is rejected
The passage says it is 'not counted among the divisions of the existent because its existence has not been established.' This is a pragmatic rejection based on insufficient proof.
Later arguments will show its proofs are weak.
3
Examine the argument and its refutation
The author presents an argument for its impossibility (sharing non-corporeality with God leads to composition) and then refutes it (sharing negative attributes does not entail composition).
This shows the theologians engage in rational argumentation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما عند المتكلمين فالموجود إن لم يكن مسبوقاً بالعدم فقديم، وإن كان مسبوقاً به فحادث. والقديم هو الواجب تعالى، وصفاته الحقيقية لما سيجيء من حدوث العالم، والحادث إما متحيز بالذات وهو الجوهر بأقسامه التي ستأتي، وإما حال في التحيز بالذات وهو العرض، وإما ما لا يكون متحيزاً ولا حالاً في المتحيز فلم يعدوه من أقسام الموجود لأنه لم يثبت وجوده لما سيأتي من ضعف أدلته، وربما يستدل على امتناعه بأنه لو وجد لشاركه الباري تعالى في التجرد، ويحتاج في الامتياز إلى فصل فيتركب، وضعفه ظاهر، لأن الاشتراك في العوارض سيما السلبية لا توجب التركب.
As for with the theologians, the existent: if it is not preceded by non-existence, it is eternal, and if it is preceded by it, then it is originated. The eternal is the Necessary Most High and His real attributes, as will come from the temporality of the world. The originated is either essentially occupying space – and that is the substance with its divisions that will come – or inhering in an occupant of space – and that is the accident – or that which is neither occupying space nor inhering in an occupant of space; they do not count it among the divisions of the existent because its existence has not been established due to the weakness of its proofs that will come. And perhaps one may argue for its impossibility by saying that if it existed it would share with the Creator Most High in being non-corporeal and would need a differentia for distinction, thus becoming composite. But its weakness is apparent because sharing in accidental attributes, especially negative ones, does not entail composition.
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage defines the division of the existent according to the mutakallimun (theologians). It begins by stating the primary division: the existent is either qadim (eternal) if not preceded by non-existence, or hadith (originated) if preceded by it. The eternal is identified as Allah and His attributes. The hadith is then subdivided into three categories: (1) that which is essentially occupying space (mutaḥayyiz bi al-dhāt) – a substance (jawhar); (2) that which inheres in an occupant of space – an accident (ʿaraḍ); (3) that which is neither. The theologians reject the third category because its existence is not proven, as will be explained later. The author then presents an argument for the impossibility of such non-corporeal entities: if they existed, they would share with God in being non-corporeal (tajarrūd), requiring a differentia (faṣl) to distinguish them, leading to composition (tarkīb). The refutation is that sharing in negative attributes (like non-corporeality) does not necessitate composition.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the theologians accept the existence of angels as non-corporeal substances.

Checkpoint
Why do the mutakallimun not count the third category (neither spatial nor inhering) among the divisions of the existent?
A) Because it is impossible by rational proof.
B) Because its existence has not been established.
C) Because it would be composite.
D) Because it shares non-corporeality with God.

Key Takeaway
The theologians hold that all originated beings are either substances (occupying space) or accidents (inhering in substances); a third category is not accepted due to insufficient proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often categorize things without thinking—like judging people or ideas. This lesson shows the precision needed to classify existence itself. When you dismiss a possibility because it doesn't fit your mental categories, are you being as careful as these theologians? Will you examine your own assumptions today?
?
The argument about non-corporeal entities seemed logical but was actually weak. Have you ever defended a belief using arguments that sound good but crumble under scrutiny? What belief are you holding onto with weak reasoning? Commit to finding stronger proofs.
?
The theologians rejected a category because of lack of evidence, not because they could disprove it. Are there areas in your life where you reject something simply because you haven't seen proof—even though it might be true? What would it mean for you to humbly say 'I don't know' and remain open?

Primary Division: Eternal and Originated, Substance and Accident
Sanusi defines the primary division of existents into eternal and originated, and subdivides originated into substance and accident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the primary classification of existents according to the mutakallimun into eternal (qadim) and originated (hadith), and the subdivision of originated into substance (jawhar) and accident (arad).

Core Idea
Every existent is either eternal or originated; every originated existent is either a substance or an accident.

Before we can discuss theological arguments, the mutakallimun needed a precise classification of everything that exists. Sanusi begins with the most basic division: that which is not preceded by non-existence is eternal (qadim), and that which is preceded by non-existence is originated (hadith). The originated is then divided into substance (jawhar) and accident (arad). This lesson establishes the foundational categories used throughout kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Sanusi uses a very compressed style here.
2
Identify the primary division
Find the sentence that divides existents into two categories based on being preceded by non-existence. Note that qadim includes the Necessary and His attributes.
The word 'qadim' is used for both Allah and His attributes.
3
See how originated is further divided
Note that the originated (hadith) is either inherently occupying space (jawhar) or inherent in what occupies space (arad). These are the only two categories of originated existents accepted by the mutakallimun.
The third category is discussed in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما عند المتكلمين فالموجود إن لم يكن مسبوقاً بالعدم فقديم، وإن كان مسبوقاً به فحادث . والقديم هو الواجب تعالي ، وصفاته الحقيقية لما سيجيء من حدوث العالم، والحادث إما متحيز بالذات وهو الجوهر بأقسامه التي ستأتي . وإما حال في التحيز بالذات وهو العرض، وإما ما لا يكون متحيزاً ولا حالا في المتحيز فلم يعدوه من أقسام الموجود لأنه لم يثبت وجوده لما سيأتي من ضعف أدلته ، وربما يستدل على امتناعه بأنه لو وجد لشارك الباري تعالى في التجرد، ويحتاج في الامتياز إلى فصل فيتركب ، وضعفه ظاهر، لأن الاشتراك في العوارض سيما السلبية لا توجب التركب .
As for the mutakallimun: The existent, if it is not preceded by non-existence, is eternal (qadīm), and if it is preceded by it, then it is originated (ḥādith). The eternal is the Necessary (al-wājib), exalted, and His real attributes, due to what will come of the origination of the world. The originated is either inherently occupying space (mutaḥayyiz bi-al-dhāt) – which is the substance (jawhar) with its divisions that will come – or inherent in what occupies space (ḥāl fī al-taḥayyuz bi-al-dhāt) – which is the accident (ʿaraḍ) – or that which is neither occupying space nor inherent in the occupant of space, and they did not count it among the divisions of the existent because its existence has not been established due to the weakness of its proofs that will come. Possibly it is argued for its impossibility by saying that if it existed, it would share with the Creator, exalted, in being abstract (tajarrud), and it would need a differentiator (faṣl) and thus become composite (murakkab). But its weakness is apparent, because sharing in accidents, especially negative ones, does not necessitate composition.

Commentary
This passage presents the classification of existents according to the mutakallimun. The author begins by stating the primary division: an existent is either not preceded by non-existence – which is qadīm – or is preceded by non-existence – which is ḥādith. The qadīm is identified with the Necessary (wājib) and His attributes. The ḥādith is then subdivided into three possibilities: (1) that which is inherently occupying space (mutaḥayyiz bi-al-dhāt), which is the jawhar (substance); (2) that which is inherent in what occupies space (ḥāl fī al-taḥayyuz), which is the ʿaraḍ (accident); and (3) that which is neither occupying space nor inherent in an occupant of space. The author explains that the mutakallimun reject this third category because its existence is not established, and its proofs are weak. He then discusses a possible argument for its impossibility: if such an entity existed, it would share with Allah in being abstract (tajarrud), and to be distinct it would need a differentiator (faṣl), leading to composition (tarkīb). The author notes the weakness of this argument, since sharing in accidental attributes, especially negative ones, does not necessitate composition.

The Rejected Third Category and the Argument from Abstractness
Sanusi rejects a third category of existent and discusses the argument from abstractness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the mutakallimun reject a third type of existent that is neither substance nor accident, and the argument used against it.

Core Idea
The mutakallimun accept only two categories of originated existents; a third is rejected due to weak proofs.

After establishing the two main categories of originated existents, Sanusi addresses a potential third category: that which is neither occupying space nor inherent in an occupant of space. The mutakallimun refuse to count this as a division of existent because its existence is not proven. He then presents and critiques an argument that such an entity would require composition, showing that the argument is weak.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the logical flow: statement of third category, rejection, argument for impossibility, rebuttal.
Pay attention to the phrase 'لم يعدوه من أقسام الموجود'.
2
Understand the third category and its rejection
Identify the third possibility: an existent that is neither occupying space nor inherent in an occupant of space. Note the reason for rejection: weak proofs.
The mutakallimun do not count it as a division of existent.
3
Analyze the argument and its rebuttal
Follow the argument: if such an entity existed, it would share with Allah in abstractness (tajarrud) and would need a differentiator (fasl) leading to composition (tarkib). Then see Sanusi's rebuttal: sharing in accidents, especially negative ones, does not necessitate composition.
The argument's weakness is shown by the fact that negative attributes do not cause composition.
