Philosophers' Classification of Existence
Sanusi enumerates the philosophers' classification of existents with definitions and clarifications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' classification of existents into Necessary, possible, substance, and accident, and the distinction between subject and locus.

Core Idea
Existence is either self-sufficient or dependent; dependent existence is either independent of a subject or dependent on it.

The philosophers, like the theologians, classify existents, but they use different criteria. Sanusi presents their classification starting from the necessary existence of God down to accidents. This passage establishes the key terms and their definitions, which are essential for understanding metaphysical debates.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the conditional structure: 'if...then...'.
2
Identify the two main divisions
Find the primary division: Necessary (wajib) vs. Possible (mumkin). Then find the subdivision of possible into substance (jawhar) and accident (arad).
The text uses 'illa' for the negative condition.
3
Understand the definitions of substance and accident
Substance is independent of a subject; accident depends on a subject. Note the terms 'subject' (mawdu) and 'locus' (mahall).
Substantial form (sura jawhariyya) is included in substance despite needing a locus.
4
Grasp the distinction between subject and locus
Locus is more general than subject; subject is a specific kind of locus. This distinction helps include substantial form in substance.
The text uses 'am' to indicate generality.
5
See how the Necessary is excluded
The definition of substance restricts existence to the possible (imkan), so the Necessary (wajib) is naturally excluded.
This shows consistency in the system.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما عند الفلاسفة فالموجود في الخارج إن كان وجوده يفتقر في وجوده إلى شيء أصلا، فهو الواجب، وإلا فالممكن والممكن إذا استغنى في الوجود عن الموضوع ، فجوهر، وإلا فعرض والمراد بالموضوع محل يقوم الحال فالصورة الجوهرية إنما تدخل في تعريف الجوهر دون العرض، لأنها وإن افتقرت إلى المحل لكنها مستغنية عن الموضوع، فإن المحل أعم من الموضوع ، كما أن الحال أعم من العرض ثم خروج الواجب عن تعريف الجوهر حيث قيد الوجود بالإمكان ظاهر .
As for the philosophers: The existent in the external world, if its existence does not need anything at all for its existence, then it is the Necessary (al-wājib). Otherwise, it is the possible (al-mumkin). And the possible, if it is independent in existence of a subject (mawḍūʿ), then it is a substance (jawhar); otherwise, it is an accident (ʿaraḍ). And the meaning of subject (mawḍūʿ) is a locus (maḥall) in which the state (ḥāl) subsists. Thus the substantial form (al-ṣūra al-jawhariyya) is included in the definition of substance (jawhar) but not of accident, because even though it needs the locus, it is independent of the subject. Indeed, the locus is more general than the subject, just as the state is more general than the accident. Then the exclusion of the Necessary from the definition of substance, since existence was restricted to possibility, is apparent.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage presents the classification of existents according to the philosophers. The author begins with a primary division: an existent either depends on nothing for its existence – this is the wājib (Necessary) – or it depends on something – this is the mumkin (possible). The mumkin is further divided: if it is independent of a subject (mawḍūʿ) in its existence, it is a jawhar (substance); if it depends on a subject, it is a ʿaraḍ (accident). The author then clarifies the term 'subject' (mawḍūʿ) by defining it as a locus (maḥall) in which a state (ḥāl) subsists. This leads to a discussion of substantial form (ṣūra jawhariyya): it is included in the definition of substance because it is independent of a subject, though it needs a locus. The locus is more general than the subject, just as the state (ḥāl) is more general than the accident (ʿaraḍ). Finally, the author notes that the wājib is excluded from the definition of substance because that definition restricts existence to the possible (imkān), which is not applicable to the wājib.

Key Terms
واجب
ممكن
جوهر
عرض
موضوع
محل
حال
صورة جوهرية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Substantial form is an accident because it exists in a locus.
Correction: Substantial form is a substance because it is independent of a subject (mawḍūʿ), even though it needs a locus (maḥall). The locus is more general than the subject.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers, what is the condition for something to be classified as a jawhar?
A) It exists in a subject
B) It is independent of a subject
C) It is necessary
D) It is possible

Key Takeaway
The philosophers classify existents into the Necessary (independent of any cause) and the possible; possible existents are further classified into substances (independent of a subject) and accidents (dependent on a subject).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just learned that only the Necessary Existent (al-wājib) depends on nothing for its existence. Everything else—including you—is possible, dependent, and contingent. Yet do you live as if your existence, your worth, and your security depend solely on the One who is Necessary? Or do you act as if the substances and accidents around you—your health, wealth, relationships—are self-sufficient? Where is your heart truly anchored: on the Necessary or on the possible? What would it take for you to shift that anchor today?
?
The philosophers define a substance (jawhar) as something that does not need a subject to exist. But you are not a substance; you are a human being whose existence depends on Allah every moment. How often do you feel the weight of that dependency? When you wake up, when you breathe, when you plan—do you acknowledge that your very being is sustained by the One who alone is independent? What would change if you lived each hour with the conscious awareness that you are always in need? Name one tangible action you can take today to remind yourself of your contingency before the Necessary.

Alternative Views on Accident Genera: Three, Four, and Motion
Taftazani presents opinions that accident genera are three or four, and discusses the category of motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the different opinions on the number of accident genera (three or four) and the debate over the categorization of motion.

Core Idea
Accident genera are either three (quantity, quality, relation) or four (adding motion), with motion's category disputed.

Scholars of kalam debated how many fundamental kinds (genera) of accidents exist. Some held that there are only three: quantity, quality, and relation. Others added a fourth: motion. This passage from Taftazani lays out these views and also discusses where motion fits among the Aristotelian categories, especially the dispute over whether motion in 'where' belongs to the category of 'where' or 'being acted upon.'

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the text ends abruptly; the rest is missing.
2
Identify the three-genera view and its criterion
Look for the reasoning: if an accident admits division by itself, it is quantity; if it necessitates relation, it is relation; otherwise, quality.
This is a classification based on inherent properties.
3
Understand the addition of motion and the exclusion of time
Observe that motion is defined as an accident whose permanence is not conceivable by itself. Time is excluded because its impermanence is due to being the measure of motion, not intrinsic.
This clarifies the scope of motion as a genus.
4
Consider the debate on motion's category
Note that the majority assign motion in 'where' to the category of 'where', but some (like al-Razi) assign it to 'being acted upon' because motion is gradual change. The text cuts off on motion in quantity, quality, and position.
This shows the fluidity of categorical boundaries.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذهب بعضهم إلى أن أجناس الأعراض ثلاثة، الكم والكيف والنسبة لأنه إن قبل القسمة لذاته فكم، وإلا فإن اقتضى النسبة لذاته فنسبة، وإلا فكيف. وزاد بعضهم قسما رابعاً هو الحركة وقال: العرض إن لم يتصور ثباته لذاته فحركة واحترز بقيد لذاته عن الزمان فإنه لا يتصور ثباته بسبب أنه مقدار الحركة، وإن تصور ثباته فنسبة، أو كم، أو كيف على ما مر. ثم الجمهور على أن الحركة في الأين من مقولة الأين وقيل من مقولة أن ينفعل، لكونها عبارة عن التغير المندرج، وإليه مال الإمام الرازي. وأما الحركة في الكم والكيف والوضع فظاهر أنها ليست
And some went to the view that the genera of accidents are three: quantity, quality, and relation, because if it [i.e., an accident] admits division by itself, it is quantity; otherwise, if it necessitates relation by itself, it is relation; otherwise, it is quality. And some added a fourth division, which is motion, and said: an accident, if its permanence is not conceivable by itself, is motion, and he guarded by the condition 'by itself' against time, for its permanence is not conceivable because it is the measure of motion; and if its permanence is conceivable, then it is relation, or quantity, or quality, as before. Then the majority hold that motion in 'where' belongs to the category of 'where', and it was said [it belongs] to the category of 'being acted upon', because it is an expression of gradual change, and Imam al-Razi inclined to this. As for motion in quantity, quality, and position, it is apparent that it is not...
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage presents alternative views on the number of accident genera. Taftazani reports that some philosophers reduce accident genera to three: quantity (al-kamm), quality (al-kayf), and relation (al-nisba). The criterion is whether the accident admits division by itself (quantity), or necessitates relation (relation), or otherwise (quality). Others add a fourth, motion (al-ḥaraka), arguing that an accident whose permanence is not conceivable by itself is motion; they exclude time from this because time's impermanence is due to its being the measure of motion, not intrinsic. If permanence is conceivable, the accident falls under relation, quantity, or quality. Then Taftazani notes the majority view that motion in the category of 'where' (al-ayn) belongs to the 'where' category, but some (including al-Razi) assign it to the category of 'being acted upon' (an yanfaʿil), because motion is a gradual change. The passage cuts off while discussing motion in quantity, quality, and position, but it suggests that such motions clearly are not a separate category.

Key Terms
الكم
الكيف
النسبة
الحركة
الأين
أن ينفعل
التغير المندرج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think motion is always a separate category of accident.
Correction: Taftazani shows that motion's status as a separate genus is debated; many subsume it under other categories like 'where' or 'being acted upon'.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does time not count as motion under the condition 'by itself'?
A) Time is not an accident.
B) Time's impermanence is due to its being the measure of motion, not intrinsic.
C) Time is permanent by itself.
D) Time belongs to the category of quantity.

Key Takeaway
The classification of accident genera varies among scholars, and motion's status as a separate category is debated.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage distinguishes between permanent accidents and motion (non-permanent). In your own spiritual state, are you more characterized by stable, permanent qualities (like patience, gratitude) or by constant change and temporary emotions? What does that reveal about the strength of your iman, and what is one step you can take today to ground yourself in a permanent quality?
?
If the classification of something as simple as motion can be debated among great scholars, what does this tell you about your own assumptions regarding the unseen realities of Allah's attributes? Are you willing to hold your categories loosely, knowing that ultimate truth belongs to Allah?
?
The criterion for classifying accidents depends on inherent characteristics. Consider your own beliefs: are they based on inherent evidence (fitrah and revelation) or on external influences? How can you ensure your core beliefs are 'by themselves' true, not merely inherited?

Objection That Categories Only Apply to External Realities and the Reply
Answers the objection that categories are for external realities, not mental considerations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection that the ten categories only apply to external realities is refuted by pointing out that some categories themselves are not external.

Core Idea
Some categories are mental relations, not physical things.

In this passage, the author addresses a potential objection to the earlier discussion about the ten categories. The objector claims that the categories only enumerate external realities, so the mental considerations discussed earlier are irrelevant. The reply shows that this objection fails because several categories are themselves not external entities.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Notice that the objector asserts that enumeration is only for external realities, and that the concepts discussed are merely mental considerations.
The objection is succinct but contains two claims: (1) categories are for external realities, (2) these concepts are mental.
2
Identify the author's reply
The author responds by stating that many categories are not external entities, giving examples: relation, action, and passion. Understand how this refutes the objection.
The reply undermines the objector's premise that categories are exclusively for external realities.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this matters: if categories include non-external items, then the earlier mental considerations can be excluded from the categories without inconsistency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : الحصر إنما هو للحقائق الخارجية، وهذه اعتبارات عقلية فلا حاجة الى ما ذكرتم : قلنا : كثير من المقولات ليست أعياناً خارجية كالإضافة، وأن يفعل وأن ينفعل .
If it is said: The enumeration applies only to external realities, and these (i.e., the things discussed) are mental considerations, so there is no need for what you have mentioned. We say: Many of the categories are not external entities, such as relation (al-iḍāfa), action (an yafʻal), and passion (an yanfaʻil).
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objector claims that the ten categories only enumerate external realities (ḥaqāʼiq khārijiyya), while the concepts like unity, point, existence, etc., are mental considerations (iʻtibārāt ʻaqliyya). Therefore, the earlier discussion about them does not affect the category count. The answer is that many categories themselves are not external entities, such as relation (al-iḍāfa), action, and passion. Thus the objection is invalid, since the categories include non-external items, and the concepts in question can likewise be outside the enumeration without breaking it.

The Impossibility of One Accident in Two Loci
Proves the impossibility of one accident in two loci with three arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why an accident cannot subsist in more than one locus and how this necessary truth is argued through three distinct proofs.

Core Idea
An accident cannot be in two places at once—it is a logical and metaphysical necessity.

This passage addresses a subtle necessary truth: the impossibility of a single accident existing in two different locations. Although this seems obvious once understood, some minds may doubt it, so the author provides multiple arguments to reinforce the conclusion. Let's explore his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once through, then follow with the literal English translation. Let the author's own argumentative flow come through before you analyze it.
Pay attention to the three distinct arguments he gives for the impossibility.
2
Analyze the three arguments
Identify each of the three proofs: (1) individuation through the locus, (2) analogy with one body in two places, (3) inability to distinguish two blacknesses. Understand the logical structure of each.
The author uses a reducible ad absurdum style for the third.
3
Contrast with the obvious case
Notice that the author contrasts this debatable necessary truth with an obvious one (accident subsisting by itself). Reflect on why one requires proof while the other is pure obstinacy.
This highlights the spectrum of clarity among necessary truths.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : المبحث الثاني : قد يكون من الضروريات ما يشتبه على بعض الأذهان فيورد في المطالب العالية، ونذكر في معرض الاستدلال ما ينبه على مكان الضرورة، أو يفيد بيان الكمية كامتناع قيام العرض بأكثر من محل واحد فإن الضرورة قاضية بأن العرض القائم بهذا المحل يمتنع أن يكون هو بعينه القائم بمحل آخر، إلا أنه بين كميته بأن تشخص ذلك العرض إنما هو بالمحل يعنى أن محله مستقل بتشخصه، فلو قام بمحلين لزم اجتماع العلتين المستقلتين على معلول واحد وهو تشخص ذلك العرض ونبه عليه بأن حصول العرض الواحد فى محلين كحصول الجسم الواحد في مكانين فلو جاز ذلك لزم جواز هذا وهو ضروري البطلان، وبأنه لو جاز قيام العرض الواحد بمحلين لما حصل الجزم بأن السواد القائم بهذا المحل غير السواد القائم بذلك، لجواز أن يكون سواداً واحداً قائماً بهما، واللازم باطل بالضرورة، وقد يكون منها ما لا يحتاج إلى التنبيه أيضاً، كامتناع قيام العرض بنفسه، فالقول به كما نقل عن أبي الهذيل أن الله تعالى مريد بإرادة عرضية حادثة لا في محل يكون مكابرة محضة، بخلاف قيام العرض الواحد بمحلين
He said: The second investigation: Some necessary [truths] may be ambiguous to some minds, so they are presented in the high-level demands, and we mention in the context of inference what alerts to the place of necessity, or benefits the clarification of the [mode of] quantification, such as the impossibility of an accident subsisting in more than one locus. For necessity judges that the accident subsisting in this locus is impossible to be the very same as the one subsisting in another locus. However, he clarified its quantification [by stating] that the individuation of that accident is only through the locus, meaning that its locus is independent in its individuation. So if it subsisted in two loci, it would necessitate the concurrence of two independent causes upon one effect, which is the individuation of that accident. And he pointed to it [arguing] that the occurrence of one accident in two loci is like the occurrence of one body in two places; so if that were permissible, this would be necessary, and it is necessarily false. And [he argued] that if the subsistence of one accident in two loci were permissible, one would not be certain that the blackness subsisting in this locus is other than the blackness subsisting in that one, because it could be one blackness subsisting in both. And the consequent is necessarily false. And some of them [i.e., necessary truths] may not need alerting either, such as the impossibility of an accident subsisting by itself. So the statement about it, as narrated from Abu al-Hudhayl, that God Almighty is willing through an accidental will originated not in a locus, is pure obstinacy, unlike the subsistence of one accident in two loci.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage is a definition and proof. It begins by stating the second investigation: some necessary truths may be unclear to some minds, so they are presented in high-level demands with reminders or clarifications of their necessity. The first example is the impossibility of an accident (عرض) subsisting in more than one locus. The author explains that necessity rules that an accident in one locus cannot be the very same accident in another. He then provides three arguments for this impossibility. First, the individuation (تشخص) of an accident is through its locus; if it were in two loci, two independent causes would produce one effect, which is impossible. Second, it is analogous to one body being in two places, which is impossible. Third, if possible, we could not distinguish two blacknesses in different loci; they might be one. Then the author notes that some necessary truths are obvious without reminder, like the impossibility of an accident subsisting in itself (e.g., Abu al-Hudhayl's view that God's will is an accident not in a locus). This is pure obstinacy, unlike the more debatable case of one accident in two loci.

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of one accident subsisting in two loci is a necessary truth that can be proven through three independent arguments.

Refuting One Accident in Two Subjects
Refutes the philosophers' claim that relations subsist as one accident in two subjects

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the philosophers' claim that a single accident subsists in two subjects fails, and how each subject has its own distinct accident similar in species.

Core Idea
Relations do not require one accident in two subjects; each subject has its own distinct accident.

The philosophers argued that certain relations like proximity and brotherhood seem to be a single accident shared between two subjects. This passage presents their claim and then refutes it by showing that each subject has its own individual accident, merely similar in species.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophers' claim
Read the beginning of the passage where the philosophers argue that one accident (like proximity) can subsist in two subjects simultaneously.
Notice the examples: proximity, adjacency, brotherhood.
2
Contrast with paternity and sonship
The philosophers contrast these relations with paternity and sonship, which clearly subsist in separate subjects.
3
Understand the refutation
The refutation denies that the accident is numerically one in both subjects; each has its own distinct accident of the same species.
Sharing species does not imply sharing individual identity.
4
Connect to the analogy
The refutation uses the analogy of differing relations like paternity and sonship to show that distinct individuals are even more apparent here.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولهذا جوزه بعض القدماء من المتكلمين الفلاسفة زعماً منهم أن القرب قائم بالمتقاربين والجوار بالمتجاورين والإخوة بالأخوين إلى غير ذلك من الإضافات المتحدة في الجانبين، بخلاف مثل الأبوة والبنوة، فإن قيام الأبوة بالأب، والبنوة بالابن. ورد بأنا لا نسلم أن الواحد بالشخص قائم بالطرفين بل القائم بكل منهما فرد مغاير للقائم بالآخر، غاية الأمر تماثلهما واتحادهما بالنوع، ولا يلزم من اشتراك النوع اشتراك الشخص، وهذا كالإضافات المتخالفة مثل الأبوة والبنوة، فإن مغايرة القائم بهذا القائم بذاك في غاية الظهور
And for this reason some of the ancient theologians among the philosophers allowed it, claiming that proximity subsists in the two proximate things, adjacency in the adjacent, brotherhood in the two brothers, and so on of the relations united in both sides, unlike such as paternity and sonship, for paternity subsists in the father and sonship in the son. And it is refuted by [saying] we do not concede that the numerically one [accident] subsists in both extremes; rather, what subsists in each is an individual distinct from what subsists in the other, the utmost being their similarity and unity in species, and the sharing in species does not necessitate sharing in individual. And this is like the differing relations such as paternity and sonship, for the distinctness of what subsists in this from what subsists in that is most apparent.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the philosophers' argument that certain relations (like proximity, adjacency, brotherhood) are a single accident subsisting in two subjects simultaneously. The author first presents their claim: they allow the subsistence of one accident in two loci because they think, for example, proximity is one relation subsisting in both proximate objects. They contrast this with relations like paternity and sonship, which clearly subsist in separate subjects. The refutation follows: we do not accept that the numerically identical accident subsists in both; rather, each subject has its own individual accident that is similar in species but not identical. The sharing of species does not entail sharing of individual. This is like the case of paternity and sonship: though different in species, the distinction between the two individuals is obvious. The point is that the philosophers' example of relations does not prove one accident in two subjects; it only proves two similar accidents.

Key Terms
إضافات
واحد بالشخص
تماثل
اتحاد النوع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Brotherhood is a single bond that exists simultaneously in two brothers.
Correction: Each brother has his own attribute of brotherhood, distinct but similar in species. The philosophers mistakenly thought it was one accident in two subjects.

Checkpoint
According to the refutation, why do proximity and adjacency not constitute one accident in two subjects?
A) Because they are not accidents.
B) Because each subject has its own distinct individual of the same species.
C) Because they are relations, not qualities.
D) Because the philosophers never actually claimed that.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' claim fails because numerical unity is not proven; similarity in species does not imply identity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
The philosophers' error was assuming unity where there was only similarity. Where might you be making a similar assumption—seeing one cause or one entity when there are actually many distinct ones? What will you do to correct this?

Abu Hashim's View on Composition and its Refutation
Presents Abu Hashim's argument and its refutation regarding the accident of composition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Abu Hashim's two arguments for composition subsisting only in two substances and the refutation that shows why his view is not necessary, including the distinction between an accident subsisting in multiple separate loci versus a composite locus.

Core Idea
Composition may be caused by divine will, and the same accident need not survive part removal.

Abu Hashim, a prominent Mu'tazilite, argued that the composition binding body parts is an accident that can only inhere in two substances simultaneously. He supported this with two key arguments: one from the need for a non-privative bond, and another from the impossibility of a single accident persisting when parts are removed. This lesson presents his reasoning and the standard refutations, which highlight alternative explanations and the possibility of new compositions arising.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its literal English translation. Notice the structure: Abu Hashim's claim, his two arguments, the refutations, and a final objection and response.
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Extract Abu Hashim's two arguments
Identify the first argument about the bond being an affirmative accident, and the second argument about the annihilation of composition when one of three parts is removed. Understand each step.
The first argument posits a necessary bond; the second argues against subsistence in more than two.
3
Understand the refutations and the final distinction
Note how the first refutation denies that the bond must be composition—it could be God's will. The second refutation questions whether the remaining composition is the same as the original. Finally, see the distinction between an accident subsisting in multiple separate loci versus a composite locus.
The objector brings examples from philosophers (unity in ten, etc.) which the author distinguishes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجوزه ابو هاشم من المعتزلة زعماً منه أن التأليف عرضي قائم بجوهرين ويمتنع قيامه بأكثر من جوهرين حتى إنه إذا ألف بين أجزاء كثيرة كان بين كل جزأين تأليف مغاير للتأليف القائم بجزئين آخرين، أما الأول فلأن عسر انفكاك أجزاء الجسم لا بد أن يكون لرابطة، وليس إلا التأليف لأنه لم يحصل عند اجتماعها وصيرورتها جسماً آخر غيره فلا يكون عدمياً، بل ثبوتياً قائماً بشيئين ضرورة، ورد بالمنع لجواز أن يكون بسبب آخر كإرادة الفاعل المختار وأما الثانى فلأنه لو قام بأكثر من جزءين كالثلاثة مثلا لانعدم بانعدام أحد الأجزاء، ضرورة انعدام الحال بانعدام المحل، الذي هو جميع الأجزاء، واللازم باطل ضرورة بقاء التأليف فيما بين الجزأين الباقيين، ورد بأنا لا نسلم أن التأليف الباقي بين الجزأين هو بعينه التأليف القائم بالثلاثة، لم لا يجوز أن ينعدم ذلك، ويحدث هذا ؟ فإن قيل قيام العرض الواحد بالكثير مما قال به الفلاسفة كالوحدة بالعشرة الواحدة والتثليث لمجموع الأضلاع الثلاثة المحيطة بسطح، والحياة ببنية متجزئة إلى أعضاء، والقيام بمجموع أجزاء زيد. قلنا : المتنازع فيه هو أن يكون العرض القائم بمحل هو بعينه القائم بالمحل الآخر إلا أن يكون العرض الواحد قائماً بمجموع شيئين صاراً بالاجتماع محلا واحداً له، كما في هذه الصورة والظاهر أن مراد أبي هاشم أيضاً هذا المعنى، إلا أنه لم يجوز القيام بما فوق الاثنين لما ذكر من لزوم انعدام التأليف عند إزالة أحد الأجزاء من الاجتماع، وكأنه يدعى القطع ببقاء التأليف دون زوال تأليف وزوال حدوث آخر
And Abu Hashim of the Mu'tazila allowed it, claiming that composition is an accident subsisting in two substances and that its subsistence in more than two substances is impossible, such that if many parts are composed together, between each two parts there is a composition different from the composition subsisting in two other parts. As for the first [argument], [he said] that the difficulty of separating the parts of a body must be due to a bond, and it is nothing but composition, because when they come together and become another body, nothing other than it occurs, so it is not privative, but rather affirmative subsisting in two things necessarily. And it is refuted by denial, because it may be due to another cause such as the will of the free agent. As for the second [argument], [he said] that if it [i.e., composition] subsisted in more than two parts, such as three, it would cease to exist with the cessation of one of the parts, necessarily due to the cessation of the state with the cessation of the locus, which is all the parts. And the consequent is false necessarily because of the remaining of the composition between the two remaining parts. And it is refuted by [saying] we do not concede that the composition remaining between the two parts is the very same composition that subsisted in the three; why is it not possible that that one ceases and this one arises? For if it is said: the subsistence of one accident in many is something said by the philosophers, like unity in ten, triangulation in the set of three lines surrounding a surface, and life in a structure divided into organs, and subsistence in the set of Zayd's parts. We say: the point of dispute is that an accident subsisting in a locus be the very same as the one subsisting in the other locus, unless the one accident subsists in a set of two things that by aggregation become one locus for it, as in these cases. And it appears that Abu Hashim also intended this meaning, except that he did not allow subsistence in more than two due to what was mentioned of the necessary annihilation of the composition upon removal of one of the parts from the aggregation, and he seems to claim certainty of the remaining of the composition without a cessation of composition and the occurrence of another.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer argument concerning Abu Hashim's view on composition (تأليف). Abu Hashim, a Mu'tazilite, allowed the subsistence of one accident in two substances but not more. He presented two arguments. First, the difficulty of separating body parts requires a bond, which is composition; it must be an affirmative accident subsisting in two things. The refutation denies this, as the bond could be God's will. Second, if composition subsisted in three parts, removing one would annihilate the composition since the locus (all parts) disappears, yet composition remains between the remaining two, so it cannot be the same composition. The refutation replies that the remaining composition may be a new one, not the same. Then an objector raises examples from philosophers (unity in ten, triangulation, life in a body) where one accident subsists in many parts. The answer distinguishes: the dispute is about an accident being the very same in separate loci, not about an accident subsisting in a composite that is one locus (like a set of parts). Abu Hashim likely intended this meaning but limited it to two because removing one part would destroy the composition, though he assumes the composition remains without the old composition—implying a new one appears.

Key Terms
تأليف
جوهر
عرضي
عدم
ثبوتي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that any accident subsisting in multiple subjects is impossible.
Correction: The author clarifies that it is possible if the subjects together form one locus, as with unity in ten or life in a body.

Checkpoint
What is Abu Hashim's second argument for why composition cannot subsist in more than two substances?
A) It would be impossible to remove one part.
B) If it subsisted in three, removing one would annihilate it, yet composition remains between the two remaining parts—so it cannot be the same.
C) Composition is not a real accident.
D) The philosophers proved it cannot.

Key Takeaway
Abu Hashim's view that composition only subsists in two substances is refuted by showing that alternative causes exist and that the same composition need not remain when parts change.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you observe the cohesion and stability of your own body or the world around you, do you subconsciously attribute it to inherent properties of matter, or do you truly acknowledge that it is sustained by Allah's will every moment? What would change if you consciously reminded yourself of this dependency throughout your day?
?
Think of a relationship or habit that you assume is still the same as it was before. Abu Hashim assumed composition remains after part removal, but the refutation shows it could be a new composition. In your life, have you assumed that a past state continues unchanged (e.g., your iman, your connection with someone) when in reality it may have ceased and been replaced by something new? How can you verify?
?
If the bond holding things together is ultimately from Allah, how does that affect your response when that bond seems to break? Do you turn to the natural cause alone, or do you recognize that Allah may be removing and replacing bonds according to His wisdom? What is one bond in your life that you need to entrust to Him today?

The Philosophers' Proof: Accidents Are Individuated by Their Substrate
Taftazani presents the philosophers' argument on individuation of accidents and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' argument that an accident's individuation must come from its substrate, and how it is refuted by allowing a free agent to assign special relations.

Core Idea
Accidents cannot be individuals without a substrate; but a free agent can override this necessity.

This passage presents a dense philosophical argument about what gives an accident its unique individuality. The philosophers systematically eliminate other possibilities—quiddity, inherent property, separate cause, or haecceity—and conclude that it must be the substrate. However, a refutation shows that a free agent could provide a special relation, preserving divine power. Let's follow the steps of this intricate reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the overall flow of the argument. Note the logical structure: elimination, conclusion, objection, response, refutation.
The argument is compact; read slowly to track each eliminated possibility.
2
Identify the four eliminated possibilities
The philosophers list four things that cannot be the cause of an accident's individuation: its quiddity, something inherent in it, something separate, or its haecceity. Understand the reason for each elimination.
The fourth (haecceity) is presented as an objection from al-Mawāqif.
3
Grasp the conclusion and its implication
After eliminating four possibilities, the philosophers assert that the individuation must come from the substrate. This implies that an accident cannot survive transfer to a different substrate; it would lose its identity.
This supports the Ashʿarī view that accidents do not persist.
4
Analyze the objection and response
The passage includes an objection: what if individuation comes from something inherent in the substrate? The response is that this leads to an infinite regress or circularity, ultimately requiring the substrate as the cause.
5
Consider the refutation
The refutation denies that a separate cause cannot have a special relation; a free agent (like God) can choose to give a particular accident its individuality without needing a preponderator. This undermines the philosophers' conclusion.
This preserves the possibility of accidents existing without a substrate in miraculous contexts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني: وهو للحكماء. إن تشخص العرض لا يجوز أن يكون لماهيته وإلا لزم انحصار الماهية في شخص ضرورة امتناع تخلف المعلول عن الموجبة، ولا لما هو حال في العرض، وإلا لزم الدور لأن الحال في الشيء محتاج إليه متأخر عنه في الوجود، فلو كان علة لتشخصه لكان متقدماً عليه، ولا لأمر منفصل عنه لأن نسبته إلى الكل على السواء فإفادته هذا التشخص دون ذلك ترجح بلا مرجح ولا لهويته على ما أورده صاحب المواقف سنداً لمنع الحصر، لأن الهوية تطلق على التشخص وعلى الوجود الخارجي، وعلى الماهية من حيث كونها مشخصة وشيء من هذه المعاني ليس بمتقدم على التشخص ليكون علة له فيتعين أن يكون تشخص العرض لمحله. فإن قيل: يجوز أن يكون لأمر حال في محله قلنا ينقل الكلام إلى علة تشخص ذلك الأمر، ويرجع آخر الأمر إلى المحل دفعاً للدور والتسلسل وإذا كان تشخصه لمحله امتنع بقاؤه بالشخص عند انتقاله عن ذلك المحل. ورد بأنا لا نسلم أن نسبة المنفصل إلى الكل على السواء لجواز أن يكون له نسبة خاصة إلى هذا التعين سيما إذا كان مختاراً وهو ظاهر.
The second: which is for the philosophers. The individuation (tashakhkhush) of an accident cannot be due to its quiddity (māhiyya), otherwise the quiddity would be confined to a single individual necessarily because of the impossibility of the effect lagging behind the sufficient cause; nor due to something inherent (ḥāl) in the accident, otherwise there would be circularity because the inherent in a thing is dependent on it and posterior to it in existence, so if it were a cause of its individuation, it would be prior to it; nor due to something separate from it because its relation to all is equal, so its conferring this individuation rather than that would be a preponderance without a preponderator; nor due to its haecceity (huwiyya) as al-Ṣāḥib al-Mawāqif cited as support to deny the enumeration, because 'huwiyya' is applied to individuation, external existence, and the quiddity insofar as it is individuating, and none of these meanings is prior to individuation to be a cause of it. So it is determined that the individuation of an accident is due to its substrate. If it is said: it may be due to something inherent in its substrate, we say: the discourse shifts to the cause of the individuation of that thing, and the matter ultimately returns to the substrate to avoid circularity and regress. And if its individuation is due to its substrate, then its remaining as the same individual when transferring from that substrate is impossible. It was refuted by: we do not grant that the relation of the separate thing to all is equal, because it is possible that it has a special relation to this particular determination, especially if it is a free agent, and that is obvious.
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof, from the philosophers, concerning the individuation (tashakhkhush) of an accident. The proof systematically eliminates possible sources of the accident's individuality: it cannot be due to its quiddity (otherwise the quiddity would have only one instance), nor due to something inherent in the accident (circular), nor due to something separate (equal relation to all, so no reason for this specific individuation), nor due to haecceity (huwiyya) as the term is ambiguous. Therefore, the accident's individuation must come from its substrate. Consequently, if the accident left its substrate, it would lose its individuality and cease to be that same accident. The refutation denies that a separate cause cannot have special relation to a particular determination, especially if it is a free agent, which can choose without a preponderator.

Key Terms
تشخص
ماهية
حال
هوية
دور
ترجح بلا مرجح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the philosophers' argument implies accidents are dependent on substrate in a way that denies divine power.
Correction: The refutation shows that a free agent (like God) can assign special relations, so accidents could be individuated without a substrate in miraculous contexts.

Checkpoint
What is the philosophers' conclusion about the cause of an accident's individuation (tashakhkhush)?
A) It is due to its quiddity.
B) It is due to its substrate.
C) It is due to a separate agent.
D) It is due to haecceity.

Key Takeaway
Accident's individuation is argued to depend on its substrate, but a free agent can override this necessity by assigning special relations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The philosophers' argument assumes that a cause must relate equally to all candidates, but the refutation says a free agent can have a special relation. When you think about your own life, do you experience Allah's actions as mechanical and uniform, or as personally tailored to your unique situation? What one area of your life have you been treating as if God's relation to it is 'equal to all' rather than specially determined for you?
?
If the philosophers were right that accidents cannot survive a substrate change, then your own actions (accidents) would be chained to their circumstances. But Allah's power allows free specification. Is there a habit or sin you cling to because you think it is 'attached' to your circumstances and cannot change? How does this lesson challenge your belief in the possibility of transformation through divine will?
?
Taftazani does not grant that a separate cause's relation to all is equal. Consider your relationship with Allah: do you assume He relates to you in the same way as to everyone else, or do you genuinely believe He has a special, intimate relation to your particular 'thisness'? What would change in your worship if you truly internalized that Allah's free agency can specify His mercy uniquely for you right now?

The Fourth Argument Against Accident Transition and Its Refutation
Sanusi presents the fourth argument against accident transition and two refutations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth argument against the transition of accidents and how it is refuted by counterexample and by positing an intermediate space.

Core Idea
The argument against accident transition fails because it applies equally to bodies.

This lesson examines a key objection to the possibility of accident transition: if an accident were moving, where would it be? The argument leads to an infinite regress, but we will see how it is refuted by considering the parallel case of moving bodies and by introducing the concept of intermediate space.

Guided Path
1
Understand the fourth argument
Read the argument carefully: if an accident transitions, during the move it must be in the old place, the new place, or some other place. But being in either old or new implies stasis, not transition, and positing a different place leads to infinite regress. This is the core objection.
2
First refutation: counterexample with body motion
Note how the same reasoning would apply to the observable motion of a body. If the argument were sound, body motion would be impossible too, yet it is observed. This shows the argument is invalid.
3
Second refutation: the third space
The second response avoids the dilemma by claiming that during transition, the accident is in a third space that is partly from the old and partly from the new. This allows gradual transition without being fully in either.
4
Recognize the limitation
The passage notes that this solution only fails for accidents in indivisible atoms (al-jawhar al-fard), where no intermediate space exists. So the transition of such accidents remains impossible.
This nuance is important for understanding the scope of the refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : أنه لو جاز انتقال العرض فهو حالة الانتقال، إما أن يكون في المحل المنتقل عنه، أو المنتقل إليه، وهو باطل لأن هذا استقرار وثبات قبل الانتقال أو بعده لا انتقال أو في محل آخر ضرورة امتناع كون العرض لا محل فينقل الكلام إلى انتقاله إلى هذا المحل ويعود المحذور، ورد أولا بالنقض بانتقال الجسم من حيز إلى حيز، فإنه حالة الانتقال إما أن يكون في الحيز المنتقل عنه، أو في المنتقل إليه، أو غيرهما، والكل باطل لما ذكرتم. فما هو جوابكم؟ فهو جوابنا. وثانيا: بأنا نختار أنه في حيز ثالث هو بعض من المنتقل عنه وبعض من المنتقل إليه، وهكذا حالة الانتقال إلى هذا المحل وإلى ما بينهما إلى ما لا يتناهى، أو ينتهي إلى جزء لا يتجزأ. غاية الأمر أنه يمتنع انتقال العرض الذي يكون في الجوهر الفرد.
Fourth: If the transition of an accident were permissible, then during the state of transition, it must either be in the place it is leaving or in the place it is entering, which is false because this would be stability and fixity before or after the transition, not transition, or in another place, due to the impossibility of an accident being without a place; so the discussion is transferred to its transition to this place and the objection returns. It is refuted first by counterexample: by the transition of a body from one space to another, for during the state of transition it must either be in the space it is leaving, or in the space it is entering, or in a different one, and all are false according to what you mentioned. So what is your answer? That is our answer. Second: we choose that it is in a third space, some of which is from the space it leaves and some from the space it enters, and likewise the state of transition to this place and to what is between them ad infinitum, or it ends at an indivisible part. The only limitation is that the transition of an accident that is in an indivisible substance is impossible.
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a specific argument against the transition of accidents. The author, al-Taftazani, presents the fourth argument: if an accident could transition, during the move it would either be in its original location or the new one, but both would imply stasis, not motion, leading to an infinite regress. The first response is a counterexample: the same reasoning would apply to the transition of a body, which is observable, so the argument is invalid. The second response posits that during transition, the accident is in a third intermediate space, partly overlapping both original and target spaces, avoiding the dilemma. The passage concludes that this only fails for accidents in indivisible atoms, where no intermediate space exists.

Key Terms
انتقال العرض
المحل
الحيز
الجوهر الفرد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the transition of accidents is impossible because during motion the accident must be fully located somewhere, but the argument shows the need for a spatial substrate.
Correction: The counterexample of body motion shows that the same reasoning would apply to bodies, yet they move; and the second response shows that during transition, the accident can be in an intermediate space.

Checkpoint
What is the second response to the fourth argument against accident transition?
A) The accident is in both the old and new places at once.
B) The accident is in a third intermediate space partly overlapping both.
C) The accident does not exist during transition.
D) The accident transitions instantly without intermediate state.

Key Takeaway
The fourth argument against accident transition is refuted by counterexample with body motion and by the concept of an intermediate space, with the exception of accidents in indivisible atoms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You witness body motion every day, yet you might deny the transition of spiritual states because they don't fit a rigid theory. Will you let the clear sign in front of you—the reality of change—shatter your intellectual pride and lead you to accept the complexity of Allah's creation?
?
The second refutation introduces an intermediate space for change. Are you willing to admit that your repentance or growth may require a gradual, messy transition, not an instant leap? What 'third space' are you avoiding because it feels incomplete? Will you enter it today?

The Proof that Accidents Cannot Subsist in Accidents
Sanusi states the rule and gives the first proof for it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two rational arguments Sanusi gives against the possibility of an accident subsisting in another accident.

Core Idea
An accident cannot inhere in another accident because it needs a spatial substrate and avoids infinite regress.

Sanusi begins by stating the position that accidents cannot subsist in accidents. He then provides two concise arguments: one based on the nature of subsistence as dependence on a spatial locus, and another based on the necessity of ending with a substance to avoid infinite regress. Let's examine these arguments closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the original Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once to get the flow of Sanusi's argument. Note that he first states the rule, then gives two reasons connected by 'because' (لأن).
2
Identify the two reasons
Look for the two 'because' clauses. The first reason concerns the meaning of subsistence as spatial dependence; the second concerns infinite regress leading to a substance.
The second reason begins with 'ولأنه لا بد بالآخرة من جوهر'.
3
Reflect on why infinite regress is a problem
Consider why an infinite chain of accidents is impossible in the kalam framework. The argument is that subsistence requires a foundation, and without a terminating substance, the chain would be absurd.
This connects to the broader kalam principle that all accidents need a substance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الرابع: لا يجوز قيام العرض بالعرض. قال: لا يجوز قيام العرض بالعرض لأن معناه التبعية في التحيز فلا يعقل فيما لا يتحيز بالذات ولأنه لا بد بالآخرة من جوهر فليس قيام البعض بالبعض أولى من قيام الكل به. واعترض بأن معنى القيام الاختصاص الناعت، فقد يكون في غير المتحيز، وقد يكون العرض نعتا لغرض آخر، لا الجوهرة كسرعة الحركة وملاسة السطح واستقامة الخط، فلذا جوزه الفلاسفة، وجعلوا النقطة قائمة بالخط والخط بالسطح. ومنهم من تمادى حتى جعلوا وحدة الأعراض ووجودها من ذلك. والمتكلمون على أن بعض هذه اعتبارات وبعضها قائمة بالجواهر وبعضها جواهر، وأما عرضية الوجود فخطأ فاحش.
Fourth chapter: An accident subsisting in an accident is not permissible. He said: An accident subsisting in an accident is not permissible because its meaning is dependence in spatiality, so it is inconceivable in what is not intrinsically spatial, and because ultimately there must be a substance, so the subsistence of some in some is not more appropriate than the subsistence of the whole in it. It is objected that the meaning of subsistence is attributive specification, which may occur in what is not spatial, and an accident may be an attribute of another accident, not of a substance, such as the speed of motion, the smoothness of a surface, and the straightness of a line; therefore the philosophers permitted it, and they made the point subsist in the line and the line in the surface. Some of them went so far as to make the unity and existence of accidents from that. The theologians hold that some of these are mental considerations, some subsist in substances, and some are substances; as for accidents being existence, it is a gross error.
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage defines the position that an accident cannot inhere in another accident. The proof given is twofold: (1) subsistence means dependence on a substrate for spatial location, so something not inherently spatial cannot support another; (2) an infinite regress is avoided by ultimately grounding in a substance. The objection from the philosophers argues that 'subsistence' can mean attributive relation, which does not require spatiality; examples include accidents describing other accidents like speed (accident of motion) or smoothness (accident of surface). The response clarifies that these examples are either mental considerations, or the supposed accidents actually inhere in underlying substances (e.g., smoothness in the surface which itself inheres in a body), or are themselves substances. The claim that accidents have independent existence is deemed a gross error.

Key Terms
قيام العرض بالعرض
التبعية في التحيز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Accidents can inhere in other accidents like a point on a line.
Correction: Sanusi argues that accidents lack spatial extension, so they cannot serve as substrates. Points and lines are either mental considerations or themselves substances.

Checkpoint
What does Sanusi say is the meaning of 'subsistence' (قيام)?
A) Dependence on a spatial location
B) Inherence in a substance
C) Being an attribute of another
D) Having independent existence

Key Takeaway
Accidents cannot subsist in accidents because they lack spatial extension and allowing it would lead to an infinite regress requiring a substance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What in your life are you depending on that, like an accident, has no stability of its own? How does this lesson force you to redirect your trust to the Self-Sufficient?
?
The proof shows that infinite regress is avoided only by a substance. Where in your own beliefs or actions are you building an endless chain of dependencies that never rest on the Eternal? What step will you take today to anchor yourself in what truly subsists?
?
If accidents cannot bear other accidents, then what does that say about relying on created things to give you lasting peace? Are you willing to examine one attachment you thought was solid and see it as accidental? Write a concrete commitment to rely on Allah alone.

The Philosophers' Objection and the Theologians' Response
Sanusi presents the philosophical objection and the theologians' response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' objection to the impossibility of accident-in-accident and the theologians' classification of the examples.

Core Idea
Speed inhering in motion is not an accident inhering in an accident, but a mental consideration or inherence in a substance.

After presenting the proof, Sanusi turns to an objection raised by philosophers, who point to cases like speed of motion, smoothness of a surface, and straightness of a line as examples of accidents describing other accidents. The theologians respond by reclassifying these cases: some are mental considerations, some actually inhere in underlying substances, and some are substances themselves. Let's examine this dialectic.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and examples
Read the Arabic passage containing the objection. Notice the key terms: 'اختصاص ناعت' and the examples: 'سرعة الحركة', 'ملاسة السطح', 'استقامة الخط'.
2
Understand the philosophers' reasoning
The philosophers argue that subsistence can mean attributive specification, which does not require spatiality. Therefore, an accident can be an attribute of another accident.
They use examples like speed of motion, where speed is an accident and motion is also an accident.
3
See how theologians respond
The theologians reclassify the examples: some are mere mental considerations, some inhere in substances (not in accidents), and some are substances. They reject the idea that accidents have independent existence as a gross error.
Focus on the classification: 'بعض هذه اعتبارات وبعضها قائمة بالجواهر وبعضها جواهر'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

This passage defines the position that an accident cannot inhere in another accident. The proof given is twofold: (1) subsistence means dependence on a substrate for spatial location, so something not inherently spatial cannot support another; (2) an infinite regress is avoided by ultimately grounding in a substance. Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Speed is an accident inhering in the accident of motion.
Correction: The theologians hold that speed is either a mental consideration or it actually inheres in the moving substance, not in motion itself.

Checkpoint
According to the theologians, what is the status of 'smoothness of a surface'?
A) It is a mental consideration.
B) It inheres in the substance underlying the surface.
C) It is a substance itself.
D) It inheres in the accident of surface.

Key Takeaway
The theologians argue that examples like speed and smoothness do not prove accident-in-accident; they are either mental considerations, attributes of substances, or substances themselves.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever redefined a theological term to make an argument easier, ignoring the foundational meanings held by the early scholars? What was at stake, and what did you lose by that redefinition?
?
The philosophers allowed accident-in-accident by reinterpreting 'subsistence' as mere attributive specification. In your own life, have you subtly shifted definitions of fundamental concepts (like tawheed, iman, or sunnah) to accommodate your desires or intellectual trends? How can you guard against that?
?
The theologians condemned the idea that existence is an accident as a 'gross error.' Are there any attributes of Allah that you have unconsciously treated as accidental or secondary rather than essential? What would it take to realign your understanding with the creed of the salaf?

First Proof Against Accident Inhering in Accident: Spatial Dependency
First proof against accident inhering in accident based on spatial dependency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first proof used by the majority of mutakallimun to deny that an accident can inhere in another accident, based on the concept of dependency in spatial occupation.

Core Idea
An accident can only inhere in something that occupies space by itself.

The mutakallimun argued that accidents cannot inhere in other accidents because the very meaning of inherence involves dependency in spatial occupation. Only a substance (jawhar) is spatially self-subsistent. This lesson examines the first of two proofs they offered.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: first the position, then the proof, then a concluding remark.
Pay attention to the key terms: قيام, العرض, التحيز.
2
Understand key terms
Focus on the definitions of قيام (inherence), العرض (accident), التحيز (spatial occupation), and متحيز بالذات (spatially self-subsistent). These are essential to the logic.
3
Follow the logical chain
Trace the argument: inherence = dependency in spatial occupation → substrate must be spatially self-subsistent → only substances are spatially self-subsistent → accidents cannot be substrates.
The final sentence adds that the spatially self-subsistent (substance) is not more deserving; it's a rhetorical reinforcement.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this proof is important: it establishes a fundamental ontological distinction between substances and accidents, and it justifies why accidents must inhere directly in substances.
5
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question and try to paraphrase the proof in your own words to ensure you have grasped the reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الرابع. جمهور المتكلمين على أنه يمتنع قيام العرض بالعرض تمسكاً بوجهين: الأول: أن معنى قيام العرض بالمحل أنه تابع له في التحيز فما يقوم به العرض يجب أن يكون متحيزاً بالذات ليصح كون الشيء تبعاً له في التحيز، والمتحيز بالذات ليس أولى من قيام الكل بذلك الجوهر.
The fourth investigation. The majority of the mutakallimun hold that it is impossible for an accident to inhere in an accident, adhering to two proofs: The first: that the meaning of an accident's inherence in a substrate is that it is dependent upon it in spatial occupation; so that in which the accident inheres must be spatially self-subsistent for the thing to be dependent upon it in spatial occupation. And the spatially self-subsistent is not more deserving of having the whole inhere in that substance.
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage defines the position of the majority of mutakallimun and presents the first proof. The position is that an accident cannot inhere in another accident. The first proof relies on the nature of inherence: for an accident to inhere in something, that thing must be spatially self-subsistent (mutahayyiz bi al-dhat). Only a substance (jawhar) is spatially self-subsistent. Therefore, an accident, which is not spatially self-subsistent, cannot serve as a substrate for another accident. The proof argues that if an accident could inhere in another accident, then ultimately the chain would need to end in a substance, but it is more appropriate for the entire series to inhere directly in the substance.

Key Terms

العرض
التحيز
المتحيز بالذات
المتكلمين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument is circular because it assumes what it wants to prove.
Correction: The argument is not circular; it defines inherence in terms of spatial dependency, then deduces that the substrate must be spatially self-subsistent, which accidents are not.

Checkpoint
According to the first proof presented, what is the meaning of an accident's inherence in a substrate?
A) It is the accident being spatially contained in the substrate.
B) It is the accident being dependent on the substrate in spatial occupation.
C) It is the accident being a quality of the substrate.
D) It is the accident being ontologically identical to the substrate.

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of accident inhering in accident is proven by the requirement that a substrate must be spatially self-subsistent, which only a substance is.

Write down one change you can make today to acknowledge your ultimate dependency on the Self-Subsistent.
?
The argument shows that an accident cannot inhere in another accident because spatial dependency requires a self-subsistent substrate. In your spiritual life, do you ever try to build your faith on something that is itself dependent (like a teacher's opinion, a strong emotion, a good deed) rather than on the uncreated, self-subsistent truth of Allah's revelation? Second Proof: Necessity of Ending in a Substance
Second proof: necessity of ending in a substance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second proof against accidents inhering in accidents, which demonstrates that a chain of accidents must ultimately terminate in a self-subsistent substance and that direct inherence in that substance is more appropriate than indirect inherence through other accidents.

Core Idea
The chain of accidents ends where self-subsistence begins.

This lesson presents the second proof from the author, which builds on the impossibility of an accident inhering in itself. If accidents could inhere in other accidents, an infinite chain would arise. This chain cannot continue indefinitely because accidents depend on a self-subsistent sustainer. The argument then shows that it is simpler and more logical for all accidents to inhere directly in the substance rather than through intermediaries.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its literal English translation to understand the flow of the argument.
Pay attention to the phrases 'ضرورة امتناع قيام العرض بنفسه' and 'لأن القائم بنفسه أحق'.
2
Identify the key claim
The passage argues that a chain of accidents must end in a substance. Identify the reason given for this necessity.
The reason is that an accident cannot inhere in itself; it needs a self-subsistent substrate.
3
Understand the preference for direct inherence
The author gives two reasons why all accidents inhering directly in the substance is more appropriate than a chain. Note these reasons.
Reason 1: Self-subsistent things are more deserving as substrates. Reason 2: All accidents are in the spatial location of the substance.
4
Reflect on the logical structure
Consider how this argument differs from an infinite regress argument; it is about ontological simplicity and fittingness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني: أنه لو قام عرض بعرض فلا بد في الآخرة من جوهر ينتهي إليه سلسلة الأعراض ضرورة امتناع قيام العرض بنفسه، وحينئذ فقيام بعض الأعراض بالبعض ليس أولى من قيام الكل بذلك الجوهر، بل هذا أولى، لأن القائم بنفسه أحق بأن يكون محلاً مقوماً للحال، ولأن الكل في حيز الجوهر تبعاً له، وهو معنى القيام.
The second: that if an accident inhered in an accident, then ultimately there would have to be a substance to which the chain of accidents ends, due to the impossibility of an accident inhering in itself. Then, the inherence of some accidents in some others is not more appropriate than the inherence of all in that substance; rather this [the latter] is more appropriate, because that which is self-subsistent is more deserving of being a constitutive substrate for the accidents, and because all [accidents] are in the spatial location of the substance dependent on it, and that is the meaning of inherence.
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof. It argues that if an accident could inhere in another accident, we would have a chain of accidents. But an accident cannot inhere in itself, so the chain must ultimately end in a substance that is self-subsistent. Then the question arises: why have intermediate accidents? It is more fitting for all accidents to inhere directly in the substance, because the substance is self-subsistent and is the proper substrate. The argument uses a principle of preferring simpler ontological dependence.

Key Terms

Correction: While a chain is involved, the argument focuses on the necessity of terminating in a substance and then argues for the preferability of direct inherence over a chain. The key point is not regress but the fittingness of direct inherence.

Key Takeaway
The second proof demonstrates that a chain of accidents must terminate in a self-subsistent substance, and that direct inherence in that substance is more appropriate than indirect inherence through other accidents.

Philosophers' Objection: Inherence as Attributive Specification
Philosophers' objection redefining inherence and allowing accident-in-accident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' redefinition of inherence as attributive specification and how it permits accidents to inhere in other accidents.

Core Idea
Inherence is not spatial dependency but one thing specifying another as its attribute.

This lesson presents the philosophers' objection to the mutakallimun's proofs that accidents cannot inhere in accidents. The philosophers reinterpret 'inherence' as attributive specification (ikhtisas na'ti) rather than dependency in spatial occupation. This redefinition opens the door for accidents to qualify other accidents, with examples like speed qualifying motion. You will see how this objection challenges the traditional view and its implications for divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the Philosophers' Objection
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Note the key claim: inherence is not about spatial dependency.
Focus on the redefinition of 'قيام' as 'اختصاص ناعت'.
2
Understand the Redefinition
The philosophers argue that inherence means attributive specification, as whiteness specifies the body, not the body specifying place. This definition does not require the substrate to be spatially located.
This allows attributes of God (non-spatial) to exist.
3
Examine the Application to Accidents
The philosophers concede that accidents ultimately terminate in a substance, but they deny all accidents inhere directly in it. They give examples: speed for motion, smoothness for surface, straightness for line.
In each example, the described thing is an accident, not the underlying body.
4
Reflect on the Consequence
This objection challenges the mutakallimun's proofs by showing that accident-in-accident inherence is possible, implying that the chain of inherence need not all be in the substance directly.
The philosophers thus permit accidents to inhere in other accidents.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعترض على الوجهين بأنا لا نسلم أن معنى قيام الشيء بالشيء التبعية في التحيز، بل معناه اختصاص الشي بالشيء نعتاً له وهو منعوا به كاختصاص البياض بالجسم لا الجسم بالمكان، والقيام بهذا المعنى لا يختص بالمتحيز كما في صفات الله تعالى عند المتكلمين وصفات الجواهر المجردة عند الفلاسفة فضلا أن عن بالمتحيز لا بالتبعية ثم انتهاء قيام العرض إلى الجوهر مما لا نزاع فيه ، إلا أنه لا يوجب قيام الكل به لجواز أن يكون الاختصاص الناعت فيما بين بعض الأعراض بأن يكون عرضاً نعتاً لعرض لا للجوهر الذي إليه الانتهاء كالسرعة للحركة والملاسة للسطح والاستقامة للخط، فإن المنعوت حقيقة بهذه الأعراض هي تلك لا الجسم فلهذا جوزت الفلاسفة قيام العرض
And an objection was raised against the two proofs: we do not concede that the meaning of a thing's inherence in a thing is dependency in spatial occupation. Rather, its meaning is the specification of a thing by a thing as an attribute for it, as specification of whiteness to body not body to place. And inherence in this sense is not exclusive to spatially located things, as in the attributes of God according to the mutakallimun and the attributes of abstract substances according to the philosophers, let alone that it is not by dependency. Then, the termination of an accident's inherence to a substance is not disputed, but it does not necessitate the inherence of all in it, because it is possible that attributive specification occurs among some accidents such that an accident is an attribute for an accident, not for the substance to which termination occurs, like speed for motion, smoothness for surface, and straightness for line. For the thing actually described by these accidents is those [accidents] themselves, not the body. Hence the philosophers permitted the inherence of an accident in an accident.
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' objection to both proofs. The objection redefines 'inherence': it is not dependency in spatial occupation but rather an attributive specification (ikhtisas na'ti). For example, whiteness specifies the body, not the body specifies the place. This kind of inherence does not require the substrate to be spatially located; it can apply to non-spatial entities like God's attributes or abstract substances. The philosophers concede that chains of accidents ultimately end in a substance, but they deny that this forces all accidents to inhere directly in the substance. Instead, one accident can be an attribute of another accident, as speed is an attribute of motion (which is an accident), smoothness of surface (an accident), straightness of line (an accident). The thing actually described by these accidents is that intermediate accident, not the underlying body. Therefore, the philosophers allow accident-in-accident.

Key Terms
اختصاص ناعت
القيام
الصفات الله
الجواهر المجردة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think this objection denies the necessity of a substance altogether.
Correction: The philosophers do not deny the ultimate substrate; they only redefine inherence and allow intermediate accident-in-accident.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers' objection, what is the meaning of inherence?
A) Dependency in spatial occupation.
B) Attributive specification of one thing by another.
C) Ontological identity.
D) Causal relation.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers argue that inherence is attributive specification, which can occur between accidents, allowing accidents to qualify other accidents without direct inherence in a substance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about God's attributes like knowledge or power, do you naturally imagine them as 'things' that are somehow attached to God? How does the philosophers' redefinition of inherence as 'attributive specification' challenge that mental picture? Will you now try to think of God's attributes as pure descriptions rather than quasi-spatial attachments?
?
This lesson shows that philosophers accept accidents inhering in accidents. Consider your own character traits: do you ever treat your own actions (accidents) as if they define you permanently (as a substance)? What would change if you saw your actions as attributes that can themselves be attributed to deeper causes? How can you use this to detach from your mistakes?
