The Zahiriyya Proof for the Impossibility of Accident Endurance
The commentator presents the Zahiriyya's two-fold argument against the persistence of accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two arguments used by the Ẓāhiriyya to deny the endurance of accidents and why the commentator considers them weak.

Core Idea
For the Zahiriyya, the very concept of an accident implies it cannot last.

The Ẓāhiriyya were a literalist school who held strong views on the nature of accidents. In this passage, the commentator presents their two-fold argument against the persistence of accidents, relying on both linguistic definition and a logical dilemma. This lesson will examine each argument and the commentator's assessment.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: a general claim followed by two reasons introduced by 'لأن' and 'ولأنه'.
The introductory phrase 'المبحث الخامس' indicates this is a new topic.
2
Understand the first argument (definitional)
The Ẓāhiriyya claim that the word for accident (العارض) inherently implies non-endurance. Ask yourself: is this a semantic or a metaphysical argument?
3
Understand the second argument (hypothetical dilemma)
If an accident could endure, it would have to endure either by its substrate's endurance (making it like the substrate) or by a separate endurance (which would allow it to survive the substrate's annihilation). Both options are deemed absurd.
Trace the reasoning: either way leads to an unacceptable conclusion.
4
Reflect on the commentator's verdict
The commentator states that both arguments are 'weak and apparent' (ضعفهما ظاهر). Consider why he dismisses them and what this sets up for the stronger proofs to follow.
The weakness likely lies in the definitional argument being too semantic and the dilemma not being exhaustive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الخامس الأعراض لا تبقى زمانين (قال: ذهب كثير من المتكلمين إلى امتناع بقاء العرض، فالظاهريون لأن استحالة البقاء معتبرة في مفهوم هذا الاسم كالعارض ونحوه ولأنه لو بقي فإما ببقاء محله فيدوم بدوامه ويتصف بسائر صفاته وإما ببقاء آخر فيمكن بقاؤه مع فناء المحل وضعفهما ظاهر
The fifth investigation: Accidents do not endure two moments. (He said: Many mutakallimūn held the impossibility of the endurance of an accident. The Ẓāhiriyya [argue] because the impossibility of endurance is considered in the concept of this name, like al-ʿāriḍ and the like, and because if it endured, then either by the endurance of its locus, so it would continue with its continuance and be described with all its attributes, or by another endurance, so its endurance would be possible alongside the annihilation of the locus. But both [options] are weak and apparent).
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage presents the position of the Ẓāhiriyya (literalist) school regarding the persistence of accidents. It begins by stating the general claim: many theologians affirm that accidents cannot last for two consecutive moments of time. The Ẓāhiriyya support this with two reasons. First, they assert that the very linguistic and conceptual meaning of an accident (like 'āriḍ) implies instability and transience, so endurance is inherently impossible for it. Second, they argue hypothetically: if an accident were to persist, it would have to do so either (a) through the persistence of its substrate (the body it inheres in), but then the accident would be co-eternal with the body and would acquire all the body's properties, which is not the case; or (b) through a separate, additional persistence that could theoretically remain even after the substrate's annihilation—a scenario they deem absurd. The commentator notes that both arguments are weak and obvious in their flaw, setting the stage for the more rigorous proofs of the Muḥaqqiqūn that follow.

Key Terms
عارض
بقاء
محل
ظاهريون
امتناع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the Zahiriyya rejection of accident endurance is based solely on the literal definition of 'accident'.
Correction: While the first argument is definitional, the second argument adds a logical dilemma concerning persistence with or without the substrate.

Checkpoint
What are the two arguments given by the Ẓāhiriyya to deny the endurance of an accident?
A) The accident's definition implies non-endurance; if it persisted it would require either the substrate's endurance or a separate endurance, both absurd.
B) The accident cannot exist without the substrate; the accident is created anew each moment.
C) The accident is not a real entity; the accident is identical with the substrate.
D) The accident's endurance would lead to a chain of infinite regress; the accident is dependent on a renewing cause.

Key Takeaway
The Zahiriyya argued that accidents cannot endure because their definition implies instability and because any supposed endurance leads to absurd outcomes; however, the commentator dismisses both arguments as weak.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Ẓāhiriyya argue that an accident, by its very nature, cannot endure. Think about your own fleeting thoughts and emotions. If even your most sincere moments of worship are accidents that do not persist, how can you ensure their acceptance and lasting reward? Does this drive you to renew your intentions and actions constantly?
?
Consider the dilemma: if your good deeds were to 'endure' by your own effort (like the substrate enduring), they would share your imperfect qualities. Or if they endure by a separate persistence, they could exist independently of you. Neither fits. In light of this, on what do you truly rely for the permanence of your deeds? Your own consistency or Allah's mercy?
?
The commentator dismisses the Ẓāhiriyya's proof as weak. Have you ever held a belief about yourself—like 'I am a patient person'—based on a shallow definition, without examining the logical implications? What would it take for you to move from a superficial understanding of your own qualities to a deeper, more rigorous self-awareness?

The First Proof: Accident Subsisting in Accident
First proof with immediate rebuttal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first proof of the Muhaqqiqun for the impossibility of accident endurance and its simple rebuttal by denying the premises.

Core Idea
If accidents endured, they would require accidents subsisting in accidents.

The Muhaqqiqun (verifying scholars) begin with a straightforward argument: if an accident were to endure, it would need to persist, and persistence itself is an accident inhering in it. This would mean an accident subsisting in another accident, which is unanimously deemed impossible. The rebuttal comes by flatly denying either that endurance is an accident or that it inheres.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text once, then its translation. Note the concise structure: conditional statement and immediate rebuttal.
The passage is short but packs a strong claim.
2
Identify the two premises
The proof has two premises: (1) if an accident endures, then endurance is an accident inhering in it; (2) an accident subsisting in an accident is impossible. The rebuttal denies either premise.
Denying any premise suffices to defeat the proof.
3
Reflect on the dialectical move
Notice that the Muhaqqiqun themselves record the rebuttal. This shows intellectual honesty and the dialogical nature of kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمحققون لوجهين : الأول : أنه لو كان باقياً يلزم قيام العرض بالعرض، وهو محال ورد بمنع المقدمتين . الثاني : لو بقي لامتنع زواله إذ لو أمكن فإما بنفسه فيمتنع وجوده، أو بزوال شرط فيتسلسل أو بطريان ضد فيدور لأن اتصاف المحل بأحد الضدين مشروط بانتفاء الآخر على أن زوال الباقي بالطارىء ليس أولى، بل بالعكس لأن الدفع أهون من الرفع أو بفاعل فيقتضي أثراً والنفي المحض لا يصلح، ورد أولاً بالنقض بالجسم، وقد يدفع بأنه يزول بأن يخلق الله تعالى فيه عرض الفناء أو لا يخلق عرضاً هو شرط البقاء، والعرض لا يصلح محلاً للعرض. وثانياً : بالقلب إذ لو لم يبق ففناؤه إما بنفسه أو بغيره . وثالثاً : بالحل إذ يجوز أن تقتضي ذاته العدم في بعض الأحوال. وأن يكون مشروطاً بأعراض تتجدد على التبادل إلى أن ينتهي إلى ما لا بدل له، فيزول عنده وأن يكون طريان الضد وانتفاء الآخر معاً كما في دخول كل من أجزاء الحلقة في حيز الآخر، وخروج الآخر عنه، وهذا لا ينافي التقدم في العقل باعتبار العلية، وأن يكون العدم الحادث أثراً للفاعل، ولو سلم فليكن بمعنى أنه لا يفعله لا بمعنى أنه يفعل عدمه) .
And the Muḥaqqiqūn [affirm it] for two reasons: First: If it were enduring, then the subsistence of an accident in an accident would be necessary, and that is impossible; it is replied by denying both premises. Second: If it endured, its cessation would be impossible; for if cessation were possible, then either (a) by itself, then its existence would be impossible, (b) by the removal of a condition, then an infinite regress would follow, (c) by the occurrence of a contrary, then there would be a vicious circle because the locus's being qualified by one contrary is conditioned by the negation of the other, and the removal of the enduring by the arriving contrary is not more appropriate – rather the opposite, because prevention is easier than removal – or (d) by an agent, then it would require an effect, and mere negation does not suffice. And it is replied first by counterexample (naqḍ) with the body; and it may be repelled by saying that it ceases by God creating in it the accident of annihilation, or by His not creating an accident that is the condition of endurance – but an accident is not fit to be a locus for an accident. Second, by inversion (qalb): if it did not endure, then its annihilation would be either by itself or by another. Third, by solution (ḥall): it is possible that its essence necessitates non-existence in some states; or that it is conditioned by accidents that are renewed in alternation until it reaches what has no substitute, then it ceases; or that the occurrence of the contrary and the negation of the other [occur] together, like the entry of each of the links of a ring into the space of the other and the exit of the other from it – and this does not contradict priority in reason by virtue of causality; or that the originated non-existence is an effect of the agent; and even if conceded, let it be in the sense that He does not do it, not in the sense that He does its non-existence.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage contains the two main proofs of the Muḥaqqiqūn (the verifying scholars) for the impossibility of an accident enduring for two moments, followed by responses to three objections. The first proof argues that if an accident endured, it would necessitate an accident subsisting in another accident (since endurance itself would be an accident inhering in the first accident), which is impossible. The response to this is to deny either that endurance is an accident or that it inheres. The second proof is more elaborate: if an accident endured, it would be impossible for it to cease to exist. The argument considers all possible ways cessation could occur: (a) self-annihilation (impossible because existence would then be impossible), (b) removal of a condition (leading to infinite regress of conditions), (c) arrival of a contrary (leading to a vicious circle because the locus's qualification by one contrary depends on the absence of the other, and removing the enduring by the arriving contrary is no more necessary than the reverse), (d) by an external agent (which would require an effect, but nonexistence is not an effect). The passage then addresses three objections. First objection (naqḍ): the body itself endures – but the reply is that God either creates an accident of annihilation in it or does not create the accident of endurance, and an accident cannot be a locus for another accident. Second objection (qalb): if the accident does not endure, its annihilation would be either by itself or by another – thus mirroring the argument. Third objection (ḥall): various solutions are proposed, such as the accident's own nature causing non-existence in some states, or its endurance being conditioned by a series of renewing accidents until one has no substitute, or that the contrary's arrival and the other's absence occur simultaneously like interlocking rings, which does not conflict with causal priority, or that non-existence is directly produced by the agent, understood as the agent not creating the accident rather than creating non-existence. This detailed dialectic shows the sophistication of kalām argumentation.

Key Terms
عرض
بقاء
قيام العرض بالعرض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the proof is irrefutable.
Correction: The text itself records a rebuttal: the premises can be denied.

Checkpoint
According to the first proof, what would be necessary if an accident endured?
A) The accident would become a substance
B) An accident would subsist in an accident
C) The accident would cease immediately
D) The accident would change its nature

Key Takeaway
The first proof attempts to show that accident endurance leads to a known impossibility, but it is rebutted by denying its premises.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Muhaqqiqun presented a proof but then immediately recorded its rebuttal. Where in your life have you been so attached to a conclusion that you ignored counterarguments? Will you now seek out and honestly consider objections to your firmly held beliefs?
?
This proof was rejected by denying its premises. Think of a time you accepted an argument without examining its foundational assumptions. What premise did you take for granted? How can you train yourself to question premises?
?
The scholars here show intellectual humility—they present both sides. Is there a belief about your own faith or practice that you avoid scrutinising for fear it might be weak? What would it cost you to test it against the strongest objections?

The Second Proof: The Impossibility of Cessation
Second proof with four-fold division

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the complex second proof of the Muhaqqiqun, which argues that if an accident endured, its cessation would be impossible, and the exhaustive enumeration of possible modes of cessation.

Core Idea
Endurance makes cessation impossible because every possible mode of cessation fails.

The second proof is more elaborate. It assumes an accident endures and then examines how it could cease to exist. The Muhaqqiqun consider four possible ways—by itself, by removal of a condition, by arrival of a contrary, or by an external agent—and argue that each leads to an absurdity. This shows that cessation is impossible, so endurance is impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic carefully, noting the conditional structure and the list of modes of cessation.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the four modes
Find each mode: (a) by itself, (b) by removal of a condition, (c) by arrival of a contrary, (d) by an agent. Understand the absurdity argued for each.
The argument for (c) involves a vicious circle and the principle that prevention is easier than removal.
3
Understand why each mode fails
For each mode, the proof shows it leads to an impossibility: self-annihilation prevents existence, infinite regress, vicious circle, or effect from non-existence.
4
Connect to conclusion
Since all possibilities for cessation are eliminated, cessation is impossible. Hence, endurance is impossible.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمحققون لوجهين : الأول : أنه لو كان باقياً يلزم قيام العرض بالعرض، وهو محال ورد بمنع المقدمتين . الثاني : لو بقي لامتنع زواله إذ لو أمكن فإما بنفسه فيمتنع وجوده، أو بزوال شرط فيتسلسل أو بطريان ضد فيدور لأن اتصاف المحل بأحد الضدين مشروط بانتفاء الآخر على أن زوال الباقي بالطارىء ليس أولى، بل بالعكس لأن الدفع أهون من الرفع أو بفاعل فيقتضي أثراً والنفي المحض لا يصلح، ورد أولاً بالنقض بالجسم، وقد يدفع بأنه يزول بأن يخلق الله تعالى فيه عرض الفناء أو لا يخلق عرضاً هو شرط البقاء، والعرض لا يصلح محلاً للعرض. وثانياً : بالقلب إذ لو لم يبق ففناؤه إما بنفسه أو بغيره . وثالثاً : بالحل إذ يجوز أن تقتضي ذاته العدم في بعض الأحوال. وأن يكون مشروطاً بأعراض تتجدد على التبادل إلى أن ينتهي إلى ما لا بدل له، فيزول عنده وأن يكون طريان الضد وانتفاء الآخر معاً كما في دخول كل من أجزاء الحلقة في حيز الآخر، وخروج الآخر عنه، وهذا لا ينافي التقدم في العقل باعتبار العلية، وأن يكون العدم الحادث أثراً للفاعل، ولو سلم فليكن بمعنى أنه لا يفعله لا بمعنى أنه يفعل عدمه) .
And the Muḥaqqiqūn [affirm it] for two reasons: First: If it were enduring, then the subsistence of an accident in an accident would be necessary, and that is impossible; it is replied by denying both premises. Second: If it endured, its cessation would be impossible; for if cessation were possible, then either (a) by itself, then its existence would be impossible, (b) by the removal of a condition, then an infinite regress would follow, (c) by the occurrence of a contrary, then there would be a vicious circle because the locus's being qualified by one contrary is conditioned by the negation of the other, and the removal of the enduring by the arriving contrary is not more appropriate – rather the opposite, because prevention is easier than removal – or (d) by an agent, then it would require an effect, and mere negation does not suffice. And it is replied first by counterexample (naqḍ) with the body; and it may be repelled by saying that it ceases by God creating in it the accident of annihilation, or by His not creating an accident that is the condition of endurance – but an accident is not fit to be a locus for an accident. Second, by inversion (qalb): if it did not endure, then its annihilation would be either by itself or by another. Third, by solution (ḥall): it is possible that its essence necessitates non-existence in some states; or that it is conditioned by accidents that are renewed in alternation until it reaches what has no substitute, then it ceases; or that the occurrence of the contrary and the negation of the other [occur] together, like the entry of each of the links of a ring into the space of the other and the exit of the other from it – and this does not contradict priority in reason by virtue of causality; or that the originated non-existence is an effect of the agent; and even if conceded, let it be in the sense that He does not do it, not in the sense that He does its non-existence.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage contains the two main proofs of the Muḥaqqiqūn (the verifying scholars) for the impossibility of an accident enduring for two moments, followed by responses to three objections. The first proof argues that if an accident endured, it would necessitate an accident subsisting in another accident (since endurance itself would be an accident inhering in the first accident), which is impossible. The response to this is to deny either that endurance is an accident or that it inheres. The second proof is more elaborate: if an accident endured, it would be impossible for it to cease to exist. The argument considers all possible ways cessation could occur: (a) self-annihilation (impossible because existence would then be impossible), (b) removal of a condition (leading to infinite regress of conditions), (c) arrival of a contrary (leading to a vicious circle because the locus's qualification by one contrary depends on the absence of the other, and removing the enduring by the arriving contrary is no more necessary than the reverse), (d) by an external agent (which would require an effect, but nonexistence is not an effect). The passage then addresses three objections. First objection (naqḍ): the body itself endures – but the reply is that God either creates an accident of annihilation in it or does not create the accident of endurance, and an accident cannot be a locus for another accident. Second objection (qalb): if the accident does not endure, its annihilation would be either by itself or by another – thus mirroring the argument. Third objection (ḥall): various solutions are proposed, such as the accident's own nature causing non-existence in some states, or its endurance being conditioned by a series of renewing accidents until one has no substitute, or that the contrary's arrival and the other's absence occur simultaneously like interlocking rings, which does not conflict with causal priority, or that non-existence is directly produced by the agent, understood as the agent not creating the accident rather than creating non-existence. This detailed dialectic shows the sophistication of kalām argumentation.

Key Terms
زوال
ضد
دور
تسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that cessation by a contrary is straightforward.
Correction: The argument shows it leads to a vicious circle because the locus's qualification by one contrary depends on the negation of the other.

Checkpoint
According to the second proof, which mode of cessation leads to an infinite regress?
A) By itself
B) By removal of a condition
C) By arrival of a contrary
D) By an external agent

Key Takeaway
The second proof systematically eliminates all possible ways an accident could cease, showing that endurance is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The proof argues that prevention (دفع) is easier than removal (رفع). In your own spiritual life, where are you relying on removal of sins after they occur rather than preventing them from taking root? What is one specific step you can take today to prevent, rather than remove, a bad habit?
?
If every accident (including your health, wealth, and life itself) is constantly renewed by Allah and cannot endure, how does that certainty affect your attachment to these things? What is one worldly thing you hold so tightly that this proof should make you loosen your grip on it?
?
The proof eliminates every possible way an accident could cease except by Allah's will. When you face a loss or change, do you truly see it as coming from Allah? How does this logical demonstration help you accept His decree with more conviction than mere emotional resignation?

Objections and Responses: Naqd, Qalb, and Hall
Three objections and their responses

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three dialectical strategies (naqd, qalb, hall) used to object to the Muhaqqiqun's proofs and how they are countered.

Core Idea
The Muhaqqiqun's proofs are tested by naqd, qalb, and hall, showing the sophistication of kalām.

After presenting the two proofs, the text records three objections raised against the second proof. Each uses a distinct argumentative technique: naqd (counterexample), qalb (inversion), and hall (solution). The Muhaqqiqun then provide responses, further refining the argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the three objections
Read the Arabic and translation, identifying the three parts: naqd, qalb, hall.
Notice the Arabic keywords: النقض, القلب, الحل.
2
Understand naqd (counterexample)
The objection uses the body as a counterexample: it endures. The response explains that God either creates an accident of annihilation or withholds the condition of endurance.
The response also notes that an accident cannot be a locus for another accident.
3
Understand qalb (inversion)
The objection inverts the argument: if an accident does not endure, its annihilation is either by itself or by another—just like the original proof. This challenges the proof's asymmetry.
The text does not provide a separate response to qalb; it is mentioned succinctly.
4
Understand hall (solution)
The third objection offers multiple solutions: essential non-existence, conditional renewal, simultaneous occurrence, and agent-caused non-existence. The response qualifies the last: the agent does not create non-existence but rather does not create the accident.
This shows the depth of possible explanations within the kalām framework.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

?

?

First Proof of Ahl al-Tahqiq and Its Rebuttal
Taftazani presents and rebuts the first argument of ahl al-tahqiq.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first argument of ahl al-tahqiq against the persistence of accidents and how Taftazani rebuts it by denying the implication and the proof of impossibility.

Core Idea
The persistence of an accident is not a new accident; it is just its continued existence.

Ahl al-tahqiq argue that if an accident persisted, its persistence would be a separate accident subsisting in it, which they consider impossible. Taftazani counters this by explaining that persistence is merely the continuation of existence, not a new attribute, and that the impossibility of an accident in an accident is not proven. This debate illustrates the dialectical method in kalām.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the passage once carefully. Notice that it begins with the proof (احتج) and then the rebuttal (ورد).
The Arabic is concise; take your time with the translation.
2
Identify the two parts: proof and rebuttal
The passage has a clear structure: first the proof of ahl al-tahqiq (if persistence then accident-in-accident, which is impossible), then the rebuttal by Taftazani (two denials). Find these parts.
The rebuttal starts with 'ورد' (and it was rebutted).
3
Understand the key rebuttal points
Taftazani denies two things: (1) that persistence is an accident subsisting in the persistent—he says it's just continuation of existence; (2) that the impossibility of accident-in-accident is proven—he says the proof is not established.
Focus on the phrase 'منع الملازمة' (denying the implication) and 'منع انتفاء اللازم' (denying the denial of the consequent).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتج أهل التحقيق بوجهين : الأول: أنه لو كان باقياً لكان بقاؤه عرضاً قائماً به ضرورة كونه وصفاً له، واللازم باطل لاستحالة قيام العرض بالعرض، ورد بمنع الملازمة فإن البقاء عبارة عن استمرار الوجود وانتسابه إلى الزمان الثاني والثالث، وليس عرضاً قائماً بالباقي، ومنع انتفاء اللازم، إذ لا يتم البرهان على امتناع قيام العرض بالعرض.
And the people of verification (ahl al-taḥqīq) argued with two proofs. The first: If it were persistent, then its persistence would be an accident subsisting in it, necessarily because it is a description of it, and the consequent is false because of the impossibility of an accident subsisting in an accident. And it was rebutted by denying the implication: persistence is an expression of the continuation of existence and its attribution to the second and third moment, and it is not an accident subsisting in the persistent; and by denying the denial of the consequent, for the proof of the impossibility of an accident subsisting in an accident is not established.
Source pages p. 162

Commentary
This passage is a proof-and-rebuttal pair. The ahl al-taḥqīq (people of verification) present their first argument: if an accident were to persist, then its persistence would be a description of the accident, so it would be an accident (the persistence) subsisting in another accident (the original accident). But they claim it is impossible for an accident to subsist in another accident, so the persistence of accidents is impossible. The author (Taftazani) then rebuts this by denying two points: first, the implication that persistence is an accident; rather, persistence is just the continuation of existence across moments, not a separate attribute. Second, even if it were an accident, the impossibility of an accident subsisting in an accident is not proven by a sound demonstration. Thus the argument fails. This shows the dialectical method of kalām where each side presents arguments and objections.

Key Terms
أهل التحقيق (ahl al-taḥqīq)
بقاء (persistence)
عرض (accident)
قيام العرض بالعرض (subsistence of accident in accident)
ملازمة (implication)
برهان (demonstration)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Persistence is an additional attribute (accident) that inheres in the accident.
Correction: Persistence is merely the continued existence of the accident itself, not a separate accident.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of rebuttal against the first proof of ahl al-taḥqīq?
A) Persistence is not an accident subsisting in the accident, and the impossibility of accident in accident is unproven.
B) The accident cannot persist because its existence is renewed each moment.
C) The notion of persistence is self-contradictory.
D) The accident's persistence is caused by God directly.

Key Takeaway
The first argument of ahl al-tahqiq fails because it assumes persistence is a separate accident and the impossibility of accident-in-accident is unproven.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear a compelling argument about your faith, do you accept its logical steps without questioning the underlying assumptions? Taftazani challenges the very implication that seems obvious. What is one belief you hold that you have never examined the 'obvious' link between premise and conclusion? Will you deliberately scrutinize it this week?
?
The author denies that the impossibility of an accident subsisting in an accident is proven. Are there principles you treat as 'proven' in your spiritual life—like 'this sin is too big to forgive' or 'I can never change'—that actually have no solid foundation? How will you test one such assumption against revelation and reason starting today?
?
Persistence is not a new accident but the continuation of the same existence. Do you view your ongoing struggles as separate new failures, or as a continuation of a deeper pattern? What is the root issue you keep avoiding, and how will you address it at its source rather than fighting each occurrence?

First Proof Against Persistence of Accidents
Presents first proof against persistence of accidents and its rebuttal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first rational proof against the persistence of accidents and its rebuttal, highlighting the key notion of entailment.

Core Idea
Persistence is not an accident—it is a continuation of existence.

This lesson presents the first of two proofs from the people of verification against the doctrine that accidents can persist. The argument is that if an accident persisted, its persistence would itself be an accident subsisting in it, which is impossible. However, the rebuttal exposes a flaw in the entailment, showing that persistence is merely the continuation of existence, not a separate accident.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation to grasp the flow.
Notice the tight logical structure: if-then, consequent, rebuttal.
2
Identify the proof's structure
Find the premise (persistence implies accident), the entailment, and the conclusion of impossibility.
3
Examine the rebuttal's two points
The rebuttal denies the entailment (persistence is not an accident) and denies the impossibility of accident-in-accident is proven.
The proof fails on both counts.
4
Learn the key terms
Focus on بقاء, عرض, قيام العرض بالعرض, and ملازمة. Their precise meanings are crucial.
These terms recur in kalam.
5
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to confirm you've grasped the main objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتج أهل التحقيق بوجهين : الأول: أنه لو كان باقياً لكان بقاؤه عرضاً قائماً به ضرورة كونه وصفاً له، واللازم باطل لاستحالة قيام العرض بالعرض، ورد بمنع الملازمة فإن البقاء عبارة عن استمرار الوجود وانتسابه إلى الزمان الثاني والثالث، وليس عرضاً قائماً بالباقي، ومنع انتفاء اللازم، إذ لا يتم البرهان على امتناع قيام العرض بالعرض.
And the people of verification argued with two proofs: First: if it were persistent, its persistence would be an accident subsisting in it, necessarily because it is a description of it. And the consequent is false because of the impossibility of an accident subsisting in an accident. And it was rebutted by denying the entailment: for persistence is an expression of the continuation of existence and its relation to the second and third time, and it is not an accident subsisting in the persistent. And by denying the falsity of the consequent, since the proof is not complete for the impossibility of an accident subsisting in an accident.
Source pages p. 162

Commentary
This passage presents the first of two proofs from the people of verification (ahl al-tahqiq) against the persistence of accidents. The proof argues that if an accident persisted, its persistence would itself be an accident subsisting in it, which is impossible because an accident cannot subsist in an accident. The rebuttal denies the entailment: persistence is not an accident but the continuation of existence, so it does not subsist as an accident. Additionally, the impossibility of an accident subsisting in an accident is not proven, so the proof fails.

Key Terms
بقاء
عرض
قيام العرض بالعرض
ملازمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Persistence is an accident that subsists in the original accident.
Correction: Persistence is not an accident; it is the continuation of existence across time, not an independent attribute subsisting in the accident.

Checkpoint
What is the main objection to the first proof?
A) The premise that an accident persists is false.
B) The persistence is not an accident subsisting in the accident.
C) The accident cannot have any attributes.
D) The proof is circular.

Key Takeaway
The first proof against persistence of accidents fails because persistence is not a separate accident but the continuity of existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The rebuttal denies the entailment and questions the proof. When you hold a belief, do you examine its logical foundations, or do you defend it emotionally? This week, identify one conviction you hold and test its logical structure like the scholars did.
?
Persistence here is defined as 'continuation of existence' rather than a separate accident. In your spiritual life, do you view your worship as discrete acts or as a continuous state of being with Allah? How would shifting to the latter perspective change your daily practice?
?
The first proof was initially presented as convincing but was later rebutted. Think of a time you were certain of something and later discovered you were wrong. Are you humble enough to welcome rebuttals? Ask Allah to grant you the humility to accept truth from any source.

The Three Universal Properties of Quantity
Enumeration of the three universal properties of quantity with explanation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three universal properties of quantity (acceptance of division, equality/inequality, and containing a counter) and how the second and third depend on the first.

Core Idea
Quantity is defined by its ability to be divided, which enables comparison and counting.

This passage from Sharh al-Maqasid explains the essential properties of quantity (al-kamm). Quantity is a fundamental category of existence, and understanding its properties is crucial for theological discussions about the nature of bodies and accidents. The author enumerates three properties and clarifies their interrelationship, including a notable disagreement with Fakhr al-Din al-Razi.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Notice that it enumerates three properties in a structured way.
The passage is a direct enumeration with explanations embedded.
2
Identify the three properties
Find the three properties: acceptance of division, acceptance of equality/inequality, and containing a counter. Note how each is defined.
The first property is further divided into imaginary and actual division.
3
Understand the dependency relation
The author states that the second and third properties are branches of the first. Also consider al-Imam al-Razi's opposing view that the second is primary.
This dependency is a key point of the passage.
4
Reflect on examples
The passage gives examples for the third property (counting discrete and continuous quantities). Think of your own examples to solidify understanding.
Discrete quantities are counted actually, continuous potentially.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الأول في الأحكام الكلية (قال : المبحث الأول في أحكامه الكلية منها، أي من خواصه قبول القسمة لذاته، وهما بأن يفرض فيه شيء غير شيء وبه عرفه الجمهور، أو فعلاً بأن ينفك ومنها قبول المساواة واللامساواة، وهي فرع الأولى، وعند الإمام بالعكس، ومنها الاشتمال على العاد. وزعم الإمام أنه الصالح للتعريف إذ المساواة اتفاق في الكم فيدور قبول القسمة مختص بالمتصل فلا ينعكس، وكأنه أخذ القبول منافياً للحصول، ولذا قال إلا إذا أخذ القبول باشتراك الاسم، وأما حمله على أنه أحد القسمة الانفكاكية فغلط بامتناعها في المقدار . بتصريحه والمنفصل من الكم ما لا يكون لأجزائه حد مشترك وهو العدد لا غير، إذ قبول الانقسام للقبول عرضي . للأحكام الكلية، وللزمان وللمكان فمن الأحكام الكلية بيان خواصها وهي ثلاث : الأولى : قبول القسمة لذاته حتى إن غيره من الأجسام والأعراض إنما تقبل القسمة بواسطته والقسمة تطلق على الوهمية وذلك بأن يفرض فيه شيء من غير شيء، وعلى الفعلية بأن ينفصل وينقطع بالفعل، أو يحدث له هويتان بعد أن كانت هوية واحدة. والجمهور عرفوا الكم بقبول القسمة فقالوا : هو عرض يقبل القسمة لذاته والمراد الوهمية لما سيجيء الثانية : قبول المساواة أو اللامساواة بمعنى أنه إذا نسب إلى كم آخر فإما أن يكون مساوياً له، أو أزيد أو أنقص، وهذه الخاصة فرع الأولى لأنه لما اشتمل على أجزاء وهمية أو فعلية لزم عند نسبته إلى كم آخر أن يكون عدد أجزائهما على التساوي، أو على التفاوت. وقال الإمام : إن قبول الانقسام إنما يلزم الكم بسبب الخاصة الأولى، لأنه لما كانت الأجسام يتقدر بعضها بالبعض من غير لزوم المساواة وجب أن يكون فيها ما يقبل المساواة، أو اللامساواة لذاته وهو المقدار، ولا يتصور اللامساواة إلا بأن يشتمل أحدهما على مثل الآخر مع زيادة، فلزم أن يقبل القسمة، أي فرض شيء غير شيء. الثالثة : اشتماله على أمر يعده أي يعينه بالإسقاط عنه مراراً إما بالفعل كما في الكم المنفصل، فإن الأربعة تعد بالواحد أربع مرات وإما بالقوة كما في المتصل، فإن السنة تعد بالشهور، والشهور بالأيام، واليوم بالساعات
The First Section on Universal Judgments. (He said: The first section on its universal judgments, among them, i.e., from its properties: acceptance of division by itself, and it is either by assuming in it something other than something – and by that the majority defined it – or actually by its separation. And among them: acceptance of equality and inequality, and it is a branch of the first, and according to al-Imam it is the opposite. And among them: containing the counter. Al-Imam claimed that it is fit for definition, because equality is agreement in quantity, then acceptance of division is specific to the continuous, so it does not reflect. And it is as if he took acceptance as contrary to occurrence, so he said 'unless acceptance is taken under homonymy.' As for its being one of the separative division, it is a mistake because of its impossibility in magnitude. By his explicit statement: The discrete quantity is that whose parts have no common boundary, and it is number only, because acceptance of division is accidental for acceptance? For universal judgments, for time and for place. So from the universal judgments is the explanation of its properties, and they are three: The first: acceptance of division by itself, so that even other bodies and accidents only accept division through it. Division is applied to imaginary division by assuming in it something from not-something, and to actual division by its separating and cutting actually, or by its having two identities after having been one identity. The majority defined quantity by acceptance of division, so they said: It is an accident that accepts division by itself, and the intended meaning is imaginary division as will come. The second: acceptance of equality or inequality, meaning that when it is related to another quantity, it is either equal to it, or more or less. And this property is a branch of the first, because since it contains imaginary or actual parts, when related to another quantity, the number of its parts must be either equal or differing. Al-Imam said: Acceptance of division follows quantity only because of the first property, because since bodies are measured by each other without necessitating equality, there must be in them that which accepts equality or inequality by itself, and that is magnitude. And inequality is only conceivable by one of them containing the like of the other with an increase, so it follows that it accepts division, i.e., assuming something other than something. The third: its containing something that counts it, i.e., specifies it by dropping from it repeatedly, either actually as in discrete quantity, for four is counted by one four times, or potentially as in continuous quantity, for the year is counted by months, months by days, and the day by hours.
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the three universal properties (khawas) of quantity (al-kamm). It begins by stating that among the properties of quantity are: (1) acceptance of division by itself, (2) acceptance of equality and inequality, and (3) containing a 'counter' (al-`add) that allows counting. The author notes a disagreement with al-Imam (Fakhr al-Din al-Razi) regarding the priority of these properties: the majority hold that acceptance of division is primary and equality/inequality is a branch, while al-Imam reverses this order, arguing that equality/inequality is primary and division is derived. The passage then elaborates on each property. The first property (acceptance of division) is explained as twofold: imaginary division (al-qisma al-wahmiyya) – assuming a part within the quantity without actual separation – and actual division (al-fi`liyya) – physical separation into distinct parts. The majority define quantity as an accident that accepts division by itself, but they mean imaginary division. The second property (acceptance of equality or inequality) is a branch of the first because having parts (real or imaginary) implies comparability. Al-Imam's argument for his ordering is given: since bodies are measured without necessarily being equal, there must be something that inherently accepts equality/inequality, which is magnitude; but inequality requires one to include the other plus an excess, which implies divisibility. The third property (containing a counter) is explained with examples: discrete quantities (like number 4 counted by 1) are counted actually, while continuous quantities (like the year counted by months) are counted potentially. The discussion is technical and reflects the integration of philosophical concepts from Aristotelian categories into kalam.

Key Terms
الكَمّ (al-kamm, quantity)
القِسْمَة (al-qisma, division)
المُسَاوَاة (al-musāwāh, equality)
اللامُسَاوَاة (al-lā-musāwāh, inequality)
العَادّ (al-`ādd, counter)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the three properties are independent of each other.
Correction: The author explicitly states that the second and third properties are branches of the first (acceptance of division). Equality/inequality and counting both presuppose divisibility.

Checkpoint
According to the majority, which property of quantity is primary and serves as the basis for the others?
A) Acceptance of equality and inequality
B) Acceptance of division by itself
C) Containing a counter
D) Being an accident

Key Takeaway
Quantity is fundamentally defined by its divisibility, from which the properties of comparability and countability derive.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage teaches that quantity inherently accepts division, both imaginary and real. Reflect on a time you divided your attention or resources. Was that division imaginary or real? How did it affect your ability to measure your priorities? Today, consider one area where you need to 'divide' time for Allah and commit to a real, not just imaginary, act of worship.
?
The second property shows that all quantities are comparable—they are either equal, greater, or lesser. In your own life, you constantly compare yourself to others. This passage reminds you that comparison only makes sense if there is a common measure. What measure do you use to judge your worth? Is it based on the divine scale of taqwa (which cannot be quantified) or on worldly quantities? Ask Allah to help you value what cannot be measured.

Two Arguments Against the Existence of Time and Their Rebuttals
Two arguments against time's existence with rebuttals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two arguments theologians used to deny the existence of time and the responses given to them.

Core Idea
Time's existence is debated; essential priority and present alternative existence provide rebuttals.

This passage introduces the theologians' stance that time does not exist as a real entity. Two proofs are presented: the first leads to infinite regress, and the second argues that time cannot exist in the present without being indivisible. Each proof is followed by a rebuttal that challenges the underlying assumptions. As you read, notice how the arguments rely on logical priority and the nature of past, present, and future.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
The text ends with an incomplete objection; focus on the two arguments and their rebuttals.
2
Identify the two arguments
The first argument uses infinite regress (tasalsul). The second uses the tripartite division of time (past, future, present). Spot each.
3
Understand each rebuttal
The first rebuttal asserts priority is by essence, not time. The second rebuttal says past and future exist in the present and alternatively.
Contrast with motion: motion exists continuously, but time does not.
4
Reflect on the incomplete objection
The text ends with 'we do not grant' (la nusallim). Consider what the objector might be denying and how the rebuttal might continue.
No need to invent; just note the interruption.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الثاني في الزمان قال: أنكره المتكلمون لوجوده: الأول: أنه لو وجد لتقدم بعض أجزائه بالضرورة وليس إلا بالزمان فيتسلسل. ورد بأنه بالذات فإن تقدم الأمس على اليوم لا يفتقر إلى عارض. الثاني: الزمان إما ماض أو مستقبل ولا وجود لهما أو حاضر ولو وجد لكان غير منقسم ضرورة امتناع اجتماع أجزاء الزمان في الوجود، وحينئذ يلزم تناهي الآنات المستلزم لوجود الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ، وهذا بخلاف الحركة، فإن الموجود منها هو الحصول في الوسط وهو مستمر من المبدأ إلى المنتهي ولا يصح في الزمان للقطع بأن زمان الطوفان لا يوجد الآن، ورد أنه لا وجود لهما مطلقاً، بل في الحال وعلى التبادل فإن قيل: بأنا لا نسلم
The Second Investigation on Time. He said: The theologians denied its existence for two reasons: First, if it existed, then some of its parts would necessarily be prior to others, and there is no priority except by time, leading to infinite regress (tasalsul). It was rebutted: priority is by essence, for the priority of yesterday over today does not require an accident. Second, time is either past or future, both of which have no existence, or present, and if it existed, it would be indivisible necessarily because it is impossible for parts of time to coexist. Then it would necessitate the finitude of instants (ānāt), which entails the existence of the indivisible part. This is unlike motion, for the existent part of motion is its occurrence in the middle, which is continuous from beginning to end. But this does not hold for time, because it is certain that the time of the Flood does not exist now. It was rebutted: they [the two] do not exist absolutely, but rather in the present and alternatively. Then it was said: we do not grant.
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage is the beginning of a new investigation into the nature of time. It presents the position of the theologians (al-mutakallimūn) who denied the existence of time as a real entity. Two arguments are given. First, if time existed, its parts would be prior to one another, and such priority itself requires time, leading to an infinite regress (tasalsul). The rebuttal states that priority can be essential (bi al-dhāt), not requiring a temporal accident; for example, yesterday is prior to today by essence, not by a third time. Second, time is either past, future (both non-existent), or present. If present, it would be indivisible (since parts of time cannot coexist). But then the instants would be finite, requiring the existence of an indivisible part (al-juz’ alladhī lā yatajazza’), which the theologians affirm. The argument also distinguishes time from motion: motion exists continuously as the mobile object is in the middle point, but time does not exist continuously because past times (e.g., the Flood) do not exist now. The rebuttal says that past and future do not exist absolutely, but rather in the present and alternatively (i.e., in a successive manner). The passage then begins an objection 'we do not grant' which is cut off.

Key Terms
زمان
تسلسل
آن
الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ
حركة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The first argument proves time's non-existence because priority necessarily requires time.
Correction: Priority can be essential, not temporal, as in the priority of yesterday over today.

Checkpoint
How does the rebuttal respond to the first argument that time would lead to infinite regress?
A) By denying that parts of time can be prior.
B) By asserting that priority is by essence, not by time.
C) By accepting infinite regress as possible.
D) By arguing that time is not composed of parts.

Key Takeaway
The theologians' denial of time is countered by acknowledging essential priority and the alternative existence of past and future in the present.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about your own mortality, do you feel time slipping away as real moments that are gone forever? How does this lesson's claim that past and future exist 'alternatively in the present' challenge your anxiety about lost time?
?
The theologians argued that time cannot be real because the present is indivisible. Yet you experience each moment as a 'now.' If the present is just an indivisible instant, what does that mean for your experience of change and the choices you make in that fleeting moment?
?
Priority by essence versus priority by accident: in your daily life, do you tend to treat your priorities as if they are essential (like yesterday's priority over today) or accidental (depending on circumstances)? How can you anchor your life on what is truly essential, as the rebuttal suggests?

The First Proof: The Dilemma of the Dimension
Sanusi presents a dilemma against the dimension theory.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first proof against the dimension theory of place, which shows that it leads to either infinite regress or impossibility.

Core Idea
Place cannot be a dimension because it would require another place, leading to infinite regress.

Sanusi begins his argument for the surface theory by presenting a dilemma: if place were a dimension, that dimension would either be movable or not. Each alternative leads to an absurdity. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind the first horn of the dilemma.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two alternatives
Read the passage and identify the two alternatives for the dimension: whether it accepts motion or not.
Notice that the passage first presents the conclusion that place is the surface, then gives the first proof.
2
Understand the first alternative
If the dimension accepts motion, it would need a place itself, leading to an infinite regress of places. The dimension would then be either inside the bodies or outside them, both problematic.
3
Understand the second alternative
If the dimension does not accept motion, then the body cannot accept it because the body already has its own necessary dimension.
4
Connect to the surface theory
Realize that these absurdities do not arise if place is a surface, which is the intended conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: المقام الأول: ان المكان هو السطح أو البعد. وحجة السطح بوجوه. الأول: أنه موجود يقبل التفاوت والإشارة والانتقال منه وإليه. والبعد الموجود إن قبل الحركة كان له مكان وتسلسل على أن جميع الأمكنة يفتقر إلى مكان فيكون داخلاً فيها بكونه أحدها خارجاً منها بكونه ظرفاً لها وإن لم يقبل لم يقبلها الجسم لما فيه من البعد اللازم. الثاني: إن تمكن الجسم حينئذ يستلزم نفوذ بعده في البعد المكاني فيكون فيه بعدان ويجتمع المثلان ويرتفع الآنات عن وحدة هذه، وعن تساوي أصل المتمكن والمكان.
He said: The first station: that place is the surface or the dimension. And the proof for the surface [is] by aspects. The first: that it is existent, capable of variation, reference, and movement from and to it. And the existing dimension: if it accepts motion, it would have a place and an infinite regress, since all places need a place, thus it would be inside them as one of them or outside them as a container for them; and if it does not accept motion, the body would not accept it because of the necessary dimension in it. The second: that the body's occupying [a place] at that time entails the penetration of its dimension into the spatial dimension, thus there would be two dimensions in it, and two similars would meet, and the atoms of time would be nullified concerning the unity of this [dimension], and concerning the equality of the basis of the placed and the place.
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage presents the first two proofs for the position that place is the surface (al-sath) rather than a dimension. It begins by stating the first proof: the surface is existent and can vary, be pointed to, and have motion from and to it. If, on the other hand, place were a dimension, then that dimension itself would either accept motion (i.e., be movable) or not. If it accepts motion, then it would require its own place, leading to an infinite regress because every place would need another place. Moreover, that dimension would either be inside the bodies as one of them or outside them as a container, but either case is problematic. If the dimension does not accept motion (i.e., it is fixed), then the body cannot accept it because the body already has its own necessary dimension. The second proof argues that if a body occupies a place by having its dimension penetrate a spatial dimension, then there would be two dimensions in the same location: the body's dimension and the spatial dimension. This would result in two similar things (two dimensions) coexisting in the same substrate, which is impossible. Also, the 'atoms of time' (al-ānāt) would be nullified regarding the unity of this arrangement, and the equality between the placed body and the place would be negated. These arguments are meant to show the absurdity of the dimension theory and to support the surface theory.

Key Terms
المقام الأول
السطح
البعد
التسلسل
الحركة
الظرف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Infinite regress is only a problem for causal chains, not for definitions of place.
Correction: The passage shows that if place itself is a dimension, it requires another place, leading to infinite regress in the definition of place itself.

Checkpoint
What happens if the existing dimension accepts motion, according to the first proof?
A) It becomes a surface.
B) It would need a place for itself, leading to infinite regress.
C) It remains motionless.
D) It turns into a body.

Key Takeaway
The first proof shows that if place were a dimension, either infinite regress or impossibility follows, so place must be a surface.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If this proof shows that even a seemingly simple concept like place requires rigorous reasoning to avoid absurdity, are you equally rigorous in examining the foundations of your own beliefs? Are you content with superficial understanding, or do you push until you see the logical necessity of what you affirm? Commit to studying one controversial topic in aqeedah until you understand its proof from the scholars.
?
When you hear an argument like the infinite regress, do you stop and fully grasp its implications, or do you hurry past it? This lesson demands that you sit with the logic until it becomes your own. Think of a belief you hold without proof—will you set time to verify it from the sources, even if it challenges you?
?
The dimension theory seems plausible at first, but Sanusi shows it leads to absurdity. Do you have any belief that seems plausible but which you have never subjected to logical scrutiny? Your iman cannot rest on comfortable assumptions. What is one point of creed you will re-examine this week?

The Second Proof: The Impossibility of Two Dimensions
Sanusi presents the second proof against the dimension theory.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second proof for the surface theory, which argues that if place were a dimension, the body's penetration into it would entail two dimensions coexisting, which is impossible.

Core Idea
Two dimensions cannot occupy the same place; thus place must be a surface.

The second proof against the dimension theory focuses on the concept of penetration: if a body occupies a place that is a dimension, its own dimension would penetrate the spatial dimension, resulting in two dimensions in one location. This leads to absurd consequences, such as the meeting of two similars and the nullification of temporal atoms.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the argument about penetration.
Notice the key terms: نفوذ (penetration), بعدان (two dimensions), المثلان (two similars), الآنات (atoms of time).
2
Identify the core consequence
If place is a dimension, the body's dimension would penetrate it, resulting in two dimensions in one location.
3
Understand the absurdities
Two dimensions in one location would cause two similars to meet (impossible) and nullify the atoms of time regarding unity and equality between body and place.
4
Connect to the conclusion
Since these consequences are impossible, place cannot be a dimension; therefore it must be a surface.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: المقام الأول: ان المكان هو السطح أو البعد. وحجة السطح بوجوه. الأول: أنه موجود يقبل التفاوت والإشارة والانتقال منه وإليه. والبعد الموجود إن قبل الحركة كان له مكان وتسلسل على أن جميع الأمكنة يفتقر إلى مكان فيكون داخلاً فيها بكونه أحدها خارجاً منها بكونه ظرفاً لها وإن لم يقبل لم يقبلها الجسم لما فيه من البعد اللازم. الثاني: إن تمكن الجسم حينئذ يستلزم نفوذ بعده في البعد المكاني فيكون فيه بعدان ويجتمع المثلان ويرتفع الآنات عن وحدة هذه، وعن تساوي أصل المتمكن والمكان.
He said: The first station: that place is the surface or the dimension. And the proof for the surface [is] by aspects. The first: that it is existent, capable of variation, reference, and movement from and to it. And the existing dimension: if it accepts motion, it would have a place and an infinite regress, since all places need a place, thus it would be inside them as one of them or outside them as a container for them; and if it does not accept motion, the body would not accept it because of the necessary dimension in it. The second: that the body's occupying [a place] at that time entails the penetration of its dimension into the spatial dimension, thus there would be two dimensions in it, and two similars would meet, and the atoms of time would be nullified concerning the unity of this [dimension], and concerning the equality of the basis of the placed and the place.
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage presents the first two proofs for the position that place is the surface (al-sath) rather than a dimension. It begins by stating the first proof: the surface is existent and can vary, be pointed to, and have motion from and to it. If, on the other hand, place were a dimension, then that dimension itself would either accept motion (i.e., be movable) or not. If it accepts motion, then it would require its own place, leading to an infinite regress because every place would need another place. Moreover, that dimension would either be inside the bodies as one of them or outside them as a container, but either case is problematic. If the dimension does not accept motion (i.e., it is fixed), then the body cannot accept it because the body already has its own necessary dimension. The second proof argues that if a body occupies a place by having its dimension penetrate a spatial dimension, then there would be two dimensions in the same location: the body's dimension and the spatial dimension. This would result in two similar things (two dimensions) coexisting in the same substrate, which is impossible. Also, the 'atoms of time' (al-ānāt) would be nullified regarding the unity of this arrangement, and the equality between the placed body and the place would be negated. These arguments are meant to show the absurdity of the dimension theory and to support the surface theory.

Key Terms
نفوذ
بعدان
المثلان
الآنات
المتمكن
المكان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Two dimensions coexist in a body conceptually, so they can coexist in place as well.
Correction: The passage argues that two similar dimensions cannot occupy the same substrate; the body's dimension and the place's dimension would be two distinct but similar dimensions in the same location, which is absurd.

Checkpoint
What does the second proof say would happen if a body's dimension penetrates the spatial dimension?
A) The body would become immaterial.
B) There would be two dimensions in one location.
C) The place would vanish.
D) The body would move infinitely.

Key Takeaway
The second proof shows that the penetration of dimensions leads to impossible consequences, so place cannot be a dimension.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How often do you accept an idea without tracing its logical consequences? This proof shows that a seemingly plausible assumption leads to impossible results. What belief have you held that, if scrutinized, would unravel into absurdity? Will you examine it today?
?
The proof hinges on the impossibility of two similars meeting. In your own life, do you try to hold two contradictory values or commitments? What would it cost you to let go of one in order to achieve unity of purpose?
?
The atoms of time would be nullified if two dimensions coexisted. Your days are made of moments—are they fragmented by conflicting pursuits, or do they flow towards a single aim? What one adjustment can you make this week to unify your time for the sake of your Creator?

Refuting the Imaginary Dimension View of Place
Refuting the imaginary dimension view of place

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why place cannot be an imaginary dimension and how its real properties prove its existence.

Core Idea
Place has real measurable properties; pure nothingness does not.

The debate over the nature of place has long occupied theologians. Some argued that place is merely an imaginary dimension, a mental construct. In this passage, the author presents a decisive refutation: place must be real because it admits equality, sensory reference, and movement — attributes impossible for pure nothingness. Let's examine the logic.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
The passage is part of a larger argument about whether place is a dimension or a surface. Here, the first option (dimension) is being refuted.
Recall that the mutakallimun often reduced place to imaginary dimensions.
2
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how the author presents the two possibilities for dimension and immediately dismisses the imaginary one.
3
Identify the proof for place's existence
The author lists several properties: equality, inequality, sensory reference, movement of bodies, size, etc. These demonstrate that place is not a mere concept.
Each property is something that cannot apply to nothingness.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Since pure nothingness cannot possess these attributes, place must be a real existent. This refutes the imaginary dimension view.
This argument is foundational for later discussions on space and place.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: احتج القائلون بكون المكان هو السطح، بأنه لا يعقل منه إلا البعد أو السطح، والأول باطل لوجوه: الأول: أنه لو كان هو البعد، فإما أن يكون متوهماً مفروضاً، على ما هو رأي المتكلمين، وهو باطل. لأن المكان موجود ضرورة واستدلالاً بأنه يقبل التساوي والتفاوت وحيث يقال: مكان هذا مساو لمكان ذاك، أو زائد عليه، أو ناقص عنه، نصف له أو ثلث أو ربع، أو غير ذلك، وبأنه يقبل الإشارة الحسية، وانتقال الجسم منه وإليه، حيث يقال: انتقل الجسم من هذا المكان إلى ذلك، والاتصاف بالصغر والكبر والطول والقصر، والقرب والبعد والاتصال والانفصال إلى غير ذلك، ولا شيء من العدم المحض والنفي الصرف كذلك.
He said: Those who hold that place is the surface argued that nothing is conceivable of it except the dimension or the surface, and the first is invalid for several reasons: The first: If it were the dimension, then it is either an imaginary, hypothetical [dimension], as is the opinion of the mutakallimun, and it is invalid. Because place is existent necessarily and by proof, in that it admits equality and difference, such that it is said: the place of this is equal to the place of that, or greater than it, or less than it, half of it, or a third, or a quarter, or otherwise; and in that it admits sense reference, and the movement of the body from it and to it, such that it is said: the body moved from this place to that; and the attribute of smallness and bigness, length and shortness, nearness and farness, connection and disconnection, etc. And nothing of pure nothingness and absolute negation is so.
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage presents the first argument against the view that place is a dimension (al-bu'd). The author begins by stating that those who claim place is the surface (السطح) argue that only the dimension or the surface are conceivable as place. The first option (that place is the dimension) is then refuted through three arguments (الوجوه). The first of these three is that the dimension could be either imaginary/hypothetical (متوهماً مفروضاً) or actual/existent (متحققاً موجوداً). The author immediately dismisses the imaginary possibility as invalid. The reasoning is that place must be a real existent (موجود) both necessarily and by inference. The proof is that place admits properties like equality, inequality, being half or double, etc. It also admits sensory reference (الإشارة الحسية) and the movement of bodies from one place to another. It can be described as small, large, long, short, near, far, connected, disconnected. None of these properties apply to pure nothingness or absolute negation. Therefore, the imaginary dimension cannot be place. The argument follows the standard kalam method of basing refutation on the observable properties of place.

السطح
المكان
متوهماً مفروضاً
متحققاً موجوداً
الإشارة الحسية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Place is merely an imaginary construct with no real existence.
Correction: The author shows that place has real properties like equality, sensory reference, and movement, which cannot belong to pure nothingness.

Checkpoint
Which of the following does the author use to argue that place is not an imaginary dimension?
A) Place is mentioned in sacred texts.
B) Place admits sensory reference and body movement.
C) The mutakallimun unanimously reject imaginary dimensions.
D) Imaginary dimensions are physically impossible.

Key Takeaway
Place must be a real existent because it has measurable and observable properties that pure nothingness lacks.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You now know that place is a real existent with measurable properties, not a figment of imagination. When you stand in prayer, do you truly perceive that space as a real creation of Allah, or do you treat it as just an abstract location? How will you change your awareness of the physical spaces you inhabit, knowing they have a reality independent of your mind?
?
The argument proves that place cannot be pure nothingness because it has properties like size and nearness. If place were just an idea, it could be changed by your thoughts—but it isn't. Does this truth humble you? Will you now reflect more deeply on the physical world around you as a sign of Allah's creative power, rather than taking it for granted?
?
The proof shows that place admits 'sense reference'—we can point to it. When you point to the Kaaba in your prayers, do you feel the reality of that sacred place as more than a direction? How can you deepen your connection to the real, physical places of worship in your life, starting today?

Second Argument: Overlapping Dimensions if Place is a Dimension
Sanusi presents the second argument against place as dimension, using the impossibility of dimension overlap.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second argument against the view that place is a dimension, which demonstrates that it leads to the absurdity of overlapping dimensions.

Core Idea
If place is a dimension, then placement requires dimension overlap, which is impossible.

The author continues to refute the idea that place is a dimension. He presents a powerful argument: if place is an existent dimension, then when a body is placed in it, two dimensions must interpenetrate. This leads to several clear absurdities. Let's examine the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the overall argument. Notice the structure: a conditional statement followed by a list of reasons why the consequence is invalid.
Pay attention to the key terms like 'tadakhul' and 'tamakkun'.
2
Identify the main claim
The argument states that if place is a dimension, then the settling of a body in that place necessitates the overlapping (tadakhul) of two dimensions: the dimension of the body and the dimension of the place.
3
Examine the three reasons against overlapping
The passage gives three reasons: (1) intuitively, a vessel filled with water has only one dimension; (2) it would entail the conjunction of two identical things in one substrate; (3) it would contradict even the most evident axioms, e.g., a dimension of one cubit could become two cubits by interpenetration.
Each reason is independent but collectively show the absurdity.
4
Understand the example of a cubit
The third reason uses an example: if a place is one cubit in dimension, overlapping would allow it to be two cubits, or the body's magnitude could differ from the place's, which is impossible.
5
Conclude the argument
All these consequences are rejected by consensus, so the premise (that place is a dimension) must be false. The argument effectively refutes the dimension theory.
This argument relies on self-evident truths (badihiyyat).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني: أن المكان لو كان هو البعد وهو موجود ضرورة واستدلالاً لزم من تمكن الجسم في المكان تداخل البعدين أي نفوذ البعد القائم به، في البعد الذي هو مكانه، لأن هذا معنى التمكن عندهم، واللازم باطل. للقطع بأنه ليس في الإناء المملوء من الماء إلا بعد واحد ولأنه يستلزم اجتماع المثلين، أعني البعدين في محل واحد هو المتمكن، ولأنه يستلزم ارتفاع الآنات عن البديهيات، لكون هذا البعد ذراعاً واحداً مثلا لجواز أن يكون ذراعين أو أكثر تداخلاً، وككون المتمكن بمكانه في المقدار لجواز أن يكون بعد أحدهما أزيد من الآخر، حصل من تداخلهما هذا المقدار المشاهد، وكل ذلك منتف بالاتفاق.
The second [argument]: That if place is the dimension—and it is existent necessarily and by proof—then the settling (tamakkun) of the body in place necessitates the overlapping (tadakhul) of the two dimensions, i.e., the penetration of the dimension subsisting in it into the dimension that is its place, because this is the meaning of settling (tamakkun) according to them. And the necessary consequence is invalid. [This is] because it is certain that there is not in a vessel filled with water except one dimension; and because it entails the conjunction of the two likes (i.e., two dimensions) in one substrate which is the settled body; and because it entails the removal of even the most evident axioms, given that this dimension is one cubit, for example, it would be possible that it be two cubits or more by interpenetration, and like the settled being with its place in magnitude, for it would be possible that the dimension of one is greater than the other, and from their interpenetration this observed magnitude would result—all of which is universally rejected.
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument (الوجه الثاني) against the view that place is the dimension (البعد). The argument assumes for objection that place is indeed an existent dimension. The author then shows that if a body settles (يتمكن) in such a place, this settling implies the overlapping (تداخل) of two dimensions: the dimension of the body (or the dimension inhering in the body) penetrating into the dimension that is its place. According to those who hold this view, settling means exactly that. This consequence (اللازم) is invalid for several reasons: First, it is intuitively certain that in a vessel filled with water there is only one dimension—not two. Second, it would entail the conjunction of two identical things (اجتماع المثلين), i.e., two dimensions, in one substrate (محل واحد), which is the body that is settled. Third, it would lead to absurdities that contradict even the most obvious axioms (أظهر البديهيات). For example, if a place is one cubit in dimension, then by overlapping it would be possible for it to be two cubits or more, or the settled body's magnitude could vary, which is absurd. All these consequences are rejected by consensus (بالاتفاق). Thus, the view that place is a dimension fails.

Key Terms
التداخل
التمكن
اجتماع المثلين
البديهيات
البعد القائم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that overlapping dimensions are possible in immaterial entities, but the author argues that even in physical examples like water in a vessel, only one dimension is observed.
Correction: The argument shows that overlapping dimensions are impossible because they would lead to contradictions in observable reality, even in physical examples.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the second argument against place being a dimension?
A) It leads to an infinite regress of places.
B) It results in overlapping of two dimensions, which is impossible.
C) It denies the existence of place altogether.
D) It confuses place with the body.

Key Takeaway
The second argument shows that the view of place as dimension collapses because it requires impossible dimension interpenetration.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

This argument requires you to reject something that might seem intuitively plausible (place as dimension). Is there a belief in your own life that feels obvious but, upon examination, could be logically flawed? Will you dare to test it?
?
The author uses 'البديهيات' (axioms) as an ultimate checkpoint. What are the non-negotiable axioms of your faith that guide your reasoning? Have you ever allowed a comfortable assumption to override them?
?
If you had to defend the impossibility of two dimensions overlapping, would your conviction rest on sensory observation or logical necessity? Which do you trust more when they conflict, and why?
