The Third Argument and Its Answer: Dimension's Need or Independence from Substrate
Arguing that dimension either needs a substrate or not, and answering all three arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third argument against place being a dimension and how the answer resolves it by distinguishing between inhering and separate dimensions in essence.

Core Idea
Dimensions differ in essence even if they share the universal concept of dimension.

This passage presents the third of three arguments against the view that place is a dimension, followed by a comprehensive answer. The argument forces a dilemma: if dimension depends on a substrate, it cannot be non-material; if it is independent, it cannot inhere in bodies. The answer resolves this by asserting that the dimension in bodies and the separate dimension are essentially different, even though they share the generic term 'absolute dimension'.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Pay attention to the structure: the argument presents a dilemma, then an objection, then a general answer.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two horns of the dilemma
Find the two possibilities: the dimension either needs a substrate (المحل) or is independent (يستغني). Note the consequences for each option.
The first horn leads to impossibility of abstract dimension; the second leads to impossibility of inherence in bodies.
3
Analyze the answer
The answer (وأجيب عن الكل) distinguishes between the dimension inhering in body and the separate dimension in essence (مخالف بالماهية). Notice how it refutes the objection about multiplicity and the conjunction of likes.
The key is that they are essentially different, so their coexistence is not problematic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث: أن البعد في نفسه إما أن يفتقر إلى المحل، فيمتنع تجرده عن المادة، على ما يدعونه في البعد الذي هو المكان، وإما أن يستغنى عنه فلا يحل في المادة، على ما هو شأن البعد القائم بالجسم، لأن معنى حلول العرض في المحل اختصاصه بحيث يفتقر إليه في التقوم، فلا يرد ما قيل: إنه يجوز أن لا يفتقر في نفسه إلى المحل، ويعرض له الحلول فيه. وأجيب عن الكل بأنه يجوز أن يكون البعد القائم بالجسم مخالفاً بالماهية البعد المفارق وإن اشتركا في ذاتي أو عرضى هو مطلق البعد، فلا اختصاصه بقبول الحركة واقتضاء المحل واختصاص البعد المفارق بإمكان النفوذ فيه، ولا يكون اجتماعهما من اجتماع المثلين، على أن ما ذكر من تعدد البعدين في التمكن واجتماع المثلين ليس بمستقيم لأن أحدها في التمكن والآخر فيه المتمكن.
The third: That the dimension in itself either needs a substrate, then its detachment from matter is impossible, as they claim concerning the dimension that is place; or it is independent of it, then it does not inhere in matter, as is the case of the dimension subsisting in the body, because the meaning of the inherence of an accident in a substrate is its specialization such that it needs it for subsistence. So what was said—that it is possible that it does not need a substrate in itself but accidentally comes to inhere in it—is not valid. And it was answered concerning all [the arguments] that it is possible that the dimension subsisting in the body is different in essence (māhiyya) from the separate dimension, even if they share a generic [property] or accident which is absolute dimension. Thus, its special quality of accepting motion and requiring a substrate is not the same; the separate dimension is characterized by the possibility of penetration into it. Nor does the conjunction of the two constitute a conjunction of two likes. Moreover, what was mentioned of the multiplicity of the two dimensions in settling (tamakkun) and the conjunction of two likes is not sound, because one of them is in the state of being settled (fī al-tamakkun) and the other is that in which the settled thing is.
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage presents the third argument (الوجه الثالث) against place being dimension, followed by a general answer (الجواب) that responds to all three arguments. The third argument states that the dimension itself either depends on a substrate (المحل) or is independent of it. If it depends on a substrate, then it cannot be abstracted from matter—contradicting the claim of those who say place is a separate, non-material dimension. If it is independent of a substrate, then it cannot inhere in matter—contradicting the case of the dimension that inheres in bodies (i.e., bodily dimensions). The objection that a dimension could be independent in itself yet accidentally inhere in matter is dismissed because inherence means the accident is specialized and dependent on the substrate for subsistence. The answer (وأجيب عن الكل) reconciles the views: it is possible that the dimension inhering in the body (البعد القائم بالجسم) is essentially different (مخالف بالماهية) from the separate dimension (البعد المفارق), even if they share the common accident of 'absolute dimension' (مطلق البعد). Consequently, the inhering dimension has properties like accepting motion and requiring a substrate, while the separate dimension has the property of allowing penetration. They are not identical, so their conjunction is not a conjunction of two likes. Furthermore, the multiplicity of dimensions in settling (tamakkun) is not sound because one dimension is the state of settling (التمكن) and the other is the thing that is settled (المتمكن)—they are not two dimensions in the same sense.

Key Terms
المحل
الاستغناء
الحلول
الماهية
البعد المفارق
مطلق البعد
التمكن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All dimensions are essentially the same, so if one inheres in matter, all must.
Correction: The inhering dimension and separate dimension are essentially different (مخالف بالماهية) even though they share the generic concept of absolute dimension. Thus, they can have different properties.

Checkpoint
How does the answer respond to the objection that dimensions in a body and separate dimensions are the same?
A) It denies that separate dimensions exist.
B) It says they are essentially different even if they share the universal 'absolute dimension'.
C) It claims that dimensions are not real.
D) It argues that bodies cannot have dimensions.

Key Takeaway
The dimension in bodies and the separate dimension are essentially different, so there is no contradiction in one requiring a substrate and the other not.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have seen how two things can share a label (like 'dimension') yet be essentially different. Where in your own understanding of faith or life have you mistakenly assumed that sharing a label means being the same? Will you take time today to examine one such assumption and ask Allah for clarity?
?
The source argues that a contradiction only arises if we ignore essential differences. Is there a belief or practice you have dismissed as contradictory to Islam, but perhaps you have not looked deeply enough into its essence? What step will you take to investigate it with sincere intention?
?
The dilemma in the lesson could have led to a wrong conclusion if the answer had not been given. Are there areas in your character where you accept a false dilemma—thinking you must choose between two extremes—when a deeper understanding could provide a middle way? What concrete change can you make this week to seek that deeper understanding?

The First Argument for Void: Two Smooth Surfaces
Presents the first argument for void and the response denying instantaneous lifting.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first argument for the possibility of void based on two smooth surfaces and the key response that undermines it.

Core Idea
Instantaneous lifting is impossible; motion requires time.

This lesson introduces the first of several arguments for the possibility of void. The argument relies on the idea of lifting one smooth surface from another instantaneously. However, as you will see, the response challenges the very possibility of instantaneous lifting, showing that motion inherently takes time.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read both the Arabic and the translation. Notice the structure: first the argument for void, then the response. Let the text's flow guide your understanding.
The argument hinges on the concept of 'instantaneous' (دفعة).
2
Identify the core assumptions
The argument assumes two smooth surfaces in contact, no body between them, and lifting one instantaneously. It also assumes air does not immediately fill the gap except through creation or pores in indivisible parts.
These premises are accepted by the opponents (the mutakallimun).
3
Analyze the response
The response denies the possibility of instantaneous lifting because lifting is a motion that requires time. It then considers two meanings of 'instantaneous': an indivisible moment (impossible) or simultaneous movement over time (allows air to enter).
4
Understand the entailment issue
Even if simultaneous lifting over time is granted, void does not necessarily follow because air can flow in from the edges during that time. The opponent cannot establish a necessary link between lifting and void.
The response concludes by questioning either the entailment or the possibility of the entailed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
احتج القائلون بإمكان الخلاء بوجوه : لأول: لو فرضنا صفحة ملساء فوق أخرى مثلها بحيث يتماس سطحاهما المستويان، ولا يكون بينهما جسم أصلاً. ورفعنا إحداهما عن الأخرى دفعة، ففي أول زمان الارتفاع يلزم خلو الوسط، ضرورة أنه إنما يمتلىء بالهواء، الواصل إليه من الخارج بعد المرور بالأطراف والمقدمات، أعني إمكان الصفحة الملساء، أي الجسم الذي له سطح مستو، ليس فيه ارتفاع وانخفاض ولا انضمام أجزاء من غير اتصال واتحاد، وكون التماس بين السطحين لا بين أجزاء لا تتجزأ من الجانبين وإمكان رفع العليا من السفلى دفعة بحيث لا يكون ارتفاع أحد الجانبين قبل ارتفاع الآخر، ليلزم التفكك، وعدم حصول الهواء في الوسط عند الارتفاع بخلق الله تعالى، أو بالوصول إليه من المنافذ، أو المسام بين أجزاء لا تتجزأ مسلمة عندهم، مبنية على أصولهم. أجيب بمنع إمكان ارتفاع العليا من السفلى حينئذ بل هو عندنا محال، جاز أن يستلزم محالاً، ولو سلم إمكان الارتفاع في الجملة. فإن أريد بكونه دفعة كونه في آن لا ينقسم أصلاً، فلا نسلم إمكانه. كيف والارتفاع حركة تقتضي زماناً، وإن أريد كون حركة الأجزاء معاً، لئلا يلزم التفكك، فلا نسلم استلزامه للخلاء، فإنه حركة لها زمان، يجوز أن يمر الهواء من الأطراف إلى الوسط، في ذلك الزمان، ففي الجملة الخصم بين منع اللزوم، ومنع إمكان اللزوم، ولا يتم المطلوب إلا بثبوتها. نعم: لو جعل اللزوم هو اللاوصول أعني لا مماسة السطحين الحاصلة عند الارتفاع، الزاماً لمن يقول بكون اللاوصول آنياً يتعين منع إمكان الملزوم.
Those who say the void is possible argued with [several] aspects: The first: if we suppose a smooth surface above another like it such that their two planar surfaces touch, and there is no body between them at all. And we lift one of them from the other instantaneously, then in the first moment of elevation the void of the middle is necessitated, because it can only be filled by air reaching it from outside after passing through the edges and preliminaries, meaning the possibility of a smooth surface, i.e., a body that has a planar surface with no elevation or depression and no joining of parts without connection and union, and that the contact is between the two surfaces not between indivisible parts from both sides, and the possibility of lifting the upper from the lower instantaneously such that there is no elevation of one side before the other, lest disintegration occur, and that air does not occur in the middle at the time of elevation by God's creation or by reaching it through vents or pores between indivisible parts - these are conceded by them, built upon their principles. It was answered by denying the possibility of lifting the upper from the lower at that time; rather according to us it is impossible, [because] it would entail something impossible. Even if the possibility of lifting in general is granted: if by 'instantaneous' is meant in an indivisible moment, then we do not grant its possibility. How could it be? Lifting is a motion that requires time. And if by 'instantaneous' is meant that the motion of the parts is simultaneous so that disintegration does not occur, then we do not grant that it entails void, for it is a motion that has time, and it is possible that air passes from the edges to the middle in that time. So in summary, the opponent is between denying the entailment and denying the possibility of the entailment, and the desired [conclusion] is not achieved except by establishing them. Yes: if the entailment is made to be the non-reach, i.e., the non-touching of the two surfaces that occurs at the elevation, as a necessary consequence for one who says that the non-touching is instantaneous, then it is determined to deny the possibility of the entailed.
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage presents the first argument for the possibility of void by those who affirm it. The argument supposes two perfectly smooth surfaces in contact with no body between them. If one is lifted instantaneously, then in the first moment of lifting, the middle must become empty because air can only enter from the sides after some time. The author elaborates on the assumptions: the possibility of a smooth surface, contact between surfaces (not between indivisible parts), instantaneous lifting without one side rising before the other, and that air does not immediately fill the gap except by creation or through pores in indivisible parts (which the opponents accept). The response denies the possibility of instantaneous lifting altogether because lifting is a motion that requires time. If 'instantaneous' means in an indivisible instant, it is impossible. If it means simultaneous movement of parts over time, then there is no entailment of void because during that time air can flow in from the edges. Thus the opponent cannot establish the necessary link between lifting and void. The response further notes that if someone objects that the non-touching itself is instantaneous, then the possibility of the entailed (i.e., instantaneous non-touching) must be denied.

Key Terms
صفحة ملساء
تماس
أجزاء لا تتجزأ
خلو الوسط
دفعة
الوصول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Instantaneous lifting is possible and would lead to void.
Correction: Lifting is a motion requiring time, so an indivisible instant cannot contain it. Even if simultaneous over time, air can enter during that time, preventing void.

Checkpoint
According to the response to the first argument, what happens to the claim of void when the lifting is considered as taking place over time?
A) Void is still necessitated because air cannot enter instantly.
B) The air cannot enter because it takes time to reach the middle.
C) The air can enter during the time of motion, so void does not occur.
D) The lifting is impossible, so the argument is moot.

Key Takeaway
The argument for void fails because instantaneous lifting is impossible and even simultaneous lifting over time allows air to fill the gap.

?
When you experience a delay in your prayers being answered or in your personal growth, do you become impatient and try to force an immediate result, forgetting that even physical motion requires time? How can you trust Allah's timing more deeply after learning that instantaneous change is impossible?
?
This lesson shows that assuming instant results can lead to false conclusions about reality. In what area of your faith or life have you assumed that change must be immediate, and what would it look like to embrace the process that Allah has ordained?

The Four Kinds of Qualities According to al-Taftazani
Al-Taftazani enumerates the four types of qualities by induction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Taftazani classifies qualities into four categories based on induction, and why this classification is not a deductive proof but an empirical summary.

Core Idea
Qualities are of four types, known by induction, not deduction.

In this passage, al-Taftazani presents a classification of qualities (kayfiyya) that is central to understanding accidents in kalam. He emphasizes that this fourfold division is arrived at through induction (istiqra'), meaning empirical observation and generalization, not through logical deduction. This sets the stage for a practical rather than purely rational categorization.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the enumerative structure and the mention of 'induction' at the start.
The passage is concisely worded; take your time.
2
Identify the four types of qualities
Locate each of the four categories: sensible qualities, psychic qualities, qualities proper to quantities, and dispositions. Note that they are listed in a specific order.
These correspond to different domains: physical, psychological, mathematical, and potentiality.
3
Understand the method of induction and disjunction
Al-Taftazani explains that the enumeration relies on induction, and he describes a disjunctive reasoning: each quality falls into one of the four. This is not a proof but a way to organize observed data.
Disjunction ('tarīd') refers to the logical 'either-or' structure.
4
Reflect on the limitation mentioned
The passage notes that some properties of these categories are obscure, indicating the classification is not absolute. Consider why this matters for the study of kalam.
This humility aligns with the inductive method.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : وتنحصر بالاستقراء أقسام الكيف أربعة : الكيفيات المحسوسة ، الكيفيات النفسانية ، الكيفيات المختصة بالكميات ، الاستعدادات . والتعويل في الحصر على الاستقراء، وقد تبين بصورة الترديد بين النفي والإثبات ويحصل بحسب اختلاف التعبير عن كل قسم بما له الخواص طرق متعددة حاصلها أن الكيف إن كان هو القسم الأول فالأول أو الثاني فالثاني أو الثالث فالثالث أو الرابع فالرابع والمنع عليه ظاهر، فلا يصلح إلا وجه ضبط لما علم بالاستقراء، على أن بعض الخواص بما فيه نوع خفاء .
He said: The divisions of quality are confined by induction to four: sensible qualities, psychic qualities, qualities proper to quantities, and dispositions. Reliance in the enumeration is on induction, and it has become evident by the form of disjunction between negation and affirmation, and by the different expression of each division according to its properties, there are multiple ways. The gist is: if the quality is the first division, then it is the first; if the second, then the second; if the third, then the third; if the fourth, then the fourth. And the objection to it is obvious, so it is not suitable except as a way of organizing what is known by induction, noting that some properties contain a kind of obscurity.
Source pages p. 221

Commentary
This passage presents the classification of qualities into four types based on induction (istiqra'). The author states that the categories of quality are confined to four: sensible qualities (e.g., sweetness, redness), psychic qualities (e.g., emotions, dispositions), qualities proper to quantities (e.g., evenness, triangularity), and dispositions (e.g., potential for reception or for action). The method of enumeration is inductive, not deductive. The author explains that the enumeration is validated by a disjunctive reasoning: each quality must belong to one of these four, and if it belongs to one, it is that one. However, this is not a rigorous proof; it is merely a way to organize what has been observed by induction. He notes that some properties of these categories are obscure, meaning the classification is not absolutely watertight but serves as a helpful summary.

Key Terms

كيفيات محسوسة (sensible qualities)
كيفيات نفسانية (psychic qualities)
كيفيات مختصة بالكميات (qualities proper to quantities)
استعدادات (dispositions)
حصر (enumeration)
ترديد (disjunction)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the fourfold division is analytically deduced from first principles.
Correction: Al-Taftazani explicitly states that the classification is based on induction (istiqra'), not deductive reasoning. He notes that the disjunctive form is not a true proof but a way to organize empirical knowledge.

Checkpoint
What method does the passage say is used to confine the divisions of quality to four?
A) Deductive reasoning from first principles.
B) Induction (istiqra').
C) Scriptural authority.
D) Consensus of scholars.

Key Takeaway
Al-Taftazani classifies qualities into four types based on empirical observation (induction), emphasizing that this enumeration is a practical summary, not a logically necessary proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You rely on induction every day to navigate life—from predicting weather to trusting that a chair will hold you. But when was the last time you recognized that your most confident classifications are merely empirical summaries, not absolute truths? Will you allow that humility to shape how you judge your own knowledge and how you treat others' differing classifications? Write one area of your life where you have assumed certainty but now realize it is only inductive.
?
Al-Taftazani admits the disjunction is not a proof and some properties are obscure. What area of your faith or practice have you assumed is perfectly clear, when in fact it may be more complex? Will you seek the truth with intellectual honesty, even if it means letting go of false certainty? Commit to investigating one such matter this week.
?
When you categorize people or ideas, do you remember that your categories are based on limited induction? How can you be more cautious in your judgments, especially about others' intentions or beliefs? Will you commit to questioning your own categories this week, and if so, what is one specific category you will examine?

The Four Primary Tangible Qualities
Defines the four primary tangible qualities and discusses their properties.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four primary tangible qualities (heat, cold, moisture, dryness), their basic properties, and the detailed analysis of heat's effects, including the refutation that cold is merely the absence of heat.

Core Idea
Four tangible qualities form the foundation of all physical bodies.

This lesson introduces the foundational tangible qualities that define physical bodies, as established by scholars. Sa'd al-Din al-Taftazani presents the consensus on heat, cold, moisture, and dryness, explains their characteristic properties, and then delves into the verification of heat's effects, demonstrating how heat functions differently depending on the recipient. The passage also refutes the view that cold is merely the absence of heat, affirming its real existence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four qualities
Read the passage and note that the author states a consensus on heat, cold, moisture, and dryness as the principles of tangible qualities. Understand that they are self-evident.
2
Understand their basic properties
Learn the initial description: heat unites similars and separates dissimilars; cold does the opposite; moisture allows easy adhesion and shape reception; dryness opposes that.
3
Examine the verification of heat
Follow the author's detailed explanation of how heat causes sublimation and rarefaction, leading to different effects in different compounds (e.g., gold, sal ammoniac, lead).
This shows that heat's effect depends on the recipient's nature.
4
Note the refutation of privation
The passage rejects the idea that cold is merely the absence of heat, asserting that cold is a real quality with its own essence.
Briefly consider why this matters for understanding reality.
5
Grasp the active quality of heat
Understand the definition of heat as an active quality causing upward movement by producing lightness, leading to gathering and separating, and how expansion and condensation relate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الأول الملموسات أصولها الحرارة قال : أطبقوا على أن أصولها الحرارة والبرودة والرطوبة واليبوسة غنية عن البيان هلية وماهية ، إلا أنه قد ينبه على بعض الخواص فيقال : الحرارة كيفية من شأنها جمع المتشاكلات وتفريق المختلفات، والبرودة بالعكس، والرطوبة بكيفية تقتضي سهولة الالتصاق والانفصال أو سهولة قبول الأشكال واليبوسة بالعكس، والتحقيق أن في الحرارة تصعيداً وإلا لطف أقبل لذلك فيحدث في المركب الذي لم يشتد التحام بسائطه تفريق الأجزاء المختلفة، ويتبعه جمع المتشاكلات وفي الذي اشتد حركة دورية كما في الذهب، أو تصعيداً بالكلية كما في النوشادر، أو سيلاناً كما في الرصاص، أو تليناً كما في الحديد، أو مجرد سخونة كما في الطلق، بحسب اختلاف القوابل ثم قال : أصول الكيفيات الملموسة، أي التي لا يخلو عنها شيء من الأجسام العنصرية، ويقع للإحساس بها أولا بين الحرارة والبرودة، والرطوبة واليبوسة. ولا خفاء في وجودها فما يقال : إن البرودة عدم الحرارة ليس بشيء ولا في ماهيتها، فما يذكر في معرض التعريف لها تنبيه على بعض مالها من الخواص لا إفادة لتصوراتها والمشهور من خواص الحرارة هي التحريك إلى فوق على ما قال في الحدود: الحرارة كيفية فعلية بحركة لما تكون فيه إلى فوق لإحداثها الخفة فيعرض أن تجمع المتجانسات، وتفرق المختلفات وتحدث بتخليها الكشف تخلخلاً من باب الكليف أي رقه قوام ومقابله التكاثف بمعنى غلظ القوام، وبتصعيدها اللطيف تكاثفاً من باب الوضع أي اجتماعاً للأجزاء الوجدانية الطبع لخروج الجسم الغريب عما بينها أو يقابله التخلخل بمعنى انتقاش الأجزاء بحيث يخالطها جرم غريب، ومعنى الفعلية ما سبق من جعل الغير شبيهاً لا مجرد إفادة أثر ما أعم من الحركة وغيرها، ليكون قولنا فعلية محركة ، بمنزلة قولنا : جسم حيوان، على ما زعم الإمام . وبالجملة فالخاصة الأولية للحرارة هي إحداث الخفة والميل المصعد، ثم يترتب على ذلك بحسب اختلاف القوابل آثار مختلفة من الجمع والتفريق والتنجير وغير ذلك.
The first chapter: Tangible qualities, their principles are heat. He said: They agreed that their principles are heat, cold, moisture, and dryness, which are free from need of explanation in existence and quiddity, except that one may point out some properties: heat is a quality whose property is to unite similar things and separate different things; cold is the opposite; moisture is a quality that requires easy adhesion and separation or easy reception of shapes; dryness is the opposite. And the verification is that in heat there is sublimation and rarefaction, so that in a compound whose components are not firmly bound, it causes separation of different parts, and it is followed by gathering of similars, and in that which is firm, it causes circular motion like in gold, or complete sublimation like in sal ammoniac, or liquefaction like in lead, or softening like in iron, or mere heat like in talc, according to the differences of the recipients. Then he said: The principles of the tangible qualities, i.e., those from which no elemental body is free, and which are first perceived by sensation, are heat and cold, moisture and dryness. There is no obscurity in their existence – so what is said that cold is absence of heat is nothing – nor in their quiddity. So what is mentioned by way of definition for them is a reminder of some of their properties, not to give conception of them. And the well-known of the properties of heat is movement upward, as stated in al-Hudud: Heat is an active quality causing movement upward in that which possesses it, by causing lightness, so that it happens that it gathers similars and separates dissimilars. And by its rarefaction it causes expansion, from the category of thinness, i.e., thinness of consistency, and its opposite is condensation meaning thickness of consistency, and by its sublimation of the subtle it causes condensation from the category of composition, i.e., aggregation of the natural parts due to the exit of the foreign body from between them, or its opposite is expansion meaning spreading of parts such that a foreign body mixes with them. And the meaning of 'active' is what preceded: making another similar, not merely giving an effect, whether movement or otherwise, so that our saying 'active causing movement' is like our saying 'animal body', according to what the Imam claimed. In summary: The primary property of heat is causing lightness and upward inclination; then depending on the differences of the recipients, various effects follow from it: gathering, separating, solidifying, and others.
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
This passage defines and discusses the four primary tangible qualities—heat, cold, moisture, and dryness. It opens by stating a consensus that these four are the foundational principles and are self-evident in both existence and quiddity. Then it lists their typical properties: heat unites similars and separates dissimilars; cold does the opposite; moisture facilitates adhesion, separation, and shape reception; dryness does the opposite. The author then provides a more detailed verification of heat's effects, explaining that heat involves sublimation and rarefaction, resulting in different outcomes depending on the compound's composition: separating parts in loose compounds, circular motion in gold, complete sublimation in sal ammoniac, liquefaction in lead, softening in iron, or mere warmth in talc. Next, the passage reaffirms that these four are the only primary tangible qualities—no elemental body lacks them, and they are first perceived by sensation. It refutes the notion that cold is merely the absence of heat, insisting that cold is a real quality. The definitions given are merely reminders of properties, not formal definitions, because the qualities are directly known. The author then elaborates on heat, quoting from al-Hudud: heat is an active quality that moves things upward by causing lightness, leading to gathering similars and separating dissimilars. It explains that expansion (takhalkhul) and condensation (takathuf) are opposite effects of heat's rarefaction and sublimation. Finally, the primary property of heat is summarized as causing lightness and upward inclination, from which various subsequent effects like gathering, separating, and solidifying arise depending on the recipients. The passage is a combination of definition, property explanation, and refutation of privation theory.

Correction: The passage explicitly states that cold is a real quality, not the absence of heat, and refutes that claim.

Defining Wetness and Defending Against Objections
Defines wetness and responds to two objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definition of wetness as a quality involving both ease of adhesion and ease of separation, and how two classic objections are resolved.

Core Idea
Wetness requires both ease of adhesion and ease of separation.

In this passage, the author defines wetness with precision and defends it against two objections. The first involves honey, which is sticky but not wetter than water; the second involves finely ground dry substances like burnt bones. Understanding how these objections are answered reveals the rigor of the definition.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of wetness
Notice that the definition includes two criteria: ease of adhesion and ease of separation. This dual requirement is crucial.
2
Examine the honey objection
The objection claims honey is stickier than water but not wetter. Consider why this challenges the definition.
Honey is more lasting in adhesion, but does it separate easily?
3
Analyze the response to honey
The response argues that honey is not easier to separate, so it does not meet the definition. Notice the role of 'ease of separation' in rebutting the objection.
4
Examine the burnt bones objection
Another objection: if ease of adhesion defines wetness, then finely ground dry things would be wet. Consider why this seems plausible.
5
Analyze the response to burnt bones
The response appeals to mixing with airy parts, but only on the opinion that air is wet. This shows the definition's dependence on background assumptions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الانفصال فهي كيفية بها يستعد الجسم بسهولة الالتصاق بالغير، وسهولة الانفصال عنه، ولا نسلم أن العسل أسهل ولا نسلم أن العسل أسهل التصاقاً من الماء، بل أدوم وأكثر ملازمة، ولا عبرة بذلك في الرطوبة، كيف وظاهر أنه ليس أسهل انفصالاً فيلزم أن لا يكون أسهل التصاقاً من الماء، وكان مراد الإمام تأويل كلامهم بما ذكر، وإلا فاعتراض ابن سينا إنما هو على ما نقله من كلامهم، لا على تفسير الرطوبة بسهولة الالتصاق والانفصال على ما يشعر به كلام المواقف ومبناه على أن لا تعرض في كلامهم للانفصال أصلا، ولا للسهولة في جانب الالتصاق، على أن ما ذكر من استلزام سهولة الالتصاق وسهولة الانفصال ممنوع، وقد يعترض على اعتبار سهولة الالتصاق بأنه يوجب أن يكون اليابس المدقوق جداً كالعظام المحرقة رطباً لكونه كذلك. ويجاب بأنه يجوز أن يكون ذلك لمخالطة الأجزاء الهوائية، وهذا إنما يتم على رأي من يقول برطوبة الهواء، وسهولة التصاقه، لولا مانع فرط اللطافة، لا على رأي الإمام
Separation is a quality by which the body is disposed to ease of adhesion to another and ease of separation from it. And we do not concede that honey is easier nor do we concede that honey is easier in adhesion than water; rather it is more lasting and more adherent, and there is no consideration of that in wetness. How so, and it is apparent that it is not easier in separation, so it follows that it is not easier in adhesion than water. And the intention of the Imam was to interpret their statement as mentioned; otherwise, Ibn Sina's objection is only against what he transmitted from their statement, not against the interpretation of wetness as ease of adhesion and separation, as suggested by the statement of al-Mawaqif, and its basis is that there is no mention of separation in their statement at all, nor of ease on the side of adhesion. Moreover, what was mentioned of the entailment of ease of adhesion and ease of separation is denied. And one may object to the consideration of ease of adhesion that it necessitates that a very finely ground dry thing, like burnt bones, is wet because it is like that. And it is answered that it may be that this is due to mixing with airy parts, and this is only valid according to the opinion of those who say that air is wet and easily adherent, were it not for the obstacle of extreme subtlety, but not according to the opinion of the Imam.
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage defines wetness (الرطوبة) as a quality characterized by both ease of adhesion and ease of separation. The author then addresses an objection regarding honey, which is more clinging than water but not wetter. The objection is that honey is not easier to separate, so it should not be considered wet. The author explains that the Imam (likely Fakhr al-Din al-Razi) interpreted the earlier scholars' definition to include both aspects, and Ibn Sina's criticism was directed at a different formulation. Another objection is raised: if ease of adhesion defines wetness, then finely ground dry things like burnt bones would be wet. The answer is that such adhesion may be due to mixing with air, but this presupposes air's wetness, which is not universally accepted.

Objection on Fire's Ease of Shape Acceptance
Objection and refutation regarding fire and ease of shape acceptance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the distinction between pure fire and fire mixed with air refutes the objection that ease of shape acceptance would make fire the wettest element.

Core Idea
Pure fire does not accept shapes easily - the apparent ease is due to air.

This passage defends the theory that wetness is the ease of accepting shapes. An objector argues that fire, being the most subtle element, accepts shapes easily. If the theory were true, fire would be wettest, which is absurd. The response distinguishes pure fire from fire mixed with air, showing that the observed ease is due to air, not fire itself.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the objector's argument: fire is the most subtle element and accepts shapes easily. If ease of shape acceptance means wetness, then fire would be wettest. But we know fire is dry, so the criterion seems flawed.
The objector uses the absurd consequence to disprove the definition.
2
Examine the answer: pure vs. mixed fire
The answer does not grant that pure fire accepts foreign shapes easily. Instead, it points out that what we observe is fire mixed with air, and it is the air that gives the appearance of ease.
This distinction is key: pure fire behaves differently.
3
Follow the counter and final response
The objector then says: if an oven is heated for months, the air inside becomes fire. So how can the ease still be due to air? The answer: even after a thousand years, the air remains mixed with fire; there is no pure fire without air.
The mixing persists indefinitely, so the ease is always from air.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعترض على اعتبار سهولة قبول الأشكال بوجوه منها : أن النار أرق العناصر ، وألطفها وأسهلها قبولاً للأشكال، فيلزم أن تكون أرطبها، وبطلانه ظاهر. وأجيب بأنا لا نسلم سهولة قبول الأشكال الغريبة في النار الصرفة وإنما ذلك فيما شاهد من النار المخالطة للهواء . فإن قيل : إذ أوقد التنور شهراً أو شهرين انقلب ما فيه من الهواء ناراً صرفة أو غالبة، مع أن سهولة قبول الأشكال محالها بل أزيد. قلنا : لو أوقد ألف سنة فمداخلة الهواء، ومخالطة الأجزاء بحالها
And it was objected to the consideration of ease of accepting shapes with several aspects, among them: that fire is the most subtle of the elements and the most refined and the easiest in accepting shapes, so it would follow that it is the wettest of them, and its falsehood is apparent. And it was answered: we do not concede that pure fire easily accepts foreign shapes; rather that is only in what is observed of fire mixed with air. For if it is said: when the oven is heated for a month or two, what is in it of air turns into pure or predominant fire, while the ease of accepting shapes is its place, even more. We say: if it were heated for a thousand years, the intermingling of air and the mixing of parts would remain as they are.
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage presents an objection to the criterion of ease of shape acceptance as a sign of wetness. The objector argues that fire, being the most subtle element, accepts shapes easily, so it should be wettest—which is absurd. The answer distinguishes between pure fire and fire mixed with air; the observed ease is due to air. The objection then imagines a long-heated oven where the air becomes fire, but the answer insists that the mixing persists regardless of duration.

Key Terms
سهولة قبول الأشكال
النار
العناصر
الهواء
التنور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Fire itself easily accepts shapes, so it would be wet if wetness is ease of shape acceptance.
Correction: Pure fire does not accept foreign shapes easily; the observed ease is due to the air mixed with it.

Checkpoint
What is the basis for denying that fire is wet?
A) Fire is dry by nature.
B) Pure fire does not easily accept foreign shapes.
C) Fire is not an element.
D) The oven argument fails.

Key Takeaway
The apparent wetness of fire (via shape acceptance) is due to its mixing with air, not pure fire itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
This passage shows that mixing can persist even after prolonged 'heating'. What mixed influences in your life—people, habits, thoughts—have become so familiar that you forget they are not truly part of you? What would it take to separate them?

Air Wetness Objection and the Distinction of 'Billa'
Sanusi refutes the air wetness objection by distinguishing wetness as quality from wetness as moisture.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between wetness as a quality (ease of shape acceptance) and wetness as moisture (billa), and how this resolves the objection that air must be wet.

Core Idea
Air is wet in one sense but not in the sense that causes cohesion.

One objection to defining wetness as ease of shape acceptance is that air would then be wet, yet mixing air with earth does not produce cohesion like water does. The answer hinges on a subtle distinction between two meanings of 'wetness'. This passage resolves the objection by clarifying that the wetness relevant to cohesion is moisture (billa), not the broader quality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Note the structure: objection, counterexample, answer.
The argument is compact; take your time.
2
Identify the objection and its refutation
Find where the opponent's claim is stated and how it is refuted. The refutation uses an agreed-upon observation about mixing wet and dry.
3
Understand the key distinction
The answer distinguishes two senses of 'wet': as a quality (ease of shape acceptance) and as moisture. The Imam (Fakhr al-Din al-Razi) explicitly identifies sensible wetness with moisture.
This distinction resolves the apparent contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها أنه يوجب كون الهواء رطباً، ويبطله اتفاقهم على أن خلط الرطب باليابس يفرقه استمساكاً عن التشتت، وخلط الهواء بالتراب ليس كذلك. والجواب : أن ذلك إنما هو في الرطب بمعنى ذي البلة، فإن إطلاق الرطوبة على البلة شائع، بل كلام الإمام صريح في أن الرطوبة التي هي من المحسوسات إنما هي البلة، لا ما اعتبر فيه سهولة قبول الأشكال، لأن الهواء رطب بهذا المعنى، ولا يحس منه برطوبة
And among them is that it necessitates that air is wet, and it is refuted by their agreement that mixing the wet with the dry gives it cohesion against dispersion, whereas mixing air with earth is not so. And the answer is that this is only in the wet in the sense of having moisture (billa), for the application of wetness to moisture is common; rather, the Imam's statement is explicit that wetness which is among the sensible things is only moisture, not what is considered in it ease of accepting shapes, because air is wet in this sense, yet no wetness is perceived from it.
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage addresses the objection that if ease of shape acceptance defined wetness, then air would be wet, but mixing air with earth does not produce cohesion like water. The answer clarifies that the criterion for wetness in the sense of moisture (بلة) is different; air is wet in that sense, but its wetness is not perceptible. The Imam explicitly states that sensible wetness is moisture, not ease of shape acceptance.

Key Terms

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that air is not wet in any sense, or that the objection proves wetness is not ease of shape acceptance.
Correction: The passage shows that air is wet in the sense of having moisture (billa), but that wetness is not perceptible. The objection is only valid if wetness is understood as moisture.

Checkpoint
Why does mixing air with earth not produce cohesion?
A) Because air is not wet in the sense of moisture.
B) Because air is wet only in the sense of moisture, but its wetness does not produce cohesion.
C) Because earth is not dry.
D) Because the Imam said so.

Key Takeaway
The wetness that causes cohesion is moisture (billa); air is wet in the sense of ease of shape acceptance but not in the sense of perceptible moisture.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Commit to pausing and analyzing key terms before forming conclusions.
?
The scholar here refines the definition of wetness to avoid a misconception. This passage shows that precision in language is not pedantic; it safeguards truth. Identify one area where you need to clarify your understanding and take a step today to look at the original sources or ask a knowledgeable person.
?
The objection seemed logical, but the answer saved the definition. In your personal life, have you ever dismissed a teaching of Islam because it seemed inconsistent with your reasoning, only to later realize you misunderstood a key term? How can you cultivate the humility to say 'I may be missing a distinction' when faced with apparent contradictions? Write one concrete action you will take to seek clarification before rejecting.

Distinction Between Dryness and Hardness
Distinguishes dryness from hardness by defining softness and hardness based on pressure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the difference between dryness and hardness, and how softness and hardness are defined in terms of acceptance or prevention of pressure.

Core Idea
Softness accepts pressure; hardness prevents it. Dryness and hardness are not the same.

This passage addresses a potential confusion between dryness and hardness. An objection is raised that if dryness is defined as difficulty accepting shapes, it becomes identical to hardness. The answer clarifies the distinct definitions of softness and hardness based on pressure, showing how they differ from dryness and wetness.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the objection: if dryness is difficulty accepting shapes, then it equals hardness, implying fire is hard. Then read the answer that distinguishes softness and hardness by pressure.
The objection serves to highlight the problem with conflating dryness and hardness.
2
Understand the definitions
Focus on the definitions: softness (layn) is the quality that accepts pressure inward, giving a consistent non-fluid nature; hardness (salabah) prevents pressure, preserving shape and resisting change. Contrast these with wetness and dryness.
Note that the passage also mentions a relation between pressure acceptance and wetness/dryness.
3
Reflect on the debate
The passage ends with a debate: are softness and hardness directly sensed or are they dispositions? Some define softness as the absence of hardness in a capable subject, making them opposites of possession and privation. Consider how this fits into broader discussions of qualities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها أنه يوجب أن يكون المعتبر في اليبوسة صعوبة قبول الأشكال فلم يبق فرق بينها وبين الصلابة ويلزم كون النار صلبة لكونها يابسة والجواب أن اللين كيفية تقتضي قبول الغمز إلى الباطن، ويكون للشيء بها قوام غير سيال فينتقل عن وضعه، ولا يمتد كثيراً بسهولة، والصلابة كيفية تقتضي بمانعة من قبول الغمز، ويكون للشيء بها بقاء شكل وشدة مقاومة نحو الانفعال فيتغايران الرطوبة واليبوسة بهذا الاعتبار. إلا أنه يشبه أن يكون مرجع قبول الغمز ولا قبوله إلى الرطوبة واليبوسة، فعلى ما ذكرنا اللين والصلابة كيفيتان متضادتان، وهل هما الملموسات أو الاستعدادات؟ فيه تردد وبعضهم على أن اللين عبارة عن عدم الصلابة عما من شأنه فبينهما تقابل الملكة والعدم
And among them is that it necessitates that what is considered in dryness is difficulty of accepting shapes, so no difference remains between it and hardness, and it would follow that fire is hard because it is dry. And the answer is that softness (layn) is a quality that necessitates the acceptance of pressure inward, and by it the thing has a consistency that is not fluid, so it moves from its place and does not stretch much easily; and hardness is a quality that necessitates a prevention of accepting pressure, and by it the thing has a preservation of shape and intensity of resistance toward being affected; thus they differ from wetness and dryness in this consideration. Except that it seems that the reference of acceptance of pressure or its non-acceptance goes back to wetness and dryness. So according to what we have said, softness and hardness are two opposite qualities. Whether they are sensed things or dispositions? There is hesitation. And some hold that softness is an expression of the absence of hardness from what is capable of it, so between them is the opposition of possession and privation.
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage addresses an objection that if dryness is defined as difficulty of shape acceptance, it becomes identical to hardness, leading to the absurd conclusion that fire is hard. The answer distinguishes softness (اللين) and hardness (الصلابة). Softness is the quality of accepting pressure inward, while hardness prevents it. Wetness and dryness are different categories. However, acceptance of pressure may relate to wetness and dryness. The passage notes that softness and hardness are opposites, and there is disagreement on whether they are directly sensed or are dispositions. Some define softness as the absence of hardness in a capable thing, making it a privation.

ملكة وعدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Dryness and hardness are identical qualities because both involve difficulty of accepting shapes.
Correction: The passage shows that hardness is defined by prevention of pressure, while dryness is about moisture absence. They are distinct categories, and the definitions of softness and hardness are based on pressure, not shape acceptance.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how are softness and hardness defined?
A) Softness is lack of hardness.
B) Softness is ease of shape change.
C) Softness accepts pressure, hardness prevents it.
D) They are the same.

Key Takeaway
Softness and hardness are opposite qualities defined by acceptance or prevention of pressure, distinct from dryness and wetness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often judge things by their surface—dryness, wetness, softness, hardness—but this lesson forces you to ask: do you distinguish between how something appears and what it actually is? In your own faith, where have you mistaken a superficial quality for a real one, and what did that cost you? This week, will you commit to examining one belief you hold not by its outer shape but by its inner resistance to pressure?
?
The scholars debated whether softness and hardness are sensed or dispositions—a distinction that seems academic until you realize that how you perceive God's commands might be a matter of your own disposition. Is your softness toward obedience a real quality or just the absence of hardness? What would it take for you to know the difference, and are you willing to ask God to show you your heart's true state?
?
This lesson hinges on a single distinction: dryness is not hardness. But in your life, how often do you conflate unrelated things? You treat a person's harsh words as proof of their character, or your own dryness in worship as strength. Before you pray next, pause: are you confusing your resistance to change with steadfastness? Let the passage's precision humble you and drive you to seek true discernment.

Qualities Relative to the Four & Disagreement on Smoothness
Classifies qualities as relative and notes disagreement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that many sensible qualities like moisture, dryness, and density are considered relative to the four primary qualities, and that smoothness and roughness are disputed.

Core Idea
All tangible qualities trace back to the four primary ones.

This passage extends the classification of sensible qualities beyond the four primary ones. The author lists several qualities that depend on heat, cold, wetness, and dryness, and he flags a disagreement concerning smoothness and roughness. He then begins to define moisture more precisely, though the text cuts off.

Definitions of Moisture, Dryness, Softness, and Hardness
The author defines moisture, dryness, softness, and hardness, and rebuts an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinctions between sensory moisture (wetness) and metaphysical moisture, and between softness/hardness and moisture/dryness.

Core Idea
Sensory moisture is wetness; softness and hardness are about pressure acceptance, not shape ease.

This lesson tackles definitions of four common tactile qualities that can easily be confused. The author clarifies that moisture in sensory terms is simply wetness, and distinguishes it from the metaphysical concept of ease of shape acceptance. He then defines softness and hardness in terms of pressure acceptance, showing they are distinct from moisture and dryness. A potential objection that conflates dryness with hardness is addressed.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation carefully. Notice the flow: objection, answer, definitions, and final discussion.
The dense Arabic may require re-reading.
2
Understand the objection and its answer
The objection claims mixing air with dust does not hold together like moist clay. The answer distinguishes sensory moisture (wetness) from metaphysical moisture (ease of shape acceptance).
This shows the importance of term precision in kalam.
3
Learn the definitions of softness and hardness
Softness allows pressure into the interior; hardness prevents it. These are defined by resistance to deformation, not by moisture content.
4
Explore the final discussion on softness/hardness
The author notes that accepting pressure may relate to moisture/dryness, and there is debate whether softness/hardness are tangible qualities or dispositive states (like privation).
This touches on the broader kalam topic of primary and secondary qualities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يفيده استمساكاً عن التشتت، وخلط الهواء بالتراب ليس كذلك. والجواب: أن ذلك إنما هو في الرطب. بمعنى ذي البلة، فإن إطلاق الرطوبة على البلة شائع، بل كلام الإمام صريح في أن الرطوبة التي هي من المحسوسات إنما هي البلة، لا ما اعتبر فيه سهولة قبول الأشكال، لأن الهواء رطب بهذا المعنى، ولا يحس منه برطوبة، ومنها أنه يوجب أن يكون المعتبر في اليبوسة صعوبة قبول الأشكال فلم يبق فرق بينها وبين الصلابة ويلزم كون النار صلبة لكونها يابسة. والجواب أن اللين كيفية تقتضي قبول الغمز إلى الباطن، ويكون للشيء بها قوام غير سيال فينتقل عن وضعه، ولا يمتد كثيراً بسهولة، والصلابة كيفية تقتضي بمانعة من قبول الغمز، ويكون للشيء بها بقاء شكل وشدة مقاومة نحو الانفعال، فيتغايران الرطوبة واليبوسة بهذا الاعتبار، إلا أنه يشبه أن يكون مرجع قبول الغمز ولا قبوله إلى الرطوبة واليبوسة، فعلى ما ذكرنا اللين والصلابة كيفيتان متضادتان، وهل هما الملموسات أو الاستعدادات؟ فيه تردد، وبعضهم على أن اللين عبارة عن عدم الصلابة عما من شأنه، فبينهما تقابل الملكة والعدم.
It benefits [by] holding fast against dispersion, and mixing air with dust is not like that. The answer: that is only in moist (ar-ratb), meaning possessing wetness (al-balla), for the application of moisture (ar-rutuba) to wetness is common, rather the statement of the Imam is explicit that moisture which is among the sensory things is only wetness, not what is considered in it ease of receiving shapes, because air is moist in this meaning, and no moisture is sensed from it. And among them is that it necessitates that the considered [quality] in dryness (al-yubusa) is difficulty of receiving shapes, so no difference remains between it and hardness (as-salaba), and it follows that fire is hard because it is dry. The answer: that softness (al-lin) is a quality that requires accepting pressure (al-ghamz) into the interior, and by it a thing has a consistency that is not fluid, so it moves from its position, and does not stretch much easily; and hardness is a quality that requires preventing the acceptance of pressure, and by it a thing has a preservation of shape and intensity of resistance towards passivity (al-infi'āl); so they are distinct, moisture and dryness with this consideration. However, it seems that the reference of accepting or not accepting pressure is to moisture and dryness. So according to what we have mentioned, softness and hardness are two opposite qualities. Are they the tangible qualities or the dispositions? There is hesitation in it. And some hold that softness is an expression of the absence of hardness from that which is apt [to have it], so between them there is the opposition of possession and privation.
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage defines several tactile qualities and rebuts a potential objection. The author begins by responding to an objection: mixing air with dust does not produce something that holds together (istamsāk) because that only occurs in a moist substance (ratb) meaning wet (dhū balla). He clarifies that moisture (rutūba) in sensory terms is actually wetness (balla), not the metaphysical definition of ease of accepting shapes. He then argues that if dryness (yubūsa) were defined by difficulty of shape acceptance, it would be identical to hardness (salāba), leading to the absurdity that fire is hard. The author defines softness (līn) as a quality that allows the object to accept pressure into its interior, giving it a non-fluid consistency that shifts position but does not stretch easily. Hardness (salāba) prevents pressure and preserves shape and resistance. Thus moisture/dryness and softness/hardness are distinct. However, he notes that accepting pressure may actually be a function of moisture and dryness. He concludes that softness and hardness are opposites, but there is debate on whether they are tangible qualities themselves or dispositions (isti'dādāt). Some say softness is merely the absence of hardness in a substance that could have it, which is a privation relation.

Key Terms
الرطوبة
اليبوسة
اللين
الصلابة
الغمز
الملكة والعدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Moisture (rutuba) is the same as ease of shape acceptance, and dryness is difficulty of shape acceptance, making moisture/dryness identical to softness/hardness.
Correction: The author clarifies that sensory moisture is wetness (balla), not ease of shape acceptance. Dryness is not defined that way; rather softness/hardness are about pressure, not shape ease.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what distinguishes softness (al-lin) from hardness (as-salaba)?
A) Softness involves ease of shape acceptance, hardness difficulty.
B) Softness allows pressure into the interior; hardness prevents it.
C) Softness is the presence of moisture; hardness is the presence of dryness.
D) Softness and hardness are the same quality with different names.

Key Takeaway
Sensory moisture is wetness; softness and hardness are defined by acceptance or prevention of pressure, making them distinct from moisture and dryness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever considered your own resistance to change (hardness) or your flexibility (softness) in accepting truth? The passage defines softness as accepting pressure into the interior. In what area of your life are you refusing to let the truth press into your soul, preferring the hardness of your own shape? What would it take for you to allow that pressure?
?
The author notes that sensory moisture is wetness, not ease of shape. But we often confuse outward appearance with inner reality. Where in your understanding of Allah's attributes have you confused a sensory metaphor with a true attribute? How can you ensure you are not falling into that confusion?
?
The passage discusses whether softness and hardness are tangible qualities or mere dispositions. Are your spiritual states (like softness of heart) merely dispositions or actual qualities you possess? Are you actively cultivating the quality of softness towards the truth, or merely assuming you have it?

Secondary Tactile Qualities: Wetness, Dryness, Viscosity, Brittleness, Subtlety, and Density
The author defines six secondary tactile qualities and relates them to the primary four.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definitions of several secondary tactile qualities and how they relate to the primary qualities of moistness and dryness.

Core Idea
Secondary tactile qualities are relative combinations of primary moistness and dryness.

After establishing the primary tactile qualities of moisture and dryness, the author now turns to secondary qualities that arise from their combinations. He defines wetness, dryness, viscosity, brittleness, subtlety, and density, showing how each relates to the primary pair. This passage also notes the controversial status of smoothness and roughness.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the six qualities
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the six qualities: wetness (al-balla), dryness (al-jafāf), viscosity (al-luzūja), brittleness (al-hashāsha), subtlety (al-laṭāfa), and density (al-kathāfa).
The passage also mentions smoothness and roughness briefly.
2
Understand the relation to the four primary qualities
The author says these qualities are 'relative to the four'—meaning the four primary tactile qualities (moist, dry, hot, cold). Focus on how wetness and dryness are defined in terms of moisture.
3
Examine the definitions of wetness, dryness, viscosity, and brittleness
Wetness is extraneous moisture on the surface; if it penetrates, it indicates intensity of absorption. Dryness is the privation of wetness. Viscosity allows easy shaping but difficult separation, arising from much moist mixed with little dry. Brittleness is the opposite.
Note the cause of viscosity: mixing much moist with little dry.
4
Explore the multiple meanings of subtlety
Subtlety has four meanings: thinness of consistency (water, air), ease of division into very small parts (sugar candy), quickness of being affected (rose), and transparency (celestial spheres). Density is the opposite in all these meanings.
This is a key point: subtlety is not a single property.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال وأما مثل البلة والجفاف واللزوجة والهشاشة، واللطافة والكثافة، فنسبة فمنتسبة إلى الأربع. وفي كون الملاسة والخشونة من الكيفيات اختلاف. وأما مثل البلة قد يعد من الملموسات البلة وهي الرطوبة الغريبة الجارية على ظاهر الجسم، فإن كانت نافذة إلى باطنها فهي من شدة الانتفاع، وإلا ظهر أن الجفاف عدم ملكة البلة. واللزوجة وهي كيفية تقتضي سهولة الشكل مع عسر التفرق واتصال الامتداد، وتحدث من امتزاج الرطب الكثير باليابس القليل، ويقابله الهشاشة، وهي ما تقتضي صعوبة التشكل، وسهولة التفرق. واللطافة وقد يقال لرقة القوام كما في الماء والهواء، ولسهولة قبول الانقسام إلى أجزاء صغيرة جداً كما في القند، ولسرعة التأثر من الملاقي كما في الورد، وللشفافية كما في الفلك، والكثافة تقابلها بمعانيها.
He said: As for examples like wetness (al-balla), dryness (al-jafāf), viscosity (al-luzūja), brittleness (al-hashāsha), subtlety (al-laṭāfa), and density (al-kathāfa), they are relative and related to the four. And regarding smoothness (al-malāsa) and roughness (al-khushūna), there is disagreement [on] whether they are among the qualities. As for like wetness, wetness is counted among the tangible things: it is the extraneous moisture flowing on the surface of the body; if it penetrates into its interior, then it is from intensity of absorption [or benefit]; otherwise it appears that dryness is the absence of the possession of wetness. Viscosity is a quality that requires ease of shape with difficulty of separation and continuity of extension, and it arises from mixing much moist with little dry. Its opposite is brittleness, which requires difficulty of shaping and ease of separation. Subtlety: it may be said for thinness of consistency as in water and air, and for ease of accepting division into very small parts as in sugar candy, and for quickness of being affected by contact as in a rose, and for transparency as in the celestial sphere. Density is its opposite in these meanings.
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage enumerates and defines a set of secondary tactile qualities that are relative to the four primary tactile qualities (moisture, dryness, heat, cold? Actually the 'four' here likely means the four primary qualities: moisture, dryness, cold, heat, as per Aristotelian physics. The author states that wetness (balla), dryness (jafāf), viscosity (luzūja), brittleness (hashāsha), subtlety (laṭāfa), and density (kathāfa) are relative to these four. He then gives detailed definitions. Wetness (balla) is the extraneous moisture on the surface of a body; if it penetrates the interior, it is of intense absorption; otherwise dryness is simply the privation of wetness. Viscosity (luzūja) is a quality that allows easy shaping but difficult separation and continuity of extension; it results from mixing much moist with a little dry. Its opposite, brittleness (hashāsha), is difficult shaping and easy separation. Subtlety (laṭāfa) is a term that can mean multiple things: thinness of consistency (like water and air), ease of division into very small parts (like sugar candy), quickness of being affected by a contact (like a rose), and transparency (like the celestial spheres). Density (kathāfa) is the opposite in all these meanings. The passage ends noting that smoothness and roughness are controversial—whether they are proper tactile qualities or not—but this is just a cue, as the detail on them comes in the next passage.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Subtlety (al-laṭāfa) only refers to thinness of consistency.
Correction: The author explicitly states that subtlety can also mean ease of division into very small parts, quickness of being affected, and transparency.

Checkpoint
What is the cause of viscosity (al-luzūja) according to the passage?
A) Mixing much dry with little moist.
B) Mixing much moist with little dry.
C) Pure moisture without dryness.
D) The absence of wetness.

Key Takeaway
Secondary tactile qualities like wetness, viscosity, and subtlety are defined in relation to the primary qualities of moistness and dryness, and subtlety has multiple distinct meanings.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You experience wetness, viscosity, subtlety, and density every day without thinking. Which of these qualities most reminds you of the intricate design of Allah's creation, and why do you rarely pause to reflect on it? How can you train yourself to see His signs in the physical properties of things?
?
The author warns that dryness is simply the absence of wetness. Consider your own spiritual state: are there areas where you are 'dry' because you have neglected the 'moisture' of remembrance and obedience? What specific step will you take today to restore that moisture?
?
Subtlety (laṭāfa) can mean transparency, as in the celestial sphere. When you look up at the sky, does its transparency lead you to contemplate the One who created it without pillars? If not, what is blocking your vision?

Numbing and Pricking as Tactile Qualities
Author defines numbing and pricking as tactile qualities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definitions of numbing (takhdīr) and pricking (mazaʿ) as tactile qualities and their physiological basis.

Core Idea
Numbing cools the spirit; pricking causes many tiny separations felt as one pain.

The author describes two specific tactile qualities: numbing and pricking. Numbing alters the spirit that carries sensation, rendering the organ unresponsive; pricking involves numerous small separations perceived as a single pain. Let's examine each definition closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on numbing
Read the first sentence describing numbing. Notice how the definition involves cooling of the organ and changes to the spirit (rūḥ), making the psychic powers unable to use it.
The term 'spirit' here refers to a subtle substance, not the soul.
2
Understand pricking
Read the definition of pricking. Note that it is a penetrating, subtle quality causing many small separations in the connection (ittiṣāl), felt collectively as one pain.
The word 'mazaʿ' may also be read as 'madaʿ' (itching) in some manuscripts.
3
Compare the two definitions
Compare how both qualities affect perception: numbing prevents sensation, while pricking creates a composite sensation. Identify the key mechanisms for each.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والتخدير وهو تبريد للعضو بحيث يصير جوهر الروح الحامل قوة الحس والحركة إليه بارداً في مزاجه غليظاً في جوهر، فلا تستعملها القوى النفسانية، ويجعل مزاج العضو كذلك، فلا يقبل تأثير القوى النفسانية. والمزع وهي كيفية نفاده جداً لطيفة تحدث في الاتصال تفرقاً كثير العدد، متقارب الوضع، صغير المقدار، فلا يحس كل واحد بانفراده ويحس بالجملة كالوجع الواحد. وأما الملاسة والخشونة فالجمهور على أنها من الكيفيات الملوسة، وقال الإمام بل من الوضع، لأن الملاسة عبارة عن استواء أجزاء الجسم في الوضع بحيث لا يكون بعضها أرفع وبعضها أخفض، والخشونة عن اختلافها، ورد بأنه يجوز أن يكون ذلك مبدأهما لأنفسهما.
And numbing (at-takhdīr): it is a cooling of the organ such that the substance of the spirit that carries the power of sensation and movement to it becomes cold in its temperament, thick in its substance, so the psychic powers do not use it, and it makes the temperament of the organ likewise, so it does not accept the influence of the psychic powers. And the pricking (al-mazaʿ): it is a very penetrating, subtle quality that occurs in the connection, a separation many in number, close in position, small in amount, so each one is not felt individually and is felt collectively like a single pain. As for smoothness (al-malāsa) and roughness (al-khushūna), the majority hold that they are among the tactile qualities. The Imam said: 'Rather they are from position (al-waḍʿ), because smoothness is the evenness of the parts of the body in position such that some are not higher and others lower, and roughness is their difference.' And it was replied that it is permissible for that to be their principle for themselves.
Source pages p. 236

Commentary
This passage defines two additional tactile qualities: numbing (takhdīr) and pricking (mazaʿ), then addresses smoothness and roughness. Numbing is described as a cooling of the organ that changes the spirit (rūḥ) carrying sensation and movement, making it cold and thick, so the psychic powers cannot use it. Pricking is a subtle, penetrating quality that causes many small separations in the continuity of the body, not felt individually but collectively as one pain. Then the author turns to smoothness and roughness. The majority of scholars (jumhūr) consider these as tactile qualities (kayfiyyāt mamlūsa). However, the Imam (al-Ghazālī) argued they are positional (waḍʿī) because smoothness is evenness in the position of parts, and roughness is their unevenness. The objection raised is that it is possible that evenness/unevenness is simply the origin (mabdaʾ) for them, not their essence; the text does not resolve the debate.

Key Terms
التخدير
المزع
القوى النفسانية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does numbing do to the spirit (rūḥ)?
A) It makes it hot and thin.
B) It makes it cold and thick.
C) It breaks it into many parts.
D) It makes it more sensitive.

Key Takeaway
Numbing and pricking are two tactile qualities defined by their effects on the spirit or on bodily continuity, each producing a distinct sensory experience.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever experienced a 'numbing' of your spirit where you became insensitive to guidance or conscience? What caused it to become cold and thick, and how can you restore its receptivity?
?
Think of a time when many small frustrations or sins accumulated into a single overwhelming pain. How does the description of pricking as many tiny separations felt collectively help you understand your experience, and what will you do to address the small separations before they accumulate?

The Nature of Smoothness and Roughness
Author reports disagreement on smoothness/roughness as tactile vs. positional.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the disagreement between the majority of scholars and al-Ghazālī on whether smoothness and roughness are tactile qualities or positional relations.

Core Idea
Is smoothness a tactile quality or a matter of position? Scholars disagree.

Smoothness and roughness are commonly regarded as tactile qualities, but the Imam al-Ghazālī proposed they are positional. This passage presents both views along with a response. Let's explore the arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on smoothness and roughness
Read the section starting from 'وأما الملاسة والخشونة'. Identify the two positions: the majority view (tactile) and al-Ghazali's view (positional).
Imam here refers to al-Ghazālī.
2
Analyze the majority view
Note that the majority (jumhūr) considers smoothness and roughness as tactile qualities (kayfiyyāt mamlūsa). This means they are perceived by touch.
3
Analyze al-Ghazali's view and the response
Read al-Ghazali's argument that they are positional, based on evenness/unevenness of parts. Then note the reply: evenness/unevenness could be the origin (mabdaʾ) of these qualities, not the qualities themselves.
The reply suggests a compromise but does not settle the issue.
