Two Arguments from al-Shifa for Alternate Causes of Whiteness
Presenting two arguments from al-Shifa for whiteness without air mixing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two arguments from Avicenna's al-Shifa that purport to show whiteness can occur without air mixing, and why these arguments are not conclusive.

Core Idea
Color diversity suggests multiple causes, but the proofs from al-Shifa are not decisive.

Sanusi presents two arguments drawn from Avicenna's al-Shifa to challenge the theory that whiteness necessarily arises from air mixing with transparent substances. The first argument observes the varied paths from white to black, while the second concerns color reflection. As we examine these arguments, we will also see Sanusi's own critical evaluation of their strength.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage carefully. Notice the two arguments are introduced with 'أحدهما' and 'ثانيهما'. After reading, compare with the English translation.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the first argument
The first argument is about the different ways colors can change from white to black. What does this variety imply about the composition of colors?
Look for the three paths: to redness then to [؟], to redness then to greyness, to greenness then to indigo.
3
Identify the second argument
The second argument is about reflection. If color differences were due to mixing, then red and green should reflect only whiteness. But what does experience show?
Note that blackness does not reflect when isolated, but might reflect when mixed.
4
Understand Sanusi's evaluation
After presenting both arguments, Sanusi comments that they are more effective in disproving one theory than another. What is his point?
He says the implications are not decisive because there are alternative possibilities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومما استدل به في الشفاء على حصول البياض من غير اختلاط الهواء بالمشف أمران :
أحدهما اختلاف طرق الاتجاه من البياض إلى السواد حيث يكون من البياض تارة إلى الغيرة (٥) ، ثم العودية ثم السواد، وتارة إلى الحمرة ثم القتمة، ثم السواد، وتارة إلى الخضرة ثم النيلية ثم السواد فإنه يدل على اختلاف ما تركب عنه الألوان، إذ لو لم يكن إلا السواد والبياض، ولم يكن البياض إلا بمخالطة الهواء للأجزاء الشفافة لم يكن في تركب السواد والبياض إلا الأخذ في طريق واحد وإن وقع فيه اختلاف فبالشدة والضعف.
وثانيهما: انعكاس الحمرة والخضرة، ونحو ذلك من الألوان فإنه لو كان اختلاف الألوان لاختلاط الشفاف بغيره لوجب أن لا ينعكس من الأحمر والأخضر إلا البياض، لأن السواد لا ينعكس بحكم التجربة. ودلالة هذين الوجهين على أن سبب اختلاف الألوان لا يجب أن يكون هو التركب من السواد والبياض، أظهر من دلالته على أن سبب البياض لا يجب أن يكون مخالطة الهواء للأجزاء الشفافة أن في الملازمتين نظراً، لجواز أن يقع مع تركب السواد والبياض على أنحاء مختلفة، وأن ينعكس السواد عند الاختلاط والامتزاج، وإن لم ينعكس عند الانفراد.
Among what he argued with in al-Shifa for the occurrence of whiteness without mixing of air with the transparent are two things: First: the difference in the paths of transition from whiteness to blackness, since it goes from whiteness sometimes to redness, then to [؟], then to blackness; sometimes to redness then to greyness then to blackness; sometimes to greenness then to indigo then to blackness. This indicates the difference of that from which colors are composed. For if there were only blackness and whiteness, and whiteness were only by mixing of air with transparent parts, then in the composition of blackness and whiteness there would be only one path, and if there were difference, it would be by intensity and weakness. Second: the reflection of redness and greenness and the like of colors. For if the difference of colors were due to mixing of the transparent with something else, then from red and green there should not reflect except whiteness, because blackness does not reflect, by the rule of experience. And the implication of these two faces that the cause of difference of colors does not have to be composition from blackness and whiteness is more evident than their implication that the cause of whiteness does not have to be mixing of air with transparent parts. For the two implications have consideration, because it is possible that composition from blackness and whiteness occurs in different ways, and that blackness reflects upon mixing and blending, even if it does not reflect when isolated.
Source pages p. 258

Commentary
This passage presents two arguments from Avicenna's al-Shifa to support the possibility of whiteness arising from causes other than air mixing. The first argument observes that the transition from whiteness to blackness takes multiple intermediate colors (redness, greyness, greenness, indigo), which suggests that colors are composed from more than just black and white; if only black and white existed, only one path would be expected, varying only in intensity. The second argument notes that when red and green reflect, they do not produce only whiteness—the expected result if color differences were solely due to mixing transparent substances with something else. The author then evaluates that these two points more strongly show that the cause of color differences is not necessarily composition from black and white, but they are less conclusive about the specific issue of whiteness being from air mixing. He remarks that the implications are open to consideration, because black and white could still compose colors in varied ways, and blackness might reflect under mixing conditions even if not in isolation. Thus the proofs are not decisive; they only suggest the possibility of alternative causes.

Key Terms
الغيرة
القتمة
النيلية
الانعكاس
التركب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The two arguments from al-Shifa conclusively prove that whiteness does not require air mixing.
Correction: Sanusi shows that the arguments are not conclusive; they only suggest possibility, and there are alternative explanations.

Checkpoint
According to the first argument, what does the variety of paths from whiteness to blackness indicate?
A) That blackness is the only real color.
B) That colors are composed from more than just black and white.
C) That whiteness always arises from air mixing.
D) That transition paths are irrelevant.

Key Takeaway
The two arguments from al-Shifa suggest alternative causes for whiteness, but they are not decisive proofs; Sanusi shows they have theoretical loopholes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that a certain good or bad outcome in your life had a single, obvious cause, only to later realize there were multiple factors at play? How does this lesson's reminder that whiteness could arise from multiple causes challenge you to hesitate before assigning a single cause to Allah's decrees?
?
Sanusi presents two arguments as possible but not decisive, showing intellectual humility. In what area of your religious knowledge are you most dogmatic? Would you be willing to acknowledge that your certainty might be based on inconclusive evidence?
?
If whiteness could come from different causes, then what other assumptions about the physical or spiritual world might you be holding too tightly? This week, identify one belief about how Allah works in your life that you assume is the only way, and ask yourself: 'Could there be another cause or path I haven't considered?'

Refuting the Denial of Real Whiteness
Refuting the claim that only blackness is real and whiteness is imaginary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection that whiteness is unreal because its substrate accepts other colors is refuted by distinguishing between real and imaginary whiteness and by denying the implication that acceptance implies absence.

Core Idea
Not every change in appearance refutes reality; what departs may be real, and what persists may be imaginary.

This passage presents a subtle philosophical objection against the reality of whiteness, based on the observation that a white surface can later become colored. The objector argues that if whiteness were real, its place would not accept other colors. Sanusi refutes this by distinguishing between real and imaginary whiteness and by clarifying what 'acceptance' truly means. Let's examine the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing it. Then read the translation. Let the flow of objection and response come through.
Notice the compressed dialectical style.
2
Identify the objection
The objector denies whiteness and affirms only blackness. Find the reason given: whiteness is removed and its place accepts colors, unlike blackness.
3
Understand the refutation
The refutation states two possibilities: real whiteness may depart, leaving an imaginary whiteness that persists due to its cause. This shows that change does not disprove reality.
Distinguish between real (حقيقي) and imaginary (تخيلي).
4
Analyze the objection-response dialogue
The objector raises a counter: acceptance implies absence. The response denies the minor premise (only other colors are accepted) and the major premise if acceptance means possibility.
Focus on the logical moves: denial of minor and major premises.
5
Summarize the key takeaway
Reflect on how the argument protects the reality of sensory qualities against a skeptical objection based on change.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد اقتصر بعضهم على نفي البياض، وأثبت السواد تمسكاً بأن البياض ينسلخ ويقبل محله الألوان بخلاف السواد، ورد بعد ثبوت الأمرين بأنه : يجوز أن يكون الحقيقي مفارقاً والتخيلي لازماً، لزوال سبب الأول، ولزوم سبب التخيلي، لا يقال محل البياض يقبل محله الألوان وكل ما يقبل الشيء فهو عار عنه، ضرورة تنافي القبول والفعل لأنا نجيب بمنع الصغرى فإنه إنما يقبل ما سوى البياض الذي فيه فلا يلزم إلا عراؤه عنه ، وإن أريد بالقبول معنى الإمكان بحيث يجامع الفعل منعنا الكبرى وهو ظاهر.
And some confined themselves to denying whiteness and affirmed blackness, clinging to the fact that whiteness is removed and its place accepts colors, unlike blackness. It is refuted after establishing the two matters that it is permissible that the real one is separate and the imaginary is necessary, due to the removal of the cause of the first and the necessity of the cause of the imaginary. One cannot say that the place of whiteness accepts colors, and that anything that accepts something is devoid of it, because of the necessary contradiction between acceptance and actuality. For we reply by denying the minor premise: it only accepts other than the whiteness that is in it, so only its absence from that other is necessary. And if by acceptance is meant the meaning of possibility such that it coexists with actuality, we deny the major premise, and it is clear.
Source pages p. 259

Commentary
This passage addresses an objection: some philosophers deny the reality of whiteness, affirming only blackness, arguing that whiteness is removed and its substrate accepts other colors, unlike blackness. The refutation first states that both real and imaginary whiteness are possible: real whiteness may depart, while an imaginary (perceptual) whiteness remains due to a persisting cause. The objection that a substrate receiving a color must be devoid of that color is countered by distinguishing: the substrate accepts colors other than the whiteness already present, so it is only devoid of those other colors, not of whiteness itself. If 'acceptance' is interpreted as mere possibility coexisting with actuality, then the major premise (that acceptance implies absence) is denied. This shows that the argument for blackness alone fails.

Key Terms
البياض
السواد
القبول
الفعل
الحقيقي
التخيلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That acceptance of colors by a substrate implies that the substrate is devoid of the accepted color.
Correction: The substrate only accepts colors other than the one it already possesses, so it is only devoid of those other colors, not of the original color. Alternatively, acceptance can mean possibility coexisting with actuality, which does not imply absence.

Checkpoint
How is the objection that the substrate of whiteness accepts colors and therefore must be devoid of whiteness refuted?
A) By denying that whiteness exists.
B) By stating that the substrate only accepts colors other than the whiteness in it.
C) By claiming that blackness is not a color.
D) By arguing that acceptance is irrelevant.

Key Takeaway
The reality of a color is not negated by the fact that its substrate can receive other colors, because change does not imply unreality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Can you identify a belief or trait you once thought was 'whiteness'—real yet changeable—that you later dismissed as unreal simply because it faded? How does this lesson challenge that dismissal?
?
What is something in your life that you cling to as 'blackness'—seemingly permanent and therefore real—that might actually be imaginary? Are you willing to examine its cause?
?
When you face instability in your own character or circumstances, do you default to assuming that what changes was never real? How would your response change if you accepted that change does not negate reality?

Conditional Nature of Acceptance and Description
Explaining the conditional proposition regarding acceptance and description.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the conditional nature of acceptance and how it resolves the apparent contradiction between being a substrate and being devoid of a quality.

Core Idea
A conditional proposition does not imply eternal impossibility—only while the condition lasts.

This lesson addresses a subtle objection in theological logic: if something that accepts a quality must be devoid of it, then it could never acquire that quality. Sanusi replies by clarifying that the proposition is conditional—it only applies as long as the state of acceptance persists. This distinction preserves the possibility of change.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage slowly. Identify the objection starting with 'وقد يقال' and the answer with 'لأن القضية مشروطة'.
2
Understand the conditional nature
Notice that the answer hinges on the word 'مشروطة' (conditional). The impossibility is tied to the state of acceptance, not an absolute rule.
3
Reflect on the theological implication
Consider how this reasoning allows a substrate to both accept and later possess a quality without logical contradiction.
This is crucial for understanding attributes and their relation to essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد يقال : لو كان القابل للشيء واجب العراء عنه لكان الاتصاف به وهو باطل وليس بشيء لأن القضية مشروطة، فلا يلزم إلا امتناع الاتصاف ما دام قابلا وهو حق .
And it may be said: if that which accepts a thing were necessarily devoid of it, then being described with it would be impossible, but that is false and is nothing, because the proposition is conditional, so only the impossibility of description as long as it accepts is necessary, and that is true.
Source pages p. 259

Commentary
This passage presents a further objection and its answer. The objection argues: if something that accepts a quality must be devoid of that quality, then it could never be described as having that quality, which is absurd. The answer clarifies that the proposition is conditional: it only prevents description as long as the receiving state lasts. Thus, it is not a universal impossibility, but a temporary one. This subtle point preserves the possibility that a substrate can both accept and possess the quality at different times.

Key Terms

الاتصاف

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the conditional proposition implies an eternal impossibility; it only applies during the state of acceptance.
Correction: The conditional nature means the impossibility is limited to the duration of the acceptance state, not permanent.

Checkpoint
What does the conditional proposition about acceptance imply?
A) That description is always impossible.
B) That description is impossible as long as acceptance continues.
C) That acceptance and description are unrelated.
D) That description is always possible.

Key Takeaway
The conditional proposition ensures that the impossibility of description is limited to the state of acceptance, not eternal.

Proof of Life's Non-Conditionality on Structure
Al-Taftazani relays a proof against conditionality of life on structure and its responses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the proof that life cannot be conditional on structure, the objection raised, and the two responses (inherence in the composite and simultaneity).

Core Idea
Life's conditionality on structure leads to impossibility or circularity; responses preserve the doctrine.

This passage presents a refined argument from the mutakallimun against the claim that life depends on bodily structure. The proof proceeds by elimination, and the responses show how life can relate to the body without absurdity. As you read, notice the logical care: each possibility is examined and then addressed.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof's two cases
The proof considers that if life were conditional on structure, either a single life inheres in two parts or a life inheres in each part. Both lead to impossibility.
Identify the two impossibilities: accident in two loci and circularity/preference without preferrer.
2
Understand the objection and counter
An objector suggests life might inhere in only some parts due to external reasons. The answer shows this would make only that part living, not the whole structure.
This blocks a potential escape from the proof.
3
Study the two responses
Two accepted answers are given: (1) life inheres in the composite whole, which is a single locus; (2) life inheres in each part but with simultaneity, avoiding circularity.
Notice how each response preserves the doctrine without logical flaw.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واستدل بعض المتكلمين على امتناع كون الحياة مشروطة بالبنية بأنها لو اشترطت فإما أن تقوم بالجزئين من البنية حياة واحدة فيلزم قيام العرض بأكثر من محل واحد وقد مر بطلانه، وإما أن يقوم بكل جزء حياة، وحينئذ إما أن يكون القيام بكل جزء مشروطاً بالقيام بالآخر فيلزم الدور أو لا فيلزم الرجحان بلا مرجح لتماثل الأجزاء واتحاد حقيقة الحياة. لا يقال لا يجوز أن يقوم بالبعض فقط لأسباب مرجحة من الخارج. لأنا نقول : فيكون الحي ذلك البعض لا البنية المؤلفة. وأجيب بأنها تقوم بالمجموع الذي هو البنية المؤلفة، وليس هذا من قيام العرض بمحلين على ما سبق، أو يقوم بكل جزء حياة، ويكون اشتراط كل بالآخر بطريق المعية دون التقدم فلا يلزم الدور المحال.
And some mutakallimun argued for the impossibility of life being conditional on structure: if it were conditional, then either a single life would inhere in the two parts of the structure, necessitating the inherence of an accident in more than one locus—which has already passed as impossible—or a life would inhere in each part. In that case, either the inherence in each part is conditional upon inherence in the other, necessitating circularity, or it is not, necessitating preference without a preferrer due to the similarity of the parts and the unity of the reality of life. It cannot be said that it is permissible for it to inhere in some parts only due to external preferential reasons, for we say: then the living being would be that part, not the composite structure. And it was answered that it inheres in the composite which is the structured body, and this is not of the type of an accident in two loci as previously discussed; or [it was answered] that a life inheres in each part, and the conditionality of each upon the other is by way of simultaneity (ma'iyyah) without priority, so no impossible circularity arises.
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage presents a proof from some mutakallimun against the claim that life is conditional on bodily structure, along with a counter-objection and then the answer. The proof proceeds by analyzing two possibilities if life were conditional on structure: (1) Either a single life (hayāt wāḥida) inheres in two parts of the structure—this would require an accident (al-ʿaraḍ) to inhere in more than one locus (maḥall), which has already been established as impossible. (2) Or a separate life inheres in each part. In this case, two sub-possibilities arise: (a) The inherence in each part is conditional upon inherence in the other—this leads to circularity (dawr). (b) The inherence is not conditional upon the other—then there is no reason (tarjīḥ) to prefer inherence in both parts over one, since the parts are similar and the reality of life is one; so this leads to preference without a preferrer (tarjīḥ bilā murajjiḥ), which is impossible. The objector then raises a possibility: life could inhere in some parts only, due to external factors giving preference. The answer is: if life only inheres in some parts, then only those parts constitute the living entity, not the composite structure. That would contradict the claim that life is conditional on the entire structure. The passage then gives two accepted answers: First, life inheres in the composite whole (al-majmūʿ) which is the structured body. This is not like an accident in two loci because the composite is a single unit. Second, life does inhere in each part, but the conditionality of each part upon the other is by way of simultaneity (maʿiyyah) without temporal priority, so there is no circularity (since circularity requires priority). This is a sophisticated defense of the view that life does not require material conditions.

Key Terms
العرض
المحل
الدور
الترجيح بلا مرجح
المعية
المجموع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That conditionality of life on structure would necessarily imply an accident in multiple loci.
Correction: The response clarifies that life inheres in the composite whole, which is a single locus in the relevant sense.

Checkpoint
According to the proof presented, what would follow if life inhered in each part of the structure without mutual conditionality?
A) Circularity.
B) Preference without a preferrer.
C) An accident in multiple loci.
D) Simultaneity.

Key Takeaway
The passage demonstrates that life's relation to structure can be understood as inherence in the composite or as simultaneous conditionality, thereby avoiding the logical pitfalls of circularity and preference without a preferrer.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your life is not ultimately dependent on your physical structure, as the proof shows. If your body is only a vessel, how does that affect the way you treat your body today? Will you prioritize your soul's health over your physical comfort, even when they conflict?
?
The argument shows that believing life is conditional on structure leads to absurdities. Are there beliefs you hold about your own worth or identity that are similarly built on shaky foundations? Will you examine them with the same logical rigor as the mutakallimun, and correct them if needed?
?
If life is a gift directly from Allah, not a product of your body, then your life is entirely in His hands. When you face fear of death or loss, can you truly internalize that your life is already secured by Him? How will you remind yourself of this truth the next time you feel anxious about your health?

The Reality of Perception as Presence at the Intellect
Al-Taftazani defines perception and explains its nature as presence of the thing at the intellect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that perception, according to the investigating philosophers, is the presence of the perceived thing or its form at the intellect, and not its external existence.

Core Idea
Perception is not external existence but intellectual presence.

This passage establishes the foundational definition of perception in Islamic philosophy. Al-Taftazani clarifies that when we perceive something, what occurs is a form in the intellect that represents the thing, not the thing itself in its external reality. This distinction is crucial for understanding the nature of knowledge and the difference between intellectual and sensory perception. Let's follow Al-Taftazani's argument as he explains the settled view of the philosophers.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Al-Taftazani uses a very technical style with references to Avicenna.
2
Identify the central claim
Find the sentence that states the reality of perception: 'حقيقة إدراك الشيء حضوره عند العقل' (the reality of perception of a thing is its presence at the intellect). Note how he distinguishes this from external existence.
He says: ليس بوجوده العيني (it is not its external existence).
3
Understand the division of perception
Look for the division into perception without an instrument (intellectual) and with an instrument (sensory). See how the example of sight and common sense are given.
The phrase 'بغير آلة' and 'بآلة' are key.
4
Examine the commentary on Avicenna
Read Al-Taftazani's explanation of the quote from al-Isharat and his handling of the apparent tautology. Notice the distinction between verbal repetition (for intellectual) and conceptual repetition (for sensory).
He resolves it by saying presence before the soul is presence before the sense.
5
Reflect on the verification argument
Consider the argument that perception is a new state of distinction and manifestation, and that this is the occurrence of a form in the intellect. Note the dismissal of the objection about attributes.
This solidifies the definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الأول
حقيقة الإدراك
قال : ومنها الإدراك ، وبيانه في مباحث :
المبحث الأول : لا خفاء أنا إذا أدركنا شيئاً كان له تميز وظهور عند العقل، وليس ذلك بوجوده العيني إذ كثيراً ما يدرك ولا وجود أو يوجد ولا إدراك، بل يوجده العقلى، وهو المعنى بالصورة، فحقيقة إدراك الشيء حضوره عند العقل.
أي من الكيفيات النفسانية الإدراك، وقد سبق نبذ من الكلام فيه، والذي استقر عليه رأي المحققين من الفلاسفة أن حقيقة إدراك الشيء حضوره عند العقل، إما بنفسه وإما بصورته المنتزعة، أو الحاصلة ابتداء المرتسمة في العقل الذي هو المدرك أو آلته التي بها الإدراك، وهذا معنى ما قال في الإشارات إدراك الشيء هو أن تكون حقيقته متمثلة عند المدرك يشاهدها ما به يدرك، على أن المراد بتمثل الحقيقة حضورها بنفسها أو بمثالها سواء كان المثال منتزعاً من أمر خارج أو متحصلا ابتداء وسواء كان منطبع في ذات المدرك، أو في آلته والمراد بالمشاهدة مطلق الحضور، وفي قوله يشاهدها مابه يدرك تنبيه على انقسام الإدراك إلى ما يكون بغير آلة فيكون ارتسام الصورة في ذات المدرك، وإلى ما يكون بآلة فيكون في محل الحس، كما في الإبصار بحصول الصورة في الرطوبة الجليدية، أو في المجاور كإدراك الحس المشترك بحصول الصورة الخيالية في محل متصل به والمراد بالمشاهدة مجرد الحضور على ما هو معناها اللغوي لا الإبصار وإدراك عين الشيء الخارجي على ما هو المتعارف ليلزم فساد التفسير، نعم تضمنت العبارة في جانب الإدراك العقلي تكراراً بحسب اللفظ كأنه قيل حضور عند المدرك حال الحضور عنده لأن ما به الإدراك العقلي هو ذات المدرك، وفي جانب الإدراك الحسي تكرار بحسب المعنى حتى كأن هناك حضورين أحدهما عند المدرك والآخر عند الآلة وليس كذلك بل الحضور عند النفس هو الحضور عند الحس وتحقيق المقام أنا إذا أدركنا شيئاً فلا خفاء في أنه يحصل لنا حال لم تكن وتكاد تشهد الفطرة بأنها بحصول أمر لم يكن لا بزوال أمر كان وما ذاك إلا تميزاً وظهوراً لذلك الشيء عند العقل، وليس ذلك بوجوده في الخارج إذ كثيراً ما ندرك ما لا وجود له في الخارج من المعدومات بل الممتنعات، وكثيراً ما يوجد الشيء في الخارج ولا يدركه العقل مع تشوقه إليه، بل بوجوده في العقل بمعنى أن يحصل فيه أثر يناسب ذلك الشيء بحيث لو وجد في الخارج لكان إياه، وهذا هو المعنى بحصول الصورة وحضورها وتمثلها وارتسامها ووصول النفس إليها ، ونحو ذلك، ولا يفهم من إدراك الشيء سواه والاعتراض بأن الإدراك صفة المدرك والحصول ونحوه صفة الصورة مما لا يلتفت إليه عند المحققين سواء جعلنا الإدراك مصدراً بمعنى الفاعل أو المفعول.
The First Investigation: The Reality of Perception. He said: Among them is perception, and its explanation is in investigations: The First Investigation: There is no hidden fact that when we perceive something, it has distinction and manifestation at the intellect, and that is not its external existence because often something is perceived while it has no existence, or exists while there is no perception. Rather, it is its intellectual existence, and that is what is meant by form. So the reality of perception of a thing is its presence at the intellect. That is, perception is among the psychic qualities, and a small part of the discussion about it has preceded. What the view of the investigating philosophers has settled upon is that the reality of perception of a thing is its presence at the intellect, either by itself or by its abstracted form, or the form originally acquired and impressed in the intellect which is the perceiver or its instrument by [the form of] which perception takes place. This is the meaning of what was said in al-Isharat: 'Perception of a thing is that its reality be represented before the perceiver, witnessed by that by which it perceives.' But the intended meaning of the representation of the reality is its presence by itself or by its likeness, whether the likeness is abstracted from an external matter or originally acquired, and whether it is impressed in the essence of the perceiver or in its instrument. And by 'witnessing' is intended absolute presence. In his statement 'witnessed by that by which it perceives' there is a hint at the division of perception into that which is without an instrument—so it is the impression of the form in the essence of the perceiver—and that which is with an instrument—so it is in the sense organ, as in sight by the occurrence of the form in the vitreous humor, or in the adjacent as in the perception of the common sense by the occurrence of the imaginative form in a place connected to it. And by 'witnessing' is intended mere presence, according to its literal meaning, not sight and perception of the external thing itself as is customarily understood, lest the interpretation become corrupt. Indeed, the expression contains in the aspect of intellectual perception a repetition in terms of wording, as if it were said 'presence before the perceiver at the moment of its presence before him' because that by which intellectual perception [occurs] is the essence of the perceiver. And in the aspect of sensory perception there is a repetition in terms of meaning, as if there were two presences: one before the perceiver and the other before the instrument. But it is not so; rather, presence before the soul is presence before the sense. The verification of the position is that when we perceive something, there is no hidden fact that a state occurs for us that was not, and nature almost testifies that it is due to the occurrence of something that was not, not by the removal of something that was. And that is nothing but distinction and manifestation of that thing before the intellect. And that is not its existence in the external world, because we often perceive what has no existence in the external world, from non-existents, even impossibles, and often a thing exists in the external world and the intellect does not perceive it, despite its longing for it. Rather, it is its existence in the intellect, meaning that an effect appropriate to that thing occurs in it such that if it existed externally it would be identical to it. This is the meaning of the occurrence of the form, its presence, its representation, its impression, and the soul's reaching it, and so on. Nothing else is understood from perception of a thing. And the objection that perception is an attribute of the perceiver, while occurrence and the like are attributes of the form, is not heeded by the investigating scholars, whether we make 'perception' a verbal noun in the sense of the agent or the object.
Source pages p. 299

Commentary
This passage defines the reality of perception and elaborates on its nature. It begins with the statement that perception involves a distinction and manifestation at the intellect, and that this is not the external existence of the perceived thing, because we often perceive non-existent things and fail to perceive existent ones. Rather, it is the intellectual existence, i.e., the form. The commentator then explains that the settled view of the investigating philosophers is that perception is the presence of the thing at the intellect, either by itself (as in intellectual perception of self-evident truths) or by its form (abstracted from matter or originally acquired). This is the meaning of the statement from Avicenna's al-Isharat: 'Perception of a thing is that its reality be represented before the perceiver, witnessed by that by which it perceives.' The passage clarifies that 'witnessing' here means absolute presence, not sight. The phrase 'that by which it perceives' indicates a division: perception can occur either directly in the intellect (without an instrument), where the form is impressed in the perceiver's essence, or through an instrument (the senses), where the form occurs in the sense organ. The example of sight is given: the form occurs in the vitreous humor. For the common sense, the imaginative form occurs in a connected place. The passage then addresses an apparent tautology in Avicenna's definition: for intellectual perception, the wording seems redundant ('presence before the perceiver at the moment of its presence'); for sensory perception, there is conceptual redundancy as if there are two presences. But the author argues that this is not a real problem: intellectual perception's instrument is the essence itself, so the repetition is only verbal; sensory perception involves the sense organ, but presence before the soul is the same as presence before the sense. The verification is that perception is a new state of distinction and manifestation at the intellect, not the external existence, because we perceive non-existents and sometimes fail to perceive existents. Rather, it is the existence of an effect in the intellect that corresponds to the object, i.e., the form. The passage concludes by dismissing the objection that perception is an attribute of the perceiver while the form is an attribute of something else; for the investigators, the terminology is flexible.

Key Terms
الإدراك
حقيقة
العقل
الصورة
الحضور
التمثل
الارتسام
المشاهدة
الحس
الآلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think perception is the same as external vision or that the presence of the form implies a physical copy.
Correction: The passage clarifies it is intellectual presence, not physical duplication.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the reality of perception?
A) The external existence of the perceived thing.
B) The presence of the thing or its form at the intellect.
C) The act of seeing with the eye.
D) The judgment that the thing exists.

Key Takeaway
The reality of perception is the presence of the thing or its form at the intellect, not its external existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How often do you mistake your mental forms for external reality? Think of a relationship or situation where your perception has been proven wrong. What will you do today to remember that your 'knowledge' is a form, not the thing itself?
?
You perceive non-existents—illusions, false ideas, evil whispers. If these are real as intellectual presences, how responsible are you for what you allow to enter your mind? Can you identify one false form you have been entertaining and resolve to replace it with truth?
?
Al-Taftazani says nature almost testifies that perception is a new state, not the removal of something. What does your soul gain from perceiving? If perception is presence, then what are you seeking when you seek knowledge: to bring truth into your intellect or to confirm your own biases?

4 min
First Objection and Answer: Knowing Heat Does Not Make the Mind Hot
Refutes the objection that knowing a quality would make the mind qualified by it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that knowledge as acquisition of a form would lead to absurdities, and the answer distinguishing between the external identity (huwiyya) and the mental form.

Core Idea
The mind does not become what it knows; forms are universal and abstract.

This lesson addresses a classic objection to the definition of knowledge as the acquisition of an equal form. If knowledge works that way, then knowing heat would make the mind hot—an obvious absurdity. The response clarifies the crucial distinction between the particular external identity and the universal mental form, showing that the objection rests on a confusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
The objection claims that if knowledge is acquiring a form equal to the object, then imagining heat or roundness would require the mind itself to become hot or round.
This leads to absurdities like the conjunction of opposites when imagining both heat and cold.
2
Identify the answer's key distinction
The answer distinguishes between the 'identity' (huwiyya) which is particular, surrounded by accidents, and effective for external attributes, and the 'form' which is universal, abstract, and not subject to external effects.
The form in the mind is not the same as the concrete reality.
3
Understand the equality between form and identity
The form is equal to the identity in the sense that if realized externally it would be that very thing, but it remains universal and abstract when in the mind.
Sometimes the intelligible is said to be the essence itself, not just equal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إشارة إلى دفع اعتراضات للإمام وغيره منها : هاهنا أن العلم لو كان حصول الصورة المساوية التي ربما تسمى ماهية الشيء لزم من تصور الحرارة والاستدارة كون القوة المدركة حارة مستديرة وكذا جميع الكيفيات، وهو مع ظهور فساده يستلزم اجتماع الضدين كالحرارة والبرودة عند تصورهما . وجوابه : أن الحار ما قام به هوية الحرارة لا صورته ، وماهيته وكذا جميع الصفات، وفرق ما بينهما ،ظاهر فإن الهوية جزئية محفوفة بالعوارض فاعلة للصفات الخارجية والصورة كلية مجردة لا تلحقها الأحكام، ولا يترتب عليها الآثار. وهذا لا ينافي مساواتها للهوية، بمعنى أنها بحيث إذا وجدت في الخارج كانت إياها، ثم الماهية والحقيقة كما تطلق على الصورة المعقولة، فكذا على الموجود العيني، وبهذا الاعتبار يقال تارة إن المعقول من السماء مساو لماهيتها، وتارة إنه نفس ماهيتها فضلاً عن المساواة .
An indication of repelling objections from the Imam and others, among them: Here, that if knowledge were the acquisition of an equal form (al-ṣūra al-musāwiya) that may be called the essence (māhiyya) of a thing, then from imagining heat and roundness it would follow that the perceiving faculty is hot and round, and likewise all qualities. And this, while its falsity is apparent, entails the conjunction of opposites like heat and cold when both are imagined. Its answer: That the hot is that to which the identity (huwiyya) of heat subsists, not its form and its essence, and likewise all attributes. The difference between the two is obvious: the identity is particular, surrounded by accidents, effective for external attributes, while the form is universal, abstract, not subject to rulings, and no effects follow from it. This does not negate its equality to the identity, in the sense that if it existed externally it would be that very thing. Then essence and reality (al-ḥaqīqa) are applied to the intelligible form as well as to the external existent. By this consideration, it is sometimes said that the intelligible of heaven is equal to its essence, and sometimes that it is its very essence, let alone equality.
Source pages p. 303

Commentary
This passage contains the first objection and its answer. The objection states: if knowledge is the acquisition of a form equal to the object (like the form of heat), then imagining heat would require the perceiving faculty itself to become hot, and similarly for roundness, leading to absurdities like the conjunction of opposites (heat and cold) when both are imagined together, which is impossible. The answer distinguishes between the external particular (al-huwiyya) and the mental form (al-ṣūra). The 'hot' thing is that which has the identity of heat—the particular individual—not the form. The difference is clear: the identity is particular, surrounded by accidents, and it causes external qualities, whereas the mental form is universal and abstract, not subject to external rulings or effects. The equality between the form and the identity means that if the form were to exist externally, it would be that very identity. Essence and reality (al-ḥaqīqa) apply both to the intelligible form and to the external existent; sometimes the intelligible of a thing is said to be equal to its essence, sometimes it is said to be its very essence.

Key Terms
صورة مساوية
هوية
ماهية
جزئية
كلية
محفوفة بالعوارض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If knowledge is the form of an object, then the mind becomes qualified by that object's qualities (e.g., imagining heat makes the mind hot).
Correction: The mind only has the universal, abstract form; the external particular identity with its qualities is not transferred to the mind.

Checkpoint
In the answer to the objection, what distinguishes the mental form from the external identity (huwiyya)?
A) The form is particular and surrounded by accidents, while the identity is universal.
B) The form is universal and abstract, while the identity is particular and effective for external attributes.
C) Both are identical in every respect.
D) The form is effective for external attributes, while the identity is not.

Key Takeaway
The mental form of a thing is universal and abstract, distinct from the particular external identity; thus, knowing heat does not make the mind hot.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you learn about a sin in detail, do you ever feel that the mere thought of it pollutes your heart? How does this lesson’s distinction between knowing and being console you, and what step will you take today to act on that knowledge rather than let it remain abstract?
?
You study the attributes of Allah—His mercy, His justice. Have you ever felt that your limited understanding somehow confines Him? If the mental form is not the reality, how does that free you to seek Him more humbly, and what will you do to turn your knowledge into worship?
?
Imagine you have memorized the definition of patience. Does that make you patient? Reflect on a situation where you knew what was right but failed to embody it. How does this lesson on the difference between the mental form and the external identity challenge you to move from knowing to being, and what specific action will you take this week to bridge that gap?

Sensation as Knowledge: An Epistemological Debate
Taftazani presents the Ash'arite view, the majority's objection, and a taxonomy of cognitive terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'arite claim that sensation is knowledge, the majority's objection based on direct experience, and the taxonomy of cognitive states used to clarify the terminology.

Core Idea
Sensation and knowledge are distinct experiences, but their relationship hinges on the definition of 'knowledge'.

This passage from Taftazani's Sharh al-Maqasid dives into a classic kalam debate: is sensation identical to knowledge? Al-Ash'ari said yes, while the majority said no, pointing to a self-evident difference. To sharpen the discussion, the author then clarifies technical terms for various mental states. Let's explore the arguments and the vocabulary.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic once without analysis, then the translation. Let the author's own flow guide you.
2
Identify the main claim and refutation
Find al-Ash'ari's statement and the majority's counterargument. Notice how the author begins to respond.
The refutation is based on a 'necessary difference' (فرق ضروري) between knowledge and sensation.
3
Study the taxonomy of cognitive states
The passage lists several terms: certain judgment, opinion, compound ignorance, belief, doubt, and estimation. Note how each is defined and whether it qualifies as 'judgment' (تصديق).
Doubt and estimation are not normally considered judgments unless doubt means judgment of equality.
4
Understand the majority's objection
The majority gives examples: seeing a color vs. knowing it, hearing a sound vs. knowing it. They claim the difference is necessary and immediate.
The Ash'arite response is cut off but begins by denying that the object of sensation can be known in the same way.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : وعند الشيخ الأشعري الإحساس بالشيء علم به، فإن إرادته لا تخالف سائر العلوم إلا باعتبار المتعلق، والطريق فمردود بما نجد من الفرق بين حالتي العلم التام بالشيء والاحساس به وإن أراد أنها أنواع من العلم فلفظي مبنى على إطلاق العلم على مطلق الإدراك، وهو إنما يقال لما عدا الاحساس، وقد يخص بالأخير، أو بإدراك المركب ويسمى إدراك الجزئي، أو البسيط معرفة، أو بالتصديق الجازم المطابق الثابت، ويسمى الخالي عن الجزم ظناً، وعن المطابقة جهلاً مركباً وعن الثبات اعتقاداً وقد لا يعتبر فيه المطابقة أيضاً، ولكون الشك تردداً في الحكم والوهم ملاحظة للطرف المرجوح كان عدهما من التصديق خطأ وإن أريد بالشك الحكم بتساوي الطرفين فهو أحد الأقسام السابقة) . فالإبصار علم بالمبصرات والسماع علم بالمسموعات، وهكذا البواقي ورده الجمهور بأنا نجد فرقاً ضرورياً بين العلم التام بهذا اللون، وبين إبصاره، وهكذا بين العلم بهذا الصوت وسماعه وبين العلم بهذه الرائحة وشمها . إلى غير ذلك . وأجيب بأنا لا نسلم ان ما يتعلق به الإحساس يمكن تعلق العلم به بطريق
He said: According to Shaykh al-Ash'ari, sensation of a thing is knowledge of it, for it does not differ from other knowledges except in terms of the object. [But] the [majority's] way [of refutation] is refuted by what we find of the difference between the two states of complete knowledge of a thing and sensation of it. And if he meant that they are types of knowledge, then it is verbal [terminology], based on applying the term 'knowledge' to perception in general, but it is only said of what is beyond sensation, and may be specifically applied to the latter, or to perception of the composite (called perception of the particular) or perception of the simple (called cognition), or to certain, corresponding, and stable judgment. That which lacks certainty is called opinion; that which lacks correspondence is called compound ignorance; that which lacks stability is called belief; and correspondence may also be disregarded. And because doubt is hesitation regarding a judgment, and estimation is consideration of the weaker side, counting them as judgments is an error, unless what is meant by doubt is judgment of equality of the two sides, then it is one of the previous categories. So vision is knowledge of visible things, hearing is knowledge of audible things, and so on for the rest. The majority refuted this by saying: We find a necessary difference between complete knowledge of this color and seeing it, and similarly between knowledge of this sound and hearing it, and knowledge of this smell and smelling it, etc. And it was answered: We do not concede that what sensation is related to can be related to knowledge in a way.
Source pages p. 312

Commentary
This passage presents a key epistemological debate in Kalam: whether sensation is identical to knowledge. Ash'ari holds that sensation of something is simply knowledge of it, differing only in the object. The author then clarifies that if Ash'ari means they are types of knowledge, it is merely a verbal dispute over the term 'knowledge'. He provides a taxonomy of cognitive states: certain judgment (تصديق جازم), opinion (ظن), compound ignorance (جهل مركب), belief (اعتقاد), doubt (شك), and estimation (وهم). Doubt and estimation are not considered judgments in the strict sense unless doubt is taken as judgment of equality. The majority rejects Ash'ari's view by pointing to a direct experiential difference between complete knowledge (e.g., knowledge of a color) and sensation (e.g., seeing that color). They claim the difference is necessary (ضروري), meaning it is self-evident. The Ash'arite response, as presented, denies that the object of sensation can be the object of knowledge in the same way. The text ends with the beginning of this response, which is incomplete in this window.

Key Terms

إحساس
تصديق
ظن
جهل مركب
اعتقاد
شك
وهم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Doubt and estimation are types of judgment (تصديق) in all cases.
Correction: The passage clarifies that doubt is hesitation and estimation is leaning toward the weaker side, so they are not judgments unless doubt is defined as judgment of equality.

Checkpoint
According to the majority, what is the fundamental difference between sensation and complete knowledge?
A) Sensation is always of particulars, knowledge of universals.
B) They find a necessary (ضروري) difference between the two states.
C) Sensation requires matter while knowledge does not.
D) Knowledge is always certain while sensation is not.

Key Takeaway
The debate reveals that precise terminology—especially what counts as 'knowledge' and 'judgment'—is essential for clarity in kalam epistemology.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you mistaken a sensory experience for complete knowledge, only to discover later that your senses deceived you? How does the lesson’s distinction between sensation and certain knowledge challenge your reliance on your senses today? What practical step will you take to verify your sensory-based assumptions this week?
?
The passage shows that even scholars debated fundamental definitions of knowledge. Examine one belief you hold that you consider ‘certain’—does it meet all three criteria (certainty, correspondence, stability)? If not, are you willing to label it as opinion or belief rather than knowledge? What would it take to upgrade it?
?
Taftazani warns against counting doubt and estimation as judgments. Where in your daily life do you treat hesitation or a hunch as if it were a firm judgment? How can you discipline your mind to distinguish between true knowledge and mere leaning? Commit to one specific moment tomorrow where you will pause and categorize your thought strictly.

Al-Ash'ari's Claim: Sensation Is Knowledge
Al-Ash'ari's equation of sensation with knowledge and the initial objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Ash'ari's rationale for equating sensation with knowledge and the first objection based on a necessary difference.

Core Idea
Sensation differs from knowledge only in object, but we intuitively sense a difference.

Al-Ash'ari argued that sensation of a thing is identical to knowledge of it, differing only in the object. This lesson presents his view and the immediate objection that we find a necessary, intuitive difference between knowing something completely and merely sensing it.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Ash'ari's position
Read the opening lines stating that sensation is knowledge, differing only in the object (mut'allaq).
Notice how al-Ash'ari reduces the difference to the object of knowledge.
2
Understand the objection
The objection points to a necessary (daruri) difference between complete knowledge and sensation. Reflect on an example: knowing the essence of red vs. seeing red.
3
Consider the verbal dispute
If al-Ash'ari means they are types of knowledge, the dispute becomes verbal. The passage hints that the real issue is the definition of 'knowledge'.
This leads to the next lesson on definitions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: وعند الشيخ الأشعري الإحساس بالشيء علم به، فإن إرادته لا تخالف سائر العلوم إلا باعتبار المتعلق، والطريق فمردود بما نجد من الفرق بين حالتي العلم التام بالشيء والاحساس به وإن أراد أنها أنواع من العلم فلفظي مبنى على إطلاق العلم على مطلق الإدراك، وهو إنما يقال لما عدا الاحساس، وقد يخص بالأخير، أو بإدراك المركب ويسمى إدراك الجزئي، أو البسيط معرفة، أو بالتصديق الجازم المطابق الثابت، ويسمى الخالي عن الجزم ظناً، وعن المطابقة جهلاً مركباً وعن الثبات اعتقاداً وقد لا يعتبر فيه المطابقة أيضاً، ولكون الشك تردداً في الحكم والوهم ملاحظة للطرف المرجوح كان عدهما من التصديق خطأ وإن أريد بالشك الحكم بتساوي الطرفين فهو أحد الأقسام السابقة. فالإبصار علم بالمبصرات والسماع علم بالمسموعات، وهكذا البواقي ورده الجمهور بأنا نجد فرقاً ضرورياً بين العلم التام بهذا اللون، وبين إبصاره، وهكذا بين العلم بهذا الصوت وسماعه وبين العلم بهذه الرائحة وشمها. إلى غير ذلك. وأجيب بأنا لا نسلم ان ما يتعلق به الإحساس يمكن تعلق العلم به بطريق آخر، ولو سلم فيجوز أن يكونا علمين متخالفين بالماهية أو الهوية، وفيه ضعف. أما أولا فلأن إمكان تعلق علم آخر به ضروري. كيف وأنا نحكم عليه عدم الإحساس أيضاً. وأما ثانياً: فلأن مقصود الجمهور نفى أن تكون حقيقة إدراك الشيء بالحس حقيقة إدراكه المسمى بالعلم، بحيث لا تفاوت إلا في طريق الحصول كما في العلم بالشيء بطريق الاستدلال أو الإلهام، أو الحدس، وإما بعد تسليم كونهما نوعين مختلفين من الإدراك فيصير البحث لفظياً مبنياً على أن العلم اسم لمطلق الإدراك، أو النوع منه، والحق أن إطلاقه على الإحساس مخالف للعرف واللغة فإنه اسم لغيره من الإدراكات، وقد يخص بإدراك الكل، أو إدراك المركب فيسمى إدراك الجزئي أو إدراك البسيط معرفة وقد يخص العلم بأحد أقسام التصديق - أعني اليقين منه، وهو ما يقارن الجزم والمطابقة والثبات فيسمى غير الجازم ظناً، وغير المطابق جهلاً مركباً، وغير الثابت اعتقاد.
He said: According to al-Shaykh al-Ash'ari, sensation of a thing is knowledge of it. For it does not differ from other kinds of knowledge except in respect of the object. But the way [of knowing] is rejected by what we find of a necessary difference between the state of complete knowledge of a thing and sensation of it. And if he means that they are types of knowledge, then it is a verbal dispute based on applying the term 'knowledge' to absolute perception, whereas it is only used for what is other than sensation. And it may be specified with the latter, or with perception of the composite, called perception of the particular, or with the simple, called 'ma'rifa', or with decisive, conforming, stable assent, and that which lacks decisiveness is called 'zann', and that which lacks conformity is called 'compound ignorance', and that which lacks stability is called 'i'tiqad', and sometimes conformity is also not considered. And because doubt is hesitation in judgment, and illusion is consideration of the weaker side, counting them as assent is an error. And if by 'doubt' is meant the judgment of equality of the two sides, then it is one of the previous divisions. So vision is knowledge of visible things, hearing is knowledge of audible things, and so on for the rest. And the majority refuted it by saying: We find a necessary difference between complete knowledge of this color and seeing it, and similarly between knowledge of this sound and hearing it, and between knowledge of this smell and smelling it, and so forth. And it was answered: We do not grant that what is subject to sensation can be subject to knowledge in another way. And if it is granted, then it is possible that they are two knowledges differing in essence or identity. But this is weak. Firstly, because the possibility of another knowledge attaching to it is necessary. How so, when we also judge it to be without sensation? Secondly, because the aim of the majority was to deny that the reality of perceiving a thing by sense is the reality of perceiving it called 'knowledge', such that there is no difference except in the way of attainment, as in knowledge of a thing by reasoning, or inspiration, or intuition. But if it is granted that they are two different types of perception, then the investigation becomes verbal, based on whether 'knowledge' is a name for absolute perception or for a type of it. And the truth is that applying it to sensation is contrary to convention and language, for it is a name for other perceptions. And it may be specified with perception of the whole, or perception of the composite—so it is called perception of the particular, or perception of the simple—called 'ma'rifa', and it may be specified with one of the divisions of assent—i.e., certainty from it, which is that which is accompanied by decisiveness, conformity, and stability, so that which lacks decisiveness is called 'zann', and that which lacks conformity is called 'compound ignorance', and that which lacks stability is called 'i'tiqad'.
Source pages p. 312

Commentary
This passage presents a key epistemological debate: whether sensation (al-ihsas) is identical to knowledge (al-`ilm). The author begins by stating al-Ash'ari's position: sensation of a thing is knowledge of it, differing from other knowledge only in its object (mut`allaq). The objection is raised by noting a necessary (daruri) difference between complete knowledge of something and sensing it. If al-Ash'ari means they are types of knowledge, the dispute becomes verbal, hinging on the definition of 'knowledge'. The author then clarifies terminology: 'knowledge' in its strict sense excludes sensation; it is applied to various cognitive states such as assent (tasdiq) which can be decisive (jazm), conforming (mutabiq), stable (thabit), and these yield certainty (yaqin). Lacking one of these gives rise to zann (conjecture), compound ignorance (jahl murakkab), or belief (i`tiqad). Doubt (shakk) and illusion (wahm) are not considered assent. The author then gives examples: seeing, hearing, smelling are claimed by al-Ash'ari to be knowledge. The majority (jumhur) rejects this by pointing to the obvious difference between knowing something thoroughly and sensing it. An attempted answer is given: it may be denied that what is sensed can be known in another way; even if granted, they could be two different knowledges. But this answer is weak: first, it is obvious that one can have knowledge of something without sensing it (e.g., we judge a thing to be without sensation); second, the majority's point is that the essence of sense perception is different from the essence of knowledge. Once it is admitted they are different types of perception, the argument becomes verbal—whether to call both 'knowledge' or not. The author concludes that applying 'knowledge' to sensation is contrary to common usage and language; 'knowledge' is reserved for other perceptions, and it may be further specified as perception of the whole, the composite, or certain types of assent such as certainty. He ends by detailing the subdivisions: non-decisive is zann, non-conforming is compound ignorance, non-stable is i`tiqad.

Key Terms
الإحساس
العلم
المتعلق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary objection to al-Ash'ari's equation of sensation with knowledge?
A) Sensation and knowledge have different objects.
B) There is a necessary difference between complete knowledge and sensation.
C) Sensation is not a type of perception.
D) The term 'knowledge' cannot be applied to sensation.

Key Takeaway
Al-Ash'ari's view that sensation is knowledge is rejected by an intuitive necessary difference, reducing the dispute to a terminological one.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the difference between sensation and knowledge is necessary and intuitive, when have you confused a strong sensory experience with true understanding? How did you realize the difference?
?
The author hints the dispute might be verbal. Does that make you content with surface-level knowledge, or does it push you to seek deeper intellectual conviction beyond what you feel or see?
?
Think of a belief you hold from sense experience alone. Is it truly knowledge, or mere sensation? If it turned out false, would your 'knowledge' collapse? What does that tell you about the reliability of your senses?

Classification of Knowledge and Related Terms
Definition and classification of knowledge, certainty, conjecture, compound ignorance, belief, doubt, and illusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definitions of knowledge, certainty, conjecture, compound ignorance, belief, doubt, and illusion within Islamic epistemology.

Core Idea
Knowledge is broader than sensation; it splits into assent types and other perceptions.

After the initial objection, the author clarifies the technical definitions of key epistemological terms. This lesson unpacks the classification of knowledge into types like yaqin (certainty), zann (conjecture), jahl murakkab (compound ignorance), i'tiqad (belief), shakk (doubt), and wahm (illusion), showing how each relates to the conditions of decisiveness, conformity, and stability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditions of assent
Read the definitions of assent (tasdiq) and its three conditions: decisiveness (jazm), conformity (mutabaqa), and stability (thubut).
These conditions are used to categorize different cognitive states.
2
Distinguish the deviations
Note how lack of decisiveness gives zann, lack of conformity gives jahl murakkab, and lack of stability gives i'tiqad. Also note that shakk and wahm are not valid assent.
3
Apply the classification
Reflect on the examples: vision, hearing, smell. According to the author, these are not knowledge in the strict sense because they lack the conditions of assent.
This connects back to the debate with al-Ash'ari.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: وعند الشيخ الأشعري الإحساس بالشيء علم به، فإن إرادته لا تخالف سائر العلوم إلا باعتبار المتعلق، والطريق فمردود بما نجد من الفرق بين حالتي العلم التام بالشيء والاحساس به وإن أراد أنها أنواع من العلم فلفظي مبنى على إطلاق العلم على مطلق الإدراك، وهو إنما يقال لما عدا الاحساس، وقد يخص بالأخير، أو بإدراك المركب ويسمى إدراك الجزئي، أو البسيط معرفة، أو بالتصديق الجازم المطابق الثابت، ويسمى الخالي عن الجزم ظناً، وعن المطابقة جهلاً مركباً وعن الثبات اعتقاداً وقد لا يعتبر فيه المطابقة أيضاً، ولكون الشك تردداً في الحكم والوهم ملاحظة للطرف المرجوح كان عدهما من التصديق خطأ وإن أريد بالشك الحكم بتساوي الطرفين فهو أحد الأقسام السابقة. فالإبصار علم بالمبصرات والسماع علم بالمسموعات، وهكذا البواقي ورده الجمهور بأنا نجد فرقاً ضرورياً بين العلم التام بهذا اللون، وبين إبصاره، وهكذا بين العلم بهذا الصوت وسماعه وبين العلم بهذه الرائحة وشمها. إلى غير ذلك. وأجيب بأنا لا نسلم ان ما يتعلق به الإحساس يمكن تعلق العلم به بطريق آخر، ولو سلم فيجوز أن يكونا علمين متخالفين بالماهية أو الهوية، وفيه ضعف. أما أولا فلأن إمكان تعلق علم آخر به ضروري. كيف وأنا نحكم عليه عدم الإحساس أيضاً. وأما ثانياً: فلأن مقصود الجمهور نفى أن تكون حقيقة إدراك الشيء بالحس حقيقة إدراكه المسمى بالعلم، بحيث لا تفاوت إلا في طريق الحصول كما في العلم بالشيء بطريق الاستدلال أو الإلهام، أو الحدس، وإما بعد تسليم كونهما نوعين مختلفين من الإدراك فيصير البحث لفظياً مبنياً على أن العلم اسم لمطلق الإدراك، أو النوع منه، والحق أن إطلاقه على الإحساس مخالف للعرف واللغة فإنه اسم لغيره من الإدراكات، وقد يخص بإدراك الكل، أو إدراك المركب فيسمى إدراك الجزئي أو إدراك البسيط معرفة وقد يخص العلم بأحد أقسام التصديق - أعني اليقين منه، وهو ما يقارن الجزم والمطابقة والثبات فيسمى غير الجازم ظناً، وغير المطابق جهلاً مركباً، وغير الثابت اعتقاد.
He said: According to al-Shaykh al-Ash'ari, sensation of a thing is knowledge of it. For it does not differ from other kinds of knowledge except in respect of the object. But the way [of knowing] is rejected by what we find of a necessary difference between the state of complete knowledge of a thing and sensation of it. And if he means that they are types of knowledge, then it is a verbal dispute based on applying the term 'knowledge' to absolute perception, whereas it is only used for what is other than sensation. And it may be specified with the latter, or with perception of the composite, called perception of the particular, or with the simple, called 'ma'rifa', or with decisive, conforming, stable assent, and that which lacks decisiveness is called 'zann', and that which lacks conformity is called 'compound ignorance', and that which lacks stability is called 'i'tiqad', and sometimes conformity is also not considered. And because doubt is hesitation in judgment, and illusion is consideration of the weaker side, counting them as assent is an error. And if by 'doubt' is meant the judgment of equality of the two sides, then it is one of the previous divisions. So vision is knowledge of visible things, hearing is knowledge of audible things, and so on for the rest. And the majority refuted it by saying: We find a necessary difference between complete knowledge of this color and seeing it, and similarly between knowledge of this sound and hearing it, and between knowledge of this smell and smelling it, and so forth. And it was answered: We do not grant that what is subject to sensation can be subject to knowledge in another way. And if it is granted, then it is possible that they are two knowledges differing in essence or identity. But this is weak. Firstly, because the possibility of another knowledge attaching to it is necessary. How so, when we also judge it to be without sensation? Secondly, because the aim of the majority was to deny that the reality of perceiving a thing by sense is the reality of perceiving it called 'knowledge', such that there is no difference except in the way of attainment, as in knowledge of a thing by reasoning, or inspiration, or intuition. But if it is granted that they are two different types of perception, then the investigation becomes verbal, based on whether 'knowledge' is a name for absolute perception or for a type of it. And the truth is that applying it to sensation is contrary to convention and language, for it is a name for other perceptions. And it may be specified with perception of the whole, or perception of the composite—so it is called perception of the particular, or perception of the simple—called 'ma'rifa', and it may be specified with one of the divisions of assent—i.e., certainty from it, which is that which is accompanied by decisiveness, conformity, and stability, so that which lacks decisiveness is called 'zann', and that which lacks conformity is called 'compound ignorance', and that which lacks stability is called 'i'tiqad'.
Source pages p. 312

Commentary
This passage presents a key epistemological debate: whether sensation (al-ihsas) is identical to knowledge (al-`ilm). The author begins by stating al-Ash'ari's position: sensation of a thing is knowledge of it, differing from other knowledge only in its object (mut`allaq). The objection is raised by noting a necessary (daruri) difference between complete knowledge of something and sensing it. If al-Ash'ari means they are types of knowledge, the dispute becomes verbal, hinging on the definition of 'knowledge'. The author then clarifies terminology: 'knowledge' in its strict sense excludes sensation; it is applied to various cognitive states such as assent (tasdiq) which can be decisive (jazm), conforming (mutabiq), stable (thabit), and these yield certainty (yaqin). Lacking one of these gives rise to zann (conjecture), compound ignorance (jahl murakkab), or belief (i`tiqad). Doubt (shakk) and illusion (wahm) are not considered assent. The author then gives examples: seeing, hearing, smelling are claimed by al-Ash'ari to be knowledge. The majority (jumhur) rejects this by pointing to the obvious difference between knowing something thoroughly and sensing it. An attempted answer is given: it may be denied that what is sensed can be known in another way; even if granted, they could be two different knowledges. But this answer is weak: first, it is obvious that one can have knowledge of something without sensing it (e.g., we judge a thing to be without sensation); second, the majority's point is that the essence of sense perception is different from the essence of knowledge. Once it is admitted they are different types of perception, the argument becomes verbal—whether to call both 'knowledge' or not. The author concludes that applying 'knowledge' to sensation is contrary to common usage and language; 'knowledge' is reserved for other perceptions, and it may be further specified as perception of the whole, the composite, or certain types of assent such as certainty. He ends by detailing the subdivisions: non-decisive is zann, non-conforming is compound ignorance, non-stable is i`tiqad.

Key Terms
التصديق
اليقين
الظن
الجهل المركب
الاعتقاد
الشك
الوهم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think doubt (shakk) is a type of assent (tasdiq).
Correction: Doubt is hesitation in judgment, not a firm assent, so it is not considered tasdiq.

Checkpoint
What is the term for a decisive assent that lacks conformity to reality?
A) Zann
B) Jahl murakkab
C) I'tiqad
D) Yaqin

Key Takeaway
Knowledge in its strict sense is decisive, conforming, stable assent; other states are classified based on missing conditions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How many of your deeply held beliefs about Allah and His messenger are truly decisive, conforming, and stable? When have you felt a belief waver, and what did you do to strengthen it? Will you now seek to verify one belief that might be mere opinion?
?
The passage warns that doubt and illusion are not true assent. Think of a time you acted on a hunch or a weak thought as if it were certainty. Did it lead you astray? How can you train yourself to distinguish between genuine knowledge and mere illusion from now on?
?
You call yourself a believer (mu'min), but the passage says i'tiqad lacks stability. Is your faith stable or does it change with moods or circumstances? What concrete step will you take today to anchor your conviction so it does not shift with every wind?

Refutation of al-Ash'ari: Majority's Argument
Majority refutation and analysis of the weak answer, concluding that sensation is not knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the majority's refutation of al-Ash'ari's equation of sensation with knowledge and why the attempted defense fails.

Core Idea
Sensing and knowing are intrinsically different; calling both 'knowledge' is a linguistic error.

The majority (jumhur) refutes al-Ash'ari by pointing to the obvious difference between complete knowledge of something and sensing it, using examples like color, sound, and smell. An attempted answer that they could be two different types of knowledge is shown to be weak. The lesson concludes that the dispute is essentially verbal, but the correct usage of 'knowledge' excludes sensation.

Guided Path
1
Read the majority's objection
The passage states we find a necessary difference between complete knowledge of a color and seeing it, and similarly for sound and smell. Reflect on this intuition.
2
Examine the attempted answer
The answer denies that what is sensed can be known in another way, or grants that they could be different knowledges. The author shows the weakness of both points.
First, we can know something without sensing it. Second, admitting they are different undercuts al-Ash'ari's claim.
3
Understand the conclusion
The author concludes that applying 'knowledge' to sensation is contrary to convention and language. Knowledge is properly for other perceptions.
This settles the debate as a linguistic clarification.

قال: وعند الشيخ الأشعري الإحساس بالشيء علم به، فإن إرادته لا تخالف سائر العلوم إلا باعتبار المتعلق، والطريق فمردود بما نجد من الفرق بين حالتي العلم التام بالشيء والاحساس به وإن أراد أنها أنواع من العلم فلفظي مبنى على إطلاق العلم على مطلق الإدراك، وهو إنما يقال لما عدا الاحساس، وقد يخص بالأخير، أو بإدراك المركب ويسمى إدراك الجزئي، أو البسيط معرفة، أو بالتصديق الجازم المطابق الثابت، ويسمى الخالي عن الجزم ظناً، وعن المطابقة جهلاً مركباً وعن الثبات اعتقاداً وقد لا يعتبر فيه المطابقة أيضاً، ولكون الشك تردداً في الحكم والوهم ملاحظة للطرف المرجوح كان عدهما من التصديق خطأ وإن أريد بالشك الحكم بتساوي الطرفين فهو أحد الأقسام السابقة. فالإبصار علم بالمبصرات والسماع علم بالمسموعات، وهكذا البواقي ورده الجمهور بأنا نجد فرقاً ضرورياً بين العلم التام بهذا اللون، وبين إبصاره، وهكذا بين العلم بهذا الصوت وسماعه وبين العلم بهذه الرائحة وشمها. إلى غير ذلك. وأجيب بأنا لا نسلم ان ما يتعلق به الإحساس يمكن تعلق العلم به بطريق آخر، ولو سلم فيجوز أن يكونا علمين متخالفين بالماهية أو الهوية، وفيه ضعف. أما أولا فلأن إمكان تعلق علم آخر به ضروري. كيف وأنا نحكم عليه عدم الإحساس أيضاً. وأما ثانياً: فلأن مقصود الجمهور نفى أن تكون حقيقة إدراك الشيء بالحس حقيقة إدراكه المسمى بالعلم، بحيث لا تفاوت إلا في طريق الحصول كما في العلم بالشيء بطريق الاستدلال أو الإلهام، أو الحدس، وإما بعد تسليم كونهما نوعين مختلفين من الإدراك فيصير البحث لفظياً مبنياً على أن العلم اسم لمطلق الإدراك، أو النوع منه، والحق أن إطلاقه على الإحساس مخالف للعرف واللغة فإنه اسم لغيره من الإدراكات، وقد يخص بإدراك الكل، أو إدراك المركب فيسمى إدراك الجزئي أو إدراك البسيط معرفة وقد يخص العلم بأحد أقسام التصديق - أعني اليقين منه، وهو ما يقارن الجزم والمطابقة والثبات فيسمى غير الجازم ظناً، وغير المطابق جهلاً مركباً، وغير الثابت اعتقاد.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that if sensation and knowledge are both perceptions, they must be the same type of thing.
Correction: They are different in essence (mahiyya) or identity (huwiyya), and calling both 'knowledge' is a verbal choice.

Checkpoint
Why is the answer that sensation and knowledge could be two different knowledges considered weak?
A) Because it denies the possibility of knowledge without sensation.
B) Because it turns the dispute into a verbal one about the definition of knowledge.
C) Because it contradicts the examples given.
D) Because it is based on a false assumption.

Key Takeaway
The majority refutes al-Ash'ari by showing an intuitive difference between sensing and knowing; the debate reduces to a linguistic convention.

?

?

?
The author warns that calling sensation 'knowledge' is a linguistic error that leads to confusion. In your daily speech, where might you be using words carelessly, conflating what you sense with what you truly know? What change will you make today?
