Defining Certainty and Opinion
Author defines certainty and opinion in relation to their opposites and affirms they are simple beliefs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between certainty and opinion based on the judgment of the impossibility or possibility of the opposite, and why each is a simple belief.

Core Idea
Certainty judges the opposite impossible; opinion judges it possible—both are simple beliefs.

The author begins by contrasting two common views on certainty and opinion. Some scholars say certainty is always accompanied by a judgment that the opposite is impossible. But the verified view is more precise: certainty is the state where if you consider the opposite, you would judge it impossible. This lesson unpacks that distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening comparison
Notice how the author first reports the apparent wording of some scholars, then presents the verified view. This is a common pattern in al-Maqasid.
The contrast sets up the key distinction.
2
Understand the verified view
Focus on the condition 'if the opposite were brought to mind'. This shows that certainty is about the mental disposition, not a constant conscious judgment.
3
Recognize simplicity
The author emphasizes that both certainty and opinion are simple beliefs (i'tiqad basit). This means they are not composed of two separate judgments (like judging the proposition and judging the opposite).
This guards against a common misunderstanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم ظاهر عبارة البعض أن اليقين يقارن الحكم بامتناع النقيض، والظن الصرف يقارن الحكم بإمكان النقيض وإن كان مرجوحاً، لكن التحقيق هو أن المعتبر في اليقين أن يكون بحيث لو أخطر النقيض بالبال لحكم بامتناعه، وفي الظن أنه لو أخطر الحكم بإمكانه حتى إن كلاً منهما اعتقاد بسيط لا يتركب عن حكمين، واعترض على اعتبار الثبات في اليقين بأنه إن أريد به عسر الزوال فربما يكون اعتقاد المقلد كذلك، وإن أريد به امتناع الزوال فاليقين من النظريات قد يذهل الذهن عن بعض مباديه فيشك فيه، بل ربما يحكم بخلافه . والجواب أنه : إن أريد بالذهول مجرد عدم الحضور بالفعل عند العقل فإمكان طريان الشك حينئذ ممنوع ، وإن أريد الزوال بحيث يفتقر إلى تحصيل واكتساب فلا يقين حينئذ بالحكم النظري، ونحن إنما نحكم بامتناع الشك في اليقين مادام يقينا.
Then the apparent wording of some [scholars] is that certainty (yaqin) is accompanied by the judgment of the impossibility of its opposite, and pure opinion (zann) is accompanied by the judgment of the possibility of its opposite even if it is preponderated. But the verified [view] is that what is considered in certainty is that it be such that if the opposite were brought to mind, one would judge its impossibility; and in opinion, that if it were brought to mind, one would judge its possibility. So that each of them is a simple belief not composed of two judgments. An objection was raised against considering stability (thubat) in certainty: if what is meant by it is difficulty of removal, then perhaps the belief of the imitator (i'tiqad al-muqallid) is like that; and if what is meant by it is impossibility of removal, then certainty concerning theoretical matters may have the mind forget some of its premises, so doubt may occur in it, and perhaps one even judges contrary to it. The answer is: if by forgetfulness (dhuhul) is meant mere absence of actual presence in the mind, then the possibility of doubt occurring then is denied; and if what is meant is removal such that it requires acquisition and earning, then there is no certainty then concerning the theoretical judgment. We only judge the impossibility of doubt in certainty as long as it is certain.
Source pages p. 314

Commentary
This passage discusses the nature of certainty (yaqin) and its conditions. The author first reports that some scholars hold that certainty is accompanied by a judgment that the opposite is impossible, while opinion (zann) is accompanied by a judgment that the opposite is possible (even if unlikely). However, the author presents the verified view: certainty is such that if one were to consider the opposite, one would immediately judge it impossible; opinion is such that one would judge the opposite possible. Importantly, both are simple beliefs, not composed of two separate judgments (like a judgment about the proposition and a judgment about the opposite). An objection is then raised against including stability (thubat) as a condition of certainty: if stability means 'difficult to remove', then the belief of a blind imitator might also be stable; if it means 'impossible to remove', then theoretical certainties can be forgotten or doubted when one is unaware of their proofs. The author answers: if forgetfulness means the proposition is not currently present in the mind, then doubt does not occur because certainty is not actual; if forgetfulness means the belief has been lost and needs to be reacquired, then such a state is not certainty at that moment. The key point: we only affirm that doubt is impossible in certainty as long as it remains certainty.

Key Terms
يقين
ظن
جزم
نقيض
اعتقاد بسيط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think certainty is a compound judgment involving both the proposition and a separate judgment about the opposite.
Correction: The author states each is a simple belief (i'tiqad basit) not composed of two judgments.

Checkpoint
According to the author's verified view, what characterizes certainty?
A) It is always accompanied by a judgment that the opposite is possible.
B) It is a compound of two judgments.
C) If the opposite is brought to mind, one judges it impossible.
D) It is difficult to remove.

Key Takeaway
Certainty is the disposition to judge an opposite impossible, opinion the disposition to judge it possible, and both are simple mental states.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your belief in Allah, do you truly judge any opposite—such as atheism or polytheism—as impossible? Or do you sometimes allow it to be a mere possibility in your mind, even if unlikely? How would your daily actions change if you genuinely held that opposite as impossible? Commit to reviewing one belief this week and asking yourself: 'If I considered the opposite, would I judge it impossible or just unlikely?'
?
You likely claim certainty in the existence of God and the truth of Islam. But when was the last time you genuinely brought an opposite to mind and observed your reaction? If you never do, are you truly certain, or just comfortable? Challenge yourself this week to intentionally consider an opposite view (e.g., 'There is no God') and monitor your inner judgment. Does it feel impossible? If not, what steps will you take to strengthen that certainty?
?
The passage distinguishes between opinion and certainty based on how you would judge the opposite. Think of an area where you act on opinion (zann) rather than certainty—perhaps in matters of religious practice. Could that opinion be elevated to certainty? What would it take? Are you willing to seek the knowledge needed to transform that opinion into certainty, or are you content with mere conjecture?

intermediate
6 min
Stability as a Condition of Certainty
Author addresses the objection against stability as a condition of certainty and provides a nuanced answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why stability is considered a condition of certainty and how the author responds to the objection about forgetfulness.

Core Idea
Stability in certainty means the impossibility of doubt while the belief is held, not that it never fades.

The author now addresses an important objection: if we require stability (thubat) for certainty, either it means difficult to remove (which applies to blind imitation) or impossible to remove (which seems false for theoretical certainties subject to forgetfulness). The answer clarifies what stability really means and preserves the integrity of certainty.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
The objection presents two possible interpretations of stability: (1) difficult to remove, (2) impossible to remove. Each leads to a problem.
Notice the structure: 'if what is meant by it is X, then Y; if what is meant is Z, then W'.
2
Identify the two horns
First horn: if stability means difficulty of removal, even a blind imitator's belief might qualify. Second horn: if it means impossibility of removal, then theoretical certainties can be forgotten or doubted when premises slip from mind.
The objection targets the necessity of stability as a condition.
3
Understand the answer
The author distinguishes two meanings of forgetfulness (dhuhul). If it means mere absence from conscious awareness, then doubt does not occur because the belief is not actually present. If it means the belief has been lost and needs reacquisition, then that state is not certainty.
The key is that certainty is defined at the moment it is held; stability does not require permanence across time.
4
Grasp the conclusion
We only judge the impossibility of doubt in certainty as long as it remains certainty. This resolves the objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم ظاهر عبارة البعض أن اليقين يقارن الحكم بامتناع النقيض، والظن الصرف يقارن الحكم بإمكان النقيض وإن كان مرجوحاً، لكن التحقيق هو أن المعتبر في اليقين أن يكون بحيث لو أخطر النقيض بالبال لحكم بامتناعه، وفي الظن أنه لو أخطر الحكم بإمكانه حتى إن كلاً منهما اعتقاد بسيط لا يتركب عن حكمين، واعترض على اعتبار الثبات في اليقين بأنه إن أريد به عسر الزوال فربما يكون اعتقاد المقلد كذلك، وإن أريد به امتناع الزوال فاليقين من النظريات قد يذهل الذهن عن بعض مباديه فيشك فيه، بل ربما يحكم بخلافه . والجواب أنه : إن أريد بالذهول مجرد عدم الحضور بالفعل عند العقل فإمكان طريان الشك حينئذ ممنوع ، وإن أريد الزوال بحيث يفتقر إلى تحصيل واكتساب فلا يقين حينئذ بالحكم النظري، ونحن إنما نحكم بامتناع الشك في اليقين مادام يقينا.
Then the apparent wording of some [scholars] is that certainty (yaqin) is accompanied by the judgment of the impossibility of its opposite, and pure opinion (zann) is accompanied by the judgment of the possibility of its opposite even if it is preponderated. But the verified [view] is that what is considered in certainty is that it be such that if the opposite were brought to mind, one would judge its impossibility; and in opinion, that if it were brought to mind, one would judge its possibility. So that each of them is a simple belief not composed of two judgments. An objection was raised against considering stability (thubat) in certainty: if what is meant by it is difficulty of removal, then perhaps the belief of the imitator (i'tiqad al-muqallid) is like that; and if what is meant by it is impossibility of removal, then certainty concerning theoretical matters may have the mind forget some of its premises, so doubt may occur in it, and perhaps one even judges contrary to it. The answer is: if by forgetfulness (dhuhul) is meant mere absence of actual presence in the mind, then the possibility of doubt occurring then is denied; and if what is meant is removal such that it requires acquisition and earning, then there is no certainty then concerning the theoretical judgment. We only judge the impossibility of doubt in certainty as long as it is certain.
Source pages p. 314

Commentary
This passage discusses the nature of certainty (yaqin) and its conditions. The author first reports that some scholars hold that certainty is accompanied by a judgment that the opposite is impossible, while opinion (zann) is accompanied by a judgment that the opposite is possible (even if unlikely). However, the author presents the verified view: certainty is such that if one were to consider the opposite, one would immediately judge it impossible; opinion is such that one would judge the opposite possible. Importantly, both are simple beliefs, not composed of two separate judgments (like a judgment about the proposition and a judgment about the opposite). An objection is then raised against including stability (thubat) as a condition of certainty: if stability means 'difficult to remove', then the belief of a blind imitator might also be stable; if it means 'impossible to remove', then theoretical certainties can be forgotten or doubted when one is unaware of their proofs. The author answers: if forgetfulness means the proposition is not currently present in the mind, then doubt does not occur because certainty is not actual; if forgetfulness means the belief has been lost and needs to be reacquired, then such a state is not certainty at that moment. The key point: we only affirm that doubt is impossible in certainty as long as it remains certainty.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Stability means the belief never wavers or is never forgotten.
Correction: The author clarifies that stability only requires that while the belief is held, doubt is impossible; temporary absence or forgetfulness does not negate certainty at the time it is held.

Checkpoint
According to the author's answer, which scenario does NOT challenge certainty?
A) When a person forgets a premise and needs to re-learn it.
B) When a person's belief is difficult to remove despite being groundless.
C) When a person has a theoretical certainty but is not currently thinking of it.
D) When a person holds a belief that is impossible to remove.

Key Takeaway
Stability as a condition of certainty means that while the belief is actually held, doubt is impossible; temporary absence or loss does not undermine the certainty at the moment it is present.

What concrete step can you take today to reinforce your certainty without dismissing your doubts?
?

How does the notion of stability as 'impossibility of doubt while the belief is held' change the way you view your own spiritual journey and moments of conviction? Will you now be more patient with yourself during periods of uncertainty or forgetfulness?
?
The objection compares the certainty of a muqallid (blind follower) to genuine certainty based on proof. In your own faith, are there areas where you simply 'imitate' others without understanding the evidence? How does this lesson push you to seek a deeper, more grounded certainty that can withstand not only doubts but also the forgetfulness of proofs? Classification of Assent and Critique of Non-Decisive Categories
Author limits assent to four types and critiques the view including doubt and whim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's classification of assent into four types and why doubt and whim are not considered judgments but mere conceptualizations.

Core Idea
Doubt and whim are not assent; they are conceptualizations without judgment.

In this passage, the author presents his refined classification of assent, limiting it to knowledge, compound ignorance, sound belief, and opinion. He then critiques a common view that includes doubt and whim as types of non-decisive assent, arguing that they lack the essential element of judgment.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to grasp the argument.
2
Identify the author's classification
Note the four types of assent the author enumerates and the reasoning that non-decisive must be preponderant.
3
Understand the Imam's classification
See how the Imam divided non-decisive into three: opinion, doubt, whim.
4
Grasp the critique
The author argues that doubt and whim are conceptualizations, not judgments, so they cannot be assent.
This is the key insight: assent requires the soul's acceptance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن التصديق على ما ذكرنا ينحصر في العلم والجهل المركب والاعتقاد الصحيح والظن لأن غير الجازم لا بد أن يكون راجحاً لأنه أقل مراتب الحكم أعنى قبول النفس وإذعانها لوقوع النسبة أو لا وقوعها، وما ذكره الإمام وجمع من المتأخرين أن غير الجازم إما أن يكون راجحاً فظن، أو مساوياً فشك، أو مرجوجاً فوهم محل نظر لأن الشك تردد في الوقوع واللاوقوع، والوهم ملاحظة للطرف المرجوح وكلاهما تصور لا حكم معه أصلاً .
For assent (tasdiq), according to what we have mentioned, is limited to knowledge ('ilm), compound ignorance (jahl murakkab), sound belief (i'tiqad sahih), and opinion (zann), because what is non-decisive (ghayr jazim) must necessarily be preponderant (rajih), since it [assent] is the lowest rank of judgment (hukm), i.e., the soul's acceptance and submission to the occurrence or non-occurrence of a relation. And what the Imam [al-Ghazali?] and a group of later scholars mentioned — that non-decisive [assent] is either preponderant (rajih) so it is opinion, or equal (musawi) so it is doubt (shakk), or preponderated (marjuh) so it is whim (wahm) — is a matter for consideration, because doubt is hesitation between occurrence and non-occurrence, and whim is consideration of the preponderated side; both are conceptualizations (tasawwur) with no judgment (hukm) whatsoever.
Source pages p. 314

Commentary
This passage concludes the discussion on the types of assent (tasdiq). The author states that, based on what has been established, assent is limited to four categories: knowledge ('ilm), compound ignorance (jahl murakkab), sound belief (i'tiqad sahih), and opinion (zann). The reasoning is that non-decisive assent (ghayr jazim) must always be preponderant (rajih) — that is, the soul's acceptance is stronger on one side — because assent is essentially the lowest form of judgment: the soul's acceptance of a proposition. The author then critiques the view of Imam al-Ghazali (or the Imam) and some later scholars, who classified non-decisive assent into three: preponderant = opinion, equal = doubt (shakk), and preponderated = whim (wahm). The author argues that doubt and whim are not judgments at all, but rather pure conceptualizations (tasawwur) without any judgment. Doubt is hesitation, not a settled belief; whim is merely considering the weaker side. Since they lack the soul's acceptance (hukm), they should not be considered types of assent.

Correction: The author argues that doubt and whim are not judgments (hukm) but mere conceptualizations (tasawwur) without the soul's acceptance, so they cannot be assent.

Checkpoint

B) Because they are conceptualizations without judgment.
C) Because they are not stable.

Key Takeaway
Assent always involves a judgment (hukm); doubt and whim lack judgment, so they are not assent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Is Doubt a Judgment? The Objection and Its Answer
Answers the claim that shakk is a judgment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the act of doubting is not itself a rational judgment but an assent about one's own state.

Core Idea
Doubt is not a judgment; declaring doubt is an assent.

This short passage presents a sharp objection-and-answer exchange. An imaginary opponent claims that doubt (shakk) is a rational judgment of equality between two sides. The author counters that if that were true, then the statement 'I am in doubt' would itself be an assent (taṣdīq) about one's psychological state, which belongs to one of the four categories of assent. This clarifies the boundary between conceptualization and judgment.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the objector's statement: 'What is meant by doubt is the judgment of equality of the two sides according to the mind.' Notice how it defines shakk as a judgment.
2
Read the answer
Read the author's reply: 'We say: This is an assent that is one of the four categories...' Understand that the author is reclassifying the statement, not the doubt itself.
3
Analyze the example
The author gives an example: saying 'I am in doubt about the difference between sahw and nisyān.' This statement is an assertion about one's mental state, which is a judgment of assent, not the original doubt.
The doubt itself remains a conceptualization (tasawwur).
4
Connect to the broader theory
Relate this to the earlier division of rational judgments into judgment and non-judgment (conceptualization). This objection helps sharpen the distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : المراد بالشك (۱) الحكم بتساوى الطرفين عند العقل .
قلنا : هذا تصديق بكون أحد الأقسام الأربعة بمنزلة قولك : أنا شاك في الفرق بين السهو والنسيان
For if it is said: What is meant by doubt (shakk) is the judgment of equality of the two sides according to the mind. We say: This is an assent (taṣdīq) that is one of the four categories, like your saying: I am in doubt about the difference between forgetfulness (sahw) and oversight (nisyān).
Source pages p. 315

Commentary
This passage is a brief objection-and-answer. The objector defines shakk (doubt) as a judgment of equality between two opposing possibilities. The author replies that if that were so, it would be an assent (taṣdīq), which belongs to one of the four categories of assent already enumerated (knowledge, compound ignorance, correct belief, or conjecture). He gives an example: the statement 'I am in doubt about the difference between sahw and nisyān' is itself an assertion that one is in a state of doubt — it is a judgment about one's psychological state, not the doubt itself. This reinforces that doubt is not a judgment about the external proposition but a state of hesitation, which is a conceptualization (taṣawwur) rather than a judgment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Shakk (doubt) is itself a judgment.
Correction: The act of doubting is not a judgment; reporting a state of doubt is an assent about one's own mental state.

Checkpoint

B) He says that such a statement is an assent (taṣdīq) belonging to one of the four categories
C) He says that shakk is a conceptualization, not a judgment
D) He provides a different definition of shakk

Key Takeaway
The statement 'I am in doubt' is an assent about one's state, not the doubt itself, which remains a non-judgmental conceptualization.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Simple and Compound Ignorance
Defines shakk and jahl murakkab and their opposition to knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definitions of shakk and jahl murakkab and why compound ignorance is opposite to knowledge.

Core Idea
Compound ignorance is firm belief contrary to reality, combined with ignorance of that ignorance.

This lesson unpacks the foundational terms shakk (doubt) and jahl murakkab (compound ignorance). The author first clarifies that doubt is a lack of assertoric knowledge, then defines compound ignorance as a firm but false belief, explaining why it is the opposite of knowledge. Understanding these definitions is essential before engaging with the Mu'tazili arguments that follow.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of shakk
The passage begins with shakk: the absence of the faculty of tasdiqi (assertoric) knowledge. Note that shakk is considered simple ignorance in that respect, though it may be knowledge in terms of tasawwur (conception).
This sets the stage for distinguishing simple from compound ignorance.
2
Understand jahl murakkab
Read the definition of jahl murakkab: firm belief that does not correspond to reality. It is 'compound' because it involves ignorance of the actual state plus ignorance of one's own ignorance.
Memorize the two components: non-correspondence + unawareness of that non-correspondence.
3
Grasp the opposition to knowledge
The author explains why compound ignorance is opposite (did) to knowledge: they cannot coexist in the same subject by essence, and they are existential opposites.
This will be key for the Mu'tazili debate in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والشك عدم ملكة للعلم التصديقي، فيكون جهلاً بسيطاً بالنظر إليه، وإن كان علماً من حيث التصور.
وأما الجهل المركب أعني الاعتقاد الجازم الغير المطابق ويسمى مركبا لأنه جهل بما في الواقع مع الجهل بأنه جاهل به فمضاد للعلم لصدق حد الضدين عليهما لكونهما معنيين يستحيل اجتماعهما لذاتهما، ولكونهما متقابلين وجوديين ليس تعقل أحدهما بالقياس إلى تعقل الآخر.
وقالت المعتزلة : هما متماثلان لأن الحقيقة واحدة والاختلاف إنما هو بالعارض، أما أولاً فلأنهما لا يختلفان إلا بمطابقة الواقع ولا مطابقته، وذلك خارج لأن النسبة لا تدخل في حقيقة المنتسبين والاختلاف بالخارج لا يوجب الاختلاف بالذات. وأما ثانياً : فلأن من اعتقد أن زيداً في الدار طول النهار وقد كان فيها إلى قبل الظهر ، ثم خرج كان له اعتقاد واحد مستمر لا اختلاف في ذاته ، مع أنه كان علماً ثم صار جهلاً .
والجواب أن المطابقة واللامطابقة أخص صفات النفس للعلم والجهل، فالاختلاف فيه يستلزم الاختلاف في الذات وظاهره معارضة، ويمكن تنزيله على المنع أي لا نسلم أن الاختلاف بالعارض لا يوجب الاختلاف بالذات، وإنما يكون كذلك لو لم يكن لازماً، ولا نسلم أن الذات واحدة بل الاعتقادات على التجدد فما دام زيد في الدار فالمتجدد علم، وحين خرج فجهل .
And shakk is the absence of the faculty of tasdiqi knowledge, so it is simple ignorance with respect to it, even though it is knowledge in terms of tasawwur. As for compound ignorance (jahl murakkab), namely firm belief that does not correspond [to reality] — it is called 'compound' because it is ignorance of what is in reality along with ignorance that one is ignorant of it — it is opposite to knowledge because the definition of the two opposites applies to them, since they are two meanings whose simultaneous coexistence is impossible by their very essences, and because they are existential opposites (mutanqabidan wujudiyyan) such that the conception of one is not by reference to the conception of the other. The Mu'tazila said: they are similar because the reality is one and the difference is only by accident: first, because they differ only by correspondence with reality or the lack thereof, and that is external, since relation does not enter into the reality of the related things, and difference by the external does not necessitate difference in essence. Second, because one who believes that Zayd is in the house all day long, while he was there until before noon and then left, has a single continuous belief with no difference in its essence, though it was knowledge and then became ignorance. The answer: Correspondence and non-correspondence are the most specific attributes (akhas sifat) of the soul regarding knowledge and ignorance; thus difference in them necessitates difference in essence. And it appears to be a counterargument, but it can be reduced to denial: we do not concede that difference by accident does not necessitate difference in essence; it would be so only if it were not necessary (lazim). And we do not concede that the essence is one; rather, beliefs are renewed (mutajaddid) — as long as Zayd is in the house, what is renewed is knowledge, and when he left, it is ignorance.
Source pages p. 316

Commentary
This passage is a definition followed by a double objection-and-answer. It begins by clarifying that shakk (doubt) is the absence of the faculty of tasdiqi (assertoric) knowledge, so it is simple ignorance in that respect, even though it may be knowledge in terms of tasawwur (conception). Then the author defines jahl murakkab (compound ignorance): it is firm belief (i'tiqad jazim) that does not correspond to reality, and it is called 'compound' because it combines ignorance of the actual state with ignorance that one is ignorant. This compound ignorance is said to be the opposite (did) of knowledge because they satisfy the definition of two opposites: they are two meanings that cannot coexist in the same subject by their very essences, and they are existential opposites (mutanqabidan wujudiyyan) such that the conception of one is not dependent on the conception of the other. Next, the Mu'tazila present two arguments for their claim that knowledge and ignorance are similar (mumathilan). First, they argue that the difference between them is only an accident (arad): i.e., correspondence or non-correspondence with reality is external (kharij) to the essence, and external difference does not imply essential difference. Second, they give an example: a person believes Zayd is in the house all day, whereas Zayd left at noon; the belief remains one continuous belief in its essence, yet it was knowledge and then became ignorance, showing that the essence does not change. The author's answer refutes both: he states that correspondence and non-correspondence are the most specific attributes (akhas sifat) of the soul regarding knowledge and ignorance, so difference in them entails difference in essence. Regarding the second argument, he says it can be reduced to denial (man'): we do not concede that difference by accident does not necessitate difference in essence — it would only be so if the accident were not necessary (lazim), but here correspondence is a necessary attribute. Moreover, we do not concede that the essence is one; rather, beliefs are renewed (mutajaddid) at each moment — when Zayd is in the house, each moment's renewed belief is knowledge; when he leaves, the renewed belief is ignorance. Thus the essence is not the same across time.

Key Terms
شك
جهل مركب
اعتقاد جازم
ضد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that compound ignorance is simply not knowing something, or that doubt is a type of knowledge.
Correction: The passage clarifies that jahl murakkab is not mere ignorance but firm false belief; shakk is not knowledge in the assertoric sense but may be knowledge in conception.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is jahl murakkab called 'compound' (murakkab)?
A) Because it combines belief and ignorance.
B) Because it is ignorance of the actual state plus ignorance that one is ignorant.
C) Because it is composed of two opposite beliefs.
D) Because it is a mixture of doubt and knowledge.

Key Takeaway
Compound ignorance is firm belief that does not match reality, compounded by ignorance of that mismatch, making it the opposite of knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a belief about Allah or His attributes that you hold firmly. Could it be that this belief is actually compound ignorance? How would you even know if you were ignorant of your ignorance? What steps will you take today to ensure your beliefs correspond to what Allah has revealed, not just what feels right?
?
The passage says compound ignorance and knowledge cannot coexist. Is there an area of your deen where you feel certain but actually avoid deeper study? Will you commit to reading a source text this week to test that certainty?
?
Have you ever been certain about a matter of aqeedah, only to later realize you were mistaken? Did you at the time think you knew, but were unaware of your ignorance? How can you cultivate humility before knowledge so you are never content with false certainty?

Mu'tazili Claim of Similarity and Refutation
Presents two Mu'tazili arguments and the author's refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazili arguments that knowledge and ignorance are similar, and the author's responses showing they are opposites.

Core Idea
Correspondence to reality is an essential attribute, so difference in it entails difference in essence.

The Mu'tazila argued that knowledge and ignorance are essentially similar, differing only accidentally by correspondence to reality. They gave two arguments: one based on the externality of relation, and one based on a continuous belief that changes from knowledge to ignorance. The author refutes both by showing that correspondence is a necessary attribute and that beliefs are renewed moment by moment.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Mu'tazili position
The Mu'tazila claim knowledge and ignorance are similar (mumathilan) because their essence is one; difference is only by an accident (correspondence). Read their first argument: the relation of correspondence is external, so it doesn't affect essence.
This is a key theological debate about the nature of knowledge.
2
Examine the second argument
Consider the example of a person believing Zayd is in the house all day when he left at noon: the belief remains one continuous entity, yet it changed from knowledge to ignorance. This suggests the essence is unchanged.
This appeals to our intuition about the continuity of belief.
3
Analyze the author's refutation
The author responds: correspondence is the most specific attribute (akhas sifat) of the soul regarding knowledge and ignorance, so difference in it entails difference in essence. He also denies that the belief's essence remains one, because beliefs are renewed (mutajaddid) at each moment.
The concept of 'renewal' (tajaddud) is crucial here.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والشك عدم ملكة للعلم التصديقي، فيكون جهلاً بسيطاً بالنظر إليه، وإن كان علماً من حيث التصور.
وأما الجهل المركب أعني الاعتقاد الجازم الغير المطابق ويسمى مركبا لأنه جهل بما في الواقع مع الجهل بأنه جاهل به فمضاد للعلم لصدق حد الضدين عليهما لكونهما معنيين يستحيل اجتماعهما لذاتهما، ولكونهما متقابلين وجوديين ليس تعقل أحدهما بالقياس إلى تعقل الآخر.
وقالت المعتزلة : هما متماثلان لأن الحقيقة واحدة والاختلاف إنما هو بالعارض، أما أولاً فلأنهما لا يختلفان إلا بمطابقة الواقع ولا مطابقته، وذلك خارج لأن النسبة لا تدخل في حقيقة المنتسبين والاختلاف بالخارج لا يوجب الاختلاف بالذات. وأما ثانياً : فلأن من اعتقد أن زيداً في الدار طول النهار وقد كان فيها إلى قبل الظهر ، ثم خرج كان له اعتقاد واحد مستمر لا اختلاف في ذاته ، مع أنه كان علماً ثم صار جهلاً .
والجواب أن المطابقة واللامطابقة أخص صفات النفس للعلم والجهل، فالاختلاف فيه يستلزم الاختلاف في الذات وظاهره معارضة، ويمكن تنزيله على المنع أي لا نسلم أن الاختلاف بالعارض لا يوجب الاختلاف بالذات، وإنما يكون كذلك لو لم يكن لازماً، ولا نسلم أن الذات واحدة بل الاعتقادات على التجدد فما دام زيد في الدار فالمتجدد علم، وحين خرج فجهل .
And shakk is the absence of the faculty of tasdiqi knowledge, so it is simple ignorance with respect to it, even though it is knowledge in terms of tasawwur. As for compound ignorance (jahl murakkab), namely firm belief that does not correspond [to reality] — it is called 'compound' because it is ignorance of what is in reality along with ignorance that one is ignorant of it — it is opposite to knowledge because the definition of the two opposites applies to them, since they are two meanings whose simultaneous coexistence is impossible by their very essences, and because they are existential opposites (mutanqabidan wujudiyyan) such that the conception of one is not by reference to the conception of the other. The Mu'tazila said: they are similar because the reality is one and the difference is only by accident: first, because they differ only by correspondence with reality or the lack thereof, and that is external, since relation does not enter into the reality of the related things, and difference by the external does not necessitate difference in essence. Second, because one who believes that Zayd is in the house all day long, while he was there until before noon and then left, has a single continuous belief with no difference in its essence, though it was knowledge and then became ignorance. The answer: Correspondence and non-correspondence are the most specific attributes (akhas sifat) of the soul regarding knowledge and ignorance; thus difference in them necessitates difference in essence. And it appears to be a counterargument, but it can be reduced to denial: we do not concede that difference by accident does not necessitate difference in essence; it would be so only if it were not necessary (lazim). And we do not concede that the essence is one; rather, beliefs are renewed (mutajaddid) — as long as Zayd is in the house, what is renewed is knowledge, and when he left, it is ignorance.
Source pages p. 316

Commentary
This passage is a definition followed by a double objection-and-answer. It begins by clarifying that shakk (doubt) is the absence of the faculty of tasdiqi (assertoric) knowledge, so it is simple ignorance in that respect, even though it may be knowledge in terms of tasawwur (conception). Then the author defines jahl murakkab (compound ignorance): it is firm belief (i'tiqad jazim) that does not correspond to reality, and it is called 'compound' because it combines ignorance of the actual state with ignorance that one is ignorant. This compound ignorance is said to be the opposite (did) of knowledge because they satisfy the definition of two opposites: they are two meanings that cannot coexist in the same subject by their very essences, and they are existential opposites (mutanqabidan wujudiyyan) such that the conception of one is not dependent on the conception of the other. Next, the Mu'tazila present two arguments for their claim that knowledge and ignorance are similar (mumathilan). First, they argue that the difference between them is only an accident (arad): i.e., correspondence or non-correspondence with reality is external (kharij) to the essence, and external difference does not imply essential difference. Second, they give an example: a person believes Zayd is in the house all day, whereas Zayd left at noon; the belief remains one continuous belief in its essence, yet it was knowledge and then became ignorance, showing that the essence does not change. The author's answer refutes both: he states that correspondence and non-correspondence are the most specific attributes (akhas sifat) of the soul regarding knowledge and ignorance, so difference in them entails difference in essence. Regarding the second argument, he says it can be reduced to denial (man'): we do not concede that difference by accident does not necessitate difference in essence — it would only be so if the accident were not necessary (lazim), but here correspondence is a necessary attribute. Moreover, we do not concede that the essence is one; rather, beliefs are renewed (mutajaddid) at each moment — when Zayd is in the house, each moment's renewed belief is knowledge; when he leaves, the renewed belief is ignorance. Thus the essence is not the same across time.

لازم
متجدد

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that a belief that changes from true to false must be one continuous thing, so its essence must be the same.
Correction: The author argues that beliefs are renewed moment by moment, so the essence changes; there is no single continuous belief.

Checkpoint
How does the author refute the Mu'tazili argument that difference by accident does not necessitate difference in essence?
A) By conceding the point but offering a different explanation.
B) By denying that correspondence is an accident, and asserting it is a necessary attribute.
C) By saying that the Mu'tazila are heretics.
D) By giving a counterexample.

Key Takeaway
Correspondence to reality is a necessary attribute of knowledge, so any difference in it indicates a difference in essence, and beliefs are renewed moment by moment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever held a belief you were certain was knowledge, only to later discover it was ignorance? In that moment, did you recognize that your very self had changed, or did you assume it was the same belief all along? Are you willing to admit that your 'knowledge' is constantly being renewed—and that you are accountable for every single moment of that renewal?
?
If correspondence to reality is a necessary attribute of true knowledge, then your own beliefs must be constantly measured against the truth. When you act on a belief without verifying it, are you treating that belief as if it were knowledge even though it might be ignorance? What will you do today to check one of your firmest assumptions against reality?
?
The Mu'tazila argued that an external relation cannot affect essence. But the author insists that some attributes are so specific they define the essence. Think of your own soul: is your relationship with truth merely an external adornment, or does it define who you are? If your beliefs changed from knowledge to ignorance without your notice, would you be the same person?

Knowledge: Eternal and Temporal, with Three Ranks and Definitions
Classifies knowledge and defines key terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the division of knowledge into eternal and temporal, the three ranks of temporal knowledge, and the key definitions of knowledge and temporal occurrence as used in kalam.

Core Idea
Knowledge divides into God's eternal knowledge and temporal knowledge, which has three ranks: potential, actual in summary, and actual in detail.

This passage from al-Sanusi's work lays the foundational classification of knowledge. It distinguishes between the eternal knowledge of Allah and temporal human knowledge, then explains the three modes temporal knowledge can take. Finally, it provides technical definitions of 'knowledge' and 'temporal' to clarify the terms used.

Guided Path
1
Read the main classification
Read the first part of the Arabic passage where knowledge is divided into eternal and temporal. Notice that eternal knowledge belongs only to Allah.
Eternal here means not preceded by non-existence.
2
Understand the three ranks
Focus on the three ranks of temporal knowledge: potential (readiness), actual in summary (a simple principle for details), and actual in detail (distinct parts).
The example of looking at a page as a whole vs. letter by letter helps.
3
Analyze the example of the page
Consider the page example: in summary you have one form for the whole, in detail you have multiple forms for each part. This illustrates the two actual ranks.
The text also mentions an alternative understanding of summary and detail.
4
Grasp the definitions
Read the definitions of 'knowledge' and 'temporal' provided in the passage. Note that 'knowledge' has multiple technical senses and 'temporal' is distinguished from essential occurrence.
These definitions are important for precision in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الثالث: العلم ينقسم إلى قديم وحادث ومراتب الحادث ثلاث، لأنه إما بالقوة وهو الاستعداد فالضروري بالحواس وللنظري بالضروري، وإما بالفعل إجمالاً بأن يكون عنده أمر بسيط هو مبدأ التفاصيل، أو تفصيلاً بأن يلاحظ الأجزاء مفصلة، وذلك كما إذا نظر إلى الصحيفة جملة ثم حرفاً حرفاً، فالحاصل في الإجمال صورة واحدة تطابق الكل لا كل واحد، وفي التفصيلي ما قال الإمام أن الصورة الواحدة لا تطابق المختلفات، متعددة فيندفع صور والمتعددة تكون تفصيلاً. اللهم إلا أن يراد بالتفصيل حصولها مرتبة، وبالإجمال دفعة). إما قديم لا يسبقه العدم وهو علم الله تعالى وإما حادث سبقه العدم فهو. (1) العلم هو الإدراك مطلقاً تصوراً كان أو تصديقاً; يقيناً كان أو غير يقيني، وقد يطلق على التعقل» أو على حصول صورة الشيء في الذهن، أو على إدراك الكلي مفهوماً كان أو حكماً أو على الاعتقاد الجازم المطابق للواقع، أو على إدراك الشيء على ما هو به، أو على إدراك حقائق الأشياء وعللها، أو على إدراك المسائل على دليل أو على المكانة الحاصلة عن إدراك المسائل. (2) الحادث هو الواقع، وحدث أمر أي وقع والحادث عند فلاسفة العرب هو ما يكون مسبوقاً بالعدم، ويسمى حادثاً زمانياً، وفرقوا بين الحدوث الزماني، والحدوث الذاتي، فقالوا:
The third topic: Knowledge is divided into eternal and temporal. The ranks of the temporal are three: because it is either potential, which is readiness – the necessary by the senses and the theoretical by the necessary; or actual in summary, namely that one has a simple thing that is the principle of details; or in detail, namely that one observes the parts in detail, like when one looks at a page as a whole then letter by letter. What is obtained in summary is a single form that corresponds to the whole, not to each one; and in detail – the Imam said that the single form does not correspond to different things; they are multiple, so images are multiple and the multiple is detail. Unless by detail is meant its occurrence in an ordered manner, and by summary its occurrence all at once. Either eternal, not preceded by non-existence, which is the knowledge of God, or temporal, preceded by non-existence, then it is. (1) Knowledge is perception absolutely, whether conception or assertion, certain or not certain; and it may be applied to intellect, or to the obtaining of the form of a thing in the mind, or to the perception of the universal whether as concept or judgment, or to firm belief corresponding to reality, or to the perception of a thing as it is, or to the perception of the realities of things and their causes, or to the perception of problems based on evidence, or to the status resulting from the perception of problems. (2) The temporal is what occurs, and 'hathatha amr' means it occurred; and the temporal according to the Arab philosophers is what is preceded by non-existence, and it is called temporal in time; and they distinguished between temporal occurrence and essential occurrence, and they said:
Source pages p. 317

Commentary
This passage classifies knowledge and explains the ranks of temporal knowledge. The author states that knowledge divides into eternal (God's knowledge) and temporal. Temporal knowledge has three ranks: potential (readiness, like sensory or theoretical knowledge acquired through necessary premises), actual in summary (a simple principle from which details are derived), and actual in detail (distinct parts observed separately). The example of looking at a page as a whole (summary) vs. letter by letter (detail) illustrates this. The passage also notes an alternative interpretation that detail means ordered occurrence and summary means simultaneous occurrence. The text then restates the division into eternal and temporal, though the latter part is interrupted by footnotes defining 'knowledge' (various definitions) and 'temporal' (as preceded by non-existence, with a distinction between temporal and essential occurrence).

Distinguishing Possibility from Occurrence in Kalam
Al-Taftazani responds to an objection distinguishing between negation of possibility and negation of occurrence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between negation of possibility and negation of occurrence, and how the argument from homogeneity of sciences defends the Mu'tazila position.

Core Idea
Denying actual occurrence does not necessarily deny possibility; an external factor can prevent occurrence while preserving possibility.

This passage addresses a subtle objection against the Mu'tazila regarding transformation in knowledge of God. The objection claims that denying actual occurrence implies denying possibility, but the answer clarifies the difference and introduces the concept of homogeneity of sciences to show how possibility and occurrence can diverge.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the objection: 'If they deny actual occurrence, they must also deny possibility.' Notice how the objector conflates the two.
The objector assumes that if something cannot actually occur, it must be impossible.
2
Understand the answer's distinction
The answer explains that the Mu'tazila did not deny possibility. They admitted transformation is possible in itself, but its occurrence is prevented by an external factor: the theoretical obligation (taklif).
This external factor is key to separating possibility from actuality.
3
Grasp the homogeneity argument
The answer then presents an argument from the homogeneity of sciences: all sciences share the same essence, so what is possible for one is possible for another. This supports the claim that transformation is possible in itself for knowledge of God.
Al-Qadi (al-Baqillani) and some theologians used this argument.
4
Reflect on the broader significance
Consider how this distinction protects the Mu'tazila from the objection and illustrates a key method in kalam: using essential homogeneity to argue from one instance to another.
This argument is a classic example of qiyas al-gha'ib 'ala al-shahid (analogy from the unseen to the seen).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : فاللازم نفي الجواز دون مجرد الوقوع .
قلنا : ليس معنى كلامهم أن في العلم بالله تعالى الانقلاب جائز وليس بواقع ، بل إنه جائز نظراً إلى كونه علماً وإنما امتنع وقوعه لعارض من خارج هو كونه مكلفاً به نظرياً فجوزه القاضي وبعض المتكلمين لأن العلوم متجانسة أى متماثلة متفقة الماهية بناء على كون التعلق بالمعلومات والتشخص الحاصل بواسطة الخصوصيات من العوارض التي ليست مقتضى الذات، وإذا كانت متماثلة وحكم الأمثال واحد جاز على كل منها ما جاز على الآخر، كما جاز على الإنسانية التي في زيد ما جاز على التي في عمرو، بالنظر إلى نفس الإنسانية .
For it is said: The necessary implication is the negation of possibility, not mere occurrence. We say: Their words do not mean that regarding knowledge of God, transformation is possible but not actual. Rather, it is possible in view of it being knowledge, and its occurrence is prevented only by an external factor, which is that he is obligated to it theoretically. So al-Qadi and some theologians allowed it because the sciences are homogeneous, i.e., similar, agreeing in essence, based on the fact that the relation to known things and the individuation occurring by means of particularities are accidents that are not required by the essence. And when they are similar, the judgment of similar things is one, so whatever is possible for one is possible for the other, just as it is possible for the humanity in Zayd what is possible for the humanity in Amr, considering humanity itself.
Source pages p. 321

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer pair. An objector says: The Mu'tazila's denial of actual occurrence logically implies denial of possibility as well, so their argument is inconsistent. The answer clarifies that the Mu'tazila did not deny possibility; they admitted that transformation is possible in itself (since all sciences are homogeneous) but denied its actual occurrence in the specific case of knowledge of God due to an external factor: the servant is theoretically obligated to acquire it. The answer then elaborates the argument from homogeneity, used by al-Qadi (al-Baqillani) and some theologians, to show that since sciences share the same essence, whatever is possible for one is possible for another, just as humanity in Zayd and Amr is the same in essence.

Correction: The answer shows that an external factor can prevent occurrence while the thing remains possible in itself, so the two are distinct.

Checkpoint

C) The essence of knowledge of God is different.
D) Al-Qadi rejected the possibility.

Key Takeaway
The distinction between possibility and occurrence, and the role of external factors, is crucial for understanding the Mu'tazila argument and for kalam reasoning more broadly.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

What external factors in your life—like spiritual neglect or reliance on habit—are blocking that transformation, and what will you do about them starting today?
?
The passage shows that possibility can remain even when actual change hasn't happened. In which area of your relationship with Allah have you given up hope for change, assuming the possibility is gone? Are you willing to see that the obstacle is merely a pardon from outside, not an impossibility from within?
?
Al-Taftazani argues that sciences are homogeneous in essence. If you truly believe that your ability to know God is the same in essence as the prophets and saints, what prevents you from attaining that knowledge? What 'external factors' are you clinging to—such as pride, laziness, or distraction—that you must remove to let the possibility become actual?

The Mu'tazila's Argument for the Permissibility of Knowledge Conversion
Author presents the Mu'tazila's reliance on homogeneity to permit conversion of necessary to theoretical knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila's argument that necessary knowledge can become theoretical in principle due to the homogeneity of sciences, and the distinction they draw between permissibility and actual occurrence.

Core Idea
Homogeneity of sciences allows conversion in principle, but external factors block actual occurrence.

The Mu'tazila faced a puzzle: if necessary knowledge is fixed, how can it become theoretical? They argued that all sciences share a common essence, so conversion is rationally permissible. However, for certain objects like knowledge of God, they denied actual occurrence to avoid entailing an impossible obligation. This passage presents their reasoning and the clarification of al-Qāḍī.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully twice
First, read the Arabic and translation to get the overall flow. Then read again, noting the key terms: تجانس, جواز, وقوع.
The passage includes an objection and response, so pay attention to the argumentative structure.
2
Identify the Mu'tazila's main claim
Find the sentence where they state their reliance on homogeneity and the denial of occurrence for knowledge of God.
The denial is due to the repugnance of obligating what is not within power.
3
Notice the distinction between jawāz and wuqū‘
The objection says permissibility should be negated, but the answer clarifies that permissibility is about the nature of knowledge, not actual occurrence.
This distinction is crucial for understanding the Mu'tazila position.
4
Understand al-Qāḍī's view
Al-Qāḍī and some theologians allowed conversion based on homogeneity, arguing that accidental attributes do not affect the essence.
They used the analogy of humanity in different individuals.
5
Synthesize the lesson
Write a one-sentence summary of how the Mu'tazila used homogeneity to permit conversion while denying actual occurrence in specific cases.
This will help consolidate the learning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تعالى في العبد علماً ضرورياً (۱) متعلقاً بما يتعلق به علمه النظري . والمعتزلة عولوا فى الجواز على تجانس العلوم، ومنعوا الوقوع فيما يكون به كالعلم بالله وصفاته المقدسة لئلا يلزم التكليف بغير المقدور وأنه قبيح يمتنع وقوعه من الله تعالى . مكلفاً فإن قيل : فاللازم نفي الجواز دون مجرد الوقوع . قلنا : ليس معنى كلامهم أن فى العلم بالله تعالى الانقلاب جائز وليس بواقع ، بل إنه جائز نظراً إلى كونه علماً وإنما امتنع وقوعه لعارض من خارج هو كونه مكلفاً به نظرياً فجوزه القاضي وبعض المتكلمين لأن العلوم متجانسة أى متماثلة متفقة الماهية بناء على كون التعلق بالمعلومات والتشخص الحاصل بواسطة الخصوصيات من العوارض التي ليست مقتضى الذات، وإذا كانت متماثلة وحكم الأمثال واحد جاز على كل منها ما جاز على الآخر، كما جاز على الإنسانية التي في زيد ما جاز على التي في عمرو، بالنظر إلى نفس الإنسانية .
He, exalted, in the servant necessary knowledge (1) related to what his theoretical knowledge is related to. And the Mu'tazila relied, regarding permissibility, on the homogeneity of the sciences, and prevented the occurrence in what would be like knowledge of God and His holy attributes, lest it entail obligation of what is not within power and that it is repugnant whose occurrence from God, exalted, is impossible. Mukallafan. If it is said: Then the necessary implication is negation of permissibility, not mere non-occurrence. We say: The meaning of their statement is not that conversion in knowledge of God, exalted, is permissible but not actual; rather, it is permissible insofar as it is knowledge, but its occurrence is prevented by an external impediment, which is its being theoretically obligatory. Thus al-Qāḍī and some theologians allowed it because the sciences are homogeneous, i.e., similar, agreeing in essence, based on the fact that relation to known things and individuation attained through particularities are accidental attributes that are not required by the essence. And if they are similar and the rule of similars is one, then whatever is permissible for one of them is permissible for the other, just as what is permissible for the humanity in Zayd is permissible for that in 'Amr, with respect to humanity itself.
Source pages p. 321

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazila's argument that necessary knowledge can in principle become theoretical because all sciences are homogeneous in essence. They deny actual occurrence for specific cases like knowledge of God due to external factors (obligation beyond power). The author then clarifies the distinction between permissibility (jawāz) and actual occurrence (wuqū‘). Al-Qāḍī and some theologians allow conversion based on homogeneity, arguing that accidental attributes like relation and individuation do not affect the essence. The author follows the argumentative order: first states the Mu'tazila position, then an objection that permissibility should be negated, then the answer explaining that permissibility is causal not actual.

علم ضروري
علم نظري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if the Mu'tazila deny actual occurrence of conversion for knowledge of God, they must also deny its permissibility.
Correction: The passage clarifies that they consider conversion permissible in essence (based on homogeneity) but prevented by an external impediment (the obligation being beyond power). Thus permissibility remains, even if occurrence does not happen.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila argued that necessary knowledge can theoretically become theoretical due to homogeneity, but they denied actual occurrence for certain objects to avoid impossible obligation, a distinction clarified by al-Qāḍī.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Mu'tazila argued that your knowledge of God could theoretically become theoretical, but they stopped short of actual occurrence. If your certainty about God were to become a matter of reasoning, would your faith survive the transition? What would that say about the foundation of your belief?
?
You assume your knowledge of God is necessary and unshakable. But this lesson forces you to consider: could your ‘necessary’ knowledge be converted if circumstances changed? Are you relying on a type of knowledge that you have never tested?
?
Al-Qāḍī allowed conversion because all sciences are homogeneous. If your knowledge of God is subject to the same rules as mundane knowledge, are you prepared to treat it with the same rigor? What would change in your daily life if you actively reasoned about God instead of passively believing?

The Two Senses of Permissibility and the Homogeneity Misunderstanding
Author distinguishes two senses of jawaz and refutes al-Āmidī's homogeneity objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between absolute permissibility and permissibility relative to essence, and how al-Āmidī's misunderstanding of homogeneity is refuted.

Core Idea
Permissibility is not automatically inherited from homogeneity; accidents can prevent it.

In his second answer, the author clarifies what 'permissibility' means after conceding homogeneity. He distinguishes two senses of jawaz and argues that mere commonality in genus does not guarantee permissibility. He then criticizes al-Āmidī for adopting the philosophers' definition of homogeneity, which leads to a mistaken objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
2
Identify the two senses of permissibility
Look for the two phrases: 'إن أريد بالجواز عدم الامتناع أصلا' and 'وإن أريد عدم الامتناع نظراً إلى ماهية العلم'. These define the two senses.
The first is absolute; the second is relative to essence.
3
Understand al-Āmidī's mistake
Find where the author mentions al-Āmidī's understanding of homogeneity as 'الاشتراك في الجنس على ما هو مصطلح الفلاسفة'. Note that the author rejects this.
Theologians have their own terminology.
4
Reflect on the author's critique
Notice that the author points out that al-Qāḍī does not use genus-commonality as a proof. Ask yourself: what does this imply about the validity of al-Āmidī's objection?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب بعد تسليم التجانس أنه إن أريد بالجواز عدم الامتناع أصلا فمجرد التجانس لا يقتضيه لجواز أن يمتنع بواسطة العوارض والخصوصيات على البعض ما يجوز للبعض الآخر. وإن أريد عدم الامتناع نظراً إلى ماهية العلم فغير متنازع وما ذكر الامدي أنه لو سلم التجانس فلا شك فى الاختلاف بالنوع والشخص، فلعل من التنوع أو التشخص يمنع ذلك مبني على أنه فهم من التجانس الاشتراك في الجنس على ما هو مصطلح الفلاسفة ولا أدري كيف ذهب عليه مصطلح المتكلمين، وأن مثل القاضي لا يجعل الاشتراك في الجنس دليلا على أن يجوز على كل من المشتركين ما يجوز على الآخر، والجمهور على أن الضروري لا يجوز أن ينقلب نظرياً وإلا لزم جواز خلو نفس المخلوق عنه التوجه والالتفات وسائر شرائط حصول الضروريات لأن ذلك من لوازم النظريات وعلى هذا لا يرد الاعتراض بأن الضروري قد لا يحصل لفقد شرط أو استعداد إلا أنهم عولوا فى استحالة الخلو عن الضروري على الوجدان وفيه ضعف لأن غايته الدلالة على عدم الخلو دون استحالته، سلمنا لكن لاخفاء (۳) أن الخلو عن الضروري إنما يمتنع مادام ضرورياً، وبعد الانقلاب لا يبقى هذا الوصف . وذهب إمام الحرمين وهو أحد قولي القاضي إلى أنه لا يجوز في ضروري هو شرط لكمال العقل الذى به يستأصل (۳) لاكتساب النظريات لأنه لو انقلب نظرياً لزم كونه شرطاً لنفسه، وهو دور.
The answer, after conceding homogeneity, is that if by permissibility is meant absolute non-impossibility, then mere homogeneity does not necessitate it, because it is possible that it is prevented by accidents and particularities for some while being permissible for others. And if by permissibility is meant non-impossibility with respect to the essence of knowledge, then that is not disputed. And what al-Āmidī mentioned – that if homogeneity is conceded, there is no doubt about difference in species and individual, perhaps it is from diversity or individuation that prevents it – is based on his understanding of homogeneity as commonality in genus according to the terminology of the philosophers, and I do not know how the terminology of the theologians escaped him, and that someone like al-Qāḍī does not make commonality in genus a proof that what is permissible for one of the common members is permissible for the other. And the majority hold that the necessary cannot become theoretical, otherwise it would necessitate the permissibility of the soul of the created being being empty of attention, focus, and the rest of the conditions for obtaining necessary knowledge, because that is among the requirements of theoretical knowledge. And on this, the objection that the necessary may not be obtained due to lack of a condition or preparedness does not apply. However, they relied for the impossibility of being empty of the necessary on immediate awareness (wijdān), and there is weakness in that, because its utmost is indication of non-emptiness, not its impossibility. We concede, but it is not hidden that emptiness of the necessary is only impossible as long as it is necessary; after conversion, this description no longer remains. And Imām al-Ḥaramayn, which is one of the two opinions of al-Qāḍī, held that it is not permissible in a necessary that is a condition for the perfection of the intellect by which one becomes qualified to acquire theoretical knowledge, because if it became theoretical, it would entail being a condition for itself, which is a vicious circle (dawr).
Source pages p. 322

Commentary
This lesson presents the second answer after conceding homogeneity, clarifying the meaning of permissibility. The author distinguishes two senses of jawāz: absolute non-impossibility vs. non-impossibility with respect to essence. He criticizes al-Āmidī's misunderstanding of the theologians' concept of homogeneity. The majority view is presented: necessary knowledge cannot become theoretical, otherwise it would lead to the possibility of the soul being empty of necessary preconditions. The author notes a weakness in relying on immediate awareness (wijdān) for impossibility. Finally, Imām al-Ḥaramayn's special argument: for necessary knowledge that is a condition for intellectual perfection, conversion would lead to circularity (dawr). The commentary follows the sequential arguments.

Key Terms
جواز
تجانس
عوارض
ماهية العلم
فلاسفة
متكلمون
قاضي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If two things share a genus, whatever is permissible for one is permissible for the other.
Correction: Accidental particularities can prevent something that is permissible in essence from being actually permissible.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the result of al-Āmidī's misunderstanding of homogeneity?
A) He correctly proves conversion impossible
B) He mistakenly adopts the philosophers' definition
C) He relies on immediate awareness
D) He agrees with the majority

Key Takeaway
Homogeneity alone does not guarantee permissibility; one must consider accidental impediments and the specific sense of jawaz.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have seen how al-Āmidī fell into error by assuming that the philosopher's definition of homogeneity applies to theological discourse. Is there a framework, ideology, or intellectual habit you've adopted from outside that you now impose on your understanding of Allah and His attributes? What would it cost you to abandon that borrowed lens and submit to the Qur'an and Sunnah's own terminology?
?
The author teaches that accidents ('awariḍ) can prevent what is essentially permissible. Think of your soul: what 'accidents' of pride, desire, or heedlessness have you allowed to block you from receiving the mercy and guidance that is, in itself, entirely possible for you? Will you remove them today by sincere repentance?
?
Homogeneity does not guarantee identical permissions. Are there people you consider 'like you' in faith or practice, yet you judge them differently in matters of what is permissible for them? How might this lesson correct your unfairness, and will you commit to evaluating each case on its own merits before Allah?

The Majority View and Imam al-Haramayn's Circularity Argument
Author presents the majority view and Imam al-Haramayn's circularity argument

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the majority argument against conversion, the weakness of relying on wijdan, and Imam al-Haramayn's circularity argument for specific necessary knowledge.

Core Idea
Necessary knowledge cannot become theoretical because it would require impossible emptiness or lead to circularity.

The author now turns to the majority position: necessary knowledge cannot become theoretical, otherwise the soul could be empty of its preconditions. He notes a weakness in the majority's reliance on immediate awareness (wijdan) to prove impossibility. Finally, he presents Imam al-Haramayn's special argument: for necessary knowledge that is a condition for intellectual perfection, conversion would create a vicious circle.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
2
Understand the majority argument
Look for the phrase 'والجمهور على أن الضروري لا يجوز أن ينقلب نظرياً'. This is the thesis. Then note the consequence: 'خلو نفس المخلوق عن التوجه والالتفات'. Ask yourself why this consequence is problematic.
The consequence is that the soul could be empty of necessary preconditions.
3
Analyze the objection and response
Find where the author mentions an objection about missing conditions and the response using 'الوجدان'. Notice the author's critique that wijdan only indicates non-emptiness, not impossibility.
4
Learn Imam al-Haramayn's argument
Read the last sentence about 'إمام الحرمين'. He argues that conversion would lead to a vicious circle (دور). Understand why this applies specifically to knowledge that is a condition for intellectual perfection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Immediate awareness (wijdan) proves the impossibility of emptiness.
Correction: Wijdan only proves actual non-emptiness, not impossibility.

Checkpoint
Why does Imam al-Haramayn argue that conversion is impossible for necessary knowledge that is a condition for intellectual perfection?
A) Because it would violate homogeneity
B) Because it would lead to a vicious circle
C) Because it is impossible by nature
D) Because the majority said so

Key Takeaway
The majority argues from the impossibility of emptiness, but the strongest case may be Imam al-Haramayn's circularity argument for condition-necessary knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'I know this is true in my heart,' are you relying on wijdan without checking if your certainty is logically sound? Could your comfort with 'just knowing' be covering a weakness in your understanding?
?
Imagine a foundational belief you hold—one that feels necessary for your faith. Would you be willing to subject it to the same rigorous analysis that Imam al-Haramayn applied to necessary knowledge? What would you do if you found it rested on circular reasoning?
?
If your intellect's perfection depends on certain necessary truths, and you have never examined whether those truths could become theoretical, then are you building your worldview on unchecked premises? Will you, from now on, investigate which of your beliefs are truly necessary and which are merely assumed?
